Jerusalem Talmud Sotah



Chapter 1



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If a man declares his jealousy<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">If a man suspects his wife of infidelity, he may subject her to the ordeal described in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5" href="/Numbers.5"><i>Num.</i> 5</a>. Since the ordeal involves a sacrifice and it is forbidden to bring profane food into the Temple precinct, the wife has to be warned in due form not to associate with the man the husband suspects to be his wife’s paramour. His right to bring his wife to the Temple can be established only by the testimony of two witnesses of good standing.</i> to his wife, Rebbi Eliezer says, he declares his jealousy in front of two [witnesses] and takes her to drink<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">I. e., he takes her to the Temple precinct for the ordeal.<br>It will be explained later that from the moment the husband establishes his right to submit his wife to the Temple ordeal, any intercourse with her is forbidden to him until she is cleansed by the ordeal. This means that without a Temple, the wife will be forbidden after the jealousy was declared and the husband has to divorce his wife and can never take her back. If he can establish the fact that she was alone with another man after being duly warned, the divorce would be the wife’s fault and no payments would be due at the time of the divorce.</i> by the testimony of one witness or his own testimony<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">In the interpretation of the Mishnah, R. Eliezer holds that once the wife was warned in due form, the fact that she had a tête-à-tête with the suspect does not need a formal proof; the husband can declare to the priest in the Temple that he saw the two together.</i>. Rebbi Joshua says, he declares his jealousy in front of two [witnesses] and takes her to drink by the testimony of two [witnesses]<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">R. Joshua requires that both the warning to the wife and the fact of a subsequent tête-à-tête have to be established in a form acceptable to the priests’ court in the Temple.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If a man declares his jealousy to his wife,” etc. It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:14" href="/Numbers.5.14"><i>Num.</i> 5:14</a>.</i>: “A spirit of jealousy overcame him and he declared his jealousy to his wife;” this implies that he should not declare his jealousy jokingly, or in the middle of a conversation, or lightly, or in a friendly manner<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The witnesses to the declaration of jealousy must be able to testify that the wife could not possibly have had the impression that the husband was less than absolutely serious.</i> but in a way that inspires fear. If he transgressed and declared his jealousy in one of the aforementioned ways: was this said as an obligation or as a necessity? If you say as an obligation, his declaration of jealousy is valid<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">While he did not do what he was supposed to do, his dereliction would not invalidate his declaration.</i>. If you say as a necessity, his declaration of jealousy is invalid. This is resolved referring to the statement<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Accepted also in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 19a" href="/Menachot.19a">Babli, <i>Menaḥot</i> 19a</a>. Any procedure that is described as תּוֹרָה or חֻקָּה in the Torah must be executed to the letter or it is invalid; quoted also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 2:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.2.3.2"><i>Soṭah</i> 2:3</a> (18a), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 7:2:2-11:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.7.2.2-11.4"><i>Pesaḥim</i> 7:2</a> (34b).<br>It follows that for the Yerushalmi, any declaration of jealousy that is not executed in a spirit of gravity is invalid.</i>: Anywhere “law” or “teaching” is mentioned, it is an absolute necessity.
The declaration of jealousy, Rebbi Joshua said in the name of Rebbi Eliezer, is obligatory<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">If the husband has reason to suspect his wife, he has no choice but is obligated to declare his jealousy. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 3a" href="/Sotah.3a">Babli, 3a</a>, the position of R. Eliezer is ascribed to R. Aqiba and that of R. Joshua to R. Ismael. Since R. Joshua is the greater authority than R. Eliezer and R. Aqiba is greater than R. Ismael, in practice the Babli takes the position opposite to the Yerushalmi.</i>; but Rebbi Joshua said, it is optional. Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Yose said before Rebbi Yasa, it turns out that Rebbi Eliezer follows the House of Shammai and Rebbi Joshua the House of Hillel<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 9:8:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.9.8.4">Mishnah <i>Giṭṭin</i> 9:11</a>; <i>Sifry Deut</i>. #269.</i>. As the House of Shammai say, a man may not divorce his wife except if he found in her a matter of nakedness<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Illegitimate nakedness: incest and adultery.</i>. If he found her behavior ugly<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">“Ugly things” in a moral sense, from which one may presume that she was unfaithful but there is no proof. The Babli uses כעור in the same sense. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 7:6:2-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.7.6.2-7"><i>Ketubot</i> 7:7</a> (fol. 31c), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 24b-25a" href="/Yevamot.24b-25a">Babli <i>Yebamot</i> 24b/25a</a> an example is given, that the husband comes home and sees a pedlar leave the house and his wife getting dressed. Our sources indicate that in such a case the husband may divorce his wife without paying, claiming the divorce is his wife’s fault. It is not mentioned that in the absence of witnesses to the presumed sex act, the House of Shammai prohibit the divorce. This may qualify the entire argument as homiletic.</i>, he cannot divorce her since he found no proof of adultery; he cannot keep her since he found her behavior ugly. Therefore, he says, it is obligatory. But was it not stated in the name of the House of Shammai: Not only that the woman must leave because of incest; from where that she must leave if her head’s [hair] is loose, if the side seams of her dress are open, or her arms stripped bare<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 90" href="/Gittin.90">Babli, <i>Giṭṭin</i> 90a/b</a>, this is a statement of R. Meïr, following the House of Hillel.</i>? The verse says, “for he found in her a matter of nakedness.” Rebbi Mana said, I confirmed this if there are witnesses, but not if there are no witnesses<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">If there are independent witnesses to the indecent behavior (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 1:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.1.1.3">Note 12</a>), the House of Shammai agree that the husband must divorce his wife; but in the case of a declaration of jealousy they might not permit a divorce except if the ordeal proves the wife guilty.</i>.
“And Rebbi Joshua [follows] the House of Hillel.” For the House of Hillel say, even if she burned his food<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 9:8:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.9.8.4">Mishnah <i>Giṭṭin</i> 9:11</a>; <i>Sifry Deut</i>. #269.</i>. Therefore, he says it is optional. If he wants to declare his jealousy, he declares his jealousy; if he wants to divorce her, he divorces her<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 7:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.7.6.1">Mishnah <i>Ketubot</i> 7:6</a> spells out that the husband can divorce his wife for cause (without payment) if he can prove indecent conduct. If he divorces her because she is a bad cook, he has to pay.</i>.
What is Rebbi Eliezer’s reason? “For he found in her a word of nakedness.<sup class="footnote-marker">16”</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:1" href="/Deuteronomy.24.1">Deut. 24:1</a>.</i>. “Nakedness”, that is the rendez-vous<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">“Meeting in secret” with another man.</i>. “Word”, that is the declaration of jealousy. “Word, word”; just as “word” mentioned there requires two witnesses<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 19:15" href="/Deuteronomy.19.15"><i>Deut.</i> 19:15</a>: “By the mouth of two witnesses or three witnesses shall a <i>word</i> (fact, circumstance) be confirmed.” The argument is based on the second hermeneutical rule (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Braita d'Rabbi Yishmael" href="/Sifra,_Braita_d'Rabbi_Yishmael"><i>Sifra</i> Introduction</a>) that the meaning of words is unchanged from one occurrence to the other.<br>In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 90a" href="/Gittin.90a">Babli, <i>Giṭṭin</i> 90a</a>, this is quoted as the argument of the House of Shammai.</i>, so “word” mentioned here requires two witnesses. “But he can take her to drink by the testimony of one witness or his own testimony,” “she was untrue to him in untruthfulness<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:12" href="/Numbers.5.12"><i>Num</i>. 5:12</a>. The root מעל denotes the sin of embezzlement or fraud (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:15" href="/Leviticus.5.15"><i>Lev</i>. 5:15</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:6" href="/Numbers.5.6"><i>Num</i>. 5:6</a>; cf. Arabic مغالة) or general mischief (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 26:40" href="/Leviticus.26.40"><i>Lev</i>. 26:40</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ezekiel 18:24" href="/Ezekiel.18.24"><i>Ez</i>. 18:24</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ezra 9:4" href="/Ezra.9.4">Ezr. 9:4</a>; cf. Arabic معالة).</i>”, about the conditions spelled out to her through the mouth of others<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">In this interpretation, מעל refers to fraud as breach of contract; it does not imply unfaithfulness of the wife, as explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:14" href="/Numbers.5.14"><i>Num</i>. 5:14</a>. Therefore, the breach of contract is that the wife went to a rendez-vous with the man forbidden to her by the declaration of jealousy in front of two witnesses. The clause (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:12" href="/Numbers.5.12"><i>Num</i>. 5:12</a>) that she breached her contract <i>with him</i> is read to mean that he is empowered to prosecute this breach before the Temple priests.</i>.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 2b" href="/Sotah.2b">Babli 2b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sotah (Lieberman) 1:1" href="/Tosefta_Sotah_(Lieberman).1.1">Tosephta 1:1</a>.</i>: “Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah says in the name of Rebbi Eliezer, he declares his jealousy by the testimony of one witness or his own testimony; he takes her to drink on the testimony of two witnesses.” It is said here: “For he found in her a word of nakedness<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:1" href="/Deuteronomy.24.1"><i>Deut.</i> 24:1</a>.</i>”; “finding” anywhere has to be validated by witnesses<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Everywhere the Torah uses the expression כִּי מָצָא, כִּי יִמָּצֵא “if it happened” the implication is that the case must be heard before a duly constituted court.</i>. How does Rebbi Eliezer explain “a word”? A circumstance that brings into the habit of nakedness<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">A “word (or case, matter) of nakedness” is not nakedness itself.</i>. May a single witness let him take her to drink<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">This seems to refer to the first version (the Mishnah) of R. Eliezer’s position. The earlier argument (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 1:1:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.1.1.5">Note 20</a>) showed that the husband is empowered to bring his wife to the Temple on his own testimony; one still needs an argument to permit a single witness to a secluded rendez-vous to allow the husband to bring his wife to the Temple.</i>? His own testimony can never obligate him to swear in money matters but it will let him take her to drink<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">R. David Fraenckel points out that this sentence is ambiguous. It either can mean that a person’s own word in money matters can never bring onto him a biblical obligation to swear, for if he agrees that he owes money he pays and if he disputes the [entire] claim and the other party has neither witnesses nor documents, no oath is due. But it also may mean that a statement of a claimant can never obligate the defendant to swear.</i>, a single witness who will obligate him to swear in money matters<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 35:30" href="/Numbers.35.30"><i>Num</i>. 35:30</a>: “A single witness should not testify in a death penalty case” is interpreted to mean that in money matters a single witness in support of a claim of money obligates the defendant to swear (Sifry <i>Num</i>. 161; the argument here is hinted at in Sifry <i>Deut</i>. 188).</i> not so much more? May a relative bring her to drink<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">No relative can be a witness in court in any proceeding. Therefore, it is most irregular that the husband’s word should carry any weight in any proceedings.</i>? Who is a closer relative than her husband? May a hearsay witness bring her to drink? What is the difference between him and a relative? A relative may be disabled today but he might be enabled later<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">No relative can be a witness in court in any proceeding. Therefore, it is most irregular that the husband’s word should carry any weight in any proceedings.</i>, a hearsay witness is not acceptable either today or later<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">While a woman may rely on hearsay testimony about her husband’s death (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 16:5:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.16.5.2-5"><i>Yebamot</i> 16:5</a> ff.), this is an extra-judicial proceeding. In judicial proceedings, hearsay testimony is strictly excluded.</i>.
What is the reason of Rebbi Joshua<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 2b" href="/Sotah.2b">Babli, 2b</a>, the argument attributed to R. Joshua refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:13" href="/Numbers.5.13"><i>Num</i>. 5:13</a>: “There is no witness <i>against her</i>”, meaning that a single witness to her adultery will make the Temple ceremony superfluous and will make her lose the <i>ketubah</i> money otherwise due to her in a divorce, but will not be valid either to prove a declaration of jealousy or a secluded rendez-vous.</i>? ““For he found in her a word of nakedness.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:1" href="/Deuteronomy.24.1"><i>Deut.</i> 24:1</a>.</i>” “Word”, that is the declaration of jealousy. “For he found”; “finding” has to be validated by witnesses<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Everywhere the Torah uses the expression כִּי מָצָא, כִּי יִמָּצֵא “if it happened” the implication is that the case must be heard before a duly constituted court.</i>. (“But he can take her to drinkby the testimony of one witness or his own testimony,” “she was untrue to him in untruthfulness”.)<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">The sentence in parentheses has been copied in error from the arguments of R. Eliezer.</i> How does Rebbi Joshua explain “nakedness”? [A prohibition of] nakedness that came because of a word<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Because of his word, the declaration of jealousy, she becomes forbidden to him even though there is no witness to her adultery.</i>.
They answered on the words of Rebbi Yose ben Jehudah: If so, there is no end to it. If he is believed, he declares his jealousy (before two) [by himself]<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">The text (in parentheses) is an obvious error but it appears also in the Rome ms. The translation [in brackets] is a conjectured emendation.<br>The problem is that according to R. Yose ben R. Jehudah’s version, the husband may state that he had declared his jealousy without the wife having a possibility of disproving his claim. He may then plant witnesses and trick her into being alone with another man in order to divorce her without payment.</i>, brings witnesses that she was in a secluded place and disables her from receiving the <i>ketubah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">The marriage contract specifying the monetary obligations of the husband towards his wife and her children. The same argument is given in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 2b" href="/Sotah.2b">Babli, 2b</a>.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, what business did she have in a secluded place<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Being alone with another man is misbehaving. It is stated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 4:12:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.4.12.1">Mishnah <i>Qiddushin</i> 4:12</a> that a woman, other than his mother or daughter, may not be alone with man to whom she is not married (unless the latter’s wife is close by). Why should the husband not be able to divorce her through her fault?</i>? But it refers to the statement in the Mishnah<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah’s version of R. Eliezer’s position. In R. Yose ben R. Jehudah’s version, at least the fact of the wife’s misbehavior must be established in court by two witnesses subject to cross-examination. But in the Mishnah’s version, two witnesses are needed only to certify the relatively minor fact of the declaration of jealousy but after that, the husband can deprive the wife of her <i>ketubah</i> money simply by stating that she misbehaved. This contradicts all rules of procedure in civil cases. The same argument is given in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 2b" href="/Sotah.2b">Babli, 2b</a>.</i>. Is he believed to declare formally his jealousy and then say that she was in a secluded place and disable her from receiving her <i>ketubah</i>? Rebbi Mana said, even for that other Tanna<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">R. Yose ben R. Jehudah.</i> it is [a] valid [objection], following him who says that he may declare his jealousy in respect to her father or her son. Is he believed to declare his jealousy with respect to her father and her son, can he then bring witnesses that she was in a secluded place with them and disable her from receiving her <i>ketubah</i>? If you ask, what business did she have in a secluded place? The Torah permitted her to be in a secluded place, since Rebbi Joḥanan said in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Ioḥai, it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 13:7" href="/Deuteronomy.13.7"><i>Deut</i>. 13:7</a>. The verse mentions maternal halfbrother, son, daughter, and wife, as persons who may be in a hidden place with him. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 80b" href="/Kiddushin.80b">Babli <i>Qiddushin</i> 80b</a>, R. Joḥanan is quoted in the name of R. Ismael, in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 21b" href="/Sanhedrin.21b"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 21b</a> and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 36b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.36b"><i>Avodah Zarah</i> 36b</a> in the name of R. Joḥanan’s teacher R. Simeon ben Yehoṣadaq.</i>: “If your brother, your mother’s son, incites you, etc.,” Your daughter, in secret; your mother, in secret; a man may be alone with his mother and dwell with her<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">This is not explicit in the verse but is inferred from the involved description of “your brother, your mother’s son”; it is spelled out in the Babli, <i>loc. cit</i>.</i>, with his daughter and dwell with her, with his sister but may not dwell with her<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">She is not mentioned in the verse.</i>.
Witnesses of a declaration of jealousy who were found to be false<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">The technical term “scheming witnesses” derives from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 19:19" href="/Deuteronomy.19.19"><i>Deut.</i> 19:19</a> where it is stated that the penalty inflicted on a false witness shall be the punishment <i>he schemed</i> to inflict on the accused. Tractate <i>Makkot</i> mainly deals with the case of false witnesses on whom the punishment intended for their victim cannot be inflicted; in that case they are whipped for committing a sin. Since males cannot be subject to declarations of jealousy, the false witnesses must be whipped.</i> are whipped. About witnesses concerning her being in a secluded place who were found to be false, you say that they are whipped; why should they not pay<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">On their testimony, the wife loses her <i>ketubah</i>-money</i>? It is not through their action that she loses her <i>ketubah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Without testimony which confirms the declaration of jealousy, their testimony is worthless. Since the verse makes no provision for partial guilt, no monetary punishment can be imposed.</i>. Or it might be, following what Rebbi Abba, Rav Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Reading רַב יְהוּדָה of a parallel quote in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 2:9:2-6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.2.9.2-6"><i>Ketubot</i> 2:10</a>. He was Samuel’s most outstanding student.</i>, said in the name of Samuel: One does not infer one thing from another in the matter of false witnesses<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">A possible explanation of this sentence, which appears here and in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 2:9:2-6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.2.9.2-6"><i>Ketubot</i> 2:10</a> without discussion, seems to be that one refrains from imposing the punishment of whipping only in cases in which the imposition of another punishment is straightforward.</i>. And here it is so.
If she was seen going to a secluded place by one witness in the morning<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">And the same witness saw the suspect man going to the same place.</i> and by one witness in the evening, it is to be treated like the following: <sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 5:4" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).5.4">Tosephta <i>Giṭṭin</i> 5:4</a>; quoted also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 7:4:2-8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.7.4.2-8">Halakhah <i>Giṭṭin</i> 7:4</a>.</i>“If she was together alone with him by the testimony of two witnesses, she needs a second bill of divorce from him; if there was a single witness she does not need a second bill of divorce from him. With one witness in the morning and one witness in the evening, such a case happened and Rebbi Eleazar ben Thaddeus<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">A Tanna whose time cannot be determined with accuracy. He seems to belong to the later Tannaïm.</i> asked the Sages, who told him that this is not being together alone.<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">In the Tosephta: “That is only one witness per incident.” It implies that also in the case of the straying wife there are no two witnesses for any incident.</i>” If he declared his jealousy before one witness in the morning and before one witness in the evening; since he is a man and she is a woman, his declaration is nothing<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">In the interpretation of S. Eisenstein, the statement should be read as: “<i>even though</i> he<i>is the same</i> man and she is <i>the same</i> woman <i>morning and evening</i>, his declaration is worthless.” It seems that this author is correct in reading this interpretation into R. Josef Caro’s lengthy discussion of this paragraph in <i>Bet Joseph,</i> <i>Ṭur Ḥošen Mišpaṭ</i> 30(7). The upshoot of the latter’s discussion is that since the declaration of jealousy in every case was made in front of only one witness, nobody is expected to take it seriously.</i>. Or is it to be treated like the following: <sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">From here to the end of the Halakhah the text is found also in the parallels <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 2:4:2-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.2.4.2-7"><i>Ketubot</i> 2:4</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 3:9:2-24" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.3.9.2-24"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 3:10</a>.</i>“One accepts the witnesses’ testimony only if they saw it together. Rebbi Joshua ben Qorḥah says, even if they saw it one after the other.<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sanhedrin 5:3-6" href="/Tosefta_Sanhedrin.5.3-6">Tosephta <i>Sanhedrin</i> 5:5</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 30a" href="/Sanhedrin.30a">Babli <i>Sanhedrin</i> 30a</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 32a" href="/Bava_Batra.32a"><i>Baba Batra</i> 32a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 165a" href="/Bava_Batra.165a">165a</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 26b" href="/Ketubot.26b"><i>Ketubot</i> 26b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 33b" href="/Gittin.33b"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 33b</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 2:4:2-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.2.4.2-7">Yerushalmi <i>Ketubot</i> 2:4</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 3:9:2-24" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.3.9.2-24"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 3:10</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 4:1:2-6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.4.1.2-6">4:1</a>. The Babli seems to imply that practice follows R. Joshua ben Qorḥa and the Yerushalmi text also has to be interpreted in the same way. For R. Joshua ben Qorḥa the two single witnesses together can testify not to a single misdeed but to a pattern of misbehavior.</i>” Rebbi Jeremiah, Rebbi Samuel bar [Rav] Isaac in the name of Rav: <sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 30b" href="/Sanhedrin.30b">Babli, <i>Sanhedrin</i> 30b</a>, essentially the same ruling.</i> The Sages agree with Rebbi Joshua ben Qorḥah with regard to witnesses of firstlings<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Accoding to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Rashi on Sanhedrin 30b" href="/Rashi_on_Sanhedrin.30b">Rashi, <i>Sanhedrin</i> 30b</a>, this refers to a firstling born in the herd of a Jew. The firstling must be given to a Cohen who, in the absence of a Temple, may slaughter and eat the firstling only after it has developed a permanent defect which disables it from ever being a sacrifice (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 15:21-23" href="/Deuteronomy.15.21-23"><i>Deut</i>. 15:21–23</a>). Since the Cohen has a monetary interest in seeing the firstling declared defective, the rabbis decreed that (a) causing a defect in a firstling is sinful and (b) the Cohen is disqualified from testifying that the defect was not induced by humans. The Cohen must find two other witnesses but they do not have to testify on the same thing.</i> and witnesses of squatters’ rights<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">In a society in which title to real estate is not necessarily established by documents, title to a property can be proven in court by testimony of undisturbed possession during three years supported by a claim of lawful acquisition (as purchase, gift, or inheritance).</i>. Rebbi Abba in the name of Rebbi Jeremiah. The same holds for testimony regarding signs<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">To testify that the person in question is an adult qualified to transact business. A person is an adult who (a) has reached the age of consent, 12 and 1 day for a female and 13 and 1 day for a male, and (b) shows signs of puberty in the form of two pubic hairs. While the court may have ways of verifying age, it needs testimony about the pubic hair. Growth of hair on any other part of the body is irrelevant. Therefore, in the following, the expression “hair on the back” or “hair on the belly” cannot mean that but must be read as “hair on the extreme right or left of the pelvic area” and “hair at the upper border of the pelvic area [Maimonides, <i>Hilkhot Edut</i> 4:7, Meïri <i>Sanhedrin</i> (ed. A. Sofer, Jerusalem 1971) p. 131].</i>. In that case, it is obvious if one says, I saw two hairs on his back and the other says, I saw two hairs on his back<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">This is valid testimony even if it is not simultaneous.</i>. If one says, I saw one hair on her back and the other says, I saw one hair on her back, that is nothing; similarly on his back and on his back<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Since one pubic hair does not make the person an adult, there is no witness who testifies to the person being an adult.</i>. If two say, we saw one hair on his back and two others say, we saw one hair on his belly<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">The first witness testifies that the person is an adult. The second witness cannot testify that the person is an adult. In a criminal case, his testimony would be inadmissible. In the case before us, he only testifies that the person has more pubic hair than indicated in the first testimony; the question is whether the second witness, following R. Joshua ben Qorḥa, may be admitted to confirm the first testimony. It is obvious that for the rabbis opposing R. Joshua ben Qorḥa there is no testimony at all.</i>? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun and Rebbi Hoshaia the son of Rebbi Shammai, one said, it is invalid, but the other said, it is valid. He who says it is invalid considers him as one who testifies to half a sign<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">The argument is accepted in both Talmudim. It says (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 19:15" href="/Deuteronomy.19.15"><i>Deut</i>. 19:15</a>): “By the mouth of two witnesses or three witnesses the matter should be established.” It follows that a witness for half the matter has no standing in court.</i>. He who says it is valid? I say, maybe they were rubbed off<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">He testifies that all he saw was one hair, showing that the two hairs seen by the previous two witnesses were not permanent hair.</i>. Two say, we saw one hair on his back and two others say, we saw one hair on his belly. Rebbi Ḥaggai said, everybody agrees that this is invalid [testimony]. Rebbi Abba said, everybody agrees that this is valid<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Since Rebbi Abba had said earlier that the Sages agree with R. Joshua ben Qorḥa that partial testimony is accepted for signs of puberty, he must hold here that everybody agrees that two partial testimonies can be combined if each part is confirmed by two witnesses.</i>. Rebbi Yudan said, this is in disagreement; Rebbi Yose said, this is in disagreement. Rebbi Yose said, does not Rebbi Yudan follow my opinion? He answered, I am disagreeing with his teacher, so much more with him<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">He disagrees with R. Abba and holds that the Sages did not accept R. Joshua ben Qorḥa’s opinion in this case. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 30b" href="/Sanhedrin.30b">Babli (<i>Sanhedrin</i> 30b</a>), R. Abba holds that the Sages accepted R. Joshua ben Qorḥa’s opinion only for questions of real estate.</i>. Rebbi Mana said, Rebbi Ḥaggai was correct. If a document was signed by four witnesses and it was disputed<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">It is claimed that the document is a forgery. In that case, the signatures of the witnesses on the document must be verified by two witnesses (or by comparison with another document signed by the same witness and which had been certified as genuine by some court of law.)</i>, if one person verified the signature of two [witnesses] and another that of the other two, is that worth anything<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">That argument seems to be incongruous here. It refers to the reading of the parallels in <i>Ketubot</i> and <i>Sanhedrin</i>: “One <i>[witness]</i> <i>said, I saw</i> one hair on his back and <i>another said</i>, <i>I</i> saw one hair on his belly. Rebbi Abba said, everybody agrees that this is valid. Rebbi Ḥaggai said, everybody agrees that this is invalid [testimony].” However, the problem with this reading is that this kind of testimony has already been rejected (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 1:2:10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.1.2.10">Note 63</a>). The argument of R. Mana II (a student of R. Yudan) must refer to the text given here.</i>? Does not every single signature need two witnesses? And here, every single hair needs two witnesses. Rebbi Ḥanania learns it from the years of squatting rights. If one [witness] testified that he ate from the property the first, second, and third years and another [witness] testified that he ate from it the fourth, fifth, and sixth, is that worth anything<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">While every witness testifies that the person in possession today was undisturbed in his possession for three years, this is not the testimony required by the court. (This case cannot be compared with that of informal testimony referred to in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 15:5:1-6:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.15.5.1-6.2">Mishnah <i>Yebamot</i> 15:5</a>, where concurrent inference from diverging statements is accepted.)</i>? Does not every single year need two witnesses? And here, ever single hair needs two witnesses.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> How does he declare his jealousy? If he said to her in front of two [witnesses]: Do not speak with the man X, and she spoke with him, she still is permitted to her house<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">To sleep with her husband.</i> and to eat heave<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">If her husband is a Cohen, she is not desecrated.</i>. If she enters a hidden room together with him and remains there long enough for impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">The minimal time needed to start intercourse, for the genitals to touch; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 4:2:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.4.2.3"><i>Yebamot</i> Chapter 4, Note 59</a>.</i>, she is forbidden to her house<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">To sleep with her husband.</i> and forbidden to eat heave; if he dies she needs <i>ḥalîṣah</i> but cannot be married in levirate<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">If the husband dies childless before the situation is resolved, she cannot be permitted to the levir since she was forbidden to the husband. But since she was not divorced, she cannot be free to marry outside the family without the formality of <i>ḥalîṣah</i>.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “How does he declare his jealousy,” etc. Does this mean only, until she talks with him? That would imply, if she went to a secluded place with him without talking, that going to a secluded place would count for nothing<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">Since only talking to Mr. X was forbidden to her. That conclusion seems to be ridiculous.</i>? It comes to tell you more: Even if she talked to him but was not with him in a secluded place, she still is permitted to her house and to eat heave, since the Mishnah uses chaste language: Do not be alone with Mr. X<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">From the start, the husband did not say “do not talk” but “do not be alone”. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 5b" href="/Sotah.5b">Babli, 5b</a>, holds that even if the husband says “talking”, it is interpreted as meaning “being alone in a hidden room”; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Sotah 5b:4:1" href="/Tosafot_on_Sotah.5b.4.1"><i>Tosafot</i> 5b, <i>s. v</i>. הא</a>.</i>.
May he declare his jealousy with respect to two men together? Rebbi Yudan said, that is in dispute. He who says that he may declare his jealousy with respect to her father and her son<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 1:1:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.1.1.8">Note 37</a>.</i>, [says] that he may declare his jealousy with respect to two men together. Rebbi Yose asked: May he declare his jealousy with respect to a hundred men<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">On <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 4:12:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.4.12.1">Mishnah <i>Qiddushin</i> 4:12</a>, which states that a woman may be alone with two other men, the Yerushalmi notes: “That is, if they are decent men. But if they are amoral, she should not be alone even with a hundred simultaneously.” R. Yose denies that anybody may hold that a man cannot declare his jealousy simultaneously with regard to two men.</i>? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said to him: If he said to her, do not enter the synagogue, she enters<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">It depends on the circumstances. If the husband wants to prohibit any social interaction to his wife, she may force a divorce through his fault (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 7:5:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.7.5.2-4"><i>Ketubot</i> 7:5</a>, fol. 31b, line 46; Babli 72a).</i>.
“With him.<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the Mishnah which states “If she enters a hidden room together with him”.</i>” Rebbi Mana said, it only said “with him.” Therefore, not one after the other<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">If they are witnesses that she and her paramour entered at different times, the husband has no case.</i>. Rebbi Abin said, even one after the other since anyhow it is obvious<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah forbids her to her husband any time she was together with her paramour in any more or less hidden place; examples are given.</i>: On a road<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">Greek πλατεῖα “open place”.</i> during the night, in a ruin during daytime, in dark passageways during daytime.
<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">An extended text in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Tahorot 6:10" href="/Tosefta_Tahorot.6.10">Tosephta <i>Taharot</i> 6:17</a>.</i>“They asked Ben Zoma: Why is a doubt in a private domain considered impure<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">This is a question about ritual impurity, not the moral impurity of the straying wife. But since adultery is called “impurity” in the paragraph of the straying wife (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:13" href="/Numbers.5.13"><i>Num</i>. 5:13</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:14" href="/Numbers.5.14">14</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:19" href="/Numbers.5.19">19</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:20" href="/Numbers.5.20">20</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:27" href="/Numbers.5.27">27</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:28" href="/Numbers.5.28">28</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:29" href="/Numbers.5.29">29</a>), the rules of ritual impurity and of moral impurity must be identical. The undisputed rule is that if a question arises in matters of ritual impurity it must be resolved by a presumption of impurity in a private domain but of purity in the public domain (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 28" href="/Sotah.28">Babli, <i>Soṭa</i> 28a/b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 37b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.37b"><i>Avodah Zarah</i> 37b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 9b" href="/Chullin.9b"><i>Ḥulin</i> 9b</a>). The argument presented by the Tosephta is rejected by the Babli in <i>Soṭa</i> in the name of R. Aqiba; in <i>Avodah Zarah</i> and <i>Ḥulin</i> the rule is accepted as an old tradition devoid of scriptural basis. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Sotah 28b:4:1" href="/Tosafot_on_Sotah.28b.4.1">Tosafot <i>Soṭa</i> 28b, <i>s. v.</i> מכאן</a>.</i>? He said to them: Where is the suspected wife? In a private domain<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">A place adequate for intercourse must by definition be private. As the preceding examples show, the definition of “private place” for all kinds of impurity are different from the rules of “private place” in civil matters and regarding the Sabbath (<i>Shabbat</i> Chapter 1).</i>. He said to them, we find that she is forbidden for her house<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">In her intimate relations with her husband, she is considered an adulteress even though there remains a doubt whether she actually committed adultery in the hidden room.</i>.” The words of the rabbis disagree, since Rebbi Ze‘ira, Rebbi Yasa said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: An underage girl who whored has no will to be forbidden to her husband<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 33b" href="/Yevamot.33b">Babli, <i>Yebamot</i> 33b</a>, the formulation is: The seduction of a minor girl is rape. The rape victim is explicitly cleared from the guilt of adultery in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:13" href="/Numbers.5.13"><i>Num</i>. 5:13</a>.<br>The Medieval authorities make it difficult to understand the position of the Babli in this matter. According to R. Abraham ben David in his glosses to the Code of Maimonides (<i>Issure Bi’a</i> 3:2, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 2:4:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.2.4.2-5"><i>Soṭa</i> 2:4</a>), the Babli accepts the opinion of the Yerushalmi. Maimonides holds that the underage girl is forbidden to her husband. As usual, he gives no reason for his decision. In the opinion of Don Vidal de Tolosa, approved by Joseph Caro, this is not because of the girl but because the husband forbade her on himself as stated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 9a" href="/Ketubot.9a"><i>Ketubot</i> 9a</a> for any case in which the husband accuses his wife of infidelity. In the case of an underage girl, this refers only to a girl married off by her father, whose marriage in valid by biblical standards, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 1:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.1.2.1"><i>Yebamot</i> 1, Note 118</a>.</i>. But did we not state:<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Tahorot 3:6" href="/Mishnah_Tahorot.3.6">Mishnah <i>Taharot</i> 3:6</a>.</i> “In any case where there is no mind to be asked, the doubtful case is pure<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">Even in a private domain.</i>”? Therefore, if there is a mind to be asked, the doubtful case is impure. But here, even while there is a mind to be asked, the doubtful case is pure!<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">The argument of the Tosephta is rejected since the minor can tell what happened even if she legally has no will (or at least the seducer could in theory be asked.)</i> <sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">An extended text in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Tahorot 6:10" href="/Tosefta_Tahorot.6.10">Tosephta <i>Taharot</i> 6:17</a>.</i>“Why is a doubt in a public domain considered pure? He said to them: We find that the public prepare their Passah [as in] purity when most of them are impure<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">If most of Israel are impure, the public service in the Temple is performed in impurity. The only private sacrifice which has the status of a public offering is the Passah lamb (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 7:8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.7.8.1">Mishnah <i>Pesaḥim</i> 7:6</a>).</i>. If known impurity was permitted in public<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">This is a matter of dispute in the Babli (e. g. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 6b" href="/Yoma.6b"><i>Yoma</i> 6b</a>), whether for public sacrifices the rules of impurity are <i>pushed aside or eliminated</i>. Following the first opinion, the invasion of impurity has to be held to a minimum. This opinion is not found in the Yerushalmi. In the second opinion, the one expressed here, the rules of impurity are disregarded; it is <i>as if impurity were purity</i>. Under the influence of the Babli, the commentators unanimously emend “in purity” to “in impurity” and even J. Sussman here indicates a corruption. The emendation is totally unnecessary.</i>, so much more for a case of doubtful impurity.”
There<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Keritot 2:4" href="/Mishnah_Keritot.2.4">Mishnah <i>Keritut</i> 2:4</a>. “Incest” always includes adultery.</i>, we have stated: “In all matters of incest, He made him who touches<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">The man whose genital touches the woman’s. This is a technical term, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 6:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.6.1.4"><i>Yebamot</i> Chapter 6, Note 11</a>.</i> equal to him who completes and one is guilty for each single intercourse<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the rules concerning a slave girl (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:20-22" href="/Leviticus.19.20-22"><i>Lev.</i> 19:20–22</a>) who according to some opinions is partially manumitted (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Keritot 11a" href="/Keritot.11a">Babli <i>Keritut</i>11a</a>.). The Babli’s tradition eliminates the rules from practical importance by restricting them to a girl living with a Hebrew slave. Since the institution of Hebrew slaves was abolished with the Babylonian exile, never to be reinstituted, the frequent discussions of the rules are purely theoretical. However, in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Kedoshim, Chapter 5 1" href="/Sifra,_Kedoshim,_Chapter_5.1"><i>Sifra Qedošim Pereq</i> 5(1</a>), at least one opinion describes the slave girl engaged to a free man in the expectation of her manumission.<br>As a slave, the girl can not marry and therefore she is free to have guiltless sex with any man not a Jew (cf. <i>Terumot</i> Chapter 8, Note 347). Upon manumission, she becomes a free Jewish woman and able to contract a valid marriage with any Jew who is not a priest. She is permitted to live with a Hebrew slave (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:4" href="/Exodus.21.4"><i>Ex.</i> 21:4</a>). Since her relationship with the slave is not a marriage, her affair with another man is not adultery. From the man’s side, the affair with the slave girl is the only sin which can be atoned for by a sacrifice if committed intentionally. A purification sacrifice is possible only for inadvertent sins; the relation with the semi-free girl can be atoned for by a reparation sacrifice. (An intentional sin can only be cleansed by God’s mercy in response to repentance.) For inadvertent sins, a purification sacrifice is due for each single transgression; one reparation sacrifice covers the entire affair.</i>. He was more stringent with the slave girl since in her case He treated the intentional as unintentional.” Rebbi Jeremiah, Rebbi Abba bar Mamai in the name of Rav: “Flow of semen,” until he ejaculates<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:20" href="/Leviticus.19.20"><i>Lev</i>. 19:20</a>: “If a man sleep with a woman by flow of semen …” The intercourse of a free man with the slave girl is not punishable unless there was an ejaculation.</i>. Here is written “flow of semen”, and here is written “flow of semen.<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">In the case of the straying wife, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:13" href="/Numbers.5.13"><i>Num.</i> 5:13</a> reads: “A man slept with her with flow of semen.” Nevertheless, the wife becomes impure and forbidden to her husband already if her paramour’s penis touches her genitals. This seems to contradict our principle that equal expressions used in different circumstances must have equal meanings.</i>” Here<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">In the case of the slave girl.</i>, you say “flow of semen” until he ejaculates. And here<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">In the case of the straying wife.</i>, you say so? Rebbi Yose said, there is a difference, for it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:13" href="/Numbers.5.13"><i>Num.</i> 5:13</a>.</i>: “She was hidden and became impure.” From the moment she was hidden, the Torah calls her “impure”. But here<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">In the case of the slave girl.</i>, “flow of semen,” until he ejaculates. For what reason is written<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">In the case of the straying wife.</i> “flow of semen”? For measures<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">To measure how long the wife must be hidden together with her paramour to be impure and subject to the ordeal. In another connection this is quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 2b" href="/Sotah.2b">Babli, 2b</a>.</i>, as it is stated<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:13" href="/Numbers.5.13"><i>Num.</i> 5:13</a>.</i>: “She was hidden and became impure”; what is counted as hiding<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 4a" href="/Sotah.4a">Babli 4a</a>. The Babli discusses why all these expressions have to be used.</i>? Time to become impure. What is the time for impurity? Time for intercourse. What is the time for intercourse? Time for ‘touching’<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">The man whose genital touches the woman’s. This is a technical term, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 6:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.6.1.4"><i>Yebamot</i> Chapter 6, Note 11</a>.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">This <i>baraita</i> exists in several versions: <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 4a" href="/Sotah.4a">Babli 4a</a>, <i>Sifry Num.</i> 7, <i>Sifry Zuṭa</i> 13, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sotah (Lieberman) 1:1" href="/Tosefta_Sotah_(Lieberman).1.1">Tosephta 1:1</a>, <i>Tanḥuma</i> and <i>Tanḥuma Buber Naśo; Num. rabba</i> 9(6).</i>“How long is ‘touching’? Rebbi Eliezer says, [time needed] to walk around a date palm<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">In Babli and <i>Sifry, Sifry Zuṭa:</i> R. Ismael.</i>; Rebbi Joshua says, to mix a cup [of wine]<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">In Babli and <i>Sifry, Sifry Zuṭa:</i> R. Eliezer.</i>; Ben Azai says, to drink it<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">In Babli and <i>Sifry, Sifry Zuṭa:</i> R. Joshua. In the Tosefta: Ben Azai says, mixing a cup to drink it.</i>; Rebbi Aqiba says, [time needed] rolling an egg<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">In Babli and <i>Sifry, Sifry Zuṭa</i>: Ben Azai says, to fry an egg. The Tosephta, which ascribes the statement to R. Aqiba, also has: to fry an egg. The usual explanation of לגלגל is: To boil an egg softly so it can roll when taken out of its shell. A soft-boiled chicken egg is considered the egg which is most quickly prepared for human consumption [Rashi in Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 80b, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 8:7:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.8.7.2-3">Yerushalmi 8:7</a> (fol. 11b line 70)].<br>Babli and <i>Sifry, Sifry Zuṭa</i> have an additional entry: R. Aqiba says, to sip one (soft-boiled) egg.</i>; Rebbi Jehudah ben Bathyra says, to sip three rolled eggs one after the other; Rebbi Eleazar ben Phineas<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">In Babli and Tosephta: R. Eleazar ben Jeremiah. This reading is suspect since this name is not reported anywhere else in talmudic literarture while R. Eleazar ben Phineas is quoted several times. Because of the dependence of the Tosephta on the Babli [cf. Epilogue in the author’s edition of <i>Ma‘aser Šeni, Ḥallah, Orlah, Bikkurim</i> (SI 23)], Babli and Tosephta cannot be considered as two independent sources.<br>This and the following entries are missing in <i>Sifry</i>.</i> says, [time needed] for the weaver to bind a thread; Miniamin<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">A student of R. Aqiba. In <i>Babli, Tanḥuma, Tanḥuma Buber, Sifry Zuṭa</i>: Polemon. Babli, Tosephta, and the <i>Tanḥumas</i> have another entry: Ḥanin ben Phineas says, that she may extract a splinter from her mouth (in <i>Tanḥumas</i>: Rebbi Ḥanin, <i>Tanḥumas Buber</i>: Ḥanan)</i> says, that she can stretch out her hand and lift a loaf of bread from the basket. And though there is no proof, at least there is a hint: ‘For a whoring woman up to a loaf of bread.’<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 6:26" href="/Proverbs.6.26"><i>Prov</i>. 6:26</a>.</i>” Rebbi Yose said, all these measurements [of time] are after she removes her underpants<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">Meaning and etymology of the word סינר are in doubt. The translation follows <i>Arukh</i> (<i>s. v</i>. סנר I) and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Rashi on Shabbat 92b" href="/Rashi_on_Shabbat.92b">Rashi (<i>Šabbat</i> 92b</a>): “Small pants which she wears for decency”. {This meaning may be compared with Arabic سنوّر “armor”.} Maimonides (Commentary to <i>Šabbat</i> 10:4), following a Gaonic commentary (B. M. Levin <i>Oṣar HaGeonim Šabbat, Oṣar HaPerušim</i> p. 51): “sash”. It is reported in <i>Megillah</i> 4:1 (75a, line 30) that from the time of the return from Babylonia, Jewish women wore a <i>sinār</i>; according to Rashi’s tradition they wore gowns and under them pants from the hips to the knees; according to Maimonides they wore long pants and a sash covering their legs front and back down from the hips. Maimonides’s interpretation is the source of the acceptation in modern Hebrew: Apron. {Cf. Greek συνωρίς, -ίδος, ἡ, “pair of horses”, also “pair of anything (fetters, manacles)”, referring to a <i>pair</i> of sashes or pants? (E. G.)}<br>R. Yose’s remark makes all the examples given meaningless for practical applications.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, each of them judged by his own experience<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 4b" href="/Sotah.4b">Babli 4b</a>.</i>. But did Ben Azai ever marry<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">What does he know about sex? He is the example of a man studying 24 hours a day, not having time for anything else.</i>? Some want to say, he had an erection. But some want to say, he was married and gave it up<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">This contradicts the story in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 63a" href="/Ketubot.63a">Babli, <i>Ketubot</i> 63a</a>, that he was engaged to R. Aqiba’s daughter who wanted to marry him after he had become the time’s most outstanding scholar but that he died before he had attained that goal.</i>. Some want to say, “the secret of the Eternal is for those who fear Him, to inform them about His covenant.<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 25:14" href="/Psalms.25.14"><i>Ps</i>. 25:14</a>.</i>”
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 28a" href="/Sotah.28a">Babli, 28a</a>, and in <i>Sifry Zuṭa</i>(<i>Naśo</i> 14), in the name of R. Aqiba. A complementary version, that three times “if she was not impure” is mentioned to permit her to her husband, presumed paramour (after the husband’s death), and heave, is in <i>Sifry Num.</i> 19.</i>: Rebbi said, three times is “impurity” said in the paragraph<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">According to Rashi, once each in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:14" href="/Numbers.5.14"><i>Num</i>. 5:14</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:27" href="/Numbers.5.27">27</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:29" href="/Numbers.5.29">29</a>.</i>, one for the husband, one for the paramour<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">That she could not marry him even if divorced or widowed.</i>, and one for heave<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">If she is married to a Cohen or is the childless daughter of a Cohen married to an Israel, who on becoming a widow would otherwise be permitted heave. Any woman who ever slept with a man whom she could not marry on the spot (even if raped) is desecrated and permanently barred from priestly status since she cannot “return to her father’s house as in her youth’ (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 22:13" href="/Leviticus.22.13"><i>Lev</i>. 22:13</a>). Since a married woman cannot marry another man without a divorce, an adulteress is automatically desecrated.</i>. For heave? Do we find that a woman is forbidden to her house<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">To sleep with her husband.</i> and permitted to eat heave? Why not? If the daughter of a Cohen was married to an Israel and raped, is she not permitted to her house<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Israel has no sacred status, his relation to his wife is not changed if she is raped.</i> but forbidden heave? We never find that a woman is forbidden to her house and permitted to eat heave<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">If A implies B it does not follow that B implies A. Therefore, the example given as objection is irrelevant. Since the wife cannot live with her husband until she is cleared from the accusation of adultery, she cannot eat heave until she is cleared.</i>! Rebbi Abin said, it was said: One for the husband, one for the paramour, and one for the levir<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">If the husband died childless after his wife was in a hidden place with another man and before she was cleared of the suspicion of adultery.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, the Mishnah says so: “If he dies she needs <i>ḥalîṣah</i> but cannot be married in levirate.”
Rebbi Jacob bar Abin stated before Rebbi Jonathan: “‘If you deviated from under your husband.’<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:20" href="/Numbers.5.20"><i>Num.</i> 5:20</a>. The <i>baraita</i> is not quoted elsewhere. The other sources point out that the raped wife is expressly excluded by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:13" href="/Numbers.5.13">v. 13</a>: “And she was not kidnapped” (<i>Sifry Num</i>. 7).</i> This excludes rape.” How do you understand this? He said to him: Just as she is “under your husband” by her consent, so also in this case by her consent.
<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">Text from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 10:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.10.1">Yebamot 10:1, Notes 49–67</a>.</i> Just as the woman accused of infidelity is forbidden to her husband, so she is forbidden to her paramour. Just as she is forbidden to her husband’s brother, so she is forbidden to her paramour’s brother. I might think that just as the co-wife of a woman accused of infidelity is forbidden to her husband’s brother, so she should be forbidden to her paramour’s brother. Let us hear from the following: “A woman’s husband went overseas; they came and told her that her husband had died, and she had a levir here; he married her in levirate and died. Then her husband returned. She is forbidden to him but her co-widow is permitted to him; she is forbidden to him but his brother’s wife is permitted to him.” Is his brother’s wife not like the co-wife of a woman accused of infidelity? That means that the co-wife of a woman accused of infidelity is permitted to her paramour’s brother. Rebbi Judan said, this follows the rabbis there, as Rebbi Hila said, one states there, for incestuous relationships no divorce is needed except for a married woman; Rebbi Aqiba says also from his wife’s sister. But following the rabbis here? Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: everybody agrees that his brother’s wife needs a bill of divorce from him because of the rule of a married wife. She is forbidden by the incest rules, and an incest-prohibited woman frees her co-wife. Rebbi Ḥinenah said, it is accepted even according to the rabbis here. They fined her but not her heirs. Rebbi Ḥananiah, the son of Rebbi Hillel, said, according to the rabbis there, she should be permitted to him<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Since one pubic hair does not make the person an adult, there is no witness who testifies to the person being an adult.</i>. Rebbi Ze‘ira in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, the co-wife of a woman accused of infidelity is forbidden, the co-wife of a divorcee is permitted. Rebbi Jacob [bar Aḥa] in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: All co-wives are permitted except the co-wife of a woman accused of infidelity. Samuel said, the divorcee herself is permitted to her house. Does he disagree? Since they were discussing co-wives they did not mention divorcees. Why is the co-wife of a woman accused of infidelity forbidden? Rebbi Joḥanan said, they touched this case because it looks like adultery. Rav said, because impurity is written there parallel to incest prohibitions.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The following are prohibited from eating heave<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">A Cohen’s wife (or the Cohen’s daughter who is the childless widow of a Non-Cohen) who is desecrated by adultery; cf. also <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 1:2:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.1.2.8">Note 123</a>.</i>: One who says, I am impure for you<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">I. e., she says that she slept with another man; there is no difference in this respect whether she committed adultery or was raped.</i>, and where witnesses proved that she is impure<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">Witnesses to the act of adultery.</i>, and one who refuses to drink<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">She maintains her innocence, even though two witnesses attested that she met the man against whom she was warned in a secluded place, but refuses to submit to the ordeal. She remains permanently forbidden to her husband and is permanently excluded from eating consecrated food.</i>, and one whose husband refuses to let her drink<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">Two witnesses attested that she met the man against whom she was warned in a secluded place; therefore, she is disabled from eating heave. She can regain her priestly status only by being cleansed by the ordeal. Since it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:15" href="/Numbers.5.15"><i>Num</i>. 5:15</a>): “This husband has to bring his wife,” without him appearing before the court of the Temple there can be no ordeal and no rehabilitation.</i>, and one whose husband slept with her on the trip<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:31" href="/Numbers.5.31"><i>Num</i>. 5:31</a> is read as meaning: If the husband is blameless, then this woman has to bear her sin. It follows that if the husband is not blameless [in sexual matters of any kind], the ordeal becomes inactive. Since the <i>Soṭah</i> is forbidden to her husband, if he sleeps with her before she is cleansed by the ordeal he is not blameless and the innocence of his wife can never be proven.</i>. What does he have to do: He brings her to the court at his place and they give him two scholars to prevent him from sleeping with her on the trip. Rebbi Jehudah says, her husband is believed about her.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “The following are prohibited from eating heave,” etc. Our Mishnah does not follow the earlier Mishnah, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 11:11:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.11.11.1">Mishnah <i>Nedarim</i> 11:12</a>.</i>: “Three [kinds of] women leave<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">The court would force the husband to divorce her.</i> and collect their <i>ketubah</i>: One who says, I am impure for you<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">A woman married to a Cohen who says that she was raped. Then she is forbidden to him not because of any fault of hers; therefore, she collects the entire amount promised her. The later Mishnah, in a change of practice, requires that the woman prove her case; if she cannot do that, she remains permitted to her husband and permitted to eat heave (because the later authorities suspected that a woman might invent the story of the rape to force a divorce). This contradicts the Mishnah here which states that a woman claiming to have been raped is permanently desecrated.</i>, [or] Heaven is between you and me<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">She claims that he is impotent and unable to have satisfactory intercourse; the truth in these intimate matters is known only to Heaven. In the later practice, the rabbi has to negotiate a settlement between the parties.</i>, [or] I am separated from the Jews<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">She made a vow forbidding to herself any sexual relation with any Jew. In the later practice, this is considered a “vow of deprivation” which the husband may annul (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:14" href="/Numbers.30.14"><i>Num</i>. 30:14</a>). He may annul his part, live with her, and let her be forbidden to all other Jews.</i>.” Rebbi Abin said, even if you say following the later Mishnah, does not the affair have likelihood<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">The situation here cannot be compared to the one described in <i>Nedarim</i>. The woman is forbidden to eat heave only after she was warned before witnesses and nevertheless was in a secluded place according to the testimony of two witnesses. It is a credible inference, even if there is no proof, that she had relations with her paramour.</i>?
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote">The same statement in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 58a" href="/Yevamot.58a">Babli <i>Yebamot</i> 58a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 24b" href="/Sotah.24b"><i>Soṭa</i> 24b</a>.</i>: “‘Except your husband,<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:19" href="/Numbers.5.19"><i>Num</i>. 5:19</a>.</i>” which excludes that other semen preceded your husband’s<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">If the affair with her paramour preceded her current marriage, the ordeal will be ineffective.</i>. Rebbi Hila said, parallel what has been said, “except the morning elevation offering.<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 28:23" href="/Numbers.28.23"><i>Num</i>. 28:23</a>. While this verse is written at the end of the list of holiday sacrifices, it is obvious that the daily sacrifice precedes all others, Therefore, מִלְּבַד means really “after”. Similarly, מִבַּלְעֲדֵי אִישֵׁךְ should be translated: “after your husband”. (In <i>Sifry Num</i>. 13, <i>Sifry Zuṭa</i> 20, the verse is interpreted in two other, much different, ways.)</i>” But did we not state: “Just as the water examines her, so the water examines him. Just as she is forbidden to her husband, so she is forbidden to her paramour”<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 5:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.5.1.1">Mishnah 5:1</a>.</i>? Just as she is forbidden to her husband’s brother, so she is forbidden to her paramour’s brother<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">This sentence is an intrusion from the preceding text, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 1:2:10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.1.2.10">Note 128</a>, induced by the similarly formulated Mishnah.</i>. Just as the water examines her for every intercourse in which she receives her husband after [she had slept with] her paramour, so it examines him<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote">This <i>baraita</i> assumes that the ordeal is effective if the husband slept with his wife after she committed adultery. But the Mishnah excluded this case!</i>? Rebbi Abin in the name of Rebbi Hila: Here, if he knows, there, if he does not know<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote">The ordeal is effective if she committed adultery before the husband even became aware of a possible affair. He is not free from sin only if he sleeps with his wife after officially charging her and receiving notice that she was alone with her paramour.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote">The same text in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 2:9:2-6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.2.9.2-6"><i>Ketubot</i> 2:10</a>. Copied in <i>Num. Rabba Naśo</i> 9(38), a text of doubtful antiquity.</i> Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Yosef sent after his wife. He said, three students should accompany her, so that if one has to absent himself for his needs, she should still be with two of them. But did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote">In the Mishnah here, which requires only two chaperones.</i>: “And they give him two scholars to prevent him from sleeping with her on the trip”? Rebbi Abin said, with her husband that makes three<sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 4:12:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.4.12.1">Mishnah <i>Qiddušin</i> 4:12</a> it is stated that a woman may be alone with two men, but not with a single man who is not her husband or close relative. The anecdotal fact told here is stated in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 7a" href="/Sotah.7a">Babli, 7a</a>, as a legal precept by R. Ḥiyya bar Yosef’s teacher Rav.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote">These sentences make no sense here; they are copied from <i>Ketubot</i>, where they refer to Mishnah 2:10, that in a city conquered by an enemy army all women, in absence of testimony to the contrary, are considered raped and, therefore, all wives of Cohanim are prohibited to their husbands. It is reported that R. Zachariah ben Haqqaṣab, who was a priest, testified that his wife’s hand did not leave his own from the moment the Roman army entered Jerusalem until it left, but he was rebuked and told that nobody may testify in his own behalf. The <i>baraita</i> notes that he refused to divorce her.</i>{Also he rented a house for her and looked after her upkeep but was never alone with her except in presence of her children. He applied to himself the verse: “I exercised myself in my worry but found no rest<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jeremiah 45:3" href="/Jeremiah.45.3"><i>Jer</i>. 45:3</a>.</i>”.}
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sifry Num</i>. 8, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bamidbar Rabbah 9:38" href="/Bamidbar_Rabbah.9.38"><i>Num. Rabba</i> 9(38</a>); a more elaborate text in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 7a" href="/Sotah.7a">Babli 7a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sotah (Lieberman) 1:1" href="/Tosefta_Sotah_(Lieberman).1.1">Tosephta 1:1</a>.</i>: “Rebbi Jehudah says, her husband is believed about her by an argument <i>de minore ad majus</i>. Since he is believed about her when she is menstruating<sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote">The menstruating woman is forbidden to her husband on penalty of extirpation (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 20:18" href="/Leviticus.20.18"><i>Lev</i>. 20:18</a>). Nobody requires chaperones during the time the wife is forbidden to her husband.</i>, when he would be subject to extirpation because of her, but for this one<sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote">A wife suspected of adultery (and even a proven adulteress) is forbidden to her husband but no penalties are specified.</i> he is not subject to extirpation because of her, is it not logical that he should be believed about her? They said to him, no. If you mention the menstruating, she will be permitted after being forbidden, what can you say about this one who may not be permitted after being forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote">If she is found guilty, she will be permanently forbidden and he will be forced to divorce her.</i>? And it says, “stolen waters are sweet”<sup class="footnote-marker">171</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 9:17" href="/Proverbs.9.17"><i>Prov</i>. 9:17</a>. She is more attractive forbidden than permitted.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah said to them, it is a decree of the verse: “The man shall bring his wife to the priest”<sup class="footnote-marker">178</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:15" href="/Numbers.5.15"><i>Num</i>. 5:15</a>.</i>, etc. They said to him, only with witnesses<sup class="footnote-marker">179</sup><i class="footnote">General statements in verses are never interpreted to override the general principles of administration of justice. Since relatives cannot testify for or against a person, the husband cannot testify for himself, just as he cannot testify against his bride whom he accuses of prenuptial adultery (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:14" href="/Deuteronomy.22.14"><i>Deut</i>. 22:14</a>).</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> They bring her to the Supreme Court in Jerusalem<sup class="footnote-marker">180</sup><i class="footnote">Galilean Amoraim in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 7b" href="/Sotah.7b">Babli, 7b</a>, try to give a reason to this statement: Every procedure characterized as <i>Torah</i>(<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:30" href="/Numbers.5.30"><i>Num</i>. 5:30</a>) must be supervised by the supreme court, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 17:11" href="/Deuteronomy.17.11"><i>Deut</i>. 17:11</a>.</i> where they try to instill fear in her, as they try to instill fear in witnesses in capital cases<sup class="footnote-marker">181</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 4:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.4.4.1">Mishnah <i>Sanhedrin</i> 4:5</a>.</i>, and say to her<sup class="footnote-marker">182</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sotah (Lieberman) 1:6" href="/Tosefta_Sotah_(Lieberman).1.6">Tosephta 1:6</a>.</i>: My daughter, much does wine cause, much does joking cause, much does youth cause, much do bad neighbors cause. Do not cause the Great Name, which will be written in holiness, to be erased by water<sup class="footnote-marker">183</sup><i class="footnote">The Babli text: Do it for the Great Name, Which will be written in holiness, that It should not become erased by water.<br>The verse requires that the curses contained in the paragraph <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:11-31" href="/Numbers.5.11-31"><i>Num</i>. 5:11–31</a> be written on a scroll and erased by holy water. While the exact text to be written is a matter of controversy among scholars living 2 generations after the destruction of the Temple, all agree that some verses containing the Divine Name must be written.</i>, and they mention in front of her things that neither she nor any of her paternal family should hear.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “They bring her to the Supreme Court in Jerusalem,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">184</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sotah (Lieberman) 1:6" href="/Tosefta_Sotah_(Lieberman).1.6">Tosephta 1:6</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 7b" href="/Sotah.7b">Babli 7b</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bamidbar Rabbah 9:42" href="/Bamidbar_Rabbah.9.42"><i>Num. rabba</i> 9(42</a>). In <i>Sifry Num</i>. 12, this is a (minority) statement of R. Ismael.</i> Just as they instill fear in her that she should change her mind, so they instill fear in her that she should not change her mind, and say to her: My daughter, if you are pure, if it is clear to you that you are pure, insist on what is clear to you, since this draught is only like solid poison which if given on healthy skin does no harm; only if it finds there a wound it starts to dissolve and penetrate.
<sup class="footnote-marker">185</sup><i class="footnote">A copy of this text in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bamidbar Rabbah 9:19" href="/Bamidbar_Rabbah.9.19"><i>Num. rabba</i> 9(19</a>); a retelling in a different Aramaic dialect in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Vayikra Rabbah 9:9" href="/Vayikra_Rabbah.9.9"><i>Lev. rabba</i> 9(9</a>), a short version in Hebrew <a class="refLink" data-ref="Devarim Rabbah 5:14" href="/Devarim_Rabbah.5.14"><i>Deut. rabba</i>5(14</a>).</i> Rebbi Zevadiah<sup class="footnote-marker">186</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bamidbar Rabbah 9:19" href="/Bamidbar_Rabbah.9.19"><i>Num. rabba</i> 9(19</a>): R. Zachariah. This is the correct name as attested to by all other quotes of his statements (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 8:8:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.8.8.7"><i>Peah</i> 8, Note 151</a>).</i>, the son-in-law of Rebbi Levi, reported the following happening. Rebbi Meïr used to preach in the synagogue of Ḥamata<sup class="footnote-marker">187</sup><i class="footnote">The hot springs of Tiberias.</i> every Friday evening.
