There was a woman who used to hear him. Once he extended his sermon. She went and wanted to come to her house but found the light had gone out. Her husband asked her, where have you been? She said to him, to hear the preacher’s voice. He said to her, so and so<sup class="footnote-marker">188</sup><i class="footnote">An oath formula, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="II Samuel 19:14" href="/II_Samuel.19.14">2S. 19:14</a>.</i>, that this woman will not enter here into her house unless she went and spat into the preacher’s face. Rebbi Meïr saw this in the holy spirit<sup class="footnote-marker">189</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Vayikra Rabbah 9:9" href="/Vayikra_Rabbah.9.9"><i>Lev. rabba</i> 9(9</a>), he was informed by the Prophet Elijah.</i> and faked pain in his eyes. He said, any woman who knows charms for the eye should come and do the charm. Her neighbors said to her, this answers your needs. Go to your house, represent yourself as a sorceress and spit in his eye. She came to him; he asked her, do you know to make a charm for the eye? In her fear of him, she said no. He said to her, if you spit into it seven times, he will feel better. After she had spat, he said to her: Go, and tell your husband, you said to me once, but she spat seven times! His students said to him, so does one denigrate the Torah? If you had ordered about him<sup class="footnote-marker">191</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bamidbar Rabbah 9:19" href="/Bamidbar_Rabbah.9.19"><i>Num. rabba</i> 9(19</a>) ומרעיין instead of ומרציין, “one makes his wife pleasing to him.” On the face of it, that might be the better text. However, the text here might be a slightly corrupt pun if one reads ומרציין for ומרצעיין, “one treats him with leather straps until he asks his wife for forgiveness” since ע had become silent in Galilean Aramaic.</i>, would we not have brought him, whipped him on the footstool, and make him agree<sup class="footnote-marker">191</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bamidbar Rabbah 9:19" href="/Bamidbar_Rabbah.9.19"><i>Num. rabba</i> 9(19</a>) ומרעיין instead of ומרציין, “one makes his wife pleasing to him.” On the face of it, that might be the better text. However, the text here might be a slightly corrupt pun if one reads ומרציין for ומרצעיין, “one treats him with leather straps until he asks his wife for forgiveness” since ע had become silent in Galilean Aramaic.</i> to make up with his wife? He said to them, the honor of Meïr should not be greater than that of his Maker. Since the verse says that the Holy Name, written in holiness, should be erased by the water in order to make peace between husband and wife, the honor of Meïr not so much more?
“And they mention in front of her things that neither she nor any of her paternal family should hear,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">192</sup><i class="footnote">A more detailed homily in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 7b" href="/Sotah.7b">Babli, 7b</a>.</i> For example, what happened between Reuben and Bilhah<sup class="footnote-marker">193</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 35:22" href="/Genesis.35.22"><i>Gen</i>. 35:22</a>.</i>, or what happened between Jehudah and Tamar<sup class="footnote-marker">194</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 38:13-26" href="/Genesis.38.13-26"><i>Gen</i>. 38:13–26</a>.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">195</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Job 15:18-19" href="/Job.15.18-19">Job 15:18–19</a>. These verses are explained as referring to Reuben and Jehudah who confessed their sins, in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bereishit Rabbah 57:3" href="/Bereishit_Rabbah.57.3"><i>Gen. rabba</i> 57(3</a>), [<a class="refLink" data-ref="Bamidbar Rabbah 13:6" href="/Bamidbar_Rabbah.13.6"><i>Num. rabba</i> 13(6</a>)]; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Makkot 11b" href="/Makkot.11b">Babli <i>Makkot</i> 11b</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 4:11:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.4.11.2-4">Yerushalmi <i>Megillah</i>4:11</a> (fol. 75c).</i>“If Sages tell,” these are Reuben and Jehudah, “they do not hide before their fathers.” What rewards did they take for this, “to them alone the Land was given; no stranger passes in their midst.” When Moses came to bless them, “Reuben may live and not die,<sup class="footnote-marker">196</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Deut</i>. 36:6.</i>” “and that for Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">197</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Deut</i>. 36:7: “Listen, o Eternal, to Judah’s voice.”</i>”.
Rebbi Ḥizqiah in the name of Rebbi Aḥa: Rebbi Ḥiyya explains three verses as praise. <sup class="footnote-marker">198</sup><i class="footnote">The parallel to the entire section is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 13:1:2-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.13.1.2-7"><i>Ketubot</i> 13:1</a> (fol. 35b/c). A different version, in the name of other authors, in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 10b" href="/Sotah.10b">Babli 10b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bereishit Rabbah 85:8" href="/Bereishit_Rabbah.85.8"><i>Gen. rabba</i>85(8</a>). One of the Babli’s authors identifies the place as העינם (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 15:34" href="/Joshua.15.34">Jos. 15:34</a>).</i>“She sat at the entrance to the source.” Is that possible? Even the most depraved prostitute would not do that<sup class="footnote-marker">199</sup><i class="footnote">To try to sleep with a man not in a room.</i>. But she lifted her eyes to the door to which all eyes are looking<sup class="footnote-marker">200</sup><i class="footnote">Usually identified, cf. <i>Berakhot</i>4, Note 234.</i>. She said before Him: Master of the Universe, let me not leave this family empty-handed. Another explanation, “at the entrance to the eyes”, that she opened his eyes saying, I am unmarried and pure. “And Eli was very old,<sup class="footnote-marker">201</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 2:22-23" href="/I_Samuel.2.22-23"><i>1S</i>. 2:22–23</a>. The same explanation in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 55b" href="/Shabbat.55b">Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 55b</a>; more in detail <i>Midrash Samuel</i> 7(4).</i>” “they would make them lie” is written<sup class="footnote-marker">202</sup><i class="footnote">The masoretic text reads יִשְׁכְּבוּן [Eli’s sons] would lie [with the women assembled before the Tent]. A sacrifice of birds is required for a woman healed from prolonged flux to be permitted again to her husband (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 15:29" href="/Leviticus.15.29"><i>Lev</i>. 15:29</a>) and also for a woman after childbirth (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 12:8" href="/Leviticus.12.8"><i>Lev</i>. 12:8</a>).</i>. That women would bring them their bird-sacrifices to be purified for their houses but since they were dragging their feet<sup class="footnote-marker">203</sup><i class="footnote">Unnecessarily preventing them from sleeping with their husbands.</i>, the Holy One, praise to Him, found them guilty as if they had slept with them. Rebbi Tanḥuma said, here, he needles<sup class="footnote-marker">204</sup><i class="footnote">Greek κεντρόω, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 3:1:22" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.3.1.22"><i>Berakhot</i> 3, Note 96</a>.</i> them: <sup class="footnote-marker">205</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 2:29" href="/I_Samuel.2.29"><i>1S</i>. 2:29</a>.</i>“why do you disregard my slaughter-sacrifice and my flour-sacrifice?” If you say that they committed a serious crime, why should he absolve them from the serious crime and needle them for a minor one? <sup class="footnote-marker">206</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 8:3" href="/I_Samuel.8.3"><i>1S</i>. 8:3</a>. Similar explanations in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 56a" href="/Shabbat.56a">Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 56a</a>.</i>“But his sons did not follow in his ways and turned after lucre.” That they took tithe but remained judging<sup class="footnote-marker">207</sup><i class="footnote">As Levites, Samuel’s sons were entitled to tithe. The Yerushalmi faults them for creating an appearance of conflict of interest; in the Babli, there is one opinion that they exacted tithes for themselves, when the farmer should have had full freedom to give the tithe to any Levite of his liking.</i>. Rebbi Berekhia: A camel-caravan came by, they put aside the needs of Israel and were going and attending to business affairs<sup class="footnote-marker">208</sup><i class="footnote">Greek πραγματεία “business, affairs, work”.</i>.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If she said, I am impure, she breaks her <i>ketubah</i> and leaves<sup class="footnote-marker">209</sup><i class="footnote">The court immediately has a bill of divorce executed for the husband and annuls her husband’s obligations towards her as specified in the <i>ketubah</i>.</i>. If she said, I am pure, one brings her to the Eastern gate, the Nikanor gate, for there one lets the suspected wives drink<sup class="footnote-marker">210</sup><i class="footnote">It is mentioned repeatedly that the woman has to stand “before the Eternal”, i. e., at a place at which the entrance to the Temple can be seen. It follows from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 3:4:1-13" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.3.4.1-13">Mishnah 3:4</a> that the procedure is not executed within the gate but in the women’s courtyard close to the gate or at least in the sanctified inner part of the gate.</i>, and one purifies the woman after childbirth, and the persons healed from skin disease<sup class="footnote-marker">211</sup><i class="footnote">Both the woman after childbirth and the person healed from skin disease are forbidden to “enter the Sanctuary” before their sacrifices have ben offered. Therefore, all their transactions have to be executed in the profane part of the gate, in view of the Temple entrance.</i>. A Cohen seizes her garment<sup class="footnote-marker">212</sup><i class="footnote">According to Rashi and Maimonides, he seizes her neckline.</i>, if it tears it tears, if it frays it frays, until he uncovers her breast<sup class="footnote-marker">213</sup><i class="footnote">The next Mishnah shows that this means “until the top of her breasts starts to be uncovered.”</i>, and he destroys her hairdo. Rebbi Jehudah says, if her breast was beautiful, he did not uncover it, if her hair was beautiful, he did not undo it<sup class="footnote-marker">214</sup><i class="footnote">According to him, only those women can be uncovered who are too ugly to inspire any sexual ideas in men who are watching. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 8a" href="/Sotah.8a">Babli, 8a</a>, Rava quotes a legal principle that a man aroused by one woman will not attack another woman even though he himself knew that this is untrue in reality (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 65a" href="/Ketubot.65a"><i>Ketubot</i> 65a</a>).</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If she said, I am impure, she breaks her <i>ketubah</i> and leaves,” etc. It is written: “The Cohen shall put the woman before the Eternal<sup class="footnote-marker">215</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:18" href="/Numbers.5.18"><i>Num</i>. 5:18</a>.</i>”, that is the Nikanor gate<sup class="footnote-marker">216</sup><i class="footnote">In ritual matters, the Nikanor gate is the entrance to the courtyard of the Tent of Meeting. In <i>Sifry Num</i>. 17, the statement is “before the Eternal”, which means in the East.</i>. Everywhere it is said “before the Eternal”, that means the Nikanor gate. <sup class="footnote-marker">217</sup><i class="footnote">An explanation why an ordeal must be performed in the Temple, based on <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sotah (Lieberman) 1:4" href="/Tosefta_Sotah_(Lieberman).1.4">Tosephta 1:4</a> (S. Lieberman in <i>Tosefta kiFshutah Soṭa</i> 1, line 19.)</i> It is written: “If a man sin against a man.<sup class="footnote-marker">218</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 2:25" href="/I_Samuel.2.25"><i>1S</i>. 2:25</a>. As R. David Fraenckel has noted, it seems that this verse is out of place and should be replaced by <a class="refLink" data-ref="I Kings 8:31-32" href="/I_Kings.8.31-32"><i>1K</i>. 8:31–32</a> which is quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sotah (Lieberman) 1:4" href="/Tosefta_Sotah_(Lieberman).1.4">Tosephta 1:4</a>, combined with <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:27" href="/Numbers.5.27"><i>Num</i>. 5:27</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:28" href="/Numbers.5.28">28</a> as support that the ordeal of the suspected wife is to be held in the Temple: “If a man sin against his neighbor and causes a curse to be put on him … and You will hear in Heaven and will judge Your servants to declare the criminal guilty to turn his way on him [her belly will extend and her genitals shrink] and to clear the just to reward him for his just behavior [if the woman was not impure but pure, she will be declared innocent and be blessed with seed.]”</i>” Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba and Rebbi Joshua ben Levi. Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba explains the verse about the paramour, Rebbi Joshua ben Levi explains the verse about the woman. Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba explains the verse about the paramour: That one feeds and sustains and you come to enjoy yourself. Rebbi Joshua ben Levi explains the verse about the woman: That one feeds and sustains and you turn your eyes to another.
Does one shame out of a doubt<sup class="footnote-marker">219</sup><i class="footnote">If the woman was innocent, she would have been debased without cause.</i>? Rebbi Simlai said<sup class="footnote-marker">220</sup><i class="footnote">In Midrashic sources, the text reads: “anywhere one finds whoring, mass destruction comes to the world and kills good and bad.” <i>Gen. rabba</i>27:10, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Vayikra Rabbah 23:9" href="/Vayikra_Rabbah.23.9"><i>Lev. rabba</i> 23:9</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bamidbar Rabbah 9:41" href="/Bamidbar_Rabbah.9.41"><i>Num. rabba</i>9(41</a>).</i>, anywhere one finds whoring, mass destruction<sup class="footnote-marker">221</sup><i class="footnote">The etymology of אנדרולמוסיה is not known. In <i>Gen. rabba, Arukh</i> reads אנדרומוסיא. While here the meaning could be “human plague”, which could be *ἀνδρολοιμός (Levy), there is another series of sayings mentioning a king who makes war on a rebellious province and causes there אנדרולמוסיה; Jastrow proposes *ἀνδρολημψία “robbery of men”. {Cf. ἀνδροληψία “seizure of foreigners in reprisal for the murder of a citizen abroad” (<i>Lex ap. D</i> 23.82) (E. G.)}. In <i>Gen. rabba</i>32(14): “A parable of a king who ordered his דורלומוסיא in a province and saved his friend by jailing him.” (Following Fürst in <i>Tanḥuma Buber</i>, vol. 2, p. כא, Note 24, the word דורמוסיות, דורמוסיאות, found in other Midrashim, is not a shortened form of אנדרולומסיה.)</i> comes to the world.
The verse said, one recites and after that one shames<sup class="footnote-marker">222</sup><i class="footnote">V. 17 indicates that as a first step, the Cohen prepares the curse-draught. After that (v. 18) he uncovers the woman’s hair. But in the Mishnah, the preparation of the draught is late (2:2). The recitation of the curses can start only after the draught is prepared.</i>. But the Mishnah says, one shames and then one recites? Rebbi Hila said, because the verse says, “he makes stand, he makes her stand<sup class="footnote-marker">223</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:18" href="/Numbers.5.18"><i>Num</i>. 5:18</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:16" href="/Numbers.5.16">16</a>.</i>”; did she ever sit<sup class="footnote-marker">224</sup><i class="footnote">It is forbidden to sit down in the Temple precinct (for everybody except kings of the Davidic line).</i>? But because of the first stand he uncovers her hair<sup class="footnote-marker">225</sup><i class="footnote">The text <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:17-20" href="/Numbers.5.17-20">vv. 17–20</a> is explanatory, not prescriptive. The uncovering of the hair is a consequence of her coming to the Temple. For a coherent parallel (non-rabbinic) reading of the text, cf. the paper by H. C. Brichto, mentioned in the Introduction.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">226</sup><i class="footnote">A parallel in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 6:4:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.6.4.2">Sanhedrin 6:8</a> (fol. 23c, line 17). Similar arguments in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 8a" href="/Sotah.8a">Babli, 8a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 45a" href="/Sanhedrin.45a"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 45a</a>.</i> The argument of Rebbi Jehudah seems inverted. There<sup class="footnote-marker">227</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 6:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.6.4.1">Mishnah <i>Sanhedrin</i> 6:5</a>, speaking about people executed by stoning. According to R. Jehudah, they are clothed in rectangular sheets of cloth.</i>, he says: “One covers a man in front and a woman front and back.<sup class="footnote-marker">228</sup><i class="footnote">But the majority holds that a woman is stoned only fully clothed. Therefore, R. Jehudah seems unconcerned that men should get bad ideas in seeing her.</i>” And here, he says so? There, she goes to her death anyhow, but here, maybe she will be found to be pure and the young priests would attack her<sup class="footnote-marker">229</sup><i class="footnote">This is an explicit statement of R. Jehudah 1:7.</i>. The argument of the rabbis seems inverted. There<sup class="footnote-marker">230</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 6:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.6.4.1">Mishnah <i>Sanhedrin</i> 6:5</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 3:8:2-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.3.8.2-7"><i>Soṭa</i> 3:9</a>.</i>, they say: “A man is stoned naked but no woman is stoned naked.” And here, they say so? There, “you shall love your neighbor as yourself,” choose for him the easiest death<sup class="footnote-marker">231</sup><i class="footnote">Since in rabbinic tradition a person is “stoned” by being pushed down from a high cliff (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 6:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.6.5.1">Mishnah <i>Sanhedrin</i> 6:6</a>), his chances of being killed instantly are greater if his fall is not cushioned by clothing. In the Babli, this is not considered a complete answer; the question is whether shame or suffering is worse.</i>. But here, <sup class="footnote-marker">232</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Ezekiel 23:48" href="/Ezekiel.23.48"><i>Ez</i>. 23:48</a>.</i>“all women should be taught and not do as your whoring.”

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If she was clothed in white, he<sup class="footnote-marker">223</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:18" href="/Numbers.5.18"><i>Num</i>. 5:18</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:16" href="/Numbers.5.16">16</a>.</i> covers her in black. If she wore gold jewelry, chains<sup class="footnote-marker">234</sup><i class="footnote">Semitic plural of Latin <i>catella</i>.</i>, nose rings and finger rings, he removes them from her in order to make her ugly. After that<sup class="footnote-marker">235</sup><i class="footnote">Obviously not after he removed her rings but immediately after he tore her garment (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 1:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.1.5.1">Mishnah 5</a>).</i> he brings an Egyption rope and binds it higher than her breasts. Anyone who wants to look may come and see, except for her male and female slaves because she feels superior to them. All women are permitted to see her<sup class="footnote-marker">236</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 8b" href="/Sotah.8b">Babli, 8b</a>, changes that into an obligation of all women present in the Temple area to come and see. This is unknown to the Yerushalmi.</i>, as it was said: “<sup class="footnote-marker">232</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Ezekiel 23:48" href="/Ezekiel.23.48"><i>Ez</i>. 23:48</a>.</i>”all women should be taught and not do as your whoring.”
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If she was clothed in white,” etc. Our Mishnah [assumes that] they are not fitting her. But if they are fitting her, it does not apply<sup class="footnote-marker">237</sup><i class="footnote">If the woman looks better in black than in white clothes, one leaves her in her white ones. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 8b" href="/Sotah.8b">Babli, 8b</a>, in this case requires that she be given a bad looking garment.</i>.
“He brings an Egyptian rope.” And why an Egyptian rope? Rebbi Isaac said, because she behaved in an Egyptian way<sup class="footnote-marker">238</sup><i class="footnote">The chapter on the incest prohibitions, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 18" href="/Leviticus.18"><i>Lev</i>. 18</a>, starts out: “You should not act in the way of the Land of Egypt”. Therefore, all infractions of the rules of sexual purity are called “Egyptian ways” even though the prohibition of adultery belongs to the “natural law” obligatory for all of mankind, even though the latter is formulated as a positive commandment (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 2:24" href="/Genesis.2.24"><i>Gen</i>. 2:24</a>): “Therefore, a man has to abandon his father and his mother (prohibition of incest in direct line) and cling to his wife (but not to another man’s wife nor to a male).”</i>. Rebbi Jeremiah asked: Does the Egyptian rope prevent, does the Egyptian basket prevent<sup class="footnote-marker">239</sup><i class="footnote">If her garment was not fastened by a rope or if her flour-offering was not presented in a basket woven from palm leaves, is the ceremony invalid? Since neither of these details is mentioned in the verse, it should be obvious that the answer is no. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 8b" href="/Sotah.8b">Babli, 8b</a>, this is the explicit deduction, based on the Mishnah, ascribed to Rav Huna, two generations before R. Jeremiah.</i>? Who pays for them? That one follows what Rebbi said, for Rebbi said<sup class="footnote-marker">240</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 4:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.4.2.1">Mishnah <i>Šeqalim</i> 4:2</a>.</i> that the water supply<sup class="footnote-marker">241</sup><i class="footnote">The open water canal which crosses the Temple yard from North to South.</i>, the city wall and its towers<sup class="footnote-marker">242</sup><i class="footnote">The part bordering on the Temple Mount.</i>, and all public works in the city<sup class="footnote-marker">243</sup><i class="footnote">On the Temple Mount and its surroundings; e. g., the public <i>miqwaot</i>.</i> are paid with the excess of the Temple treasury<sup class="footnote-marker">244</sup><i class="footnote">Any money left on the first of Nisan from the Temple Tax (half a sheqel) of the previous year could no longer be used for the service of the next year. Since it was sanctified public money, it could only be used for the needs of the Temple district other than sacrifices.</i>. The same holds here.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> By the measure a person measures one measures him. She adorned herself for sin, therefore the Omnipresent<sup class="footnote-marker">241</sup><i class="footnote">The open water canal which crosses the Temple yard from North to South.</i> made her ugly. She bared herself for sin, therefore the Omnipresent laid her bare. The genitals were first in sin and after that the belly; the genitals should be punished first and then the belly<sup class="footnote-marker">246</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:21" href="/Numbers.5.21"><i>Num</i>. 5:21</a>: “When the Eternal will make your genitals shrink and your belly extend.” (In the event, v. 27, the order is reversed.) J. C. Brichto (<i>loc. cit</i>. Note 225) identifies the symptoms as pseudocyesis, spurious pregnancy.</i>, while the remainder of the body does not escape<sup class="footnote-marker">247</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbinic tradition (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 1:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.1.3.1">Mishnah 3:4/5</a>) holds that the guilty woman dies from the draught. This is not in the Biblical text.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “By the measure a person measures one measures him,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">248</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sotah (Lieberman) 3:1" href="/Tosefta_Sotah_(Lieberman).3.1">Tosephta 3:1</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 8b" href="/Sotah.8b">Babli 8b</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 81:2" href="/Psalms.81.2"><i>Midrash Tehillim</i> 81(2</a>), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bamidbar Rabbah 9:23" href="/Bamidbar_Rabbah.9.23"><i>Num. rabba</i> 9(23</a>).</i> It was stated in the name of Rebbi Meïr: By the measure a person measures one measures him<sup class="footnote-marker">249</sup><i class="footnote">The maxim “measure for measure” is found many times; e. g. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 105b" href="/Shabbat.105b">Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 105b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 12b" href="/Megillah.12b"><i>Megillah</i> 12b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 90a" href="/Sanhedrin.90a"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 90a</a>, <i>Sifry Num</i>. 106 (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 1:9:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.1.9.2">Halakhah 9</a>), <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael</i> (ed. Horovitz-Rabin) pp. 78,81,131; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bereishit Rabbah 9:13" href="/Bereishit_Rabbah.9.13"><i>Gen. rabba</i> 9(13</a>), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shemot Rabbah 25:13" href="/Shemot_Rabbah.25.13"><i>Ex. rabba</i> 25(13</a>) [shortened 9(9)]; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 6:9-11:32" href="/Genesis.6.9-11.32"><i>Tanḥuma Noaḥ</i> 13, <i>Wa’era</i> 14</a>, <i>Bešallaḥ</i> 2,4, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 12:1-13:59" href="/Leviticus.12.1-13.59"><i>Tazria‘</i> 6</a>; and very frequently in the Gaonic literature.</i>. What is the reason? בְּסַאסְּאָה.<sup class="footnote-marker">250</sup><i class="footnote"><i>1s</i>. 27:8.</i>, seah for <i>seah</i>. Not only a <i>seah</i>, from where a three-<i>qab</i><sup class="footnote-marker">251</sup><i class="footnote">A <i>seah</i> is a Roman <i>urna</i>, 12.85 1. The <i>seah</i> contains 6 <i>qab</i> (κάβος); a <i>terqab</i> is half a <i>seah</i> (Gaonic Commentary to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Kelim 12:3" href="/Mishnah_Kelim.12.3">Mishnah <i>Kelim</i> 12:3</a>; misinterpreted as תְּרֵיקָב “two <i>qab</i>” in Graetz, <i>Geschichte der Judäer</i><sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup> vol. 3, p. 443.)</i>, half a three-<i>qab</i>, half a <i>qab</i>, a quarter [<i>qab</i>], a <i>toman</i><sup class="footnote-marker">252</sup><i class="footnote">A <i>toman</i> is 1/8 <i>qab</i>, identical to a Roman <i>hemina</i>, Greek ἡμίνα, ½<i>sextarius</i>.</i>, half a <i>toman</i>, or an <i>ukla</i><sup class="footnote-marker">253</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli עוכלא; a quarter <i>toman</i>, a Roman <i>acetabulum</i>, 6.6 cl. Qalir (<i>Silluq Šabbat Šeqalim</i>) calls it כלה and reports that it is slightly less than a hundredth of a <i>seah</i>.</i>? The verse says, “for all that is in a <i>seah</i>, breaks the <i>seah</i> noisily<sup class="footnote-marker">254</sup><i class="footnote"><i>1s</i>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 9:4:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.9.4.2">9:4</a>.</i>;” he added many <i>seot</i> here. Not only things that can be measured, from where that small coins add up to a large bill? The verse says, “one to one, to find the bill.<sup class="footnote-marker">255</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Ecclesiastes 7:27" href="/Ecclesiastes.7.27"><i>Eccl</i>. 7:27</a>. This is the end of the Tosephta and the parallel in the Babli.</i>” It happens in the world that a person stumbles in a sin for which one would be punished by death by the hands of Heaven. His ox dies, his hen is lost, his flask breaks, and he hurts his finger, by that the bill is paid in full. Another explanation: One is filled into the next and the bill is established. And what is the fulfillment of the bill? Once<sup class="footnote-marker">256</sup><i class="footnote">This explanation is the opposite of the first, that many small sins add up to one huge bill presented to a man by the Heavenly court.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">257</sup><i class="footnote">Parallels in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 90" href="/Gittin.90">Babli <i>Giṭṭin</i> 90a/b</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sotah (Lieberman) 5:9" href="/Tosefta_Sotah_(Lieberman).5.9">Tosephta 5:9</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bamidbar Rabbah 9:8" href="/Bamidbar_Rabbah.9.8"><i>Num. rabba</i> 9(8</a>).</i> It was stated in the name of Rebbi Meïr: Just as there are opinions in eating and drinking, so opinions are [varied] among men. There is a man, if a fly falls into his cup, he takes it out, throws it away and drinks. That is like a normal man who sees his wife talking to her neighbors and relatives<sup class="footnote-marker">258</sup><i class="footnote">Males. (In one version of the Tosephta, קְרוֹבוֹתֶיהָ “female relatives” but S. Lieberman comments on it as קְרוֹבֶיהָ without remark.) It may be that this refers to the case where the wife talks overly long to her male neighbors.</i>. Or you have a man who if a fly flies over his cup, takes it, pours [the contents] out, and does not taste them. That is a bad human attitude because he thinks of divorcing her<sup class="footnote-marker">259</sup><i class="footnote">Just as the fly did not touch the cup, the wife did nothing wrong; the husband simply looks for an excuse to divorce her without paying.</i>. Or you have a man who, if a fly sits on his cup, takes it and leaves [the cup] untouched. That is Pappos ben Jehudah who locked his wife in. They said to him, did your forefathers behave like this<sup class="footnote-marker">260</sup><i class="footnote">This Aramaic sentence is a commentary of the Talmud; it is not in the other sources.</i>? Or you have a man who finds a dead fly in his cup, takes it, licks it, and drinks. That is the wicked one who sees that his wife is overbearing with her slaves and slave-girls<sup class="footnote-marker">261</sup><i class="footnote">This argument (not found in the Babli, but found in Tosephta and Midrash) seems to contradict the statement of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 1:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.1.6.1">Mishnah 6</a> that every woman is supposed to feel superior to her slaves. The meaning here seems to be that she has no inhibitions to appear in undress before her slaves.</i>, where he would be commanded to divorce her<sup class="footnote-marker">262</sup><i class="footnote">The other sources have a long list of indecent behavior of the wife that should force the husband to divorce her; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 7:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.7.6.1">Mishnah <i>Ketubot</i> 7:6</a>.</i>, as it is said: “She left his house and went and married another man.” The verse called him <i>other</i> because he is not of the class of the first who expelled his wife because of a sexual transgression and the other one stumbled over her<sup class="footnote-marker">263</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 6a" href="/Sotah.6a">Babli, 6a</a>, this interpretation is part of the legal argument that a woman formally accused by her husband cannot be married in levirate after the husband’s death.</i>. If Heaven gives him credit, he will divorce her; otherwise, at the end she will bury him as it is said, “or if the latter man dies.” That man deserves to die since he brought that woman into his house.
Samuel<sup class="footnote-marker">264</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 27a" href="/Sotah.27a">Babli, 27a</a>, Samuel is reported saying that it is better to marry a woman of questionable character born of a woman of good character than to marry the daughter of a possible adulteress. The opinion attributed to Samuel here is that of R. Joḥanan in the Babli.</i> said, one separates from the whoring woman but not from her house. This parallels what Rebbi Joḥanan said: <sup class="footnote-marker">265</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Yevamot (Lieberman) 12:8" href="/Tosefta_Yevamot_(Lieberman).12.8">Tosephta <i>Yebamot</i> 12:8</a>, quoted as tannaïtic text in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 27a" href="/Sotah.27a">Babli, 27a</a>.</i>“If a married woman committed adultery, her children are her husband’s since most of the intercourse was with the husband.”
One verse says: “For swelling of the belly and shrinking of the genitals,” another verse says “her belly will swell and her genitals shrink”, and another verse says “when the Eternal will make your genitals shrink and your belly swell.”<sup class="footnote-marker">266</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:22" href="/Numbers.5.22"><i>Num</i>. 5:22</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:27" href="/Numbers.5.27">27</a>, the belly is mentioned before the genitals but in v. 21 the order is inverted.</i> Does one verse weigh more than two verses? Rebbi Mana said, here for the act, there conditional<sup class="footnote-marker">267</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:22" href="/Numbers.5.22"><i>Num</i>. 5:22</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:27" href="/Numbers.5.27">27</a> is a description of what will or did happen; v. 21 is a curse formula conditional upon the woman having sinned.</i>. Rebbi Abin said, you may even say in both cases for the act, in both cases as a condition. “For swelling of the belly and shrinking of the genitals” for the adulterer<sup class="footnote-marker">268</sup><i class="footnote">The infinitives in the sentence are gender neutral. The ordeal indirectly also affects the presumed adulterer, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 6:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.6.1.1">Mishnah 6:1</a>. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 27a" href="/Sotah.27a">Babli, 27a</a>, this is a conclusion of Rebbi.</i>. “Her belly will swell and her genitals shrink”, for the woman. Reason decides, “the genitals were first in sin and after that the belly; the genitals should be punished first and then the belly, while the remainder of the body does not escape.” Rebbi Abba the son of Rebbi Pappeus used this as conclusion of a sermon: Since for [heavenly] punishment, which is meted out sparingly, one limb is smitten and all limbs hurt, in the case of [heavenly] benefices, which are given out in abundance<sup class="footnote-marker">269</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 11a" href="/Sotah.11a">Babli 11a</a>; extensive discussion in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sotah (Lieberman) 4:1" href="/Tosefta_Sotah_(Lieberman).4.1"><i>Tosephta</i> 4:1</a>.</i>, [may we] not [expect] so much more!

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">270</sup><i class="footnote">This Mishnah is somewhat extended in the Babli, very much enlarged in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sotah (Lieberman) 3:15-16" href="/Tosefta_Sotah_(Lieberman).3.15-16">Tosephta 3:15–16</a>. It is a direct continuation of the first sentence of the preceding Mishnah.</i> Simson followed his eyes, therefore the Philistines pierced his eyes. Absalom beautified himself with his hair, therefore he was hung by his hair. Because he came to his father’s ten concubines, therefore ten lances<sup class="footnote-marker">271</sup><i class="footnote">Hebraized plural of Greek λόγχη “lance”.</i> were put into him, as it was said<sup class="footnote-marker">272</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Samuel 18:15" href="/II_Samuel.18.15"><i>2S</i>. 18:15</a>.</i>: “Ten squires, Joab’s weapons carriers, surrounded him.” And because he stole three steals<sup class="footnote-marker">273</sup><i class="footnote">In Mishnah mss. of the Maimonides tradition: שֶׁגָּנַב שְׁלֹשָׁה לְבָבוֹת “he stole three minds”; “misleading” is called “stealing the heart” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 31:20" href="/Genesis.31.20"><i>Gen</i>. 31:20</a>). Cf. also <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael, Masekhta deŠirah</i>, Chap. 6.</i>, his father’s opinion, the court’s opinion, and Israel’s opinion, therefore three staves were stuck into him, as it was said<sup class="footnote-marker">274</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Samuel 18:14" href="/II_Samuel.18.14"><i>2S</i>. 18:14</a>.</i>: “He took three staves in his hand and stuck them into Absalom’s heart.”
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Simson followed his eyes,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">275</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sotah (Lieberman) 3:15" href="/Tosefta_Sotah_(Lieberman).3.15">Tosephta 3:15</a>, [<a class="refLink" data-ref="Bamidbar Rabbah 9:24" href="/Bamidbar_Rabbah.9.24"><i>Num. rabba</i>9(24</a>)]; anonymous Babli 9b, <i>Mekhilta dR. Simeon bar Ioḥai</i> p. 74.</i> It was stated: Rebbi says, because he started going astray at Gaza, therefore his punishment was at Gaza. But is it not written: “Simson descended into Timna<sup class="footnote-marker">276</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Judges 14:1" href="/Judges.14.1"><i>Jud</i>. 14:1</a>. Gaza is mentioned only <a class="refLink" data-ref="Judges 16:1" href="/Judges.16.1">v. 16:1</a>.</i>”? Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman said, that was a formal marriage<sup class="footnote-marker">277</sup><i class="footnote">Marriage is mentioned in Timna, a prostitute in Gaza. It is presumed that the girl from Timna was converted following the rules so that the marriage was not sinful.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">278</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 10a" href="/Sotah.10a">Babli 10a</a>, with different names. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bamidbar Rabbah 9:24" href="/Bamidbar_Rabbah.9.24"><i>Num. rabba</i> 9(24</a>), the Yerushalmi version.</i> One verse says, “Simson descended into Timna”, and another verse says, “behold, you father-in-law ascends to Timna<sup class="footnote-marker">279</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 38:13" href="/Genesis.38.13"><i>Gen</i>. 38:13</a>.</i>.” Rav said, there were two Timnot, one of Jehudah and one of Simson. Rebbi Simon said, there was only one Timna(ta). Why does it mention decent and ascent? But in the case of Jehudah, because it was in the name of Heaven, ascent is written. In the case of Simson, because it was not in the name of Heaven, descent is written. Rebbi Ayvu bar Naggari said, for example like Bet Ma‘on<sup class="footnote-marker">280</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bamidbar Rabbah 9:24" href="/Bamidbar_Rabbah.9.24"><i>Num. rabba</i> 9(24</a>).</i> to which one descends from Palatatha and ascends from Tiberias.
<sup class="footnote-marker">281</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bamidbar Rabbah 9:24" href="/Bamidbar_Rabbah.9.24"><i>Num. rabba</i> 9(24</a>).</i> It is written: “They came to the vineyards in direction of Timna.<sup class="footnote-marker">282</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Judges 14:5" href="/Judges.14.5"><i>Jud</i>. 14:5</a>.</i>” Rav<sup class="footnote-marker">283</sup><i class="footnote">The title has to be “Rebbi”.</i> Samuel ben Rav Isaac said, this teaches you that his father and mother were showing him the vineyards in direction of Timna sown in several kinds and saying to him: My son, just as their vineyards are sown with several kinds so their daughters are sown with several kinds<sup class="footnote-marker">284</sup><i class="footnote">They are children of adultery. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Judges 14:4" href="/Judges.14.4"><i>Jud</i>. 14:4</a>.</i>. “And his father and mother did not know that this was from the Eternal, that he was looking for a pretext from the Philistines.” Rebbi Eleazar said, In seven places it is written: “Do not contract marriage with them.<sup class="footnote-marker">285</sup><i class="footnote">That verse is only in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 7:3" href="/Deuteronomy.7.3"><i>Deut</i>. 7:3</a>. But the prohibition of Canaanite women is also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 34:16" href="/Exodus.34.16"><i>Ex</i>. 34:16</a>. Outside the Pentateuch, the prohibition is mentioned <a class="refLink" data-ref="Malachi 2:11" href="/Malachi.2.11"><i>Mal</i>. 2:11</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ezra 9:2" href="/Ezra.9.2"><i>Ezra</i> 9:2</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Ezra 9:12" href="/Ezra.9.12">12</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Ezra 9:14" href="/Ezra.9.14">14</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nehemiah 10:31" href="/Nehemiah.10.31"><i>Neh</i>. 10:31</a>.</i>” Rebbi Abun said, to prohibit seven peoples<sup class="footnote-marker">286</sup><i class="footnote">The seven Canaanite tribes enumerated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 15:20-21" href="/Genesis.15.20-21"><i>Gen</i>. 15:20–21</a>. Philistines are not mentioned in that list.</i>. And here, it says so<sup class="footnote-marker">287</sup><i class="footnote">It is not obvious what the remark means. According to R. David Fraenckel, since Simson’s relations with Philistine women were not forbidden, why was he punished? According to R. Moses Margalit, since all non-Jewish women are forbidden in the interpretation of pentateuchal law in <i>Mal., Ezra, Neh</i>., how can it be said that his first marriage was (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Judges 14:4" href="/Judges.14.4"><i>Jud</i>. 14:4</a>) “from the Eternal”?</i>? Rebbi Isaac said, “if he scoffs about the scoffers.<sup class="footnote-marker">288</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 3:34" href="/Proverbs.3.34"><i>Prov</i>. 3:34</a>. According to R. David Fraenckel, since at least Simson’s relations with the Gazean prostitute and Dalilah were illegitimate, he deserved punishment.</i>”
It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">289</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Judges 13:25" href="/Judges.13.25"><i>Jud</i>. 13:25</a>.</i>: “The spirit of the Eternal started to ring in him in the encampment of Dan, between Ṣor‘a and Eshtaol.” Two Amoraïm. One said, when the holy spirit was resting on him, his steps were as from Ṣor‘a to Eshtaol. The other said, when the holy spirit was resting on him, his hairs were tolling like a bell and their sound went as from Ṣor‘a to Eshtaol. “The Eternal blessed him<sup class="footnote-marker">290</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Judges 13:24" href="/Judges.13.24"><i>Jud</i>. 13:24</a>.</i>”. Rebbi Huna in the name of Rebbi Yose: That his sex life was that of anybody<sup class="footnote-marker">291</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 10a" href="/Sotah.10a">Babli, 10a</a>, that his sex organ was of normal size even though otherwise he was a giant.</i>. “I shall revenge <i>one</i> revenge of my two eyes from the Philistines.<sup class="footnote-marker">292</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Judges 16:28" href="/Judges.16.28"><i>Jud</i>. 16:28</a>. The statement is also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bamidbar Rabbah 9:24" href="/Bamidbar_Rabbah.9.24"><i>Num. rabba</i> 9(24</a>).</i>” Rebbi Aḥa said, he said before Him: Master of the Universe! Give me the reward of one of my eyes in this world and the reward of the other eye be prepared for me in the World to Come. One verse says<sup class="footnote-marker">293</sup><i class="footnote">This should be <a class="refLink" data-ref="Judges 15:20" href="/Judges.15.20"><i>Jud</i>. 15:20</a>, but, as already <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Shabbat 55b:18:1" href="/Tosafot_on_Shabbat.55b.18.1">Tosaphot (<i>Šabbat</i> 55b, <i>s</i>. <i>v</i>. מעבירם</a>) noted, all texts and versions have “twenty years”. David Qimḥi, following the reading of the parallel in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bamidbar Rabbah 14:21" href="/Bamidbar_Rabbah.14.21"><i>Num. rabba</i> 14(21</a>), in his commentary to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Judges 16:31" href="/Judges.16.31"><i>Jud</i>. 16:31</a>, reads “twenty years” in the text here and quotes R. Aḥa as explanation of the duplication of the notices in 15:20, 16:31. While our text here is confirmed by the Rome ms., “40 years”, Qimḥi’s reading as supported by <i>Num. rabba</i> (whose first part probably is Provençal) can be taken as testimony for the Provençal text.</i>: “He judged Israel for forty years.” And another verse says<sup class="footnote-marker">294</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Judges 16:31" href="/Judges.16.31"><i>Jud</i>. 16:31</a>.</i>: “He had judged Israel for twenty years.” Rebbi Aḥa said, this teaches that the Philistines were afraid of him twenty years after his death as they were afraid of him during his lifetime.
“Absalom beautified himself with his hair.” Rebbi Ḥanina said, it<sup class="footnote-marker">296</sup><i class="footnote">The hair.</i> was like a large carob tree. Should I think like a javelin<sup class="footnote-marker">297</sup><i class="footnote">That he wore a pigtail.</i>? Rebbi Bibi in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: It was made like this and like that.
<sup class="footnote-marker">298</sup><i class="footnote">The following paragraphs are from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 7:3:11" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.7.3.11"><i>Peah</i> Chapter 7, Notes 71–75</a>. They are added here only because of the mention of a carob tree. Cf. also <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 91b" href="/Bava_Batra.91b">Babli <i>Baba Batra</i> 91b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 112a" href="/Ketubot.112a"><i>Ketubot</i> 112a</a>.</i> Rebbi Ḥanina said, when I immigrated here, I took my son’s belt and the belt of my father-in-law<sup class="footnote-marker">299</sup><i class="footnote">That is a bad corruption. In the text of <i>Peah</i>: חֲמֹרִי “my donkey”.</i> to measure around a young carob tree of the Land of Israel and it was not enough. I cut one carob pod and it filled my hand with honey.
Rebbi Joḥanan said, the second quality fruit we ate in our youth tasted better that the peaches we ate in our old age, because during his lifetime the world changed.
Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba said, one <i>seah</i> of Arbel grain did yield one <i>seah</i> of fine flour, one <i>seah</i> of white flour, one <i>seah</i> of dark flour, one <i>seah</i> of bran, one <i>seah</i> of coarse bran. But today, we do not even get one for one.
<sup class="footnote-marker">300</sup><i class="footnote">Parallels in <i>Midrash Samuel</i> 13, 27; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bamidbar Rabbah 9:29" href="/Bamidbar_Rabbah.9.29"><i>Num. rabba</i> 9(29</a>); <i>Tanḥuma Buber Ḥuqqat</i> 8, <i>Tanḥuma Ḥuqqat</i> 4; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 49a" href="/Sanhedrin.49a">Babli <i>Sanhedrin</i> 49a</a>.</i> It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">301</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Samuel 14:25" href="/II_Samuel.14.25"><i>2S</i>. 14:25</a>.</i>: “No man in Israel was beautiful like Absalom, to be very much praised.” I could think, in everything. The verse says, “from his foot sole to the top of his forehead<sup class="footnote-marker">302</sup><i class="footnote">But his hair was questionable.</i>”. It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">303</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 9:2" href="/I_Samuel.9.2"><i>1S</i>. 9:2</a>.</i>: “He had a son whose name was Saul, select and good, no one of the Children of Israel was better than he.” I could think, in everything. The verse says, “from his shoulder on he was higher than all the people.” But about Abner, what is written<sup class="footnote-marker">304</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Samuel 3:38" href="/II_Samuel.3.38"><i>2S</i>. 3:38</a>.</i>? “You should know that a leader and great man fell today in Israel.” <sup class="footnote-marker">305</sup><i class="footnote">To the end of the paragraph, this is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 1:1:47-48" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.1.1.47-48"><i>Peah</i> 1:1, Notes 176–179</a>. The question asked is, why did Providence let Abner lose his usual caution when dealing with Joab. The presumed argument between Saul and Abner is explained in detail in the commentary by R. Ḥananel (Kairuan, early 11th Cent.) to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 49a" href="/Sanhedrin.49a"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 49a</a>, quoted in <i>Arukh</i> (<i>s. v</i>. בור ז) as Yerushalmi text; but clearly it is a paraphrase. (Printed on the margin of the Wilna Babli).</i> Why was Abner killed? Rebbi Joshua ben Levi, Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish, and the rabbis. Rebbi Joshua ben Levi said, because he made fun of the lives of his squires as it is said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="II Samuel 2:14" href="/II_Samuel.2.14"><i>2Sam</i>. 2:14</a>): “Let the squires get up and play before us.” R. Simeon ben Laqish said, because he prefaced his name before that of David; that is what is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="II Samuel 3:12" href="/II_Samuel.3.12"><i>2Sam</i>. 3:12</a>) “Abner sent messengers to David the underling, saying: Whose is the land?” He wrote: From Abner to David. The rabbis say, because he did not let Shaul make peace with David. That is what is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 24:5" href="/I_Samuel.24.5"><i>1Sam</i>. 24:5</a>): “My father, see, but see the corner of your coat in my hand!” He said to him, what do you want from the prattling of this one, it was torn off by thorns. But when they came to the circle, he said to him (<a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 26:14" href="/I_Samuel.26.14"><i>1Sam</i>. 26:14</a>): “Can you answer, Abner?” About the corner, you said it was torn off by a thorn; were spear and pitcher cut off by a thorn? But some say, because he had it in his power to intervene for Nob, the city of priests, and he did not intervene.
<sup class="footnote-marker">306</sup><i class="footnote">The text is copied in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bamidbar Rabbah 9:29" href="/Bamidbar_Rabbah.9.29"><i>Num. rabba</i> 9(29</a>).</i>“And because he stole three steals, his father’s opinion, the court’s opinion, and Israel’s opinion.” His father’s opinion, as it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">307</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Samuel 15:7" href="/II_Samuel.15.7"><i>2S</i>. 15:7</a>.</i>: “It was after forty years.” David himself did not rule more than forty years, and here, it says so? But it is from the time that Israel demanded a king<sup class="footnote-marker">308</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Temurah 14b" href="/Temurah.14b">Babli <i>Temurah</i> 14b</a>; ascribed to R. Joshua. The source is <i>Seder ‘Olam</i> Chapter 14; cf. the extensive commentary in the author’s edition (Northvale NJ, 1998), p. 139.</i>. “For your servant made a vow<sup class="footnote-marker">309</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Samuel 15:8" href="/II_Samuel.15.8"><i>2S</i>. 15:8</a>.</i>.” He said to him, what else do you want? He said to him, write me a tablet that two men should go with me. He said to him, tell me whom you want and I shall write. He said to him, write it to open and I shall take whom I want. He wrote it open. He went and added two people each until he had added 200 people. That is what is written<sup class="footnote-marker">310</sup><i class="footnote"><i>2S</i>.15:11.</i>, “with Absalom went 200 invited people from Jerusalem, going in their simplicity.” Invited, from David. Going in their simplicity, of Absalom. “They did not know anything,” of Aḥitophel’s counsel. Rebbi Huna in the name of Rebbi Aḥa: They all were presiding judges. Then they saw the things. They went to change sides. They said: Master of all universes: Let us fall in David’s hand and do not let fall David in our hands! For if we fall in David’s hand, he will have mercy on us. But if, Heaven forbid, David would fall in our hands, we would not have mercy on him. That is what David said<sup class="footnote-marker">311</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 55:19" href="/Psalms.55.19"><i>Ps</i>. 55:19</a>; cf. <i>Midrash Tehillim</i>55[4].</i>, “He saved me in peace from my confidant, for the majority were with me!” “The court’s opinion”. “And Absalom said, who would make me the judge of the land.<sup class="footnote-marker">312</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Samuel 15:4" href="/II_Samuel.15.4"><i>2S</i>. 15:4</a>.</i>” “So Absalom would act.<sup class="footnote-marker">313</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Samuel 15:6" href="/II_Samuel.15.6"><i>2S</i>. 15:6</a>.</i>” “And Israel’s opinion.” Absalom insinuated himself with the men of Israel.<sup class="footnote-marker">313</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Samuel 15:6" href="/II_Samuel.15.6"><i>2S</i>. 15:6</a>.</i>”

Halakhah 9

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">314</sup><i class="footnote">This Mishnah and the next are reproduced and extended in Tosephta Chap. 4, <i>Mekhilta</i> בשלח פתיחתא.</i> Similarly, for good deeds. Miriam waited for Moses one hour as it is said<sup class="footnote-marker">315</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 2:4" href="/Exodus.2.4"><i>Ex</i>. 2:4</a>.</i>: “His sister stood by from afar.” Therefore, all of Israel stayed put for her for seven days in the prairie as it is said<sup class="footnote-marker">316</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 12:15" href="/Numbers.12.15"><i>Num</i>. 12:15</a>.</i>: “The people did not travel until Miriam was taken in.”
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Similarly, for good deeds. Miriam waited for Moses one hour as it is said<sup class="footnote-marker">315</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 2:4" href="/Exodus.2.4"><i>Ex</i>. 2:4</a>.</i>: ‘His sister stood by from afar’ etc. Therefore, <sup class="footnote-marker">317</sup><i class="footnote">This version corresponds to the Mishnah in the Babli.</i> Israel stayed put for her for seven days in the prairie as it is said<sup class="footnote-marker">316</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 12:15" href="/Numbers.12.15"><i>Num</i>. 12:15</a>.</i>: ‘The people did not travel until Miriam was taken in.’ ” <sup class="footnote-marker">318</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Shemot Rabbah 1:26" href="/Shemot_Rabbah.1.26"><i>Ex. rabba</i> 1:26</a>. A similar homily is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 11a" href="/Sotah.11a">Babli, 11a</a>.</i> Rebbi Joḥanan said, the entire verse was said in the language of inspiration: “His sister <i>stood by</i>,” “I saw the Eternal <i>standing</i> on the altar.<sup class="footnote-marker">319</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Amos 9:1" href="/Amos.9.1"><i>Am</i>. 9:1</a>.</i>” “His <i>sister</i>,” “say to wisdom: you are my <i>sister.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">320</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 7:4" href="/Proverbs.7.4"><i>Prov</i>. 7:4</a>.</i>” “<i>From far</i>”, “<i>from far</i> away the Eternal appeared to me<sup class="footnote-marker">321</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jeremiah 31:2" href="/Jeremiah.31.2"><i>Jer</i>. 31:2</a>.</i>.” “To <i>know</i>”, “for the earth will be full of the Eternal’s <i>knowledge</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">322</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 11:9" href="/Isaiah.11.9"><i>Is</i>. 11:9</a>.</i>” “What will be <i>done</i> with him”, “because the Eternal will not <i>do</i> anything unless He uncovered His counsel to His servants, the prophets.<sup class="footnote-marker">323</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Amos 3:7" href="/Amos.3.7"><i>Am</i>. 3:7</a>.</i>”

Halakhah 10

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Joseph had the merit to bury his father and none of his brothers was greater than he, as it is said: “Joseph ascended to bury his father.<sup class="footnote-marker">324</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 50:7" href="/Genesis.50.7"><i>Gen</i>. 50:7</a>.</i>” Who would be greater than Joseph? Only Moses could occupy himself with him. Moses had the merit to take Joseph’s bones and no one in Israel was greater than he, as it is said: “Moses took Joseph’s bones with him.<sup class="footnote-marker">325</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 13:19" href="/Exodus.13.19"><i>Ex</i>. 13:19</a>.</i>” Who would be greater than Moses? Only the Holy One, Praise to Him, could occupy himself with him, as it is said: “He buried him in the valley<sup class="footnote-marker">326</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 34:6" href="/Deuteronomy.34.6"><i>Deut</i>. 34:6</a>. Another tradition, <i>Sifry Num</i>. 32, in the name of R. Ismael counts this verse as one of the three instances where אוֹתוֹ does not mean “him” but “himself”.</i>.” Not only about Moses was this said but about all the just, as it is said: “Your righteousness will precede you, the glory of the Eternal will gather you in.<sup class="footnote-marker">327</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 58:8" href="/Isaiah.58.8"><i>Is</i>. 58:8</a>.</i>”
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Joseph had the merit to bury his father and none of his brothers was greater than he,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">328</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bereishit Rabbah 100:7" href="/Bereishit_Rabbah.100.7"><i>Gen. rabba</i> 100(7</a>).</i> Rebbi Isaac said: The glory of the Lifegiver of the Worlds<sup class="footnote-marker">329</sup><i class="footnote">The Sephardic pronunciation חַי follows Maimonides (<i>Hilkhot Yesode Hatorah</i> 2:9). Ashkenazic pronunciation follows the Biblical חֵי (<i>Dan</i>. 13:7). The Worlds are this world, the World to Come, and all other possible worlds He created.</i> was with them. It is written: “They came to the threshing place of the brambles.<sup class="footnote-marker">330</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 50:10" href="/Genesis.50.10"><i>Gen</i>. 50:10</a>.</i>” <sup class="footnote-marker">331</sup><i class="footnote">The same question, in the framework of a different sermon, in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 13a" href="/Sotah.13a">Babli, 13a</a>.</i> Since when does the bramble-bush have a threshing place? Rebbi Samuel ben Naḥman said, we checked in all of Scripture and did not find a place called “brambles”. What are “brambles”? These are the Canaanites who should have been threshed like brambles. By which merit were they saved? By the merit of: “The inhabitants of the land, the Canaanites, saw the mourning at the threshing place of the brambles.<sup class="footnote-marker">332</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 50:11" href="/Genesis.50.11"><i>Gen</i>. 50:11</a>.</i>” What act of kindness did they do for him? Rebbi Eleazar said, they unbelted<sup class="footnote-marker">333</sup><i class="footnote">As a sign of mourning, to stay in their houses.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, they undid the knots on their shoulders<sup class="footnote-marker">334</sup><i class="footnote">Also a sign of mourning, not to appear in their togas.</i>. The rabbis said, they stood up<sup class="footnote-marker">335</sup><i class="footnote">They translate “the inhabitants of the land” as: “those sitting on the earth”.</i>. Rebbi Yudan bar Shalom said, they pointed with their fingers and said, “a heavy mourning is that for Egypt”. <sup class="footnote-marker">336</sup><i class="footnote">The entire paragraph is a sermon in honor of the local burial society.</i> Now these who did no act of kindness with their hands or feet; look what the Holy One, praise to Him, did reward them with; Israel who are used to do acts of kindness with their hands or feet for their great and their little ones, [they deserve] so much more!
<sup class="footnote-marker">337</sup><i class="footnote">Line 1 in the editio princeps. This sermon is an appendix to the preceding one; also found in Gen. Rabba 100.</i>Rebbi Abbahu said, these seventy days between letters and letters<sup class="footnote-marker">378</sup><i class="footnote">Between Haman’s letters on Nisan 13 (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Esther 3:12" href="/Esther.3.12"><i>Esth</i>. 3:12</a>) and Mordokhai”s letters of Sivan 23 (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Esther 8:9" href="/Esther.8.9"><i>Esth</i>. 8:9</a>). If the months are alternatingly of 29 and 30 days, there are exactly 70 days between the dates.</i> correspond to the seventy days in which the Egyptians performed kindness with our father Jacob<sup class="footnote-marker">379</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 50:3" href="/Genesis.50.3"><i>Gen</i>. 50:3</a>.</i>.
It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">380</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Samuel 1:17-18" href="/II_Samuel.1.17-18"><i>2S</i>. 1:17–18</a>.</i>: “And David sang this elegy”, etc., “and said: To teach the people of Judah archery.” Is it reasonable that he had to teach the people of Judah, who was he<sup class="footnote-marker">381</sup><i class="footnote">Instead of מי היה, the Rome ms. reads נהיה; probably a corruption. The problem is that (1) the mention of Jehudah in a dirge about the slain of Benjamin is somewhat out of place, and (2) that David, who had a magic bow (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 18:35" href="/Psalms.18.35"><i>Ps</i>. 18:35</a>), long ago was a teacher of archery. “He” refers to David.</i>? But David said, when the just have disappeared, the haters come and attack Israel. “Is that not written in the book Yashar?” Two Amoraïm. One says, that is the book of Genesis. But the other said, that is the fifth of Numbers<sup class="footnote-marker">382</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 25a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.25a">Babli, <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 25a</a>, R. Joḥanan identifies the book Yashar with Genesis, R. Eleazar with Deuteronomy. In the Babli, the book of Yashar is <i>not</i> a book of wars. “Fifth” is the standard rabbinic name for any one of the five books of the Pentateuch.</i>. The one who said, this is the book of Genesis, is understandable<sup class="footnote-marker">383</sup><i class="footnote">Since it records the victory of Abraham over the kings of the East.</i>. But the one who said, that is the fifth of Numbers, what war is reported there? <sup class="footnote-marker">384</sup><i class="footnote">From here to the end of the paragraph, the text is also in <i>Yoma</i> 1:1, fol. 38b.</i>“The Children of Israel travelled from the springs of Bene-Ya‘aqon to Mosera; there Aaron died<sup class="footnote-marker">385</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 10:6" href="/Deuteronomy.10.6"><i>Deut</i>. 10:6</a>.</i>.” Did Aaron die at Mosera? Did he not die on Mount Hor? That is what is written: “Aaron the Cohen ascended Mount Hor and died there.<sup class="footnote-marker">386</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 33:38" href="/Numbers.33.38"><i>Num</i>. 33:38</a>.</i>” But when Aaron died and the clouds of glory disappeared, the Canaanites desired to attack Israel. That is what is written: “The Canaanite, the king of Arad, dweller in the Southland, heard that Israel came by the way of the scouts.<sup class="footnote-marker">387</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 33:40" href="/Numbers.33.40"><i>Num</i>. 33:40</a>. The same interpretation is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Rosh Hashanah 3a" href="/Rosh_Hashanah.3a">Babli, <i>Roš Haššanah</i> 3a</a>. Cf. also <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bamidbar Rabbah 19:11" href="/Bamidbar_Rabbah.19.11"><i>Num. rabba</i> 19(11</a>), <i>Tanḥuma Buber Ḥuqqat</i> 42, <i>Tanḥuma Ḥuqqat</i> 18; <i>Threni rabbati</i> 1(64); <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sotah (Lieberman) 11:1" href="/Tosefta_Sotah_(Lieberman).11.1">Tosephta 11:1</a>; <i>Sifry Num</i>. 82.</i>” What is “the way of the scouts”? He heard that Aaron died, the great scout, who did scout the way for them<sup class="footnote-marker">388</sup><i class="footnote">Since Aaron was responsible for the Ark which was the pathfinder (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 10:33" href="/Numbers.10.33"><i>Num</i>. 10:33</a>).</i>. They came and attacked them. Then Israel wanted to return to Egypt and travelled eight stations backward. The tribe of Levi ran after them and killed eight families from them<sup class="footnote-marker">389</sup><i class="footnote">In the catalog of families in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 26" href="/Numbers.26"><i>Num</i>. 26</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 8" href="/Numbers.8">8</a> families are missing compared to the enumeration of grandsons of Jacob in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 46:5" href="/Genesis.46.5"><i>Gen</i>. 46: 5</a> from Benjamin and 1 each from Simeon, Gad, and Asher.</i>. They also killed four of their families. That is what is written: “The Amramite, the Yiṣharite, the Ḥebronite, the Uzielite.<sup class="footnote-marker">390</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Chronicles 26:23" href="/I_Chronicles.26.23"><i>1Chr</i>. 26:23</a>, an isolated verse seemingly without connection to what comes before and after. This is interpreted to mean that David took care to re-establish these families after they had been decimated. The only levitic family missing in the list of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 26" href="/Numbers.26"><i>Num</i>. 26 is <i>Šim‘î</a></i>.</i>” When were they re-established? In David’s time. That is what is written: “In his days, the just will blossom<sup class="footnote-marker">391</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 72:2" href="/Psalms.72.2"><i>Ps</i>. 72:2</a>.</i>”. They said, what caused us all this bloodshed? They said, because we did not perform kindness for that just man. They went and organized a eulogy and performed kindness for that just man. The verse considers it as if he died and was buried there where they performed kindness for that just man.
“With him<sup class="footnote-marker">325</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 13:19" href="/Exodus.13.19"><i>Ex</i>. 13:19</a>.</i>”. Rebbi Crispus in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: You do it for yourself<sup class="footnote-marker">392</sup><i class="footnote">Moses brought Joseph’s coffin <i>for himself</i>, that God would do for him what he did for Joseph.</i>. Rebbi Ḥama bar Ḥanina: A parable of a king who was marrying off his son. There came a prefect<sup class="footnote-marker">393</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ἔπαρχος.</i> to carry the litter<sup class="footnote-marker">394</sup><i class="footnote">In which the bride was carried to the wedding ceremony; the organizers thought that being a litter carrier was beneath the dignity of the prefect.</i>, and they did not admit him. The king said, let him; tomorrow he will marry off his daughter and I can honor him the way he honored me. As it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">395</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 46:4" href="/Genesis.46.4"><i>Gen</i>. 46:4</a>.</i>: “I shall descend with you to Egypt and I shall bring you up, also ascending.” Why does the verse say, “also ascending”? I shall bring you up and the other tribes also I shall bring up. That teaches that every tribe brought the bones of their ancestor with them<sup class="footnote-marker">396</sup><i class="footnote">A slightly different interpretation in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bereishit Rabbah 94:6" href="/Bereishit_Rabbah.94.6"><i>Gen. rabba</i> 94(6</a>).</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">397</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sotah (Lieberman) 4:8" href="/Tosefta_Sotah_(Lieberman).4.8">Tosephta 4:8</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 13b" href="/Sotah.13b">Babli 13b</a>, <i>Sifry Num</i>. 106.</i> It was stated in the name of Rebbi Jehudah: If it had not been written, it would have been impossible to say: This teaches us that when Moses died, he was lying on the wings of the <i>Shekhina</i><sup class="footnote-marker">398</sup><i class="footnote">The Divine presence on Earth. Parallels about the burial of Moses, cf. <i>Sifry Deut</i>. 355,357, <i>Midrash Tannaïm</i> p. 224; <i>Abot dR. Nathan</i> (Schechter) A 12, B 25; <i>Bet ha-Midrasch</i> (Jellinek) vol. 1 p. 115, vol. 6 p. 71.</i> about four miles, from the inheritance of the tribe of Reuben to the inheritance of the tribe of Gad, since he died in the inheritance of the tribe of Reuben and was buried in the inheritance of the tribe of Gad. From where that he died in the inheritance of the tribe of Reuben? Because it was said, “the tribe of Reuben built Ḥešbon, Elaleh, Qiryatayim, and Nebo.<sup class="footnote-marker">399</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 32:38" href="/Numbers.32.38"><i>Num</i>. 32:38</a>.</i>” And it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">400</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 32:49" href="/Deuteronomy.32.49"><i>Deut</i>. 32:49</a>.</i>: “Ascend this ‘Abarim mountain, the Mountain of Nebo, and die on the Mountain up which you will climb.” And from where that he was buried in the inheritance of the tribe of Gad? For it was said<sup class="footnote-marker">401</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 33:20-21" href="/Deuteronomy.33.20-21"><i>Deut</i>. 33:20–21</a>.</i>: “To Gad he said, praised be He Who enlarges Gad, etc. For there the part of the lawgiver is hidden.” The Holy One, praise to Him, added: “He came at the head of the people.” The angels of the service said, “he executed the justice of the Eternal”, and Israel were saying, “and His laws with Israel.<sup class="footnote-marker">402</sup><i class="footnote">Since one cannot assume that Moses praised himself.</i>” All together were saying: “<sup class="footnote-marker">403</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 57:2" href="/Isaiah.57.2"><i>Is</i>. 57:2</a>. A fitting end of a sermon of consolation of mourners.</i> May he come in peace, may they rest on their couches, he goes in His pleasure.”

Chapter 2



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The husband; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:15" href="/Numbers.5.15"><i>Num</i>. 5:15</a>. The volume of the offering was 1/10 of an <i>epha</i> or about one US Gallon. Cf. also <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 1:6:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.1.6.3">Chapter 1, Note 239</a>.</i> brought her flour offering in an Egyptian palm-leaf basket and then puts it on her hands to tire her out. All flour offerings are from start to finish in a Temple vessel except this which at the start is in an Egyptian palm-leaf basket and at the end in a Temple vessel<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">All other offerings must be in a Temple vessel when given to a Cohen to be processed. It is true that the <i>soṭah</i>-offering also must be in a Temple vessel once the Cohen takes it, but as long as the Cohen is still occupied with the preliminaries it may not be in a Temple vessel.</i>. All flour offerings need oil and incense except this one which needs neither oil nor incense<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">This is not quite correct; the offering of the poor sinner (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:11" href="/Leviticus.5.11"><i>Lev</i>. 5:11</a>) is likewise without olive oil and incense.</i>. All flour offerings come from wheat except this one which only comes from barley. The <i>‘omer</i> flour offering, even though it comes from barley, comes as roasted kernels but this one comes as flour. Rabban Gamliel said, since she behaved like an animal so her offering is animal feed.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “He brought her flour offering,” etc. It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:18" href="/Numbers.5.18"><i>Num</i>. 5:18</a>. The verse requires that the offering be in the woman’s hands all the time the water is in the Cohen’s hand.</i>: “In the Cohen’s hand shall be the spell-inducing bitter water.” Is that to tire her out? Rather to inspire her with fear.
“Her flour-offering.” Does that mean that it is sanctified in her name<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">A flour-offering in order to be valid must be offered by a Cohen in a Temple vessel on behalf of its owner (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Menachot 1:1" href="/Mishnah_Menachot.1.1">Mishnah <i>Menaḥot</i> 1:1</a>). The Mishnah notes that “he (the husband) brings her (the wife’s) flour-offering.” Does this mean that the offering is to be offered only on her behalf?</i>? Just as it is sanctified in her name, so it is sanctified in his name<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">It must be brought in both their names.</i>. Rebbi Hiyya stated and disagreed<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">He does not disagree here at all; he supports the previous opinion. The text is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 3:6:2-6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.3.6.2-6">Halakhah 3:7</a> (fol. 19b line 37) where R. Ḥiyya disagrees. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sotah (Lieberman) 2:6" href="/Tosefta_Sotah_(Lieberman).2.6">Tosephta 2:6</a>.</i>: “It<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">If a Cohen suspects his wife of adultery, the flour-offering cannot be burnt as a Cohen’s offering (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:16" href="/Leviticus.6.16"><i>Lev</i>. 6:16</a>) and cannot be eaten as the wife’s offering (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:9" href="/Leviticus.6.9"><i>Lev</i>. 6:9</a>).</i> cannot be brought completely because of the participation by the wife. It cannot be eaten because of the participation by the husband.” He said to him, the handful<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Of any flour-offering, a handful of the flour (with the incense) has to be burned on the altar and, normally, the rest is eaten by the Cohanim within the Temple enclosure (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:7-11" href="/Leviticus.6.7-11"><i>Lev</i>. 6:7–11</a>).</i> is brought separately and the remainder is brought separately<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">This is a matter of dispute; R. Ḥiyya disagrees with the opinion that the remainders which cannot be eaten by the Cohanim have to be dispersed on the ashes but holds that they have to be burned as a kind of fuel (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 3:6:2-6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.3.6.2-6">Halakhah 3:7</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 23a" href="/Sotah.23a">Babli 23a</a>).</i>. And you say, “her flour offering”? But the expression used by the verse is used by the Mishnah: “He shall bring <i>her flour-offering</i> for her.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:15" href="/Numbers.5.15"><i>Num</i>. 5:15</a>.</i>”
May her husband dedicate for her without her knowledge? Since he is a partner in the flour offering, may he dedicate for her without her knowledge? May another person dedicate for her without her knowledge<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">In case the first question is answered in the affirmative, there is a second question to be answered.</i>? It comes following what Rebbi Joḥanan said, as Rebbi Joḥanan said, for the four who need cleansing<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Anyone whose own body was the source of impurity, when he is pure again cannot enter the Temple precinct unless he first brought a sacrifice of cleansing: The woman after childbirth (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 12:6-8" href="/Leviticus.12.6-8"><i>Lev</i>. 12:6–8</a>), the person healed from scale disease (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:1-32" href="/Leviticus.14.1-32"><i>Lev</i>. 14:1–32</a>), and the persons healed from genital discharges (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 15:14-15" href="/Leviticus.15.14-15"><i>Lev</i>. 15:14–15</a>,29–30).</i> others may dedicate without their knowledge; these are the following: Man or woman [healed from] genital discharges, one [healed from] scale disease, and the woman after childbirth; since a father may dedicate for his small son who is lying in a crib<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Who could have been afflicted with scale disease and, if female, with a discharge at birth mimicking menstruation.</i>. One understands man or woman [healed from] genital discharges or [healed from] scale disease, but a woman after childbirth? May a minor give birth? <sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Text from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 1:2:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.1.2.9"><i>Yebamot</i> 1:2, Note 153</a>.</i> Did not Rebbi Redifa, Rebbi Jonah, say in the name of Rebbi Hila: If a woman became pregnant and gave birth before she grew two hairs, she and her son will die. After she grew two hairs, she and her son will live. If she became pregnant before she grew two hairs and gave birth after she grew two hairs, she will live but her son will die. How is the situation? Since a man may dedicate for his underage daughter<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore, he should be able to dedicate for his wife who also is dependent upon him.</i>. Since she grew [pubic hair] she already left his power<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Even without growing pubic hair, if he married her off she is emancipated from him. If she is not married, she cannot be a suspected wife.</i>. But it must be since a man may dedicate for his deaf-mute wife. Here, in the case of the suspected wife, the case of the minor does not apply <sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">This statement is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 1:2:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.1.2.5">Chapter 1, Note 91</a>.</i> since Rebbi Ze‘ira, Rebbi Yasa said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: An underage girl who whored has no will to be forbidden to her husband. The case of the deaf-mute does not apply since it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:22" href="/Numbers.5.22"><i>Num</i>. 5:22</a>. The answer of the wife is a requirement that cannot be waved, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 1:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.1.1.2">Chapter 1, Note 8</a>. Therefore, a mute woman cannot undergo the ordeal.
