The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 27b" href="/Sotah.27b">Babli concurs, 27b</a>, quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bamidbar Rabbah 9:18" href="/Bamidbar_Rabbah.9.18"><i>Num. rabba</i> 9(18</a>).</i>: “The woman shall say: Amen, amen.” Rebbi Abin said, since it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:26" href="/Deuteronomy.14.26"><i>Deut</i>. 14:26</a>. “A man’s house” always means his wife. For example, since the High Priest must purge his sins and those of his house on the day of Atonement (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 16:17" href="/Leviticus.16.17"><i>Lev</i>. 16:17</a>), an unmarried High Priest cannot officiate.</i>: “You shall enjoy together with your house,” and he is the cause that he cannot enjoy with her, he may dedicate without her knowledge<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Since he cannot enjoy himself if his wife is forbidden to him, she cannot hinder him in the preparations for her rehabilitation. This answers the original question for the husband. At the same time, R. Abin disagrees with R. Joḥanan and holds that only the husband may dedicate the purgation offering of the woman after childbirth without her knowledge since he has a direct interest in it. While the woman after childbirth is permitted to her husband once she is recovered and pure, she cannot enjoy the holiday sacrifices with him as long as her sacrifice has not been handed over to the Temple personnel.</i>.
Rebbi Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Ila: He may not dedicate for her the bird elevation offering, only the bird purification offering because the latter enables her to eat family sacrifices<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">This speaks about the woman after childbirth whose husband is poor and can afford only two birds as purgation offering. It is asserted here that the husband on his own can only dedicate a sacrifice in which he has a direct interest, as stated by R. Abin. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Keritot 6:4" href="/Mishnah_Keritot.6.4">Mishnah <i>Keritut</i> 6:4</a> notes that only the purification offering enables her to eat family sacrifices. The elevation sacrifice also may be paid by him but only with his wife’s knowledge.</i>. It was stated: He may dedicate for her only something that enables her to eat family sacrifices. Rebbi Yose said, that<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">The offerings of the woman after childbirth.</i> is all. It was stated: Just as that disables her from eating family sacrifices, so she disables others from eating family sacrifices; since he is hindered from enjoying with her it is as if he disabled her from eating family sacrifices<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">In all cases, those whose impurity was produced by their own body and the suspected wife, where the husband cannot enjoy a holiday without having seen to it that her sacrifice has been presented in good order, any dereliction on his part to remove her disability is put on the same level as his hindering her from eating sacrifices. Therefore, it is his duty to bring these sacrifices and he does not need his wife’s consent for the dedication.</i>. It was stated: Even by the shearing of her head<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">The wife made a vow to be a <i>nazir</i> who may not drink any wine and the husband did not use his right to free her from any vow of self-punishment. As long as she is forbidden to drink wine, he cannot fully enjoy the holiday with her. Therefore, after she has cut her hair, the husband can dedicate the three offerings due from the <i>nazir</i> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:14" href="/Numbers.6.14"><i>Num</i>. 6:14</a>) without the knowledge of his wife (according to R. Ila only the family sacrifice which permits the wife to drink wine.)</i> she hinders him from eating family sacrifices. Since she is ugly<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Because of her long hair.</i> and he is hindered from enjoying with her it is as if he disabled her<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">By not having vetoed her vow he is responsible for the problem.</i> from eating family sacrifices.
<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">From <i>Yebamot</i> 15:3, explained there in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 2:1:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.2.1.9">Notes 43–45</a>. The words in Italics are changed in gender from the text in <i>Yebamot</i>: The text here refers to the obligations the husband takes upon himself at the time of marriage; the text in <i>Yebamot</i> refers to the receipt the divorcee writes upon delivery of the divorce settlement.</i> It was stated in the name of Rebbi Jehudah: A person brings for his wife any sacrifice she is obligated for, even if she ate suet or desecrated the Sabbath. Also, Rebbi Jehudah says, once he divorces her, he is no longer obligated for her, for <i>he</i> writes to <i>her</i><sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">While he is married, he is liable also for sacrifices she was obligated to obligations cease, before marriage. Upon divorce, all his obligations cease.</i> “any other obligations that come <i>to me because of you</i> from earlier times.”
Was it not stated<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">The objection is to the Mishnah which states that “all flour offerings are from start to finish in a Temple vessel” when the <i>baraita</i> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Menachot 1:10" href="/Tosefta_Menachot.1.10">Tosephta <i>Menaḥot</i> 1:16</a>) states that flour offerings are brought in private vessels to the Temple and only there transferred to Temple vessels.</i>: “The order of flour offerings is the following: One brings from his house in silver or gold vessels.” These would be acceptable as Temple vessels<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">If they were dedicated together with the flour, no other vessels would be necessary. But an Egyptian palm leaf basket is never acceptable as a Temple vessel. The same question and answer in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 14b" href="/Sotah.14b">Babli (14b</a>).</i>.
Rebbi Simeon bar Ioḥai stated<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 15a" href="/Sotah.15a">Babli, 15a</a>, quotes a more detailed statement by R. Simeon bar Ioḥai: Why is the sinner let off cheaply in that he saves the expenses for wine, flour, and oil, required for all other private sacrifices?</i>: Why did they say that no purification<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">The idea is that חפאת should be considered as “sin offering”. It seems more likely that the root חטא basically means “to cleanse”. The noun חֵטא then means “cleansable sin”, i. e., inadvertent sin, in contrast to פֶּשַׁע “crime, intentional sin”, for which no Temple ritual is available (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Temurah 15b" href="/Temurah.15b">Babli <i>Temurah</i> 15b</a>). The sacrifices of the persons needing purge from impurity (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 2:1:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.2.1.8">Note 15</a>) shows that “sin offering” is not a primary meaning of our word. An intentional sin can be expiated only by death or by God’s grace following sincere repentance.</i> or reparation sacrifices need wine offerings? That the sacrifice of a sinner should not be magnificent. They objected: There are the purification and the reparation offerings of the scale-diseased<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Anyone whose own body was the source of impurity, when he is pure again cannot enter the Temple precinct unless he first brought a sacrifice of cleansing: The woman after childbirth (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 12:6-8" href="/Leviticus.12.6-8"><i>Lev</i>. 12:6–8</a>), the person healed from scale disease (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:1-32" href="/Leviticus.14.1-32"><i>Lev</i>. 14:1–32</a>), and the persons healed from genital discharges (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 15:14-15" href="/Leviticus.15.14-15"><i>Lev</i>. 15:14–15</a>,29–30).</i>. If you say that he is no sinner, did not Rebbi Isaac say: “That shall be the instruction for the scale-diseased,” this is the instruction for the slanderer<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Taking apart the word מצוֹ-רע. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 15a" href="/Sotah.15a">Babli (mentioned 15a</a>, main source <a class="refLink" data-ref="Arakhin 16a" href="/Arakhin.16a"><i>Arakhin</i> 16a</a>), R. Joḥanan holds that scale-disease is a punishment for (1) calumny, (2) homicide, (3) perjury, (4) incest, (4) haughtiness, (5) robbery, (6) envy.</i>! Rebbi Hila said, since he was made to suffer and it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 25:3" href="/Deuteronomy.25.3"><i>Deut</i>. 25:3</a>. It is to be noted that in Arabic one of the meanings of both roots צרע and צ̇רע, corresponding to the Hebrew צרע “having scale-disease”, is “to humble oneself”.</i> “that your brother should [not] be contemptible in your eyes,” he is as if he had not sinned.
Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Ismael: <sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">This sentence is also quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 3:1:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.3.1.2-5">Halakhah 3:1</a>. The argument refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 2:14" href="/Leviticus.2.14"><i>Lev</i>. 2:14</a>, where “a flour offering of first fruits” is mentioned which is identified as the <i>‘Omer</i> offering (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:9-14" href="/Leviticus.23.9-14"><i>Lev</i>. 23:9–14</a>), traditionally brought from barley (since early in spring there is no wheat ready). The argument attempts to show that the offering mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 2:14" href="/Leviticus.2.14"><i>Lev</i>. 2:14</a> must be the <i>‘omer</i> offering of barley. The argument of R. Ismael is quoted only here because it cannot be sustained. The basis of the argument is the position that a word used in the legal parts of the Torah can only have one meaning. Since the construct form “<i>flour offering of</i>” used for the ritual of the suspected wife (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:15" href="/Numbers.5.15"><i>Num</i>. 5:15</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:16" href="/Numbers.5.16">16</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:18" href="/Numbers.5.18">18</a>) refers to barley, it is concluded that the “<i>flour offering of first fruits</i>” also must refer to barley. The problem is that the construct state is also used in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 2:7" href="/Leviticus.2.7"><i>Lev</i>. 2:7</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:14" href="/Leviticus.6.14">6:14</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 2:16" href="/Leviticus.2.16">16</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 4:16" href="/Numbers.4.16"><i>Num</i>. 4:16</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 4:28" href="/Numbers.4.28">28</a>:8 clearly referring to wheat offerings.</i>“Flour offering of, flour offering of.” Since “flour offering of” said there is of barley, here also it is of barley. <sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">A slightly garbled version of a text dealing with the same problem, preserved in <i>Sifra</i> <i>Wayyiqra Paršata</i> 13(4). The reading <i>Liezer</i> for the first Tanna mentioned here, as against <i>Lazar</i> as suggested by the text, follows the reading of <i>Sifra</i>. Since this Tanna is mentioned before R. Aqiba, a reading of <i>Lazar</i> would refer to R. Eleazar ben ‘Arakh.</i> Rebbi Eliezer said, it says here “milky white<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 2:14" href="/Leviticus.2.14"><i>Lev</i>. 2:14</a>, a word used in the description of the offering of first fruits. For the translation of אביב as “milky white”, see J. Milgrom, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 1-16" href="/Leviticus.1-16"><i>Leviticus</i> <i>1–16</a></i>, 1991, pp. 192–194.</i>” and it says in Egypt “milky white<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 9:31" href="/Exodus.9.31"><i>Ex</i>. 9:31</a>, referring to barley.</i>”. Since “milky white” mentioned in Egypt refers to barley, here also it refers to barley. Rebbi Aqiba said, it was said to the public, bring first fruits on Passover and bring first fruits on Pentecost<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">During or after the Holiday of Unleavened Bread (depending on the interpretation of the term “after the Sabbath”) the <i>‘Omer</i> offering is required as “first harvest” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:9-14" href="/Leviticus.23.9-14"><i>Lev</i>. 23:9–14</a>). Pentecost is described as “holiday of first fruits” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 28:26" href="/Numbers.28.26"><i>Num</i>. 28:26</a>).</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">This text is slightly garbled. A more intelligible text is in <i>Sifra</i> (but one cannot exclude the possibility that the text in <i>Sifra</i> is Amoraic and has been edited to make it more intelligible) and the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 68b" href="/Menachot.68b">Babli, <i>Menaḥot</i> 68b</a>:</i> If we find that from the kind a private person brings his obligatory offering<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">The only <i>obligatory</i> flour offerings of a private person are the purification offering of the poor (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:11" href="/Leviticus.5.11"><i>Lev</i>. 5:11</a>) and the offering for the suspected wife. The <i>voluntary</i> offerings of a private person are all high quality wheat.</i> the public bring their first fruits on Pentecost. From which kind does the private person bring his obligatory offering? From barley! Also the public should bring only from barley. If you say from wheat, the Two Breads<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Two wheat breads made from sour dough to be presented to the altar but not burned, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:17" href="/Leviticus.23.17"><i>Lev</i>. 23:17</a>. These are called “First Fruits”. If the <i>‘Omer</i> offering, whose nature is not specified in the verse, were to be brought from wheat, the Two Breads would not be baked from “First Fruits”.</i> would not be first fruits.
Rebbi Aqiba says: It was said to the public, bring First Fruits on Passover and bring First Fruits on Pentecost. <i>As</i> we find that, from the kind a private person brings his obligatory offering the public has to bring their First Fruits on Pentecost, <i>also from the kind a private person brings his obligatory offering the public brings their First Fruits on Passover</i>. From which kind does the private person bring his obligatory offering? From barley! Also the public should bring only from barley. If you say, from wheat, the Two Breads are not First Fruits.
There, said by Rebbi Jonah: “<i>Halica</i><sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">J. Levy, supported by H. L. Fleischer, sees in חילקה Latin <i>alica, halica</i> “groats of spelt”. This is confirmed by the (Galilean) spelling חליקה of the Rome ms. The spelling חילקה is Babylonian; cf. S. Lieberman, <i>Tosefta ki-Fshutaḥ Nedarim</i>, p. 456. He explains that R. Jonah derives חילקה from the Hebrew root חלק “to split” and pronounced טרגיס, <i>trayis</i> from Latin <i>tres</i> or Greek τρεῖς.<br>The Mishnah, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Makhshirin 6:2" href="/Mishnah_Makhshirin.6.2"><i>Makhširin</i> 6:2</a>, mentions “spelt groats, <i>tragos, tisana</i>” as manufactured products; R. Jonah defines these trade names. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Moed Katan 13b" href="/Moed_Katan.13b">Babli, <i>Mo‘ed Qaṭan</i> 13b</a>, R. Jonah’s definition is described as Babylonian; the Galilean definition of חילקה given by Rav Dimi is כונתא, explained by the commentary ascribed to Rashi as “spelt for chewing” and by “Rashi’s commentary” edited by E. Kupfer as “shelled spelt”, confirming the determination as <i>halica</i>.</i>, into two parts. <i>Tragos</i><sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Greek τράγος, “spelt (or other grains); goat”. Latin <i>tragos, tragum</i> “porridge”.</i>, into three. <i>Tisana</i><sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Latin “barley groats, pearl barley; barley water”; originally Latin <i>ptisana</i>, Greek πτισάνη, “peeled barley, barley gruel”.</i>, into four.” Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun asked: From there on, is it coarse flour<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">The offering of the suspected wife is defined (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:15" href="/Numbers.5.15"><i>Num</i>. 5:15</a>) as “coarse barley flour”. Is it enough if the kernels are broken into at least five parts or does there have to be a real grinding process? The question is not answered.</i>? One does not have to sift out the fine flour from in between<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">The presence of a few finely ground pieces of barley does not invalidate the offering if it is recognizable as coarse flour by the naked eye.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">The officiating priest.</i> brought a new<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">The text of the Yerushalmi is also the text of most Mishnah mss. The word is missing in the Babli mss.</i> earthenware bowl<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Greek φιάλη.</i> and filled it with half a log<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">0.27 1. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Kelim 17:11" href="/Mishnah_Kelim.17.11">Mishnah <i>Kelim</i> 17:11</a>.</i> of water from the laver; Rebbi Jehudah says, a quarter [log]<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">A quarter log is the rabbinic minimum for any liquid used in any ceremony.</i>. (Just as he<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Rebbi Jehudah; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 2:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.2.3.1">Mishnah 3</a>.</i> shortens the writing so he decreases the amount of water.) He<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">The officiating priest.</i> entered the Temple building and turned right. There was a place there, one cubit square, where a handle was fastened to a marble plate. He lifted it, took dust from under it, and put it so that it was seen on the water, as it was said<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:17" href="/Numbers.5.17"><i>Num</i>. 5:17</a>.</i>: “From the dust which will be on the ground of the Temple the Cohen shall take and put on the water.”
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “He brought a bowl,” etc. It was stated: “A new one.<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 15b" href="/Sotah.15b">Babli, 15b</a>, this is attributed to R. Ismael. The statement is not mentioned in the Midrashim. The quote seems to imply a Mishnah text similar to that of the Babli.</i>” Our Mishnah follows Rebbi Eliezer<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">The argument is mentioned in his name in <i>Sifra Meṣorah Pereq</i> 1(4).</i>, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Nega‘im</i> 14:1, referring to the purification of the person healed from skin disease.</i>: “He brought a new earthenware bowl.” Who stated “a new one”? Rebbi Eliezer! Since he explains: “Into an earthenware vessel on fresh water.<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:5" href="/Leviticus.14.5"><i>Lev</i>. 14:5</a>, speaking of the slaughtering of one of the two birds used in the purification rite. “Live” water is running water from a fountain or natural stream.</i>” Just as the water had no prior use, so the earthenware vessel should not have had any prior use. It is understandable there because he explains: “Into an earthenware vessel on fresh water;” but what may one say here<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">The argument is irrelevant for the ritual of the suspected wife. In the Babli, the connection is made by R. Ismael’s rule of <i>gezerah s̊awah</i>: If the meaning of “earthenware vessel” was determined to include “new”, the same meaning applies everywhere. The Yerushalmi rejects this application to the names of vessels of common use.</i>? Rebbi Joḥanan said, because he<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">R. Eliezer.</i> agrees with Rebbi Ismael. It was stated: From the wash basin<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">From which the priests draw the water for their ablutions; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 30:17-21" href="/Exodus.30.17-21"><i>Ex</i>. 30:17–21</a>. The statement is also anonymous in <i>Sifry Num</i>. 10, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bamidbar Rabbah 9:12" href="/Bamidbar_Rabbah.9.12"><i>Sifry zuṭa Naśo</i>, <i>Num</i>. <i>rabba</i> 9(12</a>); cf. <i>Targumim</i> to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:17" href="/Numbers.5.17"><i>Num</i>. 5:17</a>. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 15b" href="/Sotah.15b">Babli, 15b</a>, it is attributed to R. Joḥanan in the <i>editio princeps</i>, impossible in a Tannaïtic text, and to R. Ismael in the Munich ms., contradicting the next statement there. In the Babli text quoted in the 12th Century <i>Sefer Yereïm</i> 460 (ed. S. Z. Halberstam), the statement is missing; this is the only consistent Babli text, defining this <i>baraita</i> as differing from the Mishnah.</i>. Rebbi Ismael says, water from a fountain. But the Sages approve of all kinds of water<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">The last two statements are also in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 15b" href="/Sotah.15b">Babli, 15b</a>. The statement by R. Ismael requires the water to be brought from outside the Temple precinct (the Giḥon source); the rabbis permit the water to be drawn also from the water canal crossing the temple courtyard or from one of the <i>miqwaot</i> in the Temple area.</i>. Therefore, Rebbi Eliezer holds with Rebbi Ismael about the water and Rebbi Ismael with Rebbi Eliezer about the earthenware vessel. It was found stated: Rebbi Ismael says, into a new earthenware vessel. Some Tannaїm state: Into an earthenware vessel but not into a <i>maqqēdah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">From the context (here, and in <i>Sifra</i>, <i>loc. cit</i>.) it follows that the word describes a somehow defective or incomplete clay vessel. Ben Jehudah in his <i>Thesaurus</i> (p. 3662) quotes several proposed interpretations without giving his own opinion. As a Semitic word, the root is נקד, “exhibiting spots”, but cf. Greek μαγίς, -ίδος, ἡ, “kneading trough, pan, plate”; Latin <i>magis</i>, -<i>idis</i>, or <i>magida -ae</i>, “dish, platter, kneading trough”.</i>. Some Tannaїm state: Even into a <i>maqqēdah</i>. They<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Some members of the Academy.</i> wanted to say, he who says, into an earthenware vessel but not into a <i>maqqēdah</i>, is Rebbi Eliezer<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Since it may be assumed that “a new vessel” means: not a defective one.</i> but those who say, even into a <i>maqqēdah</i>, are the rabbis. It all is the rabbis’. He who says, into an earthenware vessel but not into a <i>maqqēdah</i>, if most of it was removed but a small part was left; he who says, even into a <i>maqqēdah</i>, if a small part was removed but most of it was left<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">A broken vessel is acceptable if it represents the major part of a complete vessel. This statement is not in the Babli; since the Babli requires a new vessel (or at least one renewed by firing), it will reject a broken one.</i>.
Some Tannaїm state: “In an earthenware vessel”, not if it is sooty. Some Tannaїm state: “In an earthenware vessel”, even if it is sooty. They wanted to say, he who says “in an earthenware vessel”, not if it is sooty, is Rebbi Eliezer, but he who says, “in an earthenware vessel”, even if it is sooty, follows the rabbis. All is of the rabbis: He who says “in an earthenware vessel”, not if it is sooty, follows the rabbis, and he who says, “in an earthenware vessel”, even if it is sooty, follows the rabbis<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">A clean vessel is prescribed but an infraction of this rule does not invalidate the procedure. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 15b" href="/Sotah.15b">Babli disagrees, 15b</a>, and declares a sooty vessel unacceptable under any circumstance as long as the soot has not been removed by firing the vessel. Since the statement in the Babli is attributed to Rava who is described as knowledgeable in the rules of the Yerushalmi, the statement in the Babli is a direct polemic against the Yerushalmi.</i>.
There<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Parah 11:8" href="/Mishnah_Parah.11.8">Mishnah <i>Parah</i> 11:8</a>. The hyssop was used to sprinkle water mixed with ashes of the red cow to cleanse a person defiled by the impurity of the dead. From the biblical expression (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19:9" href="/Numbers.19.9"><i>Num</i>. 19:9</a>) מֵי נִדָּה חַטָּאת הִיא “sprinkling water, it is purifying”, the water is called in Talmudic terminology מֵי חַטָּאת or simply חַטָּאת “purifying (water)”. Cf. also <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 2:1:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.2.1.8">Note 36</a>.</i>, we have stated: “A hyssop which was used to sprinkle is acceptable [to be used] to purify the sufferer from skin disease<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">Hyssop alone is required for purifying (from the impurity of the dead) (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19:18" href="/Numbers.19.18"><i>Num</i>. 19:18</a>); for the cleansing from skin disease it is used among other things (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:6" href="/Leviticus.14.6"><i>Lev</i>. 14:6</a>).</i>.” Rebbi Immi in the name of Rebbi Eleazar<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">The Amora, ben Pedat.</i>: That [was stated] to exclude the opinion of Rebbi Eliezer<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">Tosephta <i>Nega‘im</i> 8:2: “A hyssop acceptable for purifying is acceptable for the sufferer from skin disease. If he used it for sprinkling to purify it is still acceptable for the sufferer from skin disease. Rebbi Eliezer says, the cedar wood, hyssop, and crimson strip mentioned in the Torah (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:6" href="/Leviticus.14.6"><i>Lev</i>. 14:6</a>) cannot have been previously used for any purpose.”<br>The prohibition of prior use is spelled out in the Torah for the red cow only (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19:2" href="/Numbers.19.2"><i>Num</i>. 19:2</a>). By rabbinic tradition, the prohibition is extended to the water used for purifying as explained at length in Mishnah <i>Parah</i>. R. Eliezer extends the prohibition to anything used in any ritual of purification by hyssop and water.</i>, as it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">This <i>baraita</i> is not quoted in any other source; the Tosephta quoted in the preceding Note shows that the source of the <i>baraita</i> is the school of R. Eliezer.</i>: If he sprinkled with it for purifying, it is disqualified for the sufferer from skin disease. If he sprinkled with it for the sufferer from skin disease, it is disqualified for purifying. <sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">The argument presented here is intended to show an error in the position of R. Eliezer. The argument of R. Yose (ben Ḥalaphta) is not found in any other source.<br>The argument goes as follows: Everybody agrees that the rules of purification from skin disease are not as stringent as those from the impurity of the dead. If using hyssop for purifying from the impurity of the dead would disqualify it as instrument for purifying from skin disease, one must require that one hyssop cannot be used for several people defiled by contact with a corpse. But is was general practice that the Temple provided a purification service where a person was standing in a window and was sprinkling continuously on the people walking by below, always using the same hyssop (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Parah 11:4" href="/Mishnah_Parah.11.4">Mishnah <i>Parah</i>11:4</a>); no dissent by R. Eliezer is recorded. Therefore, practice must follow the Mishnah, not R. Eliezer.</i> Rebbi Yose said, would that not be an argument <i>de minore ad majus</i>? Since for the sufferer from skin disease, for whose ceremony use does not disable, if he sprinkled with it for purifying, it is disqualified for the sufferer from skin disease; purifying, for whose ceremony use does disable, if he sprinkled with it for purifying, is it not logical that it should be disqualified for purifying?
If he used it to sprinkle on the sufferer from skin disease, is it acceptable to use it for another sufferer from skin disease? If somebody said it is acceptable, Rebbi Jehudah and Rebbi Eleazar<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">No R. Eleazar (one of the Tannaïm, ben Pada or ben Shamua‘) is mentioned in any of our other sources as joining R. Jehudah in this controversy.</i> will agree with him. But with him who said, it is disqualified, Rebbi Jehudah and Rebbi Eleazar will disagree. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">Tosephta <i>Nega‘im</i> 8:2, <i>Sifra Meṣora</i>‘ (13).</i>: Rebbi Jehudah said, it was my Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that in the Academy of R. Tarphon, the students had to serve their teacher on the Sabbath, taking turns.</i> and I followed Rebbi Tarphon to his house. He said to me, Jehudah, my son, give me my sandals. I gave them to him. He reached with his hand to the window and gave me a stick<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">Hyssop does not grow solid stems. Therefore, this must refer to the cedar branch used in the ceremony. It is to be assumed that cedar and hyssop, mentioned in parallel in the verse (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:6" href="/Leviticus.14.6"><i>Lev</i>. 14:6</a>), follow parallel rules.</i> from there. He said to me, Jehudah, my son, with this one I cleansed three sufferers from skin disease and I taught with it seven practices. That it is <i>yabrut</i><sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">In the Tosephta אברית, in <i>Sifra</i> ברות. The exact kind of cedar mentioned is not known. Since <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Parah 11:6" href="/Mishnah_Parah.11.6">Mishnah <i>Parah</i> 11:6</a> states that any kind of hyssop known by a qualifying adjective is invalid (examples given are: blue hyssop, Roman hyssop, desert hyssop), R. Tarphon insists that any tree of the cedar family qualifies even if its trade name does not even contain the word “cedar”.</i>, on its head is a leaf<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">A cedar stick without any green needles is invalid.</i>, it is one cubit long, its width is about one fourth that of a bed’s leg (split one into two and two into four)<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Even a very thin branch will do.</i>, and one sprinkles and repeats and does it a third time<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">This answers our question.</i>, and one purifies when there is a Temple and when there is no Temple, and one purifies in the countryside<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the final purification rite which gives access to the Sanctuary presupposes a Sanctuary, the preliminary rite which re-integrates the sufferer from skin disease into society (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:1-9" href="/Leviticus.14.1-9"><i>Lev</i>. 14:1–9</a>), while requiring the services of a Cohen such as R. Tarphon, is independent of the Temple service. While R. Tarphon was old enough to have served in the Temple, R. Jehudah was born after its destruction.</i>.
“Water<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:17" href="/Numbers.5.17"><i>Num.</i> 5:17</a>: מֵי הַמָּרִים הַמְאָרֲרִים.</i>”, I could think it should look like water. The verse says, “dust”<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">A translation (Arabism) of the word עָפָר “dust”; cf. Arabic ارض.</i>. Or “dust”, I could think it should look like ink. The verse says, “water”. How is that? The looks of water and the looks of dust<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">The dust should be a powder on the clear water.</i>. The Sages estimated half a <i>log</i> of water from the wash basin. But did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">In the Mishnah.</i>: “Rebbi Jehudah says, a quarter [log]”. Rebbi Jehudah follows his particular way, as we have stated: “Just as he shortens the writing so he decreases the amount of water.”
There<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Yom</i> a 3:10, speaking of Queen Helena of Adiabene.</i>, we have stated: “Also she made a golden plate with the paragraph of the suspected wife written on it.” When the sun rose, sparks were reflected on it and one knew that the sun had risen<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">The golden tablet was fixed on the Eastern wall of the Temple building.</i>. What was written on it? Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">The short form ריש לקיש is a Babylonism.</i> said in the name of Rebbi Yannai: Alef-Bet was written on it<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">Single letters were written on the tablet, each letter being the first of its word. (Cf. P. Kahle, Masoreten des Westens, II, pp. 88–95).</i>. But did we not state: In the script there it was written here, not heavy, not thin, but average<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">The suspected wife’s scroll had to be written in the way the letters were on the queen’s tablet, not ornamental and not in <i>italics.</i> The questioner thought that the entire tablet had to be copied as it was written; then an abbreviated version would be impossible.</i>. Explain “the Alef there” by “from the Alef there”<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">For each letter one copies the entire word. The first sentence of the scroll would read on the tablet as אם ל ש א א ו ל ש ט ת א ה מ ה ה ה which would be copied as אם לא שכב איש אתך ואם לא שטית טומאה תחת אישך הנקי ממי המרים המאררים האלה where every first letter was copied exactly from the tablet and the rest of the word added in the same style of script.</i>; “the Bet there” by “from the Bet there”. Rebbi Hoshaia stated: The entire paragraph of the suspected wife was written there, and from it he was reading and explaining all details of the paragraph<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">While writing, the Cohen explained the text homiletically to the suspected wife, as explained in the next paragraph, so that she would understand the meaning of the ceremony.</i>: Why water, dust, and writing? Water, from the place she came; dust, the place she goes to; writing, before Whom she will have to be accountable in the future.
<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Vayikra Rabbah 18:1" href="/Vayikra_Rabbah.18.1"><i>Lev. rabba</i> 18(1</a>); cf. <i>Abot dR.</i> <i>Natan,</i> A 19, B 32; <i>Derekh Ereṣ</i> 3 in the name of Ben Azai.</i> There <sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Abot</i> 3:1: "Aqabiah ben Mehallalel says, consider three things and you will not commit any transgression: Know from where you came, where you are going, and before Whom you will be held accountable in the future. From where you came, from a stinking drop. Where you are going, to a place of dust, worms, and vermin. Before Whom you will be held accountable in the future, before the King over emperors and kings, the Holy One, praise to Him."</i>, we have stated: “Aqabiah ben Mehallalel says, consider three things and you will not commit any transgression.” Rebbi Abba ben Rav Pappaeus and Rebbi Joshua from Sikhnin in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: All three Aqabiah derived from the same verse<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Ecclesiastes 12:1" href="/Ecclesiastes.12.1"><i>Eccl</i>. 12:1</a>.</i>: “Remember your Creator,” your fountain, your cavern, your Creator<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">Based on three possible vocalizations of the biblical בראך.. The vocalization בוראֲך instead of the masoretic בראֶך is the manuscript’s.</i>. Your fountain, from where you came. Your cave, to where you are going. Your Creator, before Whom you will have to be accountable in the future.
Three things have to be executed in the name [of the woman]<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">A similar text in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 2:7" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).2.7">Tosephta <i>Giṭṭin</i> 2:7</a>.</i>. “He shall write <i>for her.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:1" href="/Deuteronomy.24.1"><i>Deut</i>. 24:1</a>, speaking of a bill of divorce. The document has to be written <i>for her</i>, otherwise it is invalid (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 3:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.3.2.1">Mishnah<i>Giṭṭin</i> 3:2</a>, <i>Sifry</i> <i>Deut</i>. 269, quoted many times in both Talmudim).</i>” “He shall execute <i>for her</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:30" href="/Numbers.5.30"><i>Num</i>. 5:30</a>. The Cohen has to conduct the ceremony of the suspected wife for that particular woman, otherwise it is invalid. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 18a" href="/Sotah.18a">Babli (18a</a>) refers this only to the scroll which is to be written for the woman, which has to be written and erased with that particular person in mind.</i>” “Or manumission was not given <i>to her</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:20" href="/Leviticus.19.20"><i>Lev</i>. 19:20</a>, speaking of a slave girl. The document of manumission has to be executed for the particular slave girl. This requirement is then extended in the Tosephta to the manumission of male slaves.</i>” <sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 16b" href="/Sotah.16b">Babli 16b</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sotah (Lieberman) 1:8" href="/Tosefta_Sotah_(Lieberman).1.8">Tosephta 1:8</a>; <i>Sifry</i> <i>Num</i>. 11, <i>Sifry Zuṭa</i> <i>Naśo</i>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bamidbar Rabbah 9:13" href="/Bamidbar_Rabbah.9.13"><i>Num</i>.<i>rabba</i> 9(13</a>).</i> Three things have to be seen: The ashes of the cow<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">Some ash has to be visible on the water used to purify from the impurity of the dead.</i>, the dust of the suspected wife<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">As described in the Mishnah.</i>, and the spittle of the sister-in-law<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 25:9" href="/Deuteronomy.25.9"><i>Deut</i>. 25:9</a>, in the ceremony of <i>ḥalîṣah</i>; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 12:5:1-6:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.12.5.1-6.8">Mishnah <i>Yebamot</i> 12:6</a>.</i>. Rebbi Ismael stated: Also the blood of the bird for the sufferer from skin disease<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:5" href="/Leviticus.14.5"><i>Lev</i>. 14:5</a>; the healed patient has to be purified by being sprinkled with spring water mixed with the blood of a bird.</i>. Rebbi Ze‘ira said, the Sages estimated that the blood of a small bird becomes negligible in a quarter [log] and the blood of a large bird does not render a quarter [log of water] negligible<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">Taking exactly one quarter <i>log</i> (135 dl, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 2:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.2.2.1">Note 55</a>) will prevent any problems.</i>. As it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Metzora, Chapter 1 5" href="/Sifra,_Metzora,_Chapter_1.5"><i>Sifra</i> <i>Meṣora‘</i> <i>Pereq</i> 1(5</a>); <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 16b" href="/Sotah.16b">Babli 16b</a>.</i>: “In the blood<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:6" href="/Leviticus.14.6"><i>Lev</i>. 14:6</a>. The Cohen has to dip the hyssop and a living bird “in the blood of the slaughtered bird on the flowing water”. The “fresh water” is in a vessel but was taken from a spring. The blood of the slaughtered bird is on the fresh water in the vessel. The simple meaning of the verse, that the bird’s blood must form a layer on the fresh water, obviously cannot be meant.</i>”, should that be only blood? The verse says, “fresh water”. If fresh water, should that be all fresh water? The verse says, “in the blood”. How is this? Fresh water in which the bird’s blood is recognizable. The Sages estimated, a quarter [log]. Rebbi Pedat in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: The water of a suspected wife becomes disqualified by staying overnight<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">Following the opinion that the water has to be taken from the water basin in the Temple. Any water taken from there and sanctified in a temple vessel belongs to the service of that day; once the day has passed (which in the Temple is counted from dawn to dawn), its service cannot be made up (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 4:6:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.4.6.5"><i>Sukkah</i> 4:7</a>). But according to the opinion that the water may come from outside sources, the position of R. Joḥanan could be explained. However, R. Joḥanan holds everywhere that practice follows the anonymous <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Yevamot 4:11" href="/Mishnah_Yevamot.4.11">Mishnah (<i>Yebamot</i> 4:11, Note 177</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 43a" href="/Chullin.43a">Babli <i>Ḥulin</i> 43a</a>).</i>. Rebbi Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Abina: Nothing of which the altar has no part becomes disqualified by staying overnight<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">R. Joḥanan will hold that water in the basin is for the altar in the water offering on Tabernacles (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 4:6:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.4.6.5"><i>Sukkah</i> 4:7</a>). The problem is not discussed in the Babli; Maimonides (<i>Soṭah</i> 4:12) follows R. Joḥanan as the overriding authority.</i>.
“And from the dust.<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:17" href="/Numbers.5.17"><i>Num</i>. 5:17</a>: “And from the dust which will be on the floor of the Sanctuary, the Cohen has to take and put on the water.” The parallel version in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 15b" href="/Sotah.15b">Babli, 15b</a>, [and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bamidbar Rabbah 9:13" href="/Bamidbar_Rabbah.9.13"><i>Num</i>. <i>rabba</i> 9(13</a>)], is formulated differently.</i>” One could think, from what lies in a chest. The verse says, “which will be on the floor of the Sanctuary”. If “which will be on the floor of the Sanctuary”, one could think only that he has to dig with a pick-axe<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">This דקל is not Aramaic “date palm” but the equivalent of Mishnaic דקר “pick-axe” by a change of liquids.</i>, the verse says “which will be”. How is that? If there is nothing there, one brings and puts it there. <sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">R. Abin reformulates the preceding argument from a slightly different angle. His polemic is directed against a <i>baraita</i> preserved only in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 15b" href="/Sotah.15b">Babli (15b</a>, bottom; variant readings in the critical edition p. רלו) which requires the dust to be prepared outside, brought inside, and spread on the floor of the Sanctuary.</i> Rebbi Abin said, “which will be on the floor of the Sanctuary”, one could think [the dust] qualified only if he digs with a pick-axe, the verse says “which will be”. From anywhere. “The Sanctuary”, that includes the Tabernacle, Nob, Gibeon, Shilo, and the Eternal House<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">The order given here is also in the Munich ms. of the Babli. The historic order would be: the Tabernacle, Shilo, Nob, Gibeon, and the Eternal House (the Temple which in rabbinic tradition cannot be replaced by a Sanctuary at any other place, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Zevachim 14:8" href="/Mishnah_Zevachim.14.8">Mishnah <i>Zebaḥim</i> 14:8</a>.)</i>.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Then he starts writing the scroll. From where does he start writing? (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:19" href="/Numbers.5.19"><i>Num</i>. 5:19</a>) “If no man has lain with you, …” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:20" href="/Numbers.5.20"><i>v</i>. 20</a>) “but if you deviated from under your husband,” etc. He does not write (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:21" href="/Numbers.5.21"><i>v</i>. 21</a>) “the Cohen has to administer the oath of curse to the woman,” but he writes “may the Eternal make you a curse and a swear-word among your people when the Eternal will make your hips diminish and your belly inflate.” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:22" href="/Numbers.5.22"><i>v</i>. 22</a>) “This curse-water would come into your intestines to inflate belly and diminish hips.” He does not write (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:22" href="/Numbers.5.22"><i>v</i>. 22</a>) “the woman shall say, Amen, Amen.”<br>Rebbi Yose says, he does not interrupt<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">He requires that verses 19–22 be written in their entirety.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah said<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that verses 19–20 indicate a preparatory speech by the Cohen, that the valid curses, designated as such, are only in v. 21 and part of v. 22.</i> he does not write anything but (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:21" href="/Numbers.5.21"><i>Num</i>. 5:21</a>): “May the Eternal make you a curse and a swear-word among your people when the Eternal makes your hips diminish and your belly swell.” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:22" href="/Numbers.5.22"><i>v</i>. 22</a>) “This curse-water would come into your intestines …” He does not write (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:22" href="/Numbers.5.22"><i>v</i>. 22</a>) “the woman shall say, Amen, Amen.”
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Then he starts writing the scroll,” etc. Rebbi Crispus said, Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">The short form ריש לקיש is a Babylonism.</i> disagreed. One said, he administers the oath and then he writes; the other one said, he writes and then he administers the oath. They wanted to say that he who says, he administers the oath and then he writes, follows what is written in the verse<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">Administering the oath is mentioned in verses 19 and 21; writing the curses is only mentioned in v. 23, followed by the commandment to let the woman drink the water in v. 24. But then the ceremony is interrupted by presenting the flour offering and it is stated (vv. 26, 27) that only after that the water is to be given.</i> but he who says, he writes and then he administers the oath, to join the oath to the drinking<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 17b" href="/Sotah.17b">Babli [17b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 19b" href="/Sotah.19b">19b</a>, quoted in <i>Num</i>. <i>rabba</i> 5(45)] solves the problem by requiring that the scroll be written after the wife has agreed to the oath but before it is administered.</i>. There are Tannaїm who state: The conditions<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">All details of the procedure outlined in the verses. The first opinion holds that the entire procedure is invalid if one detail is wrong. The second opinion holds that the procedure becomes invalid only if the water is given before the oath was administered.</i> and the oaths impede; there are Tannaїm who state: The oaths impede, the conditions do not impede. They wanted to say that he who says, the conditions and the oaths impede, holds that “law” and “teaching” impedes<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">It is a generally accepted principle (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 19a" href="/Menachot.19a">Babli <i>Menaḥot</i> 19a</a>) that any biblical precept characterized as חוּקָּה or תּוֹרָה is invalid if not executed to the letter. The procedure of the suspected wife is called תורה in v. 30.</i>; but he who says, the oaths impede, the conditions do not impede, does not agree. Certainly he does agree<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">Nobody disagrees with the rule expressed in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 2:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.2.3.2">Note 124</a>.</i> but they<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">The sequence of execution of the ceremony may not follow the sequence of the verses; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 3:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.3.1.1">Mishnah 3:1</a>.</i> are not [written] in order. It turns out that this disagreement is like the previous disagreement. He who says, the conditions and the oaths impede, agrees with him who says, he administers the oath and then he writes; but he who says, the oaths impede, the conditions do not impede, agrees with him who says, he writes and then he administers the oath.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He does not write on a wooden plank, or on papyrus, or on διφθέρα<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">Greek “hide prepared for writing.” The traditional interpretation of the Mishnaic word is “rough parchment; incompletely tanned skin.”</i>, only on a scroll, as it is said: “In a book<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:23" href="/Numbers.5.23"><i>Num.</i> 5:23</a>.</i>”. He does not write with gum<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>commis, gummi</i>; Greek κόμμι, τό “gum”.</i> or vitriol nor with anything leaving a permanent impression except with ink; it is written “he shall wipe off”, writing that can be wiped off<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">This is the commentary on <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 2:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.2.4.1">Mishnah 5</a>.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Rebbi Yose says, he does not interrupt,” etc. Levi bar Sisi asked before Rebbi: Does the suspected wife’s scroll defile the hands<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">All biblical texts defile the hands by a rabbinic decree, to make it impossible to a fellow of the faithful to study the Bible with food in his hand, to protect the books from mice and other animals found around food; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Yadayim 3" href="/Mishnah_Yadayim.3">Mishnah <i>Yadaim</i> 3</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 14a" href="/Shabbat.14a">Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 14a</a>. The decree applies to any text at least 85 letters long. The impurity of hands, being secondary, would not cause any trouble in the Temple. The Cohen simply would have to wash his hands before handling the cup of water from which the woman drinks.</i>? He said to him, that is a question. Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">The Amora.</i> said, it is no question. Did they not decree impurity of the hands for books only because of their holiness? And that one has to be erased<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">The entire scroll has to be erased, including the two instances of the Divine Name which in any other circumstance may not be erased. How can such a text claim holiness?</i>! One only questions because it had to be erased<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">R. Yose’s argument is irrelevant. There remain two questions: (1) After the script has been erased, does the sheet retain holiness and must be buried, or may it be re-used for profane purposes? If it retains holiness it induces impurity of the hands. (2) If for some reason the scroll was not erased, for example if the wife admits her infidelity, the scroll has to be buried or otherwise hidden (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 3:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.3.3.1">Mishnah 3:3</a>). The question is whether this is a biblical requirement because of the sanctity of the writing, or rabbinical to make sure the scroll could not be used for any other woman (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 2:2:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.2.2.9">Note 104</a>). The question remains unanswered; Maimonides (<i>Abot Haṭum’a</i> 9:11) disregards the question and follows R. Yose.</i>.
How many letters have to be written unnecessarily<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">In a way that makes the text unacceptable; e. g. with one of the proscribed types of ink. Then erasing the Name will be sinful.<br><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Sotah 20a" href="/Tosafot_on_Sotah.20a">Tosaphot (20a</a>) have a completely different reading: כמה ימחק “How much does he have to erase? Rebbi Ḥanin stated …”</i> for him to be guilty? Rebbi Ḥanin stated: The House of Shammai say, one; the House of Hillel say, two. Rebbi Hilai said, the reason of the House of Hillel: That he could have written Yah<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">The shortest divine Name which may not be erased.</i>. Rebbi Yudan asked, if he wrote from memory<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">A Torah scroll written from memory, not from a corrected original, cannot be used (<i>Megillah,</i> Yerushalmi 4:1; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 18b" href="/Sotah.18b">Babli 18b</a>). This is a rabbinic requirement.</i>, or rubbed it off with dust<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">The verse requires erasing with water. The answer to this question is not given; the negative answer is obvious from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 2:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.2.3.2">Note 124</a>.</i>, or wrote one word and wiped it off immediately<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">Before the ink has time to dry. That procedure saves much time and work. No answer is given. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 18a" href="/Sotah.18a">Babli, 18a</a>, the procedure is declared invalid.</i>? It was stated: Rebbi Eleazar ben Shamua said, one does not write on leather from an unclean animal<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">A Torah scroll must be written on leather or parchment from an animal whose kind could be eaten. This is derived in both Talmudim from the verse <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 13:9" href="/Exodus.13.9"><i>Ex.</i> 13:9</a>: “That the Eternal’s Torah shall be in your mouth.”</i>. Rebbi Simeon said, since you said it is written to be erased, why can he not write? It was stated: Rebbi Eleazar ben Simeon said, I prefer the words of Eleazar ben Shamua over my father’s words. Maybe she will say: I will not drink. Then the Name would be hidden<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 3:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.3.3.1">Mishnah 3:3</a>. The matter is one of good behavior, not of a biblical precept.</i> on leather from an unclean animal.
There is written “book”, and here is written “book”<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">The divorce document is called “a book of divorce” in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:1" href="/Deuteronomy.24.1"><i>Deut.</i> 24:1</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 2:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.2.3.1">Mishnah <i>Giṭṭin</i> 2:3</a> states that one may write on anything, e. g. on a leaf which was plucked from its plant; according to some authorities the husband may even write the bill on the horns of a cow and deliver the cow to his wife. But for the document of the suspected wife one requires regular writing leather.</i>. There, you say anything which is plucked. And here, you say so? He said to him, it is different here because it is written “in a book”<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:23" href="/Numbers.5.23"><i>Num.</i> 5:23</a>. This is more explicit in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 2:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.2.4.1">Mishnah 5</a>, quoted in <i>Sifry Num.</i> 16; <i>Num. rabba</i> 5(47).</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">This is the commentary on <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 2:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.2.4.1">Mishnah 5</a>.</i>“And he writes”. I could think [one writes] with ink, or vermilion, or gum, or copper sulfate<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">Greek χάλκανθον. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Rashi on Eruvin 13a" href="/Rashi_on_Eruvin.13a">Rashi (<i>Erubin</i> 13a</a>) translates Romance <i>adrement,</i> Latin <i>atramentum.</i> Pliny (<i>Hist. Nat.</i> xxxiv.32) writes that the Greeks call <i>chalcanthum</i> what in Rome is called <i>atramentum sutorium,</i>“blacking for leather”.</i>, the verse says, “he shall wipe off”<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:23" href="/Numbers.5.23"><i>Num.</i> 5:23</a>.</i>. If he has to erase, I could think [one writes] with drinks or fruit juice; the verse says, “he shall write”<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">In the sense of a permanent writing.</i>. How is that? Writing that can be wiped off. What is this? This is ink without vitriol. But did we not state: If he wiped off from a Torah scroll it is valid. Explain it following the Tanna who stated: Rebbi Meïr said, all the time we were studying with Rebbi Ismael, we put no vitriol in the ink<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 13a" href="/Eruvin.13a">Babli (<i>Erubin</i> 13a</a>) reports that R. Ismael required that a Torah scroll must be written so that it could serve as the suspected wife’s scroll.</i>.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Why does she repeat<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">In the Bablylonian Mishnah: על מה היא אומרת “What does she imply by repeating”. The reference is to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:22" href="/Numbers.5.22"><i>Num.</i> 5:22</a>.</i>: “Amen Amen”? Amen on the curse, Amen on the oath, Amen from this man, Amen from any other man. Amen that I was not defiled and if I was defiled it should hurt me<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babylonian Mishnah, this sentence comes after the following.</i>. Amen that I did not deviate preliminarily married<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 1:1:15" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.1.1.15"><i>Yebamot,</i> Chapter 1, Note 63</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 6:2:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.6.2.6"><i>Peah</i> Chapter 6, Note 46</a>.</i> and married, waiting for the levir and taken in<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">If the first husband had died childless and the levir brings her to the Temple as his wife. Only R. Aqiba considers sexual relations of the not-yet-married widow a crime. All other authorities will remove the mention of the widow “waiting to be married by the levir”.</i>. Rebbi Meїr says, Amen that I was not defiled, Amen that I shall not be defiled.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">In the ms. and <i>editio princeps</i>: Halakhah 5.</i>: “He does not write on a wooden plank,” etc. Rebbi Eleazar in the name of Rebbi Yose ben Zimra<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 36a" href="/Shevuot.36a">Babli, <i>Šebuot</i> 36a</a>, this is a statement of R. Yose ben R. Ḥanina, a younger contemporary of R. Yose ben Zimra. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bamidbar Rabbah 9:46" href="/Bamidbar_Rabbah.9.46"><i>Num. rabba</i> 9(46</a>) the reading is that of the Yerushalmi.</i>: “Amen” [is said] for acceptance, “Amen” for an oath, “Amen”, may the words be confirmed. “Amen” [is said] for acceptance, from the suspected wife<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:22" href="/Numbers.5.22"><i>Num.</i> 5:22</a>.</i>. “Amen” for an oath: “To keep the oath I had sworn to your forefathers”, etc<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jeremiah 11:5" href="/Jeremiah.11.5"><i>Jer.</i> 11:5</a>: “To keep the oath I had sworn to your forefathers to give to them the Land flowing with milk and honey as today; I answered and said, <i>Amen,</i> o Eternal.”</i>. “Amen”, may the words be confirmed<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote">1<i>K.</i> 1:36.</i>: “Benaiah ben Yehoyada (the priest) answered the king and said Amen”, etc. Rebbi Tanḥuma said, that does not prove that Amen means an oath, it implies nothing<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote">The verse from Jeremiah only confirms what can be learned from the verse in Kings, that Amen is an affirmation.</i>. But the following says: “that you pass by the covenant of your God and His curse<sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 29:11" href="/Deuteronomy.29.11"><i>Deut.</i> 29:11</a>. The curse is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 28:15-68" href="/Deuteronomy.28.15-68"><i>Deut.</i>28:15–68</a>.</i>,” because oath means curse, as you say<sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:21" href="/Numbers.5.21"><i>Num.</i> 5:21</a>. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 35b" href="/Shevuot.35b">Babli, <i>Šebuot</i>35b</a>, this is a tannaїtic statement. In <i>Sifry Num.</i> 14 the argument is: From the verse one infers that any oath implies a potential curse on the person taking the oath.</i>: “The Cohen has to administer to the woman the oath of the curse.”
<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph and the next are also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:5:2-14" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.5.2-14"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:5</a> (fol. 60d). In the Babli, the parallel is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 27b" href="/Kiddushin.27b"><i>Qiddušin</i> 27b</a>.</i> From where did they learn rollover of oaths<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote">If a person is required to swear, the opposing party can add to the contents of the oath any statement similar to the one which causes the oath to be forced. For example, if a person has a monetary claim on another person for which he has partial proof, enough to force the opposing party to swear, he can add to the text of the oath of disclaimer any other financial claim which he might have but for which the proofs in his hand are not sufficient to force the defendant to swear. This is the topic of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:5:2-14" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.5.2-14"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:5</a>. It is seen in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 2:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.2.6.1">Mishnah 7</a> that some connection of the additions to the original claim is required.</i>? From the suspected wife: “The woman shall say, Amen, Amen”; Amen from this man, Amen from any other man. That refers to subjects he is able to make her swear about<sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote">While the husband must specify the name of the man he suspects of an affair with his wife in order to declare his jealousy and bring her to the Temple, he could have named any other man. Therefore, he can add the names of other men to the formula of the oath.</i>. What about subjects he is not able to make her swear about? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, let us hear from the following: “Amen that I was not deviant preliminarily married<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 1:1:15" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.1.1.15"><i>Yebamot,</i> Chapter 1, Note 63</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 6:2:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.6.2.6"><i>Peah</i> Chapter 6, Note 46</a>.</i> and married, waiting for the levir and taken in<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">If the first husband had died childless and the levir brings her to the Temple as his wife. Only R. Aqiba considers sexual relations of the not-yet-married widow a crime. All other authorities will remove the mention of the widow “waiting to be married by the levir”.</i>.” Can he make her swear when she is preliminarily married or waiting for the levir<sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 4:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.4.1.1">Mishnah 4:1</a> states that there is no declaration of jealousy admitted in these two cases since the ceremony applies only to “a wife under her husband (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:19" href="/Numbers.5.19"><i>Num.</i> 5:19</a>)”. Nevertheless, the husband can force her to swear about her behavior when she was his wife but not under him.</i>? Nevertheless one rolls over.
Some Tannaїm state: Since this one is subject to curse and oath, so also all who have to swear are under curse and oath<sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote">This is the ninth hermeneutical rule of R. Ismael: “Anything which was in a set and was singled out to teach, was not singled out for itself but to teach about the entire set.” The rules of oaths derived from the ceremony of the suspected wife must apply to all members of the set of oaths. The same argument in <i>Sifry Num.</i> 14, copied in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bamidbar Rabbah 9:46" href="/Bamidbar_Rabbah.9.46"><i>Num. rabba</i> 9(46</a>).</i>. Some Tannaїm state: This one is subject to curse and oath, but no others who have to swear are under curse and oath. They<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote">The members of the Academy of Tiberias.</i> wanted to say, the one who said, since this one is subject to curse and oath, so also all who have to swear are under curse and oath, is understandable. The one who said, this one is subject to curse and oath, but no others who have to swear are under curse and oath, can you infer for rollover only but not for curse and oath<sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote">If one negates the 9th hermeneutical rule, one also has to negate the possibility of rollover of oaths in general. It is reported in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sanhedrin 7:5" href="/Tosefta_Sanhedrin.7.5">Tosephta <i>Sanhedrin</i> 7:11</a> that Hillel, about 100 years before R. Ismael, accepted only seven rules (numbers 1–6, 13). The <i>baraita</i> may be genuine but it is not practice.</i>? Some Tannaїm state: Since this one is subject to Amen, Amen, so also all who have to swear are under Amen, Amen. Some Tannaїm state: This one is subject to Amen, Amen, but no others who have to swear are under Amen, Amen. They<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote">The members of the Academy of Tiberias.</i> wanted to say, the one who said, since this one is subject to Amen, Amen, so also all who have to swear are under Amen, Amen, is understandable. The one who said, this one is subject to Amen, Amen, but no others who have to swear are under Amen, Amen, did one not infer rollover of oaths from the suspected wife? Can you infer for rollover only but not for Amen, Amen<sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote">If one negates the 9th hermeneutical rule, one also has to negate the possibility of rollover of oaths in general. It is reported in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sanhedrin 7:5" href="/Tosefta_Sanhedrin.7.5">Tosephta <i>Sanhedrin</i> 7:11</a> that Hillel, about 100 years before R. Ismael, accepted only seven rules (numbers 1–6, 13). The <i>baraita</i> may be genuine but it is not practice.</i>?
Rebbi Abba bar Mamal said, who stated “the one waiting for her levir”? Rebbi Aqiba! For Rebbi Aqiba said, there exists a bastard from a sister-in-law<sup class="footnote-marker">175</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 1:1:21" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.1.1.21"><i>Yebamot</i>, Chapter 1, Note 30</a>; Babli <i>Y ebamot</i> 92a. R. Aqiba declares as a bastard any child from a union not explicitly permitted.</i>. Rebbi Ilai said to him, why should a bastard be relevant for jealousy? The Torah said: “He shall declare his jealousy to his wife”, even a partial wife<sup class="footnote-marker">176</sup><i class="footnote">Since the widow of a childless man cannot possibly marry any man but her levir (unless she is released by the latter), and she can be married by the levir without any ceremony, she may be considered as engaged to be married by him.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">177</sup><i class="footnote">This text is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 1:1:2-26" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.1.1.2-26"><i>Yebamot</i> 1:1</a>, explained there in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 2:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.2.3.2">Notes 93–101</a>.</i> Rebbi Shammai said, did not Rebbi Yannai say the following: More than 30 Elders voted, from where that <i>qiddushin</i> have no legal effect on a sister-in-law? The verse says, “the wife of the deceased may not belong to any outside unrelated man, her levir shall come to her”, that she cannot have any existence with another man. Rebbi Joḥanan said to him, is that not a Mishnah? “Or after your levir will have performed <i>ḥalîṣah</i> with you, she is not preliminarily married.” And Rebbi Yannai praised him “those who pour out gold from the wallet,” “my son, they should not be removed from your eyes,” “get wise, my son, and make me happy”, “give to the wise that he shall become wiser,” “let the wise listen that he increase in knowledge.” Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, after all these praises I can explain it following Rebbi Aqiba since Rebbi Aqiba said that there exists a bastard from a sister-in-law! Could he not object: Why should a bastard be relevant for jealousy? The Torah said: “He shall declare his jealousy to his wife”, even a partial wife. A woman waiting for her levir who whored, Rebbi Eleazar said she is permitted to her house, Rebbi Joshua ben Levi said she is forbidden to her house<sup class="footnote-marker">178</sup><i class="footnote">“Her house” is really her future house, that of her levir. This is the answer to the question asked if we follow R. Eleazar.<br>The opinion of R. Joshua ben Levi is in the Babli ascribed to Rav Hamnuna (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 81a" href="/Yevamot.81a"><i>Yebamot</i> 81a</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 92b" href="/Yevamot.92b">92b</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 95a" href="/Yevamot.95a">95a</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 96a" href="/Yevamot.96a">96a</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 80b" href="/Gittin.80b"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 80b</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 18b" href="/Sotah.18b"><i>Soṭa</i> 18b</a>.) In the last source, the decision that practice does not follow Rav Hamnuna is explicitly attributed to Yerushalmi sources.</i>. What do the rabbis say? Rebbi Mana said, did not Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa say in the name of Rebbi Eleazar, if a woman waiting for her levir died, her mother is permitted to [the levir]<sup class="footnote-marker">180</sup><i class="footnote">Since a man is forbidden to marry his deceased wife’s mother, even if the wife died while being only preliminarily married to him, it follows that the one waiting for her levir is unrelated to the levir for R. Eleazar. She is unable to be unfaithful to him and the question of R. Ilai cannot be asked.</i>, since we have stated: “This is the rule: He cannot stipulate about any intercourse she could have had and would not be forbidden to him.<sup class="footnote-marker">181</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 2:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.2.6.1">Mishnah 7</a>. R. Abba bar Mamal is correct in the tradition of R. Eleazar. For him, only R. Aqiba could let the levir include a question about the time between the first husband’s death and his marriage.</i>”
How could one state this Mishnah<sup class="footnote-marker">182</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 2:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.2.6.1">Mishnah 7</a>, just quoted. In the tradition of the Babli and of the independent Mishnah mss., <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 2:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.2.6.1">Mishnah 7</a> is part of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 2:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.2.5.1">Mishnah 6</a>. Then one has a real problem, since in our interpretation <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 2:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.2.5.1">Mishnah 6</a> is R. Aqiba’s and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 2:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.2.6.1">Mishnah 7</a> the rabbi’s.</i>? Not the rabbis nor Rebbi Aqiba! But it is as follows. He who says that she is permitted to her house [follows] the rabbis against Rebbi Aqiba<sup class="footnote-marker">183</sup><i class="footnote">Then <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 2:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.2.6.1">Mishnah 7</a> has to be separated from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 2:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.2.5.1">Mishnah 6</a> as in the Yerushalmi text.</i>. He who says that she is forbidden to her house [follows] Rebbi Aqiba against the rabbis<sup class="footnote-marker">184</sup><i class="footnote">There is no problem; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 2:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.2.6.1">Mishnah 7</a> is part of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 2:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.2.5.1">Mishnah 6</a>.</i>. Rebbi Yannai said, a woman waiting for her levir who whored is permitted to her house<sup class="footnote-marker">185</sup><i class="footnote">R. Yannai is the one who in the preceding paragraph had stated that a woman waiting for her levir is unable to contract a marriage outside the family. He will also hold that contracting such a marriage is a criminal act since that is formulated as a prohibition in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 25:5" href="/Deuteronomy.25.5"><i>Deut</i>. 25:5</a>. As R. Yose bar Abun notes here, the prohibition covers only marriage, not extramarital relations.</i>. One had stated this: “It was hidden from her husband’s eyes<sup class="footnote-marker">186</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:13" href="/Numbers.5.13"><i>Num</i>. 5:13</a>. This <i>baraita</i> is not found in any other source. In a later paragraph, the expression quoted is interpreted to mean that a blind man cannot declare his jealousy and in <i>Sifry Num</i>. 7 that if the husband ever had looked the other way he never again could invoke the procedure of the suspected wife.</i>,” not from her levir’s eyes. Rebbi Jacob bar Zavdi in the name of Rebbi Abbahu: It happened, she was the wife of a Cohen, and they permitted her to her house. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, no whipping is involved.
Not that Rebbi Meïr was saying that the water checks her outside of its time<sup class="footnote-marker">187</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the statement in the Mishnah that the husband may stipulate that the wife swear not to be unfaithful in the future. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Sotah 18a" href="/Tosafot_on_Sotah.18a">Tosaphot (18a</a>) has a fuller version: לא שֶׁהָיָה רִבִּי מֵאִיר אוֹמֵר. הַמַּיִם בּוֹדְקִין אוֹתָהּ מֵעַכְשָׁיו אֶלָּא הַמַּיִם פְּקוּדִים בָּהּ לִכְשֶׁתִּטַּמֵּא הַמַּיִם בּוֹדְקִין אוֹתָהּ לְמַפְרֵעַ. “Not that Rebbi Meïr was saying that the water checks her <i>now but that the water is deposited in her body and if she would be defiled the water would check her</i> retroactively.” This is very close to the text of a <i>baraita</i> in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 18b" href="/Sotah.18b">Babli (18b</a>) and is suspect against the testimony of both mss.<br>The usual meaning of למפרע is “retroactively (in time); in inverse order (of an arrangement)”. Since, as noted by J. Levy in his Dictionary in the name of H. L. Fleischer, מפרע seems to be an Arabism and Arabic פרע means both ascending and descending, the translation of מפרע chosen here is “not in time (possibly earlier, possibly later).” Then the longer text would be unnecessary and the meaning is clear: The water will have no effect any time a sin was not committed. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sotah (Lieberman) 2:2" href="/Tosefta_Sotah_(Lieberman).2.2">Tosephta (2:2</a>), the reading is: That even after 20 years the water may reawake and check her out.</i>
“The teaching about jealousies<sup class="footnote-marker">188</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:29" href="/Numbers.5.29"><i>Num</i>. 5:29</a>. The text seems to be a composition of two different texts. In <i>Sifry zuṭa</i> [also similarly in <i>Num. rabba</i> 5(51)] one reads: “‘This is the teaching about jealousies’; the jealous husband may express his jealousy in Shiloh and in the Eternal House (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 2:2:10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.2.2.10">Note 120</a>). I could think, also at a local altar?
