The verse says: <i>This</i>.” The argument is obsolete since it is generally accepted doctrine that after the building of the Temple no local altar was permitted. The question arose since v. 15 requires the husband to bring his wife <i>to the Cohen</i>, perhaps not necessarily to the Tabernacle. But since the dust has to be taken from the floor of the Sanctuary, an altar without Sanctuary is excluded. The special emphasis of <i>this</i> indicates that no substitute for the Tabernacle is acceptable.</i>”. This is a teaching of the Temple. <sup class="footnote-marker">189</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 18b" href="/Sotah.18b">Babli, 18b</a>, the text of this <i>baraita</i> reads: “‘<i>This</i> is the teaching about <i>jealousies</i>’, which teaches that a woman drinks and repeats {from the several jealousies}. Rebbi Jehudah says <i>this</i> {a singular}, a woman does not drink and repeat. Rebbi Jehudah said, Neḥemiah the ditch-digger testified before Rebbi Aqiba that a woman drinks and repeats and we accepted his testimony from two husbands but not from one husband. But the Sages say that a woman <i>does not</i> drink and repeat whether from one husband or from two husbands.” The Babli has a complicated explanation to harmonize the anonymous first source (which has to represent the opinion of the majority, the Sages) with the contradictory statement at the end. The text of <i>Num. rabba</i> 5(51), is clearly an explanation of the position of the Babli.</i> A woman does not drink and repeat. Rebbi Jehudah said, Neḥemiah the ditch-digger testified before Rebbi Aqiba that a woman drinks and repeats. Rebbi Aqiba said, I shall explain. From one husband a woman does not drink and repeat, from two husbands a woman drinks and repeats. But the Sages say she drinks and repeats<sup class="footnote-marker">190</sup><i class="footnote">In the Rome ms: “<i>A woman does not</i> drink and repeat.” This text, which eliminates the disagreement with the Babli, is in contradiction to the following.</i> whether from one husband or from two husbands. Korkemit shall prove it, who drank and repeated and did it a third time from one husband before Shemaia and Abtalyon<sup class="footnote-marker">191</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Eduyot 5:6" href="/Mishnah_Eduyot.5.6">Mishnah <i>Idiut</i> 5:6</a> she is called Karkemit the libertine (the freedwoman). It is unclear whether at the end there is a difference between the two Talmudim since it is not stated whether Korkemit was repeatedly accused of relations with the same man.</i>.
There, we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">192</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Eduyot 5:6" href="/Mishnah_Eduyot.5.6">Mishnah <i>Idiut</i> 5:6</a>: “He (Aqabia ben Mehallalel) said: One does not make the proselyte or the freedwoman drink, but the Sages say, one makes her drink.”</i>: “One does not make the proselyte drink, etc. But the Sages say, one makes her drink.” Where do we hold? If about an Israel who married a proselyte, it already is written<sup class="footnote-marker">193</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:12" href="/Numbers.5.12"><i>Num</i>. 5:12</a>. The text is addressed to all Jewish men.</i>: “The sons of Israel” (not proselytes)<sup class="footnote-marker">194</sup><i class="footnote">This text is an intrusion from the later statement but is also found in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bamidbar Rabbah 9:34" href="/Bamidbar_Rabbah.9.34"><i>Num. rabba</i> 9(34</a>). The text quoted by R. Abraham ben David of Posquières (Ravad) in his commentary to <i>Idiut</i> has the order inverted and then the clause makes sense: אִם בְּגֵר שֶׁנָּשָׂא בַת יִשְׂרָאֵל. כְּבָר כְּתִיב. בְּנֵי יִשְׂרָאֵל. לֹא גֵרִים. אִם בְּיִשְׂרָאֵל שֶׁנָּשָׂא גִיּוֹרֶת. כְּבָר כְּתִיב וְהֵבִיא הָאִישׁ אֶת אִשְׁתּוֹ אֶל הַכֹּהֵן. “If about a proselyte who married a Jewish girl, it already is written: ‘The sons of Israel’, not proselytes. If about an Israel who married a proselyte, it already is written: ‘The man has to bring his wife to the Cohen.’ ” Ravad declares his text to be difficult; the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 26a" href="/Sotah.26a">Babli, 26a</a>, explicitly rejects the inference from “the sons of Israel”.</i>. If about a proselyte who married a Jewish girl, it already is written<sup class="footnote-marker">195</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:15" href="/Numbers.5.15"><i>Num</i>. 5:15</a>. Any Jewish man is included, even if he is not the son of an Israel.</i>: “The man has to bring his wife to the Cohen.” But we must hold about a proselyte who married a proselyte. What is the reason of Rebbi Aqiba<sup class="footnote-marker">196</sup><i class="footnote">This is clearly in error; it must be Aqabia, not Aqiba; correctly in the text quoted by Ravad and in <i>Num. rabba</i>. Aqabia had no rabbinic title.</i>? “The children of Israel”, not proselytes. What is the reason of the Sages? “You shall say to them<sup class="footnote-marker">197</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:12" href="/Numbers.5.12"><i>Num</i>. 5:12</a>; the text is addressed to everybody who has to hear the commandments, including the proselytes; argument approved in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 26a" href="/Sotah.26a">Babli, 26a</a>.</i>”, to add everything written in that paragraph. What is written in that paragraph? “A man slept with her”. His<sup class="footnote-marker">198</sup><i class="footnote">In Ravad’s text: Another man’s lying …</i> lying with her makes her forbidden, then her husband declares his jealousy and makes her drink.
“It was hidden from her husband’s eyes<sup class="footnote-marker">199</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:13" href="/Numbers.5.13"><i>Num</i>. 5:13</a>.</i>”, that excludes the blind man who has no eyes<sup class="footnote-marker">200</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tanḥuma Naśo</i> 7, <i>Tanḥuma Buber Naśo</i> 11 (Note 62); <i>Sifry Num</i>. 7, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bamidbar Rabbah 9:36" href="/Bamidbar_Rabbah.9.36"><i>Sifry zuṭa Naśo; Num. rabba</i> 9(36</a>); <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 27a" href="/Sotah.27a">Babli 27a</a> (the only other source which also includes the blind wife).</i>. It is the same for the blind man and the blind woman. This <i>baraita</i> follows Rebbi Jehudah who frees him from all obligations in the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">201</sup><i class="footnote">Quoted also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 31a" href="/Kiddushin.31a">Babli <i>Qiddušin</i>31a</a>. Since some of the great Babylonian authorities were blind (Rav Sheshet and Rav Yoseph), practice does not follow R. Jehudah. (Nevertheless, Maimonides disqualifies the blind man from the ceremony of the suspected wife).</i>.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Everybody agrees that he may not stipulate with her about before she was preliminarily married or about after she was divorced<sup class="footnote-marker">202</sup><i class="footnote">He cannot ask her to swear that she did not have any intercourse while not being married to him.</i>. If she was secluded after that<sup class="footnote-marker">203</sup><i class="footnote">After the divorce. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:2-4" href="/Deuteronomy.24.2-4"><i>Deut</i>. 24:2–4</a> prohibits the first husband from remarrying his divorcee if she had married another man after her divorce (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:2" href="/Deuteronomy.24.2"><i>Deut</i>. 24:2</a>: “She went and became another’s [wife]”.) Remarrying a wife who was promiscuous after divorce but not otherwise married is not forbidden.</i>, became impure<sup class="footnote-marker">204</sup><i class="footnote">Unmarried intercourse is punishable as harlotry according to some authorities (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 6:5:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.6.5.4"><i>Yebamot</i> 6:5, Note 107</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 61b" href="/Yevamot.61b">Babli <i>Yebamot</i> 61b</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Emor, Chapter 1" href="/Sifra,_Emor,_Chapter_1"><i>Sifra Emor Pereq</i> 1</a>).</i>, and after that he took her back, he cannot stipulate with her. This is the rule: He cannot stipulate about any intercourse she could have had and would not be forbidden to him<sup class="footnote-marker">181</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 2:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.2.6.1">Mishnah 7</a>. R. Abba bar Mamal is correct in the tradition of R. Eleazar. For him, only R. Aqiba could let the levir include a question about the time between the first husband’s death and his marriage.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Everybody agrees that he may not stipulate with her about before she was preliminarily married or about after she was divorced,” etc. Rebbi Yannai said, it is obvious that a man may forgive about his jealousy<sup class="footnote-marker">205</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 25a" href="/Sotah.25a">Babli, 25a</a>, this husband may annul his declaration of jealousy only if the wife did not meet her paramour at a secluded place. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 8:6:2-12" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.8.6.2-12">Yerushalmi, <i>Sanhedrin</i> 8:6</a> (fol. 26b, line 33), he may retract his declaration any time before the scroll is erased. In the paragraph here, the argument is as in the Babli.</i>. If he divorced her it is as if he had forgiven. How is that? If he had declared his jealousy, divorced her, took her back, and then she was at a secluded place. If you say, if he divorced her it is as if he had forgiven, he has to declare his jealousy a second time. But if you say, if he divorced her it is as if he had not forgiven, he does not have to declare his jealousy a second time. But if he had declared his jealousy, she was at a secluded place and he knew it<sup class="footnote-marker">206</sup><i class="footnote">From that moment on, she is forbidden to him. If he divorces her instead of taking her to the Temple, there is a question whether the divorce annuls the prohibition or not. If the divorce annuls the prohibition, he may take her back and the water will check her out in the future. If the divorce does not annul the prohibition, it is sinful for him to take her back without having her checked out and therefore the entire procedure is invalid since the water acts only if the husband himself is totally free from any sexual misbehavior (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:31" href="/Numbers.5.31"><i>Num</i>. 5:31</a>).</i>, then he divorced her, took her back, and then she was at a secluded place, if she drank and the water did nothing to her, she is pure. Or maybe that since the water does not check out a woman forbidden to her husband, she might be impure.

Chapter 3



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The officiating priest.</i> took the flour-offering from the Egyptian palm-leaf basket<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">See <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 2:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.2.1.1">Mishnah 2:1</a>.</i>, put it into a Temple vessel and laid that on her hands. The Cohen puts his hands under hers and performs the weave<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The prescribed movements for the dedication of private offerings. The movements in the six directions (fore and aft, right and left, up and down) are imitated today as the motions of the palm-branch on Tabernacles.</i>. He weaved, presented<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Before the altar receives its part, the entire offering in the vessel is presented to the altar at its South-West corner.</i>, took a fistful and put it into the fire. The remainder is eaten by the priests<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">As are all offerings except those of a priest; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:7-11" href="/Leviticus.6.7-11"><i>Lev</i>. 6:7–11</a>.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “He took the flour-offering,” etc. It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:25" href="/Numbers.5.25"><i>Num.</i> 5:25</a>: “The Cohen shall take the jealousy-flour-offering from the woman’s hand, weave it before the Eternal and present it to the altar.”</i>: “The Cohen shall take the jealousy-flour-offering from the woman’s hand.” Does he perform the weave? Does not she perform the weave<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The verse requires that the Cohen perform the weave. But in the case of an animal sacrifice it is required (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:30" href="/Leviticus.7.30"><i>Lev.</i> 7:30</a>) that the individual bringing the sacrifice be the actor: “His hands shall bring the Eternal’s gift, the fat on the breast he has to bring, to weave it before the Eternal.” In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 19a" href="/Sotah.19a">Babli, 19a</a>, the parallel expressions <i>his hands, the woman’s hand</i> are taken as proof that the woman has to take part in weaving her gift just as a man has to take part in the weaving of his. The formulation of the Yerushalmi may be interpreted as a question: Since the Mishnah requires the woman to participate and the verse requires the Cohen to perform the weave, how can the Mishnah be justified?</i>? But from here [one understands that] he takes it from a profane vessel and puts it into a Temple vessel. <sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The rest of the paragraph is also in <i>Qiddušin</i> 1:8 (fol. 61c).</i>“The Cohen puts his hands under hers and performs the weave.” Does the Cohen put his hands under hers? Is that not objectionable<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, this would be written כעור. Is the bodily contact between the Cohen and an otherwise married woman not immoral? It is not directly forbidden since the woman has to be pure to enter the Temple but this makes the situation only worse.</i>? He brings a kerchief<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Between his and her hands, not to touch the woman.</i>. Does that not separate<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">It is a general principle that the Temple service has to be by the priest directly. Since the verse requires the priests to serve in the Temple, they may not wear shoes (even in winter) since then the shoes would be in the Temple, not the Cohen directly. If the Cohen has to perform the weave, his hands cannot be separated from the Temple vessel. If, as indicated in the previous Note, the participation of the woman is implicitly required by the verse, her hands do not count as separation.</i>? He brings an elderly Cohen<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Presumably he is past all sexual feelings.</i>. You may even say, a young man, since bad inclinations do not happen at that hour<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">The fear of the Temple will banish all bad thoughts. The commentaries read the sentence to mean that in the short time needed for the weave, bad thoughts cannot develop. This is obviously false.</i>. Rebbi Ḥiyya stated<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Qiddušin:</i> R. Ḥiyya stated, it happens (that the Cohen has sinful thoughts). Maybe he implies that since the weaving can be done by two priests for the handless woman, it can be done by two priests for all women. There is no parallel to this <i>baraita</i> in any other known source.</i>: If the suspected wife has no hands, two priests weave in her stead.
Rebbi Yose said: In all cases, the following is an open question for the rabbis: What does he present? The body of the flour offering or the body of the vessel<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">When the Cohen brings the offering to the altar, does he touch the wall of the altar with the Temple vessel or does he have to turn the vessel so that some of the flour touches the wall? The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 14b" href="/Sotah.14b">Babli, 14b</a>, states that the vessel is presented, based on <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 2:8" href="/Leviticus.2.8"><i>Lev.</i>2:8</a>.</i>?
From where that weaves precede presentations<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Menachot 5:6" href="/Mishnah_Menachot.5.6">Mishnah <i>Menaḥot</i> 5:6</a> states that weave always precedes presentation. One has to wonder about Rashi’s Yerushalmi text since in his commentary to that Mishnah he follows R. Jeremiah.</i>? Rebbi Jeremiah in the name of Rebbi Pedat: They learned that from the case of the suspected wife<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Since <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:25" href="/Numbers.5.25"><i>Num.</i> 5:25</a> clearly prescribes first weave and then presentation.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, the suspected wife is different because of its novelty and one cannot infer from anything that is different because of its novelty<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">While not in the tannaïtic rules, this is a generally recognized hermeneutical principle in both Talmudim; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 45a" href="/Ketubot.45a">Babli <i>Ketubot</i> 45a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 27a" href="/Sanhedrin.27a"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 27a</a>. The novelty status of the suspected wife’s offering is explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 2:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.2.1.1">Mishnah 2:1</a>; therefore, procedural instructions for this offering cannot imply similar instructions for the other flour offerings.</i>. Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya understood it from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:7" href="/Leviticus.6.7"><i>Lev.</i> 6:7</a>. This paragraph deals with technicalities of all flour offerings, whether they need weaving or not. Any special ceremony for certain offerings must precede the ceremonies common to all offerings.</i>: “This is the teaching about the flour offering, present.” Where is the weave? It already preceded. Rebbi Yose said, explain it about flour offerings that do not need weaves; you cannot infer anything. Which [verse] says anything? “You shall bring,<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 2:8" href="/Leviticus.2.8"><i>Lev.</i> 2:8</a>: “You shall bring the offering made <i>from these</i> to the Eternal; the Cohen shall bring it and present it to the altar.” “These” are flour and olive oil.<br>The <i>baraita</i> is also quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 60b" href="/Menachot.60b">Babli, <i>Menaḥot</i> 60b</a>.</i>” to include the <i>‘Omer</i> flour offering in presentations. “He shall present,” to include the suspected wife’s flour offering in presentations. It is written after that<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 2:9" href="/Leviticus.2.9"><i>Lev.</i> 2:9</a>.</i>: “He shall lift a fist full”; where is the weave<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Which is prescribed for the offering of the suspected wife in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:25" href="/Numbers.5.25"><i>Num.</i>5:25</a>..</i>? It already preceded.
There, it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Menachot 7:1" href="/Mishnah_Menachot.7.1">Mishnah <i>Menaḥot</i>7:1</a>. The list contains the offerings of the <i>‘Omer</i> and the suspected wife. Such a list is necessary since the flour offerings accompanying an animal sacrifice (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 15:1-16" href="/Numbers.15.1-16"><i>Num.</i> 15:1–16</a>), as well as the private offerings of a Cohen, are burned completely.</i>: “The following flour offerings have a handful taken and the remainders are eaten.” Rebbi Abba bar Mamal and Rebbi [Samuel]<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">This is the correct name. Possibly the name was written ר״ש in a common source of the mss. and was interpreted wrongly by some intermediate scribe.</i> bar Rav Isaac were sitting together. Rebbi Abba bar Mamal asked from Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac: From where [do we know that] the remainders of the <i>‘Omer</i> offering are eaten<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">The paragraph of the <i>‘Omer</i> offering (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:9-14" href="/Leviticus.23.9-14"><i>Lev.</i> 23:9–14</a>) prescribes weaving but is silent about anything done after the weaving. It might be concluded that the general rules of the flour offering specified in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:7-11" href="/Leviticus.6.7-11"><i>Lev.</i> 6:7–11</a> do apply. These include that a Cohen has to present the offering to the altar, that he take a handful to the altar to be burned, and that the remainder be eaten under the rules of most holy sacrifices. However, those rules presuppose that pieces of incense are put on top of the offering; this does not apply to the <i>‘Omer</i> offering. Therefore, the details of the treatment of the <i>‘Omer</i> offering seem to be undefined.</i>? He said to him: Did not Rebbi Joḥanan say<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 2:1:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.2.1.9">Chapter 2, Note 39</a>.</i> in the name of Rebbi Ismael: “Offering of<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:15" href="/Numbers.5.15"><i>Num.</i> 5:15</a>, “an offering of jealousy”.</i>, offering of<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 2:14" href="/Leviticus.2.14"><i>Lev.</i> 2:14</a>, “an offering of First Fruits,” taken to refer to the <i>‘Omer</i> offering. These are the only flour offerings referred to in the construct state; this is taken as indication that they follow parallel rules except as indicated otherwise in the biblical text.</i>.” Since “offering of” mentioned there<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:15" href="/Numbers.5.15"><i>Num.</i> 5:15</a>, “an offering of jealousy”.</i> is from barley, so also “offering of” mentioned here<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 2:14" href="/Leviticus.2.14"><i>Lev.</i> 2:14</a>, “an offering of First Fruits,” taken to refer to the <i>‘Omer</i> offering. These are the only flour offerings referred to in the construct state; this is taken as indication that they follow parallel rules except as indicated otherwise in the biblical text.</i> is from barley. Since the remainders of the offering of the suspected wife are eaten<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">This is not prescribed in the biblical text but since a handful must be taken to the altar it is accepted that this offering follows the rules of all offerings of which a handful is burned on the altar; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 3:1:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.3.1.5">Note 26</a>.</i>, so the remainders of the <i>‘Omer</i> offering are eaten. Rebbi (Aqiba)<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">This attribution is certainly incorrect. Probably one should read “R. Jacob”; one Amora of this name was known as one of the colleagues of R. Jeremiah.</i> said: After they got up, Rebbi Abba bar Mamal was standing with Rebbi Jeremiah. He<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Rebbi Jeremiah said to R. Abba bar Mamal.</i> said to him: Look, how he made your question fly away! From where [do we know that] the remainders of the offering of the suspected wife are eaten<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">For that offering also, the handful for the altar is mentioned but nothing else.</i>? Rebbi Ze‘ira<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">R. Jeremiah’s teacher; he called the specialist for <i>baraitot</i> in his academy.</i> brought Rebbi Isaac Aṭoshiyya, who stated for him: “Any flour offering mixed with oil<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:10" href="/Leviticus.7.10"><i>Lev.</i> 7:10</a>: “Any flour offering mixed with oil or dry shall belong to all sons of Aaron, to each man as to his brother.”</i>”. Where do we hold? If about mixed wheat flour, it already had been said<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">The list of private flour offerings from wheat flour is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 2:1-10" href="/Leviticus.2.1-10"><i>Lev.</i>2:1–10</a> and there it is emphasized that the remainders have to be eaten by the sons of Aaron.</i>. So if it does not refer to mixed wheat flour, apply it to mixed barley flour. Another [<i>baraita</i>] states: “Or dry<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:10" href="/Leviticus.7.10"><i>Lev.</i> 7:10</a>: “Any flour offering mixed with oil or dry shall belong to all sons of Aaron, to each man as to his brother.”</i>”. Where do we hold? If about dry wheat flour<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">The purification offering of the poor sinner (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:11-13" href="/Leviticus.5.11-13"><i>Lev.</i> 5:11–13</a>) is from wheat flour and has to be eaten by the Cohen.</i>, it already had been said. So if it does not refer to dry wheat flour, apply it to dry barley flour. Rebbi Yose said, we deal with mixed wheat flour and dry wheat flour, and it was said for a purpose<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">The argument of the preceding <i>baraitot</i>, which in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 72b" href="/Menachot.72b">Babli (<i>Menaḥot</i>72b</a>) is a pseudo-tannaïtic statement by Ḥizqiah, is irrelevant since the verse teaches important new information for all flour offerings that are eaten (also noted in the Babli).</i>. “[It] shall belong to all sons of Aaron, to each man as to his brother.” A man takes his part even if he is blemished<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">He has a bodily defect which disables him from serving in the Temple (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:17-19" href="/Leviticus.21.17-19"><i>Lev.</i> 21:17–19</a>).</i>. A minor does not take a part even if he is unblemished<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Tzav, Chapter 10 9" href="/Sifra,_Tzav,_Chapter_10.9"><i>Sifra Ṣaw Pereq</i> 10(9</a>); <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 72b" href="/Menachot.72b">Babli <i>Menaḥot</i> 72b</a>. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 102a" href="/Zevachim.102a"><i>Zebaḥim</i> 102a</a> it is stated more in detail that the right of a blemished Cohen to eat of the holy food is established in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21" href="/Leviticus.21"><i>Lev.</i> 21</a> but his right to take part in the distribution of food in the Temple is derived from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:11" href="/Leviticus.6.11"><i>Lev.</i> 6:11</a> [from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Tzav, Chapter 3 5" href="/Sifra,_Tzav,_Chapter_3.5"><i>Sifra Ṣaw Pereq</i> 3(5</a>)]. One really needs <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:10" href="/Leviticus.7.10"><i>Lev.</i> 7:10</a> only to show that a minor cannot claim a part in the distribution (cf. <i>Šiṭṭa Mequbeṣet, Zebaḥim</i> 102a).</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said: Because the Torah added a detail in one case, can you add that in every case? But “remembrance<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 2:9" href="/Leviticus.2.9"><i>Lev.</i> 2:9</a>: “The Cohen has to lift its remembrance” which is the fistful of flour with the incense, to be burned on the altar.</i>, remembrance<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Num. 5:26: "The Cohen has to lift a fistful for its remembrance," speaking of the flour offering of the suspected wife.</i>”. “You shall bring,<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 2:8" href="/Leviticus.2.8"><i>Lev.</i> 2:8</a>: “You shall bring the offering made <i>from these</i> to the Eternal; the Cohen shall bring it and present it to the altar.” “These” are flour and olive oil.<br>The <i>baraita</i> is also quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 60b" href="/Menachot.60b">Babli, <i>Menaḥot</i> 60b</a>.</i>” to include the <i>‘Omer</i> flour offering in presentations. “He shall present it,” to include the suspected wife’s flour offering in presentations. It is written after that<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">While in the preceding paragraph the following verse was from the rules of the suspected wife, here the verse is taken from the general rules of a flour offering, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 2:10" href="/Leviticus.2.10"><i>Lev.</i>2:10</a> to imply that every flour offering of which only a fistful is burned on the altar is eaten by the Cohanim.</i>: “What is left from the offering is most holy for Aaron and his sons.”

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The officiating priest.</i> gives her to drink and after that he presents her offering<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">As the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 19b" href="/Sotah.19b">Babli (19b</a>) points out, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 3:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.3.1.1">Mishnah 1</a> only described all the ritual acts to be performed for the flour offering but did not determine the time sequence.</i>. Rebbi Simeon says, he sacrifices her offering and afterwards gives her to drink as it is said<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:27" href="/Numbers.5.27"><i>Num.</i> 5:27</a>.</i>: “After that he shall give the water to the woman to drink.” If he gave her to drink and after that sacrificed her offering, it is valid<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Even for R. Simeon.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “He gives her to drink and after that he sacrifices her offering,” etc. What is the reason of the rabbis? “They shall come into her<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:24" href="/Numbers.5.24"><i>Num.</i> 5:24</a>: “The curse-water shall come into her as a bitter one.” This is written before the description of the presentation of the flour offering in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:25" href="/Numbers.5.25">v. 25</a>.</i>.” What is the reason of Rebbi Simeon? “After that he shall give to the woman to drink<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:27" href="/Numbers.5.27"><i>Num.</i> 5:27</a>.</i>.” How does Rebbi Simeon explain the rabbis’ reason, “they shall come into her”? All of them, not part only<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">She has to drink the rather small amount of water completely.</i> How do the rabbis explain Rebbi Simeon’s reason, “after that he shall give the water to the woman to drink”? Against her will, without her agreement<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 3:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.3.3.1">Mishnah 3</a>. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 19b" href="/Sotah.19b">Babli, 19b</a>, this is the position of the rabbis.</i>. Rebbi Simeon agrees with the rabbis that if he gave her to drink and after that presented her offering, it is valid. The rabbis agree with Rebbi Simeon that if he presented her offering and after that gave her to drink, it is valid<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">This statement is not in the Babli. As Tosaphot (19a, s. v. ואחר) points out, the Babli does not object.</i>. What is the difference between them? The commandment. The rabbis say that the flour offering checks her out<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 20b" href="/Sotah.20b">Babli, 20b</a>, this is given as R. Simeon’s reason to require sacrificing the offering before the drink.</i> but Rebbi Simeon says, the water is<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Reading אִין as הֵן, equivalent of Babylonian אִינּוּן “they are”. Instead of אין, the Rome ms. has היו. The past tense does not agree with the rest of the text; it should be rejected. Note that there also exists a Babylonian אִין, equal to הֵן “yes”.</i> what checks her out. What is the reason of the Sages? “A flour offering of remembrance remembering iniquity<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:15" href="/Numbers.5.15"><i>Num.</i> 5:15</a>.</i>.” What is the reason of Rebbi Simeon? “They shall come into her<sup class="footnote-marker">47,</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:24" href="/Numbers.5.24"><i>Num.</i> 5:24</a>: “The curse-water shall come into her as a bitter one.” This is written before the description of the presentation of the flour offering in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:25" href="/Numbers.5.25">v. 25</a>.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">The same explanationas anonymous text in Sifry Num. 18; copied in Num. rabba 9(18).</i>.” How do the rabbis explain Rebbi Simeon’s reason, “they shall come into her”? This teaches that it shakes all her limbs<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 3:4:1-13" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.3.4.1-13">Mishnah 4</a>.</i>. How does Rebbi Simeon explain the rabbis’ reason, “a flour offering of remembrance remembering iniquity”? This teaches that all iniquities she has committed are remembered at that time<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Before the Heavenly Court which decides about the water’s action.</i>.
The rabbis say, he writes, erases, gives to drink, and sacrifices [the flour offering]. Rebbi Simeon says, he writes, sacrifices, erases, and gives to drink. Everybody agrees that erasing immediately precedes drinking; only that the rabbis say, he writes, erases, gives to drink, and sacrifices, while Rebbi Simeon says, he writes, sacrifices, erases, and gives to drink. It was stated: “She may always change her mind as long as her offering was not sacrificed. If her offering was sacrificed and she said I do not want to drink’ one makes her gargle<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Before the Heavenly Court which decides about the water’s action.</i> and forces her to drink”. Following Rebbi Simeon. But following the rabbis she already drank<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>baraita</i> can only be from the school of R. Simeon since for the rabbis the offering is sacrificed only after she drank.</i>. We have stated: “She did not finish drinking<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 3:4:1-13" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.3.4.1-13">Mishnah 3:4</a>: The water acted as poison when she was still standing in the courtyard of the Temple.</i>”. Some Tannaïm state: She did not move from there<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">After drinking the water, she had to remain in the courtyard until the fistful of her offering was burned on the altar.</i>. He who says, she did not finish drinking, Rebbi Simeon<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Since he was reported to hold that the water is checking her out. In this interpretation, the report in the Mishnah of a disagreement between the rabbis and R. Simeon is followed by an anonymous Mishnah following R. Simeon. This implies (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 4:11:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.4.11.7"><i>Yebamot</i> 4, Note 175</a>, ‘<i>Orlah</i> 2, Note 30) that the Yerushalmi decides that practice follows R. Simeon.</i>. He who says, she did not move from there, the rabbis<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Since it was stated in the preceding paragraph that the water is only activated by the burning of the offering on the altar. This reason is given in the Babli for R. Simeon to require the early sacrificing of her offering, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 3:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.3.2.2">Note 51</a>. It shows that the Babli decides that practice does <i>not</i> follow R. Simeon.</i>.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If she said “I shall not drink<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Since she is forbidden to her husband from the moment she was found to have been in a secluded place with her paramour until after she drank the water, by refusing to drink she accepts to be divorced by her husband without any <i>ketubah</i> money but without explicitly admitting guilt.</i>” before the scroll was erased, her scroll is put away<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Since it was written in holiness and contains the Divine Name, it cannot be destroyed.</i> and her offering dispersed on the ashes<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">Since the offering was already dedicated in a Temple vessel, it cannot be returned to profane status. It is put on the ashes taken from the altar.</i>. Her scroll is not acceptable to be used to make another suspected wife drink<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 2:2:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.2.2.9">Halakhah 2:2, Note 104</a>.</i>. If she said “I am impure” after the scroll was erased, the water is poured out and her offering dispersed on the ashes<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Since there is no longer any doubt, the water cannot be used, neither can the offering. By her confession of adultery she has to be divorced by her husband, without him having to pay the <i>ketubah</i>. On her confession she cannot be prosecuted for adultery because confessions are not acceptable in a court of law since the testimony of close relatives is not admissible and everybody is a relative to himself.</i>. If she said “I shall not drink” after the scroll was erased, one makes her gargle and forces her to drink against her will<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Since she caused the Divine Name to be erased, if she does not drink she commits blasphemy.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Before the scroll was erased,” etc. It was stated: Her scroll is put away under the hinges of a [gate of] the Temple hall. Why? In order to shred it<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sotah (Lieberman) 2:2" href="/Tosefta_Sotah_(Lieberman).2.2">Tosephta 2:2</a>. The scroll has to be destroyed to avoid its being used again, but this must be done in holiness and without anybody touching the Divine Names.</i>. A small hollow space was where the water was poured out<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">There is no parallel to this statement in any known tannaїtic source; one does not know where this hollow space was. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 34a" href="/Pesachim.34a">Babli <i>Pesaḥim</i> 34a</a>, the open space between the ramp leading to the top of the altar and the altar is called <i>lul</i>; in the parallel <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Zevachim 7:2" href="/Tosefta_Zevachim.7.2">Tosephta <i>Zebaḥim</i> 7:6</a> this is called חלון “window” or רבוכה ,רביבה “hollow with masonry walls.” That hollow could not be classified as “small”.</i>. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sotah (Lieberman) 2:2" href="/Tosefta_Sotah_(Lieberman).2.2">Tosephta 2:2</a>, referring to the water which no longer is needed.</i>: There is no holiness in it. May it be used to moisten mortar<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">It is sinful to use Temple property for private use. But the water might be used in ongoing repairs on the Temple mount.</i>? What should be wrong? It is poured out! It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">In another, otherwise undocumented <i>baraita</i>. Since it is unknown which of the two <i>baraitot</i> represents practice, the unneeded water cannot be used for anything.</i>: There is holiness in it.
“If she said ‘I am impure’ after the scroll was erased, the water is poured out and her offering dispersed over the ashes.” This proves that the suspected wife’s offering is holy before the scroll is written<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">Since following the rabbis the offering is transferred to the Temple vessel only after she drank the water, if her offering is treated as holy but invalid by being poured over the ashes of the sacrifices, the question arises when the offering acquires the holiness of a Temple offering. The question is obvious even for R. Simeon since it is forbidden to bring profane food into the Temple precinct even though according to him the case of the Mishnah could not arise.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, there is more: Rebbi Ḥiyya stated, her offering is holy once she is on her way<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">From the moment the husband had dedicated the flour, irrespective of the vessel it was brought in.</i>. Rebbi Yose bar Abun said, our Mishnah states this<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 4:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.4.1.1">Mishnah 4:2</a>, detailing the cases in which the procedure becomes inactive. If the husband had relations with his wife while she was forbidden to him, he must divorce her since without the Temple procedure she cannot become permitted to him. But because it is his fault that she cannot rehabilitate herself, he has to pay the full amount of his <i>ketubah</i> obligation.</i>: “Or if her husband had sexual relations with her on the road.”
“If she said ‘I shall not drink’ after the scroll was erased, one makes her gargle and forces her to drink against her will.” <sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 2:4:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.2.4.3">Chapter 2, Notes 135–136</a>.</i> How many letters have to be erased? Rebbi Ḥanin stated: The House of Shammai say, one; the House of Hillel say, two. Rebbi Hilai said, the reason of the House of Hillel: That he could have written Yah. <sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">In a discussion between R. Jehudah and R. Aqiba, quoted in full in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 19b" href="/Sotah.19b">Babli 19b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sotah (Lieberman) 2:3" href="/Tosefta_Sotah_(Lieberman).2.3">Tosephta 2:3</a>.</i> Rebbi Aqiba said to him: But why does one make her drink? If it is to check her out, she already is completely checked out<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, בדוקה ועומדת “lawfully checked out”; in the Tosephta בדוקה ומנוולת “checked out and degraded”.</i>! But Rebbi Aqiba must hold that the one who says “I shall not drink” is as the one who says “I am impure for you”<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">One admits her guilt, the other accepts the consequences as if she were guilty; since the proceedings are extra-judicial, there is no reason to treat the two cases differently.</i>. But does Rebbi Aqiba not agree that “one makes her gargle until her face turned yellow and forces her to drink against her will”? He does, if she started to drink<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">In the Tosephta, following the rabbis who make the entire procedure depend on the burning of the flour offering, R. Aqiba holds that she does not have to drink as long as the offering was not burned. Since for the rabbis the offering follows the drinking, he treats the one who says “I am impure” exactly as the one who pays the fine without admitting guilt. In the Babli, R. Aqiba’s argument is explained away: one accepts her statement that she is impure only if made in a self-assured way, when she can be believed; but if she makes the confession while trembling, one suspects that she might be pure but has been intimidated by the procedure and therefore her guilt has not been established. Both sources disagree with the Yerushalmi.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> She does not finish drinking before her face becomes yellow, her eyes protrude, and her veins swell. She is taken outside so that she does not pollute the temple courtyard. If she has merit, it suspends. There is merit which suspends for one year, there is merit which suspends for two years, there is merit which suspends for three years. From this, Ben Azai concluded that one is obligated to teach his daughter Torah, so that when she needs to drink, she will know that the merit will assist her. Rebbi Eliezer says: whoever teaches his daughter Torah teaches her debauchery. Rebbi Joshua said: a woman prefers one <i>qab</i> and frivolity to nine <i>qabim</i> and abstinence. He used to say: a foolish pious man, a sly wicked man, a predatory woman, and hits by the predatory, these are the ones who ruin the world.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “She does not finish drinking”, etc. But did we not think to say “All of them, not part only<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 3:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.3.2.2">Note 48</a>; it was agreed that the water will not become active until the woman finished drinking.</i>”? It is as if somebody said, X did not finish drinking when he started shaking<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">This is a manner of speech meaning “immediately”.</i>.
“There is merit which suspends for one year, there is merit which suspends for two years, there is merit which suspends for three years.” There is merit which suspends for one year,” from Nabuchadneṣar: “At the end of twelve months.<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Daniel 4:26" href="/Daniel.4.26"><i>Dan</i>. 4:26</a>. It is written in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Daniel 4:24" href="/Daniel.4.24">v. 24</a>: Therefore, o king, take my advice: atone for your sins by deeds of charity and for your misdeed by kindness to the poor, then your peace will be prolonged.<br>In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 20b" href="/Sotah.20b">Babli, 20b</a>, and in <i>Sifry Num</i>. 8, this is attributed to R. Ismael as maximum time lag for the water’s action.</i>” “There is merit which suspends for two years,” from Amnon: “It was after two years.<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Samuel 13:23" href="/II_Samuel.13.23"><i>2S</i>. 13:23</a>. Absalom managed to kill Amnon two years after the latter had raped Tamar.</i>” “There is merit which suspends for three years,” from Ahab: “They sat for three years when there was no war between Aram and Israel.<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Kings 22:1" href="/I_Kings.22.1"><i>1K</i>. 22:1</a>. Everywhere in talmudic literature is the nameless “king of Israel” of Chap. 22:1–38 identified with Ahab mentioned in vv. 39–40. The three years are counted from the judicial murder of Nabot.</i>” Rebbi Yose said, it was said that the entire twelve months he<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">Probably referring to Nabuchadneṣar; but in the current text it refers to the first year of Ahab after Nabot’s murder.</i> occupied himself with deeds of charity. And all these two<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Sotah 20b:14:1" href="/Tosafot_on_Sotah.20b.14.1"><i>Tosaphot</i>, 20b s. v. יש</a>, the reading is “three years.” This reading is slightly suspect since the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 21a" href="/Sotah.21a">Babli (21a</a>) accepts only the merit of Torah study as a saving device for Jews (works of charity really being the main vehicle of salvation for Gentiles).</i> years he occupied himself with Torah. That is what it says: “They were in a Yeshiva<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">A talmudic academy.</i> for three years when there was no war between Aram and Israel.”
<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">In different wording, the following two paragraphs are in <i>Sifry Num</i>. 8, copied in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bamidbar Rabbah 9:39" href="/Bamidbar_Rabbah.9.39"><i>Num. rabba</i> 9(39</a>).</i>“An offering of remembrance,” a general statement; “referring to sin”, a detailed statement. If a general statement is followed by a specific one, the general does not mean more than the specific<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">The fifth hermeneutical rule of R. Ismael: The only purpose of the offering is to lead to the punishment of the wife should she be found guilty. In the <i>Sifry</i>, the argument is in R. Ismael’s name.</i>. If you say so, do you not find the argument disadvantaged? If punishment, which is given out sparingly, can refer, then reward, which is given out in abundance<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 1:7:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.1.7.5">Halakhah 1:7, Note 269</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 11a" href="/Sotah.11a">Babli 11a</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sotah (Lieberman) 4:1" href="/Tosefta_Sotah_(Lieberman).4.1">Tosephta 4:1</a> notes that the second of the Ten Commandments defines the measure of reward as at least 500 times the measure of punishment (since “thousands” are at least 2000, the ratio is ≥ 2000/4).</i>, should do so much more! But, “an offering of remembrance,” if she has merit, “referring to sin”, if she has no merit. It was stated: Rebbi Tarphon said, all statements of “remembrance” in the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">All occurences of זכרון in the Pentateuch except <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:15" href="/Numbers.5.15"><i>Num</i>. 5:15</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:18" href="/Numbers.5.18">18</a>. R. Tarphon holds with R. Simeon in the next Mishnah that it is preposterous to assume that the procedure has any value if it is inactive in most cases.</i> have a good meaning except this one which is for punishment. Rebbi Aqiba said to him: If it only said, “referring to sin”, and then was silent, I would agree with your statement. But since it says “an offering of remembrance,” this must be for a good [purpose].
It was stated: Rebbi Jehudah says in the name of Rebbi Eleazar ben Matthias: It says, “but if the woman was not impure but was pure<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:28" href="/Numbers.5.28"><i>Num</i>. 5:28</a>.</i>.” Would we not know that if she was not impure she was pure? Why does the verse say, “but she was pure”<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Sifry Num</i>. 19, the double expression is justified: R. Ismael holds that she only is permitted to her husband if the procedure had shown her innocence. An anonymous source notes that if she got a divorce after the procedure or her husband died, she was free to marry the man she was suspected of having an affair with; if she was divorced for adultery, the adulterer would be forbidden to her.<br>The Yerushalmi text is copied in <i>Num. rabba</i> 9(50).</i>? Only that at the end the Omnipresent rewards her for the abuse, that if she was sterile she will become pregnant<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 36a" href="/Sotah.36a">Babli, 36a</a>, and <i>Sifry Num</i>. 19, the first clause, about the sterile becoming pregnant, is R. Aqiba’s; all the others are attributed to R. Ismael. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 31b" href="/Berakhot.31b">Babli <i>Berakhot</i> 31b</a>, the attributions are switched.</i>, [if she] was having difficult births she will have easy ones, [if she] had ugly children she will have good looking ones<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">This clause is missing in the parallels. In Babylonian historical spelling, it would be כעורים.</i>, black ones she will have white ones, short ones she will have tall ones, females she will have males, single children she will have twins. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">This attribution to a second generation Amora is quite impossible in a tannaїtic text; in the Babli and <i>Sifry Num</i>. the argument is R. Ismael’s. It seems that the Yerushalmi refers to R. Simeon ben Ioḥai, quoted in the next sentence.</i> said to him: If it were so then all women should be misbehaving in order to become pregnant! But does Rebbi Simeon not interpret “she will be found innocent and become pregnant with seed”? He does, legitimate seed, not illegitimate<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 4:1:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.4.1.2-5">Halakhah 4:1</a>, a similar (anonymous) argument is quoted to show that a divorcee married to a priest cannot be subjected to the procedure of the suspected wife because even if she was innocent she could not have legitimate seed from the husband forbidden to her; her children from the priest are desecrated.</i>.
Ben Azai does not follow Rebbi Eleazar ben Azariah<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">R. Eleazar ben Azariah obligates women to listen to the Torah but not to study it.</i>, as it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ḥagigah</i> Babli 3 a; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 1:1:2-10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.1.1.2-10">Yerushalmi 1:1</a>.</i>: It happened that Rebbi Joḥanan ben Beroqa and Rebbi Eleazar Ḥasma were walking from Jabneh to Lydda when they were visiting Rebbi Joshua in Beqiïn<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli: פקיעין. The locality is not identified; it cannot be the place of the same name in Galilee.</i>. He asked them, what was new today in the House of Study? They said to him, we all are your students and drink from your waters. He said to them, even so, it is impossible that there not be a new idea every day in the House of Study. Who stayed there over the Sabbath? They said to him, Rebbi Eleazar ben Azariah. And what was his text? “Assemble the people, men, women, and children.<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 31:12" href="/Deuteronomy.31.12"><i>Deut</i>. 31:12</a>.</i>” What did he say about this? Since the men come to learn and the women to hear, why do the children come? It must be to give a reward to those who bring them. He said to them, the generation is not orphaned which counts Rebbi Eleazar ben Azariah among them.
<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 66b" href="/Yoma.66b">Babli <i>Yoma</i> 66b</a>; <i>Num</i>.<i>rabba</i> 9(53).</i> A lady asked Rebbi Eliezer, why did one sin of the golden calf lead to three different kinds of death<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">In the aftermath of the sin of the golden calf, it is stated (1) that the Levites killed about 3000 men (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 32:28" href="/Exodus.32.28"><i>Ex</i>. 32:28</a>), (2) that the people died of a plague (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 32:35" href="/Exodus.32.35"><i>Ex</i>. 32:35</a>), (3) that Moses ground the golden calf into dust, spread it on water and let the people drink, in imitation of the <i>soṭah</i> ritual (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 32:20" href="/Exodus.32.20"><i>Ex</i>. 32:20</a>).</i>? He said to her, the wisdom of a woman is only in her spinning rod, as is written: “All wise women span with their hands.<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 35:25" href="/Exodus.35.25"><i>Ex</i>. 35:25</a>. R. Eliezer intended to insult the woman who had dared to study Torah (In the Babli, she is called “a wise woman” and her question is dealt with at length by Rashi in his commentary on the Pentateuch). It is not clear whether this represents the general teaching of the House of Shammai or that of R. Eliezer alone.<br>Since it is common practice to teach Pentateuch and its interpretation to girls it is impossible to understand how Maimonides and Joseph Caro could have R. Eliezer’s statement included in their Codes.</i>” His son Hyrkanos said to him, in order not to give her an answer you made me lose 300 <i>kor</i> of tithes every year<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">The lady must have been exeedingly rich if her tithes, given to a Cohen, amounted to 300 <i>kor</i> or 9’000 <i>modii</i>.</i>. He answered him: May the words of the Torah be burned and not be delivered to women! After she had left, his students said to him, rabbi, this one you pushed away with a stick, what do you explain to us? <sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, this answer and the following one are attributed to "one of Rav or Levi ben Sissi.”</i> Rebbi Berekhiah, Rebbi Abba bar Cahana in the name of Rebbi Eliezer: Anybody against whom there were witnesses and warning was executed in court<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">According to rabbinic theory, nobody can be convicted by biblical standards unless (1) the crime was witnessed by two witnesses of sterling character and (2) criminal intent is proven by two witnesses who testify that the criminal was informed that his intended deed would subject him to prosecution and nevertheless he persisted in his criminal behavior.</i>. Anybody against whom there were witnesses but no warning was checked similar to a suspected wife<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">One has to assume that this procedure was part of the police powers granted to Moses in his position of king of the Israelites, and that the guilty persons died from the gold water.</i>. Anybody against whom there were neither witnesses nor warning died from a plague. Rav and Levi bar Sissi both say: One who sacrificed, burned, and poured out a libation was executed in court<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">Since these are acts of Temple worship, as idolatrous practices they are capital crimes (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 7:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.7.6.1">Mishnah <i>Sanhedrin</i> 7:6</a>).</i>. He who clapped his hands, danced, laughed, was checked similar to a suspected wife<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">These are sinful practices if in idolatrous intent but not criminally prosecutable.</i>. If he enjoyed himself silently, he died from a plague<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">Since this sin was known only to God, its punishment was by the Hand of Heaven.</i>.
Who is a foolish pious man? He saw a baby struggling in a river<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">Really, “making bubbles”, because it is drowning. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 21b" href="/Sotah.21b">Babli, 21b</a>, it is a woman drowning whom he cannot save because it would hurt his salvation if he looked at a woman.</i>. He said, after I take off my phylacteries<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">Since the phylacteries contain biblical verses, they are holy. He is too ignorant to realize that the commandment to save a life overrides all other commandments except those involving murder, incest and adultery, and idolatry.</i>, I shall save him. By the time he takes off his phylacteries, that one is dead. He saw a prime fig and said, I shall give it to the first person I shall meet. He saw a preliminarily married girl and ran after her<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is a commandment to keep one’s word, he brings himself into a situation in which he can be killed. The Mishnah quoted states that if one observes a person bent on committing a murder, a homosexual rape, or the rape of a preliminarily married girl (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:23-24" href="/Deuteronomy.22.23-24"><i>Deut</i>. 22:23–24</a>), one may kill the attempting murderer or rapist with impunity. If the girl is afraid of him when he presents the fig and runs away, religious law would force him not to run after the girl.</i>. That is what we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 8:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.8.7.1">Mishnah <i>Sanhedrin</i> 8:7</a>: These are the people one may save [from sinning] by killing them: “If somebody pursues another to kill him, or after a male (for homosexual rape), or after a preliminarily married girl [one may kill the perpetrator]; but one who pursues an animal (for sodomy), desecrates the Sabbath, or commits idolatry one may not save by killing him.” A bystander can kill a person bent on committing a capital crime only if thereby he saves another human being.</i>: “If somebody pursues another to kill him, after a male, or after a preliminarily married girl.”
“A sly wicked man.” Rebbi Zeriqan in the name of Rav Huna: That is one who chooses leniencies for himself and teaches restrictions to others<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 21b" href="/Sotah.21b">Babli 21b</a>, as one possibility of many.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">The same text <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 1:4:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.1.4.4"><i>Berakhot</i> Chapter 1, Notes 192–199</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 1:6:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.1.6.9"><i>Yebamot</i> Chapter 1, Notes 256–261</a>. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 6b" href="/Eruvin.6b">Babli <i>Erubin</i> 6b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 13b" href="/Eruvin.13b">13b</a>.</i> In this matter, it was stated: “About anybody who wants to take upon himself the stringencies both of the House of Shammai and the House of Hillel it was said: ‘The silly one walks in darkness’. The leniencies of both of them, he is called wicked. Only either following the words of the House of Shammai in their leniencies and stringencies, or following the words of the House of Hillel in their leniencies and stringencies.” That is, before there came the disembodied voice. But after the disembodied voice was heard, “practice follows the House of Hillel forever.” And any who transgresses the words of the House of Hillel is deserving of death. It was stated: There came the disembodied voice and said: “Both of them are the words of the Living God, but practice follows the House of Hillel forever.” Where was the disembodied voice heard? Rav Bebai in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: The disembodied voice was heard at Yabneh.
A predatory woman<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">The Babli has no parallel explanation since it interprets אשה פרושה to mean “a pious woman”.</i>. That is one who sits and makes fun of the words of the Torah: “She said, you shall come to me … and he slept with her that night.<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 30:16" href="/Genesis.30.16"><i>Gen</i>. 30:16</a>, Leah buying a night with Jacob from her sister Rachel. R. Abbahu’s explanation is slightly more elaborate in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bereishit Rabbah 72:4" href="/Bereishit_Rabbah.72.4"><i>Gen. rabba</i> 72(4</a>).</i>” Rebbi Abbahu said, one could think that this was the [divine] intention. He only knew that she was doing this only to produce more tribes.
“Hits by the predatory”. That is the one who counsels the orphans to hide support from the widow<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 21b" href="/Sotah.21b">Babli, 21b</a>, this is an opinion of R. Joḥanan.</i>. As the following: Rebbi Sabbatai’s widow was wasting the estate. The orphans came before Rebbi Eleazar. He said to them, what can one do for you; you are stupid<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">This is a corruption in the Leiden and Rome mss. (from their common <i>Vorlage</i>). The text is better in the Geniza ms: אָמַר לוֹן וּמַה נֵיעֲבַד לְכוֹן אַלוֻךְ אִימָּה שַׁטְייָ מִדְּנַפְקוֹן אָֽמרִין ל׳ כְּתוֹבֵיהּ עִמְדוּ עַד נֵימַר לְכוֹן מַה אָמַר. “He said to them, what can I do for you, your charge is a senile mother. When they left, his scribe told them: stay here until I shall explain to you what he meant.”<br>Occupational names of the measure <i>pĕ‘ôlāh</i> have been recognized by L. Ginzburg in the Yerushalmi: קְרוֹבָה the liturgical poet, דְּרוֹשָׁה the preacher, כְּתוֹבָה the scribe; all masculine. In the Geniza texts published by M. A. Friedman, Jewish Marriage in Palestine, Tel Aviv and New York 1981, one finds four כתובא times and כתובה three times.</i>. Some scribe came out and said to them: I shall tell you what he said: Behave as if you would sell<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>ketubah</i> was a lien on the deceased’s real estate. The crooked counsel was that the heirs should loudly declare their intention to sell the real estate, which would force the widow eventually into lengthy court proceedings to recover her lien. The idea was to force the widow into demanding to be paid her <i>ketubah</i>, which immediately would terminate her right to support but would allow the heirs to somewhat drag their feet before paying up. The honorable thing would have been to offer the widow to pay her the <i>ketubah</i> in a lump sum, in the hope that she would accept the offer. Since then the initiative would be the heirs’, the widow’s support would have to be paid until the money was delivered in full.</i> and she will demand her <i>ketubah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">Greek φερνή “dowry”.</i> and lose her support. In the evening she came before Rebbi Eleazar. He said, this one was hit by the predatory. It should come over me<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">An oath formula, “such and such shall come over me if I intended that”.</i> if I had intended that<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">He disclaimed authorship of the counsel his (?) scribe had given in his name. It is not reported whether he reversed the transaction.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">The same text <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 8:7:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.8.7.2"><i>Peah</i> 8:8, Notes 112–115</a>; it is referred to in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 21b" href="/Sotah.21b">Babli 21b</a>.</i> A student of Rebbi had 199 denar; Rebbi used to let him receive the tithe of the poor once every three years. His students cast an evil eye on him and completed for him. The next time, when he wanted to let him receive as usual, he said: My teacher, I have the measure. He said, this one was hit by a beast of prey. He gave a hint to his student who took him to a store, made him spend a carat, then he (Rebbi) let him receive as he was used to do.
They added to these<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">To the list of the destroyers of the world.</i> a fasting virgin<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 22a" href="/Sotah.22a">Babli (22a</a>): A praying virgin.</i>, a neighborly widow, and a child outgrowing his months<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 22a" href="/Sotah.22a">Babli (22a</a>), except the Munich ms.: “a premature baby”.</i>. A fasting virgin; she pretends to have lost her hymen by fasting<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">But in reality she slept around.</i>. A neighborly widow, by running around<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote">The Geniza ms. probably has another reading which, however, is not readable. The translation follows <i>Arukh</i>, s. v. חגל.</i> she spreads bad reputations. A child outgrowing his months: Rebbi Ḥilqiah in the name of Rebbi Simon. That is one who is more advanced in his studies than usual for his age and he denigrates those older than him. Rebbi Yose said, that is one nine-years-old who looks like a twelve-years-old. He sleeps with any of the incest prohibitions in the Torah and they are executed because of him but he is free from prosecution<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">The sex act of a minor less than 9 years old has no legal consequences. If the boy is 9 years and one day old, his sex act counts: if a married woman sleeps with him, she is criminally liable for adultery but he is not responsible since he is a minor. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 3:10:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.3.10.2"><i>Yebamot</i>, Chapter 3, Note 143</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Rebbi Simeon says, merit does not suspend in the case of the bitter water. If you say that merit suspends in the case of the curse water, you weaken the water in the eyes of all women who drink it and you smear the reputation of the pure women who drank because one would say that they are impure but that merit suspended for them.<br>Rebbi says, merit suspends in the case of the curse water, but she does not have children<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">Childbirth is a sign of purity, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:28" href="/Numbers.5.28"><i>Num</i>. 5:28</a>.</i>, does not get better, but degenerates and in the end she dies that death<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">Supposing that the (not biblical) symptoms described in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 3:4:1-13" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.3.4.1-13">Mishnah 4</a> indicate that she would die immediately after drinking.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Rebbi Simeon says, merit does not suspend in the case of the bitter water,” etc. “Rebbi says, merit suspends in the case of the curse water,” etc. Rav Hamnuna said, the degenerating woman is permitted to her house<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">In the Bable, 26a, this is a comment of Rav Huna on <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 4:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.4.3.1">Mishnah 4:4</a> that even a Cohen’s wife, who would be forbidden to her husband if she was raped, is permitted to her husband after she survived drinking the bitter water. The Babli adds the condition that she not exhibit the features described in the verse, a swollen belly without a pregnancy. That condition is missing in the Yerushalmi.</i>. This parallels what was stated: Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar says, even a pure woman who drank will in the end die in great pain because she brought herself into that great doubt<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">That two witnesses confirmed her being alone with another man. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sotah (Lieberman) 2:3" href="/Tosefta_Sotah_(Lieberman).2.3">Tosephta, 2:3</a>, this is a statement of R. Simeon ben Eleazar in the name of R. Meïr</i>.
“And if the woman was not impure but was pure,<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:28" href="/Numbers.5.28"><i>Num.</i> 5:28</a>.</i>” this refers to the one who is pure, not that there came witnesses declaring her impure<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote">“And if the woman was not impure but was pure, then she will be cleansed and bear children.” If she is not impure, it should be clear that she is pure. It is explained that if she was not impure, i. e., if there are no proofs available against her sufficient for a conviction in court, then if she is pure the water will cleanse her and make her fertile. But if her case could be adjudicated in a human court, the Temple should not be used and the water will be just drinking water (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 6a" href="/Sotah.6a">Babli 6a</a>).<br>The Geniza text makes this slightly more clear by quoting: “And if the woman was not impure but was pure, then she will be cleansed”</i>. “If the woman was not impure but was pure,” this refers to the one who is pure, not one whose merit suspended [punishment] for her<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote">The latter will not experience any change in her fertility status.</i>. This follows the one who said that merit suspends and it is not recognizable. But following him who said that merit suspends and it is recognizable<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote">There seems to be no reason for Rav Hamnuna’s position.</i>? Rebbi Isaac said, here we deal with one who drank and the water did not test her. It comes to tell you that the water will not test a woman who is forbidden to her house<sup class="footnote-marker">161,</sup><i class="footnote">“And if the woman was not impure but was pure, then she will be cleansed and bear children.” If she is not impure, it should be clear that she is pure. It is explained that if she was not impure, i. e., if there are no proofs available against her sufficient for a conviction in court, then if she is pure the water will cleanse her and make her fertile. But if her case could be adjudicated in a human court, the Temple should not be used and the water will be just drinking water (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 6a" href="/Sotah.6a">Babli 6a</a>).<br>The Geniza text makes this slightly more clear by quoting: “And if the woman was not impure but was pure, then she will be cleansed”</i><sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote">There is no inference possible on Rav Hamnuna's statement.</i>. Rebbi Yudan said, this follows the one who said that merit suspends and it is not recognizable. Why was it not recognized? Because merit suspended for her. “The man will be free of sin<sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:31" href="/Numbers.5.31"><i>Num.</i> 5:31</a>.</i>,” he should not worry that maybe merit suspended for her<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote">The husband who brought his wife to the Temple where the water had no influence on her is free to sleep with her and is told not to worry that she still might be forbidden to him.</i>. Should I think that she also should not worry? The verse said, “but that woman shall carry her sin<sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:31" href="/Numbers.5.31"><i>Num.</i> 5:31</a>.</i>”. [This interpretation] follows the one who said that merit suspends and it is not recognizable.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If her flour offering became impure before it was sanctified in a Temple vessel, it is treated like all other flour offerings and should be redeemed<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote">Its holiness is transferred to a sum of money which then is used to buy a replacement offering. The original offering then becomes profane (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Menachot 12:1" href="/Mishnah_Menachot.12.1">Mishnah <i>Menaḥot</i> 12:1</a>)..</i>. After it was sanctified in a Temple vessel, it is treated like all other flour offerings and should be burned<sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote">Outside of the holy precinct, on the burning place organized for invalid sacrifices.</i>. The flour offerings are burned<sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote">Even if they are pure and still in the palm-leaf basket.</i> in the following cases: One who says, I am impure for you, or that witnesses declared that she was impure; one who refuses to drink, or whose husband does not want to let her drink, or whose husband slept with her on the road. In addition, the flour offerings of women married to Cohanim are burned<sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote">Where it has to be burned is a matter of dispute in the Halakhah.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If her flour offering became impure before it was sanctified in a Temple vessel,” etc. It was stated: If her flour offering became impure before it was sanctified in a Temple vessel, it is treated like all other flour offerings and should be redeemed; after it was sanctified in a Temple vessel, it is treated like all other flour offerings and should be burned<sup class="footnote-marker">171</sup><i class="footnote">This statement is that of the Mishnah (the one missing word, ואם, is also missing in most mss. of the Babli and of the Maimonides tradition of the Mishnah). The quote seems to be in opposition to statements similar to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sotah (Lieberman) 2:4" href="/Tosefta_Sotah_(Lieberman).2.4">Tosephta 2:4</a>: “After it was sanctified in a Temple vessel, one has to wait until it spoils and then one takes it out to be burned.”</i>.
“One who says, I am impure” Is it not like a purification offering whose owners died? And for a purification offering whose owners died, the money should be thrown into the Dead Sea<sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote">From the Rome ms. Word missing in the Leiden ms. by an oversight.</i>. It is compared to a hung reparation offering<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote">The sacrifice of a person who worries that possibly he could have sinned, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:17-19" href="/Leviticus.5.17-19"><i>Lev.</i> 5:17–19</a>. Similarly, the offering of the suspected wife is brought for a case of doubt.</i>. If it is compared to a hung reparation offering, even if it was sanctified in a Temple vessel<sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote">This is compared to the reparation offering introduced into the Temple courtyard.</i>! Rebbi Mattaniah said, it is compared to a hung reparation offering that was slaughtered, as we stated there: “If it became clear to him after it was slaughtered, the blood has to be poured out and the meat has to be taken out to the burning place.<sup class="footnote-marker">175</sup><i class="footnote">It became clear either that an inadvertent sin was committed, and then a purification offering is required, not a reparation offering, or that no sin was committed, and then no offering is required.
