In the latter case, since the offering was brought for peace of mind, it certainly was the intention of the person to have a valid sacrifice even in case he did not sin. This differs from the case of a sacrifice brought in error which is treated according to the rules of profane animals introduced in error into the Temple; Mishnah <i>Keritut</i> 6:1–2. (In the Mishnah, there are dissenting opinions that the hung reparation offering is a valid offering in all cases.)<br>R. Mattaniah compares transferring the offering into the Temple vessel to slaughtering the animal since the transfer enables the priest to offer a fistful on the alter just as slaughter enables the priest to bring the blood to the altar.</i>”
It was stated: If her offering became impure before the fistful was sacrificed<sup class="footnote-marker">177</sup><i class="footnote">It is unclear what this sentence implies here. It is obvious that an impure sacrifice is forbidden as sacrifice and as food. In the Babli (note 178), the entire text of the Mishnah is quoted. Therefore, it seems that the sentence is simply a quote from the Mishnah; the note “etc.” is missing.</i>; <sup class="footnote-marker">178</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sotah (Lieberman) 2:5" href="/Tosefta_Sotah_(Lieberman).2.5">Tosephta 2:5</a> and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 6b" href="/Sotah.6b">Babli 6b</a>. There, the text reads: “If her husband or she died before the fistful was sacrificed, the rest is forbidden. But if the fistful was sacrificed and then her husband or she died, the rest is permitted because it was brought from the start to resolve a doubt. Her doubt was atoned for and went away.” For most flour offerings, a fistful is offered on the altar, the rest is eaten by the Cohanim under the rules of most holy food.</i> if she died or her husband died, the rest is forbidden because it was brought from the start to resolve a doubt. Her doubt was atoned for and went away<sup class="footnote-marker">179</sup><i class="footnote">This clause makes more sense in the Tosephta/Babli text, that the rest is permitted to the Cohanim if it became permitted by the rules, i. e., that the fistful was duly put into the fire on top of the altar. It is reasonable that a permitted remainder cannot retroactively become forbidden since the doubt of adultery either was resolved by the procedure or became moot by the death of one of the parties.</i>. If there came witnesses declaring that she was impure<sup class="footnote-marker">180</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sotah (Lieberman) 2:5" href="/Tosefta_Sotah_(Lieberman).2.5">Tosephta 2:5</a>. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 3:5:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.3.5.3">Note 161</a>; then there no longer is any doubt; the procedure becomes inactive and the offering should not be brought.</i>, in any case her offering is forbidden. What means “in any case”? Whether the fistful was taken or not, whether it was sacrificed or not. Rebbi Hila said, whether the fistful was taken or not as long as it was not sacrificed, but if it was sacrificed the rest is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">181</sup><i class="footnote">Once it was food for the Cohanim it cannot lose that status except by impurity.</i>.
We have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">182</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Menachot 6:1" href="/Mishnah_Menachot.6.1">Mishnah <i>Menaḥot</i> 6:1</a>. In that Mishnah, the anonymous rabbis hold that the entire offering is burned on the altar in one piece.</i>: “Rebbi Simeon says, a fistful is taken from the sinner’s flour offering<sup class="footnote-marker">183</sup><i class="footnote">The flour offering of the very poor person who either refused to testify, was unmindful of his impurity in dealing with the Temple and its appurtenances, or had forgotten an oath he had imposed on himself, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:11-13" href="/Leviticus.5.11-13"><i>Lev.</i>5:11–13</a>.</i> of a Cohen. The fistful is sacrificed separately and the rest is sacrificed separately.” Both of them explained the same verse: “It shall be the Cohen’s as a flour offering<sup class="footnote-marker">184</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:13" href="/Leviticus.5.13"><i>Lev.</i> 5:13</a>. Since one speaks of a flour offering, it is diffult to understand why “it should be like a flour offering”.</i>.” The rabbis say, it is like his voluntary flour offering. Since his voluntary flour offering is brought entire, that one also is brought entire<sup class="footnote-marker">185</sup><i class="footnote">Voluntary flour offerings are described in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 2:1-11" href="/Leviticus.2.1-11"><i>Lev.</i> 2:1–11</a>. It is stipulated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:16" href="/Leviticus.6.16"><i>Lev.</i> 6:16</a> that no part of a priest’s flour offering may be eaten. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Tzav, Chapter 8 5" href="/Sifra,_Tzav,_Chapter_8.5"><i>Sifra Ṣaw Pereq</i> 8(5</a>) disagrees with the Yerushalmi; it interprets <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:16" href="/Leviticus.6.16"><i>Lev.</i> 6:16</a> to deal mainly with the Cohen’s obligatory offerings and only in a derivative fashion with voluntary offerings.</i>. Rebbi Simeon says, the tenth of an <i>epha</i><sup class="footnote-marker">186</sup><i class="footnote">The amount of flour required for the purification sacrifice, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:11" href="/Leviticus.5.11"><i>Lev.</i> 5:11</a>. An <i>epha</i> was 3 <i>seah.</i></i> of a Cohen is like the tenth of an <i>epha</i> of an Israel. Since a fistful is taken from the tenth of an <i>epha</i> of an Israel, so a fistful is taken from the tenth of an <i>epha</i> of a Cohen. Maybe, since this one is eaten, the other is also eaten<sup class="footnote-marker">187</sup><i class="footnote">This shows that the offering of a Cohen cannot simply be compared to that of an Israel since the result would contradict biblical precepts.</i>? The verse<sup class="footnote-marker">188</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:16" href="/Leviticus.6.16"><i>Lev.</i> 6:16</a>.</i> says, “Any flour offering of a Cohen shall be totally burned; it shall not be eaten.” The rest, under which category is it brought, under the rules of a fistful or under the rules of remainders<sup class="footnote-marker">189</sup><i class="footnote">This question is about the opinion of R. Simeon. For the rabbis, the offering is brought to the altar in one piece and burned as a sacrifice. But for R. Simeon, since the fistful is brought to the altar as a sacrifice, it makes sense to inquire whether the rest is burned under the same rules or not. If the same rules were to apply, it is difficult to see why there should be two distinct offerings.</i>? If you want to say, under the rules of a fistful, one cannot bring them during the night, one cannot bring them after death, and he is forbidden to think about them<sup class="footnote-marker">190</sup><i class="footnote">Sacrifices can be offered in the Temple only between the morning and evening daily sacrifices. Remainders of sacrifices for which blood and fat were offered during daytime can be brought to the altar during the night.<br>A sacrifice can be brought only during one’s lifetime.<br>In talmudic theory (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Zevachim 2:2" href="/Mishnah_Zevachim.2.2">Mishnah <i>Zebaḥim</i> 2:2</a>), a sacrifice is either valid or invalid from the start. Therefore, the biblical prohibitions of פִּגּוּל and נוֹתָר (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:5-7" href="/Leviticus.19.5-7"><i>Lev.</i> 19:5–7</a>) are interpreted to mean that the sacrifice becomes permanently prohibited if any of the prescribed actions in the Temple were executed with the idea that the meat should be eaten out of its allotted time or place. This means that the Cohen, by thinking to eat from the rest of the offering the next day or outside the Temple courtyard while dealing with the fistful taken for the altar, will invalidate the offering. This danger is restricted to the fistful, whose correct treatment will permit the rest to be eaten by the Cohanim. What these think while eating the rest is irrelevant; the only actions which are invalidated by wrong thoughts are those on which something else depends, either that part of the sacrifice becomes permitted as food, or that people are purified or otherwise enabled by it.</i>. If you want to say, under the rules of a remainder, one can bring them during the night, one can bring them after death. Is he forbidden to think about them? Let us hear from the following: Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Simeon<sup class="footnote-marker">191</sup><i class="footnote">While both mss. read here “R. Simeon ben Eleazar”, the continuation of the paragraph shows that the author must be R. Eleazar ben R. Simeon. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 23" href="/Sotah.23">Babli, 23a/b</a>, and the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sotah (Lieberman) 2:6" href="/Tosefta_Sotah_(Lieberman).2.6">Tosephta, 2:6</a>, read: Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Simeon says, the fistful is sacrificed separately and the rest is dispersed.</i> says, the fistful is sacrificed separately and the rest dispersed over the ashes. Rebbi Joḥanan asked, where are we holding? If the upper ashes<sup class="footnote-marker">192</sup><i class="footnote">The ashes on the top of the altar are hot and spreading the offering out means burning it on the altar. If that were the meaning, R. Eleazar’s position is that of his father and does not have to be mentioned.</i>, Rebbi Simeon already said it. If it cannot refer to the upper ashes, let it refer to the lower ashes<sup class="footnote-marker">193</sup><i class="footnote">The ashes removed from the altar to the floor of the courtyard (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:2" href="/Leviticus.6.2"><i>Lev.</i>6:2</a>).</i>. That means, one can bring them during the night, one can bring them after death, and he can think about them<sup class="footnote-marker">194</sup><i class="footnote">Anything not destined for the altar cannot permit anything else. Therefore, any wrong intention the Cohen may have while depositing the rest on the ashes is irrelevant; he may think what he wishes. Similarly, since the burning of the fistful permits the consumption (or dispersion) of the remainder by the Cohanim, if the owner of the offering dies after the burning of the fistful it cannot have any influence on the status of the rest.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, he is forbidden to think about them since they are not qualified as food either for humans or for the altar<sup class="footnote-marker">195</sup><i class="footnote">The argument of the previous Note is valid only for the offering of an Israel, for whom the fistful really permits the remainder to the Cohanim. But for the offering of a Cohen, the offering of the fistful according to R. Eleazar ben R. Simon does not permit anything, not even to bring the rest onto the altar. Therefore, the sacrificing of the fistful cannot lift the rules of פִּגּוּל and נוֹתָר for the Cohen’s offering.</i>. Rebbi Abba bar Mamal asked: Does Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Simeon follow the rules of his father or the rules of the rabbis? According to the rules of his father, it should be brought on top [of the altar]. According to the rules of the rabbis, why should he take a fistful<sup class="footnote-marker">196</sup><i class="footnote">They require that the entire offering be burned, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 3:6:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.3.6.5">Note 182</a>.</i>? He follows his father’s rules. Rebbi Simeon says, the tenth of an <i>epha</i> of a Cohen is like the tenth of an <i>epha</i> of an Israel. Since a fistful is taken from the tenth of an <i>epha</i> of an Israel, so a fistful is taken from the tenth of an <i>epha</i> of a Cohen. Maybe, since this one is eaten, the other is also eaten? The verse says, “Any flour offering of a Cohen shall be totally burned; it shall not be eaten.” Then it should be burned totally! You bound it to “it shall not be eaten”; you did not bind it to “it has to be sacrificed in its entirety.<sup class="footnote-marker">197</sup><i class="footnote">R. Eleazar ben R. Simeon accepts the comparison of the obligatory to the voluntary offering of a Cohen, called “binding (הֶקֵּשׁ) of one verse to the other”; it is only to modify the rule of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:16" href="/Leviticus.6.16"><i>Lev.</i> 6:16</a>, which deals with voluntary offerings, not that of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:15" href="/Leviticus.6.15"><i>Lev.</i>6:15</a>, which deals with an obligatory offering of another kind.</i>”
Rebbi Yose said, Rebbi accepted the opinion of Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Simeon and taught accordingly<sup class="footnote-marker">198</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that the last sentence of the Mishnah can be explained only following R. Eleazar ben R. Simeon.</i>. Rebbi Abba bar Cohen asked before Rebbi Yose: Why Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Simeon? Even following his father, Rebbi Simeon! He said to him, “eating” is on the altar, “burning” is not on the altar<sup class="footnote-marker">199</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbinic usage follows biblical usage in this case. The burning of sacrifices on the altar is never called “burning”, but “eating (by the fire)”, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:3" href="/Leviticus.6.3"><i>Lev.</i> 6:3</a>. On the other hand, the burning outside the Temple is always called “burning”, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:12" href="/Leviticus.4.12"><i>Lev.</i> 4:12</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:21" href="/Leviticus.4.21">21</a>; 16:27. If the Mishnah uses the expression “burning”, it implies that this cannot be on the altar.</i>. Rebbi Ḥanina objected before Rebbi Mana: Did not Rebbi Ḥiyya disagree: “It cannot be burned completely because of the wife’s part<sup class="footnote-marker">200</sup><i class="footnote">The offering for a Cohen’s suspected wife. The first clause is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sotah (Lieberman) 2:6" href="/Tosefta_Sotah_(Lieberman).2.6">Tosephta, 2:6</a>. For the intelligible full text of this discussion, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 2:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.2.1.3">Chapter 2:1, Note 7</a> ff.</i>; it cannot be eaten because of the husband’s part.” He said to him, Rebbi accepted the opinion of Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Simeon, the elder Rebbi Ḥiyya accepted the opinion of the latter’s father Rebbi Simeon.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The offering of an Israel’s daughter married to a Cohen is burned<sup class="footnote-marker">201</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is the husband’s money; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 2:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.2.1.3">Chapter 2:1, Note 7</a>.</i>, the offering of a Cohen’s daughter married to an Israel is not burned<sup class="footnote-marker">202</sup><i class="footnote">The woman becomes a member of her husband’s clan.</i>. What is the difference between a Cohen and a Cohen’s daughter? The flour offering of a Cohen’s daughter is eaten, that of a Cohen is not eaten. A Cohen’s daughter is desecrated, a Cohen is not desecrated<sup class="footnote-marker">203</sup><i class="footnote">This is explained in the Halakhah. An illicit marriage bars the wife and her children from ever marrying into the priesthood; a Cohen who divorces his forbidden wife returns to his priestly status.</i>. A Cohen’s daughter may defile herself for the dead, a Cohen may not defile himself for the dead<sup class="footnote-marker">204</sup><i class="footnote">The prohibition of the impurity of the dead is clearly addressed to <i>the sons of Aaron, Lev.</i> 21:1.</i>. A Cohen eats most holy [food] a Cohen’s daughter may not eat most holy [food]<sup class="footnote-marker">205</sup><i class="footnote">This is a clearly biblical precept; spelled out in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:11" href="/Leviticus.6.11"><i>Lev.</i> 6:11</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:22" href="/Leviticus.6.22">22</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 7:6:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.7.6.2-4">7:6</a>.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “The offering of an Israel’s daughter married to a Cohen,” etc. What is the difference between a Cohen and a Cohen’s daughter? “The flour offering of a Cohen’s daughter is eaten, that of a Cohen is not eaten.” For it is written, “any flour offering of a Cohen shall be total, it should not be eaten<sup class="footnote-marker">207</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:16" href="/Leviticus.6.16"><i>Lev.</i> 6:16</a>.</i>;” not the Cohen’s daugher’s. Rebbi Abbahu asked before Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: Is it not written: “If a Cohen acquire a person with his money<sup class="footnote-marker">208</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 22:11" href="/Leviticus.22.11">Lev. 22:11</a>. The verse states that slaves of a Cohen may eat of his sanctified food and we hold (cf. <i>Yebamot</i> 7:1) that the slaves of a Cohen’s daughter may eat if and only if she can eat. Should the mention of the masculine form “Cohen” not exclude the daughter of a Cohen. Accepted without discussion in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 23b" href="/Sotah.23b">Babli 23b</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Tzav, Chapter 8 4" href="/Sifra,_Tzav,_Chapter_8.4"><i>Sifra Ṣaw Pereq</i> 8(4</a>).</i>,” should that apply to a Cohen but not ro a Cohen’s daughter? How is that? “The Cohen anointed in his stead, one of his sons;<sup class="footnote-marker">209</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:15" href="/Leviticus.6.15"><i>Lev.</i> 6:15</a>. Verse 16 is an appendix to a paragraph speaking only of the (male) High Priest. The son of a Cohen’s daughter belongs to his father’s clan, not hers.</i>” one whose son fills his place, that excludes her whose son does not fill her place.
<sup class="footnote-marker">210</sup><i class="footnote">The entire paragraph is from <i>Sifra Emor Pereq</i> 2(7–8). For the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 23b" href="/Sotah.23b">Babli, 23b</a>, it seems obvious that a woman forbidden to a Cohen becomes desecrated; one only needs a verse to free the Cohen from desecration.</i>“‘A Cohen’s daughter is desecrated, a Cohen is not desecrated.’ For it is written: ‘He shall not desecrate his seed in his clan.<sup class="footnote-marker">211</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:15" href="/Leviticus.21.15"><i>Lev.</i> 21:15</a>, speaking of the High Priest who married a widow, divorcee, or desecrated woman. The children of such a forbidden union are desecrated in their clan and are permanently barred from any priestly function.</i>’ Not only that he desecrates his seed; from where [that he desecrates] herself<sup class="footnote-marker">212</sup><i class="footnote">A woman who ever slept with a man whom she could not lawfully marry is considered a whore prohibited to a Cohen. By marrying her, he desecrates her permanently.</i>? It is a conclusion of an argument. Since the seed who did not commit any sin is desecrated, she, who committed a sin<sup class="footnote-marker">213</sup><i class="footnote">While it is stated two times that a Cohen sins in marrying a woman forbidden to him (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:7" href="/Leviticus.21.7"><i>Lev.</i> 21:7</a>), it is never stated that the forbidden woman sins in letting herself be married by the Cohen. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Emor, Chapter 1 12" href="/Sifra,_Emor,_Chapter_1.12"><i>Sifra</i>[<i>Emor Pereq</i> 1(12</a>)] derives a warning to her from the double prohibition in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:7" href="/Leviticus.21.7"><i>Lev.</i> 21:7</a>.</i>, is it not logical that she should be desecrated? He himself gives a counter-argument since he sinned and is not desecrated. No. If you talk about the man who is not otherwise desecrated<sup class="footnote-marker">214</sup><i class="footnote">He is not permanently desecrated even if he sleeps with a slave girl, a Gentile, or a whore.</i>, what can you say about a woman who can be otherwise desecrated? Since she can be otherwise desecrated, it is an argument that she should be desecrated! If you wish<sup class="footnote-marker">215</sup><i class="footnote">If you consider the pseudological argument invalid.</i>, you can say, he shall not desecrate - “he shall not repeatedly desecrate,’<sup class="footnote-marker">216</sup><i class="footnote">The root חול, חלל “to desecrate” appears in the Pentateuch also in Hiph‘il. The use of the intensive instead of the causative in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:15" href="/Leviticus.21.15"><i>Lev.</i> 21:15</a> is taken to imply more than one desecration. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 77a" href="/Kiddushin.77a">Babli, <i>Qiddušin</i> 77a</a>, derives different disabilities from the same argument.</i>” even to desecrate one who is enabled.
“A Cohen’s daughter may defile herself for the dead.” Rebbi Dosa from Malḥiyya, Rebbi Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: A Cohen’s daughter is permitted to leave the Land<sup class="footnote-marker">217</sup><i class="footnote">A Cohen is prohibited from defiling himself in the impurity of the dead, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:1-4" href="/Leviticus.21.1-4"><i>Lev.</i> 21:1–4</a>. By rabbinic decree, a Cohen is forbidden to leave the Land of Israel since the other countries, even disregarding the biblical impurity inherent in them, might be full of unrecognized graves so that the Cohen would automatically incur at least the doubt of contamination by a grave.</i>. What is the reason? “Say to the Cohanim<sup class="footnote-marker">218</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:2" href="/Leviticus.21.2"><i>Lev.</i> 21:2</a>: “Say to the Cohanim, the <i>sons</i> of Aaron.” The prohibitions in this paragraph inherently concern only the males of the family. This is explicit in <i>Sifra Emor, Introduction;</i> alluded to in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 23b" href="/Sotah.23b">Babli, 23b</a>.</i>.” Not to the daughters of Cohanim. If it were not so, what would he say? Because she is included in the decree, she cannot leave. Why should she not be included in the decree and be forbidden to leave<sup class="footnote-marker">219</sup><i class="footnote">Since the prohibition is rabbinic anyhow, how can the extent of the prohibition be measured by biblical standards? It is unlikely that the prohibition was known before the war of Bar Kokhba. We find king Yannai outside the Land, but he was a Sadducee and opposed to rabbinic prohibitions. But it is reported that R. Eleazar ben Azariah, a Cohen, descendant of Ezra in the tenth generation, went to Rome.</i>? If you say so, you push aside the paragraph on impurities<sup class="footnote-marker">220</sup><i class="footnote">Since rabbinic decrees are גדירה, fences around the Law, they should not prohibit what is explicitly permitted in the Pentateuch.</i>.
“A Cohen eats most holy [food], a Cohen’s daughter may not eat most holy [food].” For it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:6" href="/Leviticus.7.6"><i>Lev.</i> 7:6</a>): “Every male among the Cohanim shall eat it.”

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> What is the difference between a man and a woman? A man is dishevelled and with open seams<sup class="footnote-marker">221</sup><i class="footnote">A man afflicted with skin disease must have dishevelled hair and wear clothes open at the seams (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:45" href="/Leviticus.13.45"><i>Lev.</i>13:45</a>). It is written in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:44" href="/Leviticus.13.44">v. 44</a>: He is a <i>man</i> with skin disease.</i>, no woman is dishevelled and with open seams. A man can make his son a <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">222</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 4:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.4.6.1">Mishnah <i>Nazir</i> 4:6</a>. The <i>nazir</i> is forbidden all grape products, may not cut any hair, and may not defile himself in the impurity of the dead. A father can decree that his underage son be a <i>nazir</i> as long as neither the child nor the relatives object. He then is responsible for the cost of all sacrifices due at the end of the <i>nezirut</i> period.</i><i>,</i> no woman can make her son a <i>nazir.</i> A man can shave using his father’s vow of <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">223</sup><i class="footnote">“Shaving” stands here for the entire ceremony which ends the <i>nezirut</i> period, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:13-21" href="/Numbers.6.13-21"><i>Num.</i> 6:13–21</a>. If both father and adult son were <i>nezirim</i>, the father had already bought the required sacrifices (one male and one female sheep, and one ram) when he died before using them, the father’s dedication is validly transferred to the son.</i><i>,</i> no woman can shave using her father’s vow of <i>nazir.</i> A man can betrothe his daughter<sup class="footnote-marker">224</sup><i class="footnote">If a man marries off his underage daughter, the marriage is valid by biblical standards. This is derived from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:16" href="/Deuteronomy.22.16"><i>Deut.</i> 22:16</a>, where the father declares: I gave my daughter to this man as a wife. After the father’s death, the widow may only marry off her underage daughter by rabbinic standards. A fully adult daughter must find her own husband.</i>, no woman can betrothe her daughter. A man can sell his daughter<sup class="footnote-marker">225</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:7" href="/Exodus.21.7"><i>Ex.</i> 21:7</a>: “If a <i>man</i> sell his daughter as a slave”. In rabbinic theory, the institution of Hebrew slavery was bound to that of the Jubilee year. The latter is predicated on the clan holdings of land received under Joshua. Therefore, the Jubilee year should have been disestablished with the first Assyrian deportations of the Ten Tribes.</i>, no woman can sell her daughter. A man is stoned naked<sup class="footnote-marker">226</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Sanhedrin</i> 6:3–4.</i>, no woman is stoned naked. A man is hanged<sup class="footnote-marker">227</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 21:22" href="/Deuteronomy.21.22"><i>Deut.</i> 21:22</a>: “If a <i>man</i> was guilty of a capital crime and executed, you should hang <i>him</i> on a wooden pole.” This refers explicitly to a man.</i>, no woman is hanged. A man is sold for his theft, no woman is sold for her theft.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “What is the difference between a man and a woman? A man is dishevelled and with open seams,” etc. “A man<sup class="footnote-marker">228</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:44" href="/Leviticus.13.44"><i>Lev.</i>13:44</a>: “A <i>man</i> afflicted with skin disease is he, impure is he, the Cohen shall certainly declare him impure if his disease is on his head.”<br>A slightly more complete text in <i>Sifra Tazria‘ Pereq</i>12(1); a shortened text in Babli 23 a, <i>Keritut</i>8b, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Arakhin 3a" href="/Arakhin.3a"><i>Arakhin</i>3a</a>.</i>”. This refers not only to a man; from where for a woman? The verse says, “afflicted with skin disease,” whether man, or woman, or minor. If it is so, why is “a man” written? For the next theme<sup class="footnote-marker">221</sup><i class="footnote">A man afflicted with skin disease must have dishevelled hair and wear clothes open at the seams (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:45" href="/Leviticus.13.45"><i>Lev.</i>13:45</a>). It is written in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:44" href="/Leviticus.13.44">v. 44</a>: He is a <i>man</i> with skin disease.</i>, “a man is dishevelled and with open seams, no woman is dishevelled and with open seams.”
“A man can make his son a <i>nazir</i>,” and “a man can shave”. Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Meїr<sup class="footnote-marker">229</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 23" href="/Sotah.23">Babli (<i>Soṭa</i> 23 b</a>; <i>Nazir</i> 25 a/b, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 28b" href="/Sotah.28b">28b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 30a" href="/Sotah.30a">30a</a>, 61b) and dependent sources [<a class="refLink" data-ref="Bamidbar Rabbah 10:20" href="/Bamidbar_Rabbah.10.20"><i>Num. rabba</i> 10 (20</a>] disagree and quote R. Joḥanan insisting that according to the biblical text, nobody can impose a state of <i>nazir</i> on another person but that it is traditional practice (in the words of the Midrash: “going back to Moses on Mount Sinai”) that a father may force his son to be a <i>nazir</i>, implying that nobody can dissent.<br>The same paragraph is found in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 4:6:2-8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.4.6.2-8"><i>Nazir</i> 4:6,</a> fol. 53c.</i>: In twenty-four matters are the House of Shammai lenient while the House of Hillel are restrictive and that is one of them: The House of Shammai say, a man cannot make his son a <i>nazir</i>, but the House of Hillel say, a man can make his son a <i>nazir</i>.
“A man can sell his daughter,” for it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">230</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:7" href="/Exodus.21.7"><i>Ex</i>. 21:7</a>.</i>: “If a man sell his daughter as a handmaid. “A man can betrothe his daughter,” as it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">231</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:16" href="/Deuteronomy.22.16"><i>Deut.</i> 22:16</a>.</i>: “I gave my daughter as a wife but he hated her.”
“A man is stoned naked, no woman is stoned naked.” For it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">232</sup><i class="footnote">There is no such verse. Probably the reference is to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 24:14" href="/Leviticus.24.14"><i>Lev.</i> 24:14</a>: וְרָֽגְמוּ אֹתוֹ which means “they shall stone him;” this verse is quoted in <i>Sifry zuṭa Šelaḥ</i> 36, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 43a" href="/Sanhedrin.43a">Babli <i>Sanhedrin</i> 43a</a>.</i>, “you shall stone him,” not his garment. Rebbi Ḥaggai asked before Rebbi Yose: Is it not written<sup class="footnote-marker">233</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:24" href="/Deuteronomy.22.24"><i>Deut.</i> 22:24</a>, speaking of the inhabitants of a city whose inhabitants, men and women, turn to idolatry. If the prior argument were correct, the verse would disprove the Mishnah.</i>: “You shall stone them with stones,” does that mean “them but not their garments”? How is that? A man is not greatly degraded by this, so he is stoned naked, but a woman who would be greatly degraded by it cannot be stoned naked<sup class="footnote-marker">234</sup><i class="footnote">There is no biblical source for the statement of the Mishnah. Since a garment would soften the blows of the stones, the agony of the man to be executed would be prolonged if he were clothed; since he probably would not mind being seen naked, it is to his advantage being executed while naked. But for a woman, the mental anguish of being naked in public would be much greater than the increased physical pain; therefore a woman has to be executed fully clothed.<br>The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 23b" href="/Sotah.23b">Babli <i>Soṭa</i> 23b</a> refers to the argument in a very abbreviated way which presupposes the knowledge of the Yerushalmi’s argument. The original argument of the Yerushalmi is accepted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 43a" href="/Sanhedrin.43a">Babli <i>Sanhedrin</i> 43a</a>; in 45a the inference is attributed to R. Jehudah who requires that a woman be stoned naked. By contrast, in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Kedoshim" href="/Sifra,_Kedoshim"><i>Sifra Qedošim Parašah</i> 10(4</a>), an appropriate verse is quoted, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 20:2" href="/Leviticus.20.2"><i>Lev.</i> 20:2</a>: “Every <i>man</i> in Israel or among the sojourners in Israel who gives of his children to the Moloch shall certainly die, the people of the land shall stone <i>him</i> with stone.”</i>.
“A man is hanged<sup class="footnote-marker">227</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 21:22" href="/Deuteronomy.21.22"><i>Deut.</i> 21:22</a>: “If a <i>man</i> was guilty of a capital crime and executed, you should hang <i>him</i> on a wooden pole.” This refers explicitly to a man.</i>, no woman is hanged.” Because it is written, “one shall hang <i>him</i>”, not her.
“A man is sold for his theft,” for his theft but not for the double restitution<sup class="footnote-marker">228</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:44" href="/Leviticus.13.44"><i>Lev.</i>13:44</a>: “A <i>man</i> afflicted with skin disease is he, impure is he, the Cohen shall certainly declare him impure if his disease is on his head.”<br>A slightly more complete text in <i>Sifra Tazria‘ Pereq</i>12(1); a shortened text in Babli 23 a, <i>Keritut</i>8b, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Arakhin 3a" href="/Arakhin.3a"><i>Arakhin</i>3a</a>.</i>. For his theft but not for his perjury<sup class="footnote-marker">229</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 23" href="/Sotah.23">Babli (<i>Soṭa</i> 23 b</a>; <i>Nazir</i> 25 a/b, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 28b" href="/Sotah.28b">28b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 30a" href="/Sotah.30a">30a</a>, 61b) and dependent sources [<a class="refLink" data-ref="Bamidbar Rabbah 10:20" href="/Bamidbar_Rabbah.10.20"><i>Num. rabba</i> 10 (20</a>] disagree and quote R. Joḥanan insisting that according to the biblical text, nobody can impose a state of <i>nazir</i> on another person but that it is traditional practice (in the words of the Midrash: “going back to Moses on Mount Sinai”) that a father may force his son to be a <i>nazir</i>, implying that nobody can dissent.<br>The same paragraph is found in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 4:6:2-8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.4.6.2-8"><i>Nazir</i> 4:6,</a> fol. 53c.</i>. For his theft he is not sold twice. There is only a monetary claim on him. That means, for one theft, but for two thefts he can be sold a second time<sup class="footnote-marker">230</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:7" href="/Exodus.21.7"><i>Ex</i>. 21:7</a>.</i>. Rebbi Jeremiah asked: If he stole from a partnership, how are you treating this? As one theft or as two thefts<sup class="footnote-marker">231</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:16" href="/Deuteronomy.22.16"><i>Deut.</i> 22:16</a>.</i>? If he stole and removed [things] in the night, we would say that if the owners realized [the loss] in the meantime, there are two thefts; otherwise, it is one theft.

Chapter 4



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A preliminarily married woman<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. D<i>emai</i> 4, Note 19.</i> or one who waits for her brother-in-law<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Her husband died childless; she waits to be married by her brother-in-law. In the meantime, the brother-in-law declared his jealousy and brought witnesses that she had an illicit rendezvous. While a woman waiting for her brother-in-law is unable to marry outside the family without <i>ḥalîṣah</i>, her infidelity in her widowhood is not criminal adultery (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 2:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.2.1.1"><i>Yebamot</i>, Chapter 2, Note 6</a>). Nevertheless, if a brother-in-law “bespoke” her, she is as if preliminarily married to him and if she violates his demand that she not be with a suspected paramour, he cannot marry her unless she is cleared by the <i>Soṭah</i> ritual. Since this is impossible and she has brought the situation on herself by her action, she has to receive <i>ḥalîṣah</i>, be divorced, and cannot claim her <i>ketubah</i> money.</i> neither drinks nor collects her <i>ketubah</i>, since it is said<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:29" href="/Numbers.5.29"><i>Num.</i> 5:29</a>. “Under her husband” is only a wife living with her husband. In <i>Sifry Num.</i> 20 (differently in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 24a" href="/Sotah.24a">Babli 24a</a>, attributed to the same authors) there is a discussion on which woman is considered living with her husband.</i>: “.. who will deviate from under her husband,” which excludes a preliminarily married woman and one who waits for her brother-in-law.<br>A widow [married to] a High Priest<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:14" href="/Leviticus.21.14"><i>Lev.</i> 21:14</a>.</i>, a divorcee<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:7" href="/Leviticus.21.7"><i>Lev.</i> 21:7</a>.</i> or one who received <i>ḥalîṣah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">She is a divorcee by rabbinic standards; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 2:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.2.4.1">Mishnah <i>Yebamot</i> 2:4</a>.</i> to a common priest, a female bastard<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 1:5:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.1.5.2"><i>Yebamot</i> Chapter 1, Note 176</a>.</i> or a Gibeoness<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 2:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.2.4.1"><i>Yebamot</i> Chapter 2, Note 72</a>.</i> to an Israel, or an Israel woman married to a bastard or a Gibeonite, neither drinks nor collects her <i>ketubah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">The Halakhah leaves open the possibility that this latter rule may be a rabbinic decree.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “A preliminarily married woman or one who waits for her brother-in-law,” etc. Why can he not make her drink? It is a decision of the verse: “The man shall bring his wife to the Cohen<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:15" href="/Leviticus.5.15"><i>Lev.</i> 5:15</a>. A man can bring his wife only if he is living with her.</i>.” Then he should not be able to declare his jealousy<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">If “his wife” in this paragraph means only the definitively married one, the entire procedure should be impossible for the preliminarily married woman.</i>! The Torah said, “and he declared his jealousy to his wife,” “and he declared his jealousy to his wife,<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:14" href="/Leviticus.5.14"><i>Lev.</i> 5:14</a>; if the meaning of “his wife” were constant in this verse, the second clause should have read “and he declared his jealousy to her.” The woman who is a wife but not a wife in the first sense is the preliminarily married one. Therefore, the second mention of “wife” adds the preliminarily married one as object of jealousy.</i>” even if she is only partially his wife.
Does our Mishnah follow the House of Shammai, since the House of Shammai say, “she takes her <i>ketubah</i> but does not drink”<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 4:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.4.2.1">Mishnah 4:3</a>. If the husband died after she was in a secluded place with her paramour but before she could drink.</i>? Rebbi Yose said, there the reason of the House of Shammai is that she may say, bring me my husband, then I shall drink<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Since the verse requires the husband to bring his wife to the Temple, she can claim that it is not her fault that she could not cleanse herself.</i>. But here, she knew that the preliminarily married cannot drink<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">It is clear that the House of Shammai also hold that women have to study all the rules (and texts) of the oral law; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 3:4:11" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.3.4.11"><i>Berakhot</i> Chapter 3, Note 181</a>. If R. Eliezer in Halakhah 3:4 excludes women from the study of Torah, he can mean only the study of the Pentateuch, not that of the oral tradition.</i>; why did she bring herself into that great doubt? In order to disqualify herself from receiving her <i>ketubah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">For the preliminarily married woman, this refers only to pre-talmudic times, when the <i>ketubah</i> was given at the time of <i>qiddušin.</i> But later practice was to deliver the document only at the time of the actual marriage (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 5:1:2-6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.5.1.2-6"><i>Ketubot</i>5:1</a>).</i>.
Rebbi Jehudah asked: As you say, “and he declared his jealousy to his wife,” “and he declared his jealousy to his wife,<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:14" href="/Leviticus.5.14"><i>Lev.</i> 5:14</a>; if the meaning of “his wife” were constant in this verse, the second clause should have read “and he declared his jealousy to her.” The woman who is a wife but not a wife in the first sense is the preliminarily married one. Therefore, the second mention of “wife” adds the preliminarily married one as object of jealousy.</i>” even if she is only partially his wife, similarly, “under your husband,” even if he is only partially your husband<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">R. Jehudah notes that “under your husband” is repeated (vv. 19,20). Therefore, if the repetition of “his wife” (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 4:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.4.1.4">Note 12</a>) also includes a partial wife ( preliminarily married or one who waits for her brother-in-law), the repetition here should also give the partial husband the right to bring his partial wife to the Temple. There is no answer given; R. Jehudah’s argument is contradicted by the verse he himself quotes, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 1:3:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.1.3.3">Chapter 1, Notes 154–156</a>.</i>. How is that? If he declared his jealousy to her while she was preliminarily married, then he wed her<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">“He took her in”, i. e., he brought her to his house in the final wedding ceremony to live with him.</i>, and she went to a secluded place, he makes her drink on the basis of his declaration of jealousy<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">The final wedding activates the prior declaration of jealousy. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 25a" href="/Sotah.25a">Babli agrees, 25a</a>.</i>. If he<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">The brother-in-law.</i> declared his jealousy to her while she was waiting for her brother-in-law, then he<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">The brother-in-law.</i> wed her, and she went to a secluded place, he makes her drink on the basis of his declaration of jealousy<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">The final wedding activates the prior declaration of jealousy. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 25a" href="/Sotah.25a">Babli agrees, 25a</a>.</i>. If he declared his jealousy to her while she was preliminarily married, then he took her in, she went to a secluded place, and after that he had sex with her, she has to leave with her <i>ketubah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 3:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.3.5.1">Mishnah 3:6</a>, the ceremony is ineffective for a woman whose husband had slept with her while she was forbidden to him. Since it is his action that (a) keeps his wife permanently forbidden to him and, (b) prevents his wife from proving her innocence, he as the guilty party is forced to divorce her and to pay the full amount of the <i>ketubah.</i></i>; otherwise<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">If the husband did not sleep with his wife after she became forbidden to him, he has the choice to divorce her because of her fault, without paying any of his own money.</i> she has to leave without <i>ketubah</i>. [If he declared his jealousy to her while she was waiting for her brother-in-law, then he took her in, she went to a secluded place, and after that he had sex with her, she has to leave with her <i>ketubah;</i> otherwise without <i>ketubah.</i>]<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">From the Rome ms., probably omitted by the scribe of the Leiden ms. because of the parallel text.</i> If her husband declared his jealousy and died, she became destined for her brother-in-law who married her, then she went to a secluded place, he makes her drink on the basis of [the first husband’s] declaration of jealousy<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Since by biblical standards the brother-in-law’s marriage is an automatic consequence of the first husband’s death.</i>. If her husband did not declare his jealousy and died, she became destined for her brother-in-law who declared his jealousy but did not manage to take her in before he died and she became destined for his brother, the latter cannot make her drink since she became destined for him only because of the first brother<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Since the second brother never became her husband, his declaration is irrelevant to the third brother.</i>. But if her brother-in-law declared his jealousy, took her in, and died, then she became destined for a brother-in-law who took her in, when she went to a secluded place he makes her drink on the basis of his<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">The brother-in-law.</i> declaration of jealousy.
There<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 4:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.4.3.1">Mishnah 4:4</a>; a fuller text in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sotah (Lieberman) 5:4" href="/Tosefta_Sotah_(Lieberman).5.4">Tosephta 5:4</a> and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 26a" href="/Sotah.26a">Babli 26a</a> as the minority opinion of R. Simeon ben Eleazar; as an alternative explanation in <i>Sifry Num.</i> 19.</i>, we have stated: “A she-ram<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">A woman lacking secondary female sex characteristics, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 1:1:25" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.1.1.25"><i>Yebamot</i>, Chapter 1, Note 65</a>.</i>, an old woman<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">A post-menopausal woman.</i>, or a sterile one neither drinks nor collects her <i>ketubah</i>,” as it is said<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:28" href="/Numbers.5.28"><i>Num.</i> 5:28</a>.</i>: “She will be declared innocent and bear seed,” [this refers to] one able to bear seed; it excludes one who is unable to bear seed. They objected, is there not the widow [married to] the High Priest, is she not able to bear seed<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Why is she excluded from drinking (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 4:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.4.1.1">Mishnah 2</a>)?</i>? There is a difference, since it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:15" href="/Leviticus.21.15"><i>Lev</i>. 21:15</a>.</i>: “He may not desecrate his seed among his people.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:15" href="/Leviticus.21.15"><i>Lev</i>. 21:15</a>.</i>” There<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Since any child of the widow will be desecrated, it is not counted as the High Priest’s child. Therefore, as far as he is concerned, his wife is unable to bear children.</i>, we have stated: “A bastard disables and enables to eat. How is this?” And here he says so? Rebbi Tanḥuma said, there, “she has no issue” of any kind, but here<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 7:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.7.7.1">Mishnah <i>Yebamot</i>7:7</a>. An Israel woman widowed from a Cohen may eat heave as long as <i>any</i> Jewish descendant of her husband’s is alive; a Cohen woman widowed from an Israel may not eat heave as long as <i>any</i> Jewish descendant of her husband’s is alive.</i> one requires enabled seed, not disabled seed. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, the water only serves to permit her to her house; but about this one one tells him to divorce once she went to a secluded place<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">He rejects the earlier attempt to find a biblical source to the rejection of the widow and explains <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:15" href="/Leviticus.21.15"><i>Lev.</i> 21:15</a> as: “He is forbidden to desecrate his seed.” The rule excluding the High Priest’s widow is rabbinical. If the Temple court refuses to administer the water, the widow remains permanently forbidden to her husband, who therefore is forced to divorce her.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The following cannot drink or collect their <i>ketubah:</i> One who says, I am impure for you, or one for whom came witnesses declaring her to be impure<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">They are divorced for adultery; in this case no <i>ketubah</i> money is due.</i>; one who says “I will not drink”<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">She accepts the monetary penalty for adultery without admitting guilt.</i>. But one whose husband refuses to make her drink, or whose husband copulated with her on the road, collects her <i>ketubah</i> but does not drink<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">In both cases the husband prevented her from clearing her name; he has to divorce her but must pay. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 4:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.4.1.1">Note 2</a>.</i>. About those whose husbands died before she would drink, the House of Shammai say, they collect the <i>ketubah</i> but do not drink<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Since the verse requires the husband to bring his wife to the Temple, she can claim that it is not her fault that she could not cleanse herself.</i>, but the House of Hillel say, they neither drink not collect the <i>ketubah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">She cannot drink because she has no husband to bring her to the Temple. But having brought her problem on herself by having had a secret rendez-vous, the husband’s heirs can tell her to prove her claim to the <i>ketubah</i> money by proving her innocence, following the general principle of monetary claims הַמּוֹצִיא מֵחֲבֵרוֹ עָלָיו הָרְאָיָה “the burden of proof is on the claimant” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 3:8:2-8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.3.8.2-8">Yerushalmi <i>Sanhedrin</i>3:9</a>, fol. 21b; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 46b" href="/Bava_Kamma.46b">Babli <i>Baba Qama</i> 46b</a>).<br>The reading here is that of all Yerushalmi sources and a number of Mishnah mss.; the reading of the Babli here is “either they drink or they do not collect their <i>ketubah</i>”, but the text is emended in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 38b" href="/Yevamot.38b">Babli, <i>Yebamot</i> 38b</a>, to conform to the Yerushalmi reading.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “The following cannot drink or collect their <i>ketubah</i>: One who says, I am impure for you,” etc. Rebbi Joshia said, Ze‘ira told me in the name of the people of Jerusalem<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 25a" href="/Sotah.25a">Babli (25a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 88a" href="/Sanhedrin.88a"><i>Sanhedrin</i>88a</a>): Ze‘ira the Jerusalemite.</i>: In three cases, if they want to forgive, they may forgive. These are: The suspect wife<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">The Babli explains: The husband may repeal his declaration of jealousy; then there is no case. However, the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 25a" href="/Sotah.25a">Babli (25a</a>) restricts this to the time before the wife had a secret rendez-vous whereas the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 8:6:2-12" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.8.6.2-12">Yerushalmi (<i>Sanhedrin</i> 8:6</a>, fol. 26b) lets the husband rescind his action any time before the scroll was erased.</i>, the rebellious son<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Described in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 21:18-21" href="/Deuteronomy.21.18-21"><i>Deut.</i> 21:18–21</a>; <i>Sanhedrin</i> Chapter 8.</i>, and the rebellious Elder<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 17:8-12" href="/Deuteronomy.17.8-12"><i>Deut.</i> 17:8–12</a>. The Babli in the name of “the colleagues in the South” and the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 8:6:2-12" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.8.6.2-12">Yerushalmi <i>Sanhedrin</i> 8:6</a> in the name of R. Jehudah ben Bathyra of Nisibis disagree in this case since the authority of the Supreme Court has to be upheld.</i>, etc.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A woman pregnant by another man or nursing another man’s [child]<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">A pregnant widow or divorcee is (rabbinically) not permitted to remarry until the baby is weaned.</i> may neither drink nor collect her <i>ketubah</i>, the words of Rebbi Meïr, but the Sages say, he<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">The second husband may live separated from his wife until the baby is weaned and there is no risk that the life of the baby be endangered by another pregnancy and a premature stop of nursing.</i> may separate from her and take her back later. A she-ram<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">A woman lacking secondary female sex characteristics, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 1:1:25" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.1.1.25"><i>Yebamot</i>, Chapter 1, Note 65</a>.</i>, an old woman<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">A post-menopausal woman.</i>, or a sterile one, neither drinks nor collects her <i>ketubah</i>; Rebbi Eleazar says, he may marry another wife and be fruitful and multiply with her<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">He takes <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:28" href="/Numbers.5.28"><i>Num.</i> 5:28</a> as a statement of fact, not a commandment.</i>. All other women either drink or cannot collect their <i>ketubah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Since by not drinking they forbid themselves to their husbands; they would have the status of women refusing to live with their husbands who are divorced without payment of the <i>ketubah</i> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 5:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.5.6.1">Mishnah <i>Ketubot</i> 5:7</a>).</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> A woman pregnant by another man or nursing another man’s [child],” etc. A man should not marry a woman pregnant by another man or nursing another man’s [child], but if he did marry her, the verse says about him<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 23:10" href="/Proverbs.23.10"><i>Prov</i>. 23:10</a>.</i>: “Do not remove the eternal boundaries and do not enter the orphans’ field.” He who marries a woman pregnant by another man or nursing another man’s [child] has to divorce her and should never retake her, the words of Rebbi Meĩr, but the Sages say, he<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">The second husband may live separated from his wife until the baby is weaned and there is no risk that the life of the baby be endangered by another pregnancy and a premature stop of nursing.</i> may separate from her<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 26a" href="/Sotah.26a">Babli (26a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 36b" href="/Yevamot.36b"><i>Yebamot</i> 36b</a>), the Sages require a divorce but permit a remarriage.</i> and take her back later. Does Rebbi Meїr always fine people<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Since he has no biblical source for his prohibition of remarriage, it is purely a punitive measure against people who flout rabbinic maxims. Does he always prohibit remediation for actions of that kind?</i>? Let us hear from the following: <sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yebamot</i> Chapter 12, Notes 103, 107, 108.</i>“The underage woman who performed <i>ḥalîṣah</i> should repeat <i>ḥalîṣah</i> once she becomes of age but if she did not repeat it, the <i>ḥalîṣah</i> is valid.” Rebbi Mana said it without attribution, Rebbi Isaac the son of Rebbi Ḥiyya the scribe<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Yebamot</i>: מטי בה “turns to it”. The text here seems original.</i> in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Yebamot</i>: R. Jonah. The text here seems original.</i>: It is Rebbi Meїr who said that one does not perform <i>ḥalîṣah</i> or levirate with an underage girl because she might turn out to be a she-ram<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Since R. Meїr accepts the validity after the fact; he does not always refuse remediation.</i>.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Tosephta Nidda 2:2, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 60b" href="/Ketubot.60b">Babli <i>Ketubot</i> 60b</a>.</i>: “A nursing woman whose husband died should not remarry<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">In Tosephta, Babli, and sources dependent on the Babli: לא תינשא ולא תתארס “not remarry, not even preliminarily marry”. The Yerushalmi does not mention preliminary marriage.</i> until 24 months later, the words of Rebbi Meїr<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Tosephta Nidda 2:1–3 notes that the normal period of nursing a baby is 24 months. Therefore, a normal contract for a wet-nurse is for 24 months.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah says, eighteen months<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that a baby may be weaned after 18 months without danger to his health even under the sanitary conditions of his time.</i>. Rebbi Jonathan ben Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">In the mss. of the Babli, R. Nathan bar Yoseph or R. Nathan bar Yose, but in the Gaonic sources mostly R. Jonathan Bar Yose. In <i>Ma‘serot</i> 5:4, he is called R. Jonathan ben R. Yose and characterized as a student of R. Aqiba.</i> says, the House of Shammai say 24 months, but the House of Hillel say eighteen months. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said, following him who said 24 months, she is permitted to remarry after 21 months; following him who said eighteen months, she is permitted to remarry after fifteen months, since the milk terminates only after three months<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Of a new pregnancy. The baby is not hurt if his mother starts a new pegnancy within three months of the end of his projected nursing period.</i>.”
Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa said, Aqabiah asked Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish who instructed him: 24 months. Rebbi Jeremiah: Aqabiah asked Rebbi Ḥanina who instructed him: 24 months. How? Two cases were there, one in the name of Rebbi Ḥanina and one in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish. Rebbi Mana instructed: eighteen months, and fasted that entire day<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">He felt uneasy about his decision even though in general R. Jehudah’s opinion is followed in a dispute with R. Meїr.</i>. Rebbi Mar Uqba instructed in Arbela: 24 months, even if the baby had died<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, he reports that R. Ḥanina had permitted him to marry after 15 months. The Babli (Note 55) decides on 24 months but permits immediate remarriage if the baby had died since Jewish women are not suspected to kill their children for a new husband.</i>.
The Sages agree with Rebbi Eleazar that if he had [another] wife and children, she drinks and collects her <i>ketubah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">They only hold that the sterile woman cannot be made to drink on the possibility that the husband would at some time in the future marry a child-bearing wife.</i> If he had [another] wife and children but they died between the declaration of jealousy and her secret rendez-vous, she already was eligible to drink<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">The status at the time of the declaration of jealousy is determining.</i>. If he did not have [another] wife and children but they died between the declaration of jealousy and her secret rendez-vous, she already was barred from drinking<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">This sentence makes no sense. One may conjecture that the original text stated that if the husband acquired a child-bearing wife after declaring his jealousy, it has no influence on the status of his first wife.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A Cohen’s wife drinks and is permitted to her husband<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Since she is cleared, she is permitted to her husband who is forbidden to be married even to a rape victim.</i>; the wife of a eunuch<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">As Tosaphot point out (26a, <i>s. v</i>. אשת סריס), the “eunuch” may be either a person born with defective testicles or a person castrated by chemical means. The verse prohibits only a surgically castrated man to marry into the congregation.</i> drinks. One may declare one’s jealousy with respect to any incest-‘prohibited person<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Even a person who is entitled to be alone with the wife, such as her father, who is forbidden to her by an incest prohibition.</i>, except for a minor and a non-man<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">An animal, according to the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 26b" href="/Sotah.26b">Babli (26b</a>) and the Rome ms.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “A Cohen’s wife drinks,” etc. Otherwise, what could we say? She drank and the water had no influence on her; she is pure! I could say, merit suspended for her<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Chapter 3, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 4:4:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.4.4.2-3">Halakhah 5</a>.</i>. That is according to him who says that merit suspends and it is not recognizable. But following him who says that merit suspends and it is recognizable? Rebbi Isaac said, really we deal with the case that she drank and the water had no influence on her; that you should not say since the water has no influence on a rape victim, but only on a seduced woman, this one is a rape victim. But a rape victim in the priesthood is like a willing one for an Israel, therefore it is necessary to say that she is permitted to her house<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">The same argument is anonymous in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 26a" href="/Sotah.26a">Babli, 26a</a>.</i>. From where that rape disables a married woman in the priesthood<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">For the Babli, the fact that a raped wife is forbidden to a Cohen is immediately obvious from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:7" href="/Leviticus.21.7"><i>Lev</i>. 21:7</a> since the definition of a זוֹנָה, usually translated as “prostitute” is (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Emor, Chapter 1 7" href="/Sifra,_Emor,_Chapter_1.7"><i>Sifra Emor Pereq</i> 1(7</a>) = <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 61b" href="/Yevamot.61b">Babli Yebamot 61b</a>) שֶּׁנִּבְעֲלָה בְּעִילַת זְנוּת “who was the object of an unlawful intercourse”. The formulation in the passive clearly implies that a rape victim is included.</i>? Since in matters of crawling things, which are a trifling matter, He made forced [impurity] equal to intentional [impurity], in the weighty matter of the deviant not so much more<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">This argument is based on the identity of the word “impurity” used both for ritual impurity (in this case, by touching a dead reptile) and guilt; cf. M. Cohen, מונחי טומאה וטהרה בלשון המקרא ויחסם למושגי איסור והיתר של לשון חכמים, 306.–289 ,בית מקרא כה,ד [קלה] תשנג Touching a dead reptile is a minor impurity which can be repaired immediately by immersion in water before sundown. There is no difference between intentional or unintional defilements. By contrast, sexual defilement has far-reaching consequences.<br>In an elliptic version the argument is in <i>Sifry</i> <i>Num</i>. 7. One needs the detailed argument here to understand the text there.</i>? Rebbi Jacob bar Idi stated before Rebbi Jonathan: “If you deviated from under your husband and became defiled,” that excludes rape. How do you understand this? He said to him, just as “under your husband” is by consent, so “if you became defiled” is by consent. “She was not raped”, she is forbidden. Therefore, if she was raped, she is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">This second argument is accepted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 26a" href="/Sotah.26a">Babli (26a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 56b" href="/Yevamot.56b"><i>Yebamot</i> 56b</a>).</i>. But there is a rape victim which is forbidden for an Israel. Who is that? That is one who started by consent and ended up raped<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">She started playing with another man and ended up being raped against her will. The Babli does not consider this case as one of consensual sex.</i>. And there is one who is not a rape victim which is permitted to an Israel. Who is that? That is one who started as a rape victim and ended up consenting<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">This case is accepted by the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 51b" href="/Ketubot.51b">Babli, <i>Ketubot</i> 51b</a>.</i>. Like that woman who came to Rebbi Joḥanan and said to him, I was raped. He said to her, but was it not sweet to you in the end<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">He wanted to forbid her to her (Israel) husband, following the opinion of Samuel’s father (Abba bar Abba) who considers rape that in the end is accepted by the victim as consensual sex; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 51b" href="/Ketubot.51b">Babli <i>Ketubot</i> 51b</a>.</i>? She said to him, if a person dips a finger into honey and puts it into his mouth on the Day of Atonement, does he not feel bad about it and at the end it is sweet? He accepted her<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">She is permitted to her husband. This argument is reproduced in the Babli by Rava who is known as a frequent mouthpiece of Yerushalmi arguments. The same argument used in the opposite sense in a tannaїtic statement is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Niddah 45a" href="/Niddah.45a">Babli, <i>Niddah</i> 45a</a>.</i>.
“If you deviated from under your husband and became defiled,<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:20" href="/Numbers.5.20"><i>Num</i>. 5:20</a>.</i>” that excludes<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">One would have expected “includes” since her husband has a penis if he is permitted to her (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 4:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.4.4.1">Note 68</a>). <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 26a" href="/Sotah.26a">Babli 26a</a>, <i>Sifry</i> <i>Num</i>. 13 read: “Other than your husband”, that includes a eunuch’s wife.</i> a castrate’s wife. “And a man ejaculated into you<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:20" href="/Numbers.5.20"><i>Num</i>. 5:20</a>.</i>”, that includes a castrate. “Except for a minor and a non-man,” e. g., an ape.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> For the following the court will declare jealousy: One whose husband had become a deaf-mute, or insane, or was confined in a jail; not to make her drink but to make her ineligible for her <i>ketubah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Since <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:14" href="/Numbers.5.14"><i>Num</i>. 5:14</a> requires the husband to declare his jealousy in order to bring her to the Temple. R. Yose requires the presence of the husband only for the trip to the Temple and the ceremony (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:15" href="/Numbers.5.15">vv. 15</a> ff.)</i>. Rebbi Yose says, also to make her drink: When her husband will be freed from jail he should make her drink.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “For the following the court will declare jealousy,” etc. “The children of Israel.<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is copied verbally from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.4.1">Halakhah 1, Notes 10–12</a>, but the meaning is different.</i>”
<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is copied verbally from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.4.1">Halakhah 1, Notes 10–12</a>, but the meaning is different.</i> Why can he not make her drink<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">Why does the majority not empower the freed or healed husband to make his wife drink?</i>? It is a decision of the verse: “The man shall bring his wife to the Cohen<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:15" href="/Numbers.5.15"><i>Num</i>. 5:15</a>.</i>.” Then he should not be able to declare his jealousy<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">The court as guardian of the incapacitated man should not be able to do what he himself could not do at the moment.</i>! The Torah said, “and he declared his jealousy to his wife,” “and he declared his jealousy to his wife,<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:14" href="/Numbers.5.14"><i>Num</i>. 5:14</a>; the court is empowered even if the man is temporarily disabled from acting as a husband.</i>” even if she is only partially his wife.
From where that the intercourse of a disabled disables<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">Disables a woman of the priestly clan (a wife or a daughter) from eating sanctified food such as heave; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 7:5:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.7.5.2-5"><i>Yebamot</i> 7:5</a>.</i>? What are we talking about? If about a married woman, there is a serious [offense].<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">Adultery is a capital crime.</i> If about a widow married to the High Priest, it already is written “The man shall bring his wife to the Cohen.<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 4:5:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.4.5.3">Note 87</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 4:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.4.1.1">Mishnah 1</a> already stated that a forbidden wife is not counted as a wife and we just have established that the intercourse with a man who cannot be legally married bars a woman from priesthood.</i>” What is equal between them is: Everybody’s intercourse disqualifies those whose descendant would be disqualified<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">The child of the otherwise married woman is a bastard, the child of the widow married to the High Priest is desecrated.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">This text is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 7:5:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.7.5.5"><i>Yebamot</i> 7:5, Notes 111–116</a>.</i> These are: “A child of nine years and one day, an Ammonite, Moabite, Edomite, or Egyptian proselyte, or a bastard, desecrated, Gibeonite, Samaritan, and Gentile who had intercourse with the daughter of a Cohen, a Levite, or an Israel, disqualified her for the priesthood. Rebbi Yose said, everybody’s intercourse disqualifies those whose descendant would be disqualified; his intercourse does not disqualify those whose descendant would not be disqualified. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, in all cases, if his daughter is permitted to you, so is his widow; if his daughter is not permitted to you, neither is his widow.”

Chapter 5



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Just as the water checks her out so it checks him<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The wife’s paramour. It is moreover stated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 9:9:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.9.9.2">Halakhah 9:9</a> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 28a" href="/Sotah.28a">Babli 28a</a>) that the procedure becomes inactive if the husband had ever misbehaved sexually.</i> out, as it is said, “it will come, it will come”. Just as she is forbidden to the husband so she is forbidden to her paramour as it is said, “she was impure, she was impure,” the words of Rebbi Aqiba<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">This will be explained in the Halakhah.</i>. Rebbi Joshua said, that was the inference of Zachariah the butcher’s son<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">An early Tanna, student of Rabban Joḥanan ben Zakkai.</i>. Rebbi said, the two times it said, “she became impure, she became impure,” once for the husband and once for the paramour<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:13" href="/Numbers.5.13"><i>Num</i>. 5:13</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:14" href="/Numbers.5.14">14</a> it is stated twice “she became impure”, meaning “she became forbidden”; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 4:4:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.4.4.2">Chapter 4, Note 75</a>, and the Introduction.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Just as the water checks her out,” etc. We stated “they came, they came”. Some Tannaïm stated “they will come, they will come”. He who said “they came, they came” is Rebbi Aqiba, he who said “they will come, they will come” is Rebbi Ismael. We stated “she became impure, she became impure”, some Tannaïm stated “and she became impure, and she became impure”. He who said “she became impure, she became impure” is Rebbi Aqiba, he who said “and she became impure, and she became impure” is Rebbi Ismael<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">R. Ismael holds that דִּבְּרָה תוֹרָה בִלְשׁוֹן בְּנֵי אָדָם “the Torah speaks in everyday language” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 8:1:12" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.8.1.12"><i>Yebamot</i> 8:1, Note 72</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 1:1:2-3:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.1.1.2-3.7"><i>Nedarim</i> 1:1</a>; <i>Šabbat</i> 19:2; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 31b" href="/Berakhot.31b">Babli <i>Berakhot</i> 31b</a> and 18 other places). This means that any verse containing no linguistic feature noted as an exception in <i>Gesenius-Kautzsch</i> must be interpreted following the hermeneutical rules detailed in the introduction to <i>Sifra</i>. R. Ismael’s argument here is a straightforward application of his rule 3: בנין אב מכתוב אחד “meaning established by one verse”. Since both וּבָאוּ (vv. 22,27) and וְנִטְמָאָה (v. 29) [correctly in <i>Midrash</i> <i>of the 32 Rules</i>: וְהִיא נִטְמָאָה (vv. 13,14)] are well defined by the first occurrence, the second occurrence has the parallel meaning but refers to a different subject. That this second subject is the suspected adulterer is never indicated in the verse but since he is the only other person possibly being impure in this situation, the transfer of the meanings to him makes sense.<br>R. Aqiba negates absolutely that “the Torah speaks in everyday language”. For him, any <i>vaw</i> prefixed to a word has a hidden meaning. [Since his co-student Aquila translated the Torah in this sense “before Rebbis Eliezer and Joshua” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 1:9:2-20" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.1.9.2-20"><i>Megillah</i> 1:11</a> fol. 71c, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 3a" href="/Sotah.3a">Babli 3a</a>), one may attribute this transformation of the Pentateuch into a book of codes to the tradition of their common teacher Rabban Joḥanan ben Zakkai.] R. Aqiba is not deterred by the fact that in וּבָאוּ the <i>vav</i> is both conjunctive and conversive; any <i>vav</i> that is translated even in the LXX by καὶ must indicate a duplication of meaning. If the duplication of subjects were not intended, then Scripture would have found a way to use only באו (or imperfect יבואו) without the <i>vav</i>.<br>The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 28a" href="/Sotah.28a">Babli, 28a</a>, takes notice only of the teachings of R. Aqiba.</i>.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">In order to prove that the adulterer is punished by the bitter water just as the adulteress is.</i>, בוא ובה<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">A copyist’s error makes this expression unintelligible. It must read ובא ובה. Than means that instead of וּבָאוּ בָהּ “they will come into her” (v. 27), the final <i>vaw</i> is transferred from the end of the first word to the beginning of the second to read וּבָא וּבָהּ “it will come, and into her”, implying that the water will come into somebody and into her, establishing that the presumed adulterer can also be checked out by the water.
