The technique of transferring letters is accepted not only by the Yerushalmi (also <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 5:1:2-12" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.5.1.2-12"><i>Nazir</i> 5:1</a>, 43d line 67; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 1:3:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.1.3.2-3"><i>Horaiot</i> 1:3</a>, 46a, line 24) but possibly also in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 48a" href="/Yoma.48a">Babli (<i>Yoma</i> 48a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 111b" href="/Bava_Batra.111b"><i>Baba batra</i> 111b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 25a" href="/Zevachim.25a"><i>Zebaḥim</i> 25a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 44b" href="/Bekhorot.44b"><i>Bekhorot</i> 44b</a>).<br>(Such an argument is quite impossible in a paleo-Hebrew text where the words are separated by a physical divider. The technique of moving letters from the end of one word to the beginning of the following, so much in fashion in modern criticism, presupposes that a change occured at a late time when there were no longer any paleo-Hebrew texts in use.)</i>. Is that written? It follows what Rebbi Immi said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: For interpretation, one removes from its beginning to its end<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">It really should be “from end to beginning”.</i>. Rebbi Ḥanina in the name of Rebbi Jeremiah: Even a middle word. “You have to pour oil on.” You have to pour oil on a flour offering, to subject all flour offerings to pouring<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 1:6" href="/Leviticus.1.6"><i>Lev</i>. 1:6</a>: “You have to break it into little pieces and pour oil over it; [because] it is a flour offering”, speaking of a pan-fried offering. For some flour offerings it is only specified that oil has to be included, or that oil has to be put on it. From this verse one concludes that “putting on” or “mixing” means “pouring oil over the flour”. The argument here proposes to read וְיָצַקְתָּ שֶׁמֶן מִנְחָ֑ה instead of וְיָצַקְתָּ עָלֶיהָ שֶׁ֑מֶן מִנְחָה הִֽיא. This is based on the teaching of R. Aqiba who disregards masoretic accents in all cases (cf. H. Guggenheimer, <i>The Scholar’s Haggadah</i>, Northvale NJ 1995, pp. 306–307) but in the Babli (cf. Note 8) is strongly opposed by Rava who objects to applying a surgeon’s knife to a verse (cf. the explanations of R. Ḥananel in ‘<i>Arukh</i>, s. v. גרע and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 111b" href="/Bava_Batra.111b">Rashbam in <i>Baba batra</i> 111b, s. v. אלא</a>.)</i>.
“The spell acting”. Rebbi Tanḥuma said, what is the count of<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Sotah 27b:3:1" href="/Tosafot_on_Sotah.27b.3.1">Tosaphot (27b, s. v. כשם</a>) read correctly מנין. The reference is to the (Alexandrinian) custom of using letters as numerals. The count of ה̇מ̇א̇ר̇ר̇י̇ם̇ is 5+40+1+200+200+10+40 = 496 = 2·248.</i> “the spell acting”? It corresponds to the 248 parts of her body and the 248 parts of his body<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Ahitot</i> 1:8. A part is either a bone or a vital organ, such as the genitals. No talmudic source, in contrast to medieval rabbinic texts, makes a difference between the Number of parts of the male and those of the female.<br>The implication of the sermon is that the bodies of adulterer and adulteress together bring on the evil spell.</i>.
Did we not state: “Just as the water checks her out so it checks him out”? <sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">The next few sentences are copied from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 1:2:10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.1.2.10">Chapter 1, Note 128</a>. But see the Tosaphot text in which only one sentence from there (and <i>Yebamot</i> Chapter 10, Notes 49 ff.) appears.</i> Just as she is forbidden to her husband, so she is forbidden to her paramour. Just as she is forbidden to her husband’s brother, so she is forbidden to her paramour’s brother. Just as the water checks her out for every single intercourse for which she receives her husband after [intercourse with] her paramour, so it checks him out. Since she in general becomes forbidden, whether by him or by another [man], she is checked out<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 2:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.2.4.1">Mishnah 2:5</a> that the husband may stipulate that the water check her out not only for adultery with the named respondent but with any man whosoever.</i>. But when he<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">The husband.</i> makes [her] drink, he<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">The suspected adulterer.</i> is checked out<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">The water is presumed to act at a distance, to make the adulterer’s belly swell and his waist disappear. (Cf. Note 14).</i>. If it checked him out but not her, I would say that merit suspends for her<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Chapter 3, Halakhot 4,5.</i>. That is according to him who says that merit suspends and it is not recognizable. But following him who says that merit suspends and it is recognizable? Look, she was not recognized! But I could say that she drank uncovered water<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Water which was left standing uncovered and unattended may contain snake poison, cf. <i>Terumot</i> 8:5, in particular Notes 113 ff.</i> and it<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Meaning the adulterer’s.</i> swelled. They should not have her<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">The scribe wrote מבדקונַיה. It seems that the ms. before him had מבדקוניה, “to check him out”, but the scribe recognized that the masculine was inappropriate and indicated that one should read מבדקונַהּ, “to check her out”.</i> checked out in this way but correctly<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Reading כדין “correctly” with Tosaphot, against a nondescript כדון “so” in the text. It would be against all rules if water which was left unattended in a vessel were used in the Temple.</i>! But I could say that he was in secret with other women<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">The accused boy friend was an adulterer but not with that woman.</i>. Did we not think to say, when he makes [her] drink, he is checked out<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">The water acts at a distance only from a woman with whom he actually committed adultery; the explanation attempted in the previous sentence is impossible.</i>? Explain it that he had criminal intent but she acted in error; then the water checked him out but not her<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">If either it was a case similar to date rape or that the adulterer impersonated the husband.</i>. [<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Added from Tosaphot, missing in the mss.</i> If the water checked her out but not him,] I would say that merit suspends for (her) [him]<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">From Tosaphot text.</i>. That is according to him who says that merit suspends and it is not recognizable. But following him who says that merit suspends and it is recognizable? Look, she was not recognized! But I could say that she drank uncovered water and her belly swelled. They should not have her checked out in this way but correctly!<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Reading כדין “correctly” with Tosaphot, against a nondescript כדון “so” in the text. It would be against all rules if water which was left unattended in a vessel were used in the Temple.</i> But I could say that she was in secret with other men. Then if he<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">The husband.</i> divorced her, she would be permitted to him.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">The suspected adulterer.</i> Explain it that he acted in error but she had criminal intent; then the water checked her out but not him. If he had criminal intent but she acted in error, then it is obvious that she is permitted to her house<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">The husband.</i>. If he<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">The husband.</i> divorced her, would she be permitted to him<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">The suspected adulterer.</i>? Is it possible to say that he had criminal intent and you say so? If he acted in error but she had criminal intent, it is obvious that she is forbidden to her house. If he<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">The husband.</i> divorced her, would she be permitted to him<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">The suspected adulterer.</i>? Is it possible to say that she left because of him and you say so<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">The Yerushalmi forbids the wife even to the unintentional adulterer; e. g., if she told him that she was single.</i>? From where do we know that all depends on her? Simeon bar Abba in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Is is written<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 18:20" href="/Leviticus.18.20"><i>Lev</i>. 18:20</a>.</i>: “Do not ejaculate semen into your neighbor’s wife to be impure through her.” It depends on her; if she had criminal intent, she is forbidden, acted in error she is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Everything depends on her status; since a rape victim is permitted to her husband, the divorced rape victim is permitted to the rapist but the woman who pretended to be single is forbidden to her boyfriend after her divorce.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> On the same day<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">According to the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 28a" href="/Berakhot.28a">Babli (<i>Berakhot</i> 28a</a>), any Mishnah which starts “on the same day” was formulated on the day Rabban Gamliel was deposed. There is no proof that this ever was a Galilean tradition.</i>, Rebbi Aqiba explained: “Any clay vessel into which some of them would fall, all inside shall become impure.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:33" href="/Leviticus.11.33"><i>Lev.</i> 11:33</a>, speaking of dead reptiles (which are carriers of original impurity) falling into a clay vessel. In the biblical laws of impurity, no defilement is imparted to a clay vessel touched by impurities from the outside. But if the clay vessel encloses a space that can be covered and something of original impurity enters the space (even before it touches any wall), the entire vessel becomes impure in the first degree. {Degrees of impurity are explained in the commentary to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 2:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.2.3.1"><i>Demay</i>, Chapter 2, Note 137</a>.} Therefore, any food inside the vessel becomes impure in the second degree. If that food touches food susceptible to tertiary impurity, the latter becomes impure in the third degree. Which food can become impure in the third and fourth degrees is a matter of discussion in the Halakhah.<br>The same verse states that a clay vessel can be purified only by being broken. The shards become pure writing material.</i>” It does not say “is impure” but “shall become impure”<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Probably he reads יְטַמֵּא “it will defile” in place of יִטְמָא “it shall become impure”. Revocalization of the consonantal text is a technique accepted by R. Aqiba, rejected by R. Ismael.</i>; this teaches about the secondarily impure loaf that it defiles the tertiary.<br>Rebbi Joshua said, who would remove the dust from your eyes, Rabban Joḥanan ben Zakkai, since you were saying that a future generation would purify the tertiary loaf since there is nothing about it in the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that the possibility of tertiary impurity for heave and sacrifices and quaternary for sacrifices is traditional rather than biblical.<br>The slightly enlarged text is also in <i>Sifra Šemini Parasha</i> 7(12).</i>, but behold, your student Aqiba supports it by a verse from the Torah, as it has been said, “everything inside shall become impure.”
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “On the same day, Rebbi Aqiba explained,” etc. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, Rav and Samuel. One said, Rebbi Aqiba referred to both heave and profane food, but the other referred to heave but not to profane food<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">The question is whether profane pure food touched by something impure in the second degree remains pure or becomes impure in the third degree.</i>. But one does not know who said what. Since Rebbi Yose said in the name of Rebbi Jonah, they said, Rav in the name of the elder Rebbi Ḥiyya: The tertiary comes because of the reptile<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">See Note 34. An additional argument, mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 29b" href="/Sotah.29b">Babli 29b</a>, is needed to clarify the statement of R. Aqiba. An original source of impurity is either a “father of impurity” which in touching a pure vessel, person, or food induces primary impurity, or a “grandfather of impurity” creating a “father of impurity” by contact (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 2:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.2.3.1"><i>Demay,</i> Chapter 2, Note 137</a>). If a piece of “grandfather of impurity” falls into the clay vessel, the vessel becomes “father of impurity” and the loaf lying in it becomes impure in the first degree. In that case, one has ample biblical references that the loaf is still active in transmitting impurity, generating secondary impurity; the tertiary impurity mentioned by R. Aqiba is not addressed. Therefore, it is important to state that <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:33" href="/Leviticus.11.33"><i>Lev.</i> 11:33</a> is written in a paragraph dealing with the impurity of dead reptiles which are only “fathers of impurity”, inducing impurities that can be washed off by immersion in water. In the entire paragraph, no mention is made of sanctified food. Therefore, the impurity of dead reptiles implies the existence of tertiary impurity of profane food.</i>. This implies that he said that Rebbi Aqiba referred to both heave and profane food<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 29a" href="/Sotah.29a">Babli, 29a</a>, knows only that Rav holds that tertiary impurity does not exist for profane food. It does not mention any contrary opinion.</i>. A Mishnah disagrees with Rav: <sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Ṭevul Yom</i> 4:1.</i>“If tithe food was prepared [for impurity] by a fluid<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Solid food can become impure only if it was in contact with either water or human body fluids under certain circumstances; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 2:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.2.3.2"><i>Demay</i> Chapter 2, Note 141</a>.</i> and a <i>ṭevul yom</i><sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">A person who was impure and cleansed himself by immersion in water during daytime. It is explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 22:6-7" href="/Leviticus.22.6-7"><i>Lev.</i>22:6–7</a> that impurity is removed by immersion in water during daytime “and when the sun disappears he will be pure; afterwards, he may eat sanctified food.” (The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 2" href="/Berakhot.2">Babli, <i>Berakhot</i>2a/b</a>, discusses whether וּבָא הַשֶּׁמֶשׁ means “the sun disappears” or “the sun rises”). The <i>ṭevul yom</i> therefore is no longer impure but before sundown he is not pure for sanctified food; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 2:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.2.3.1"><i>Demay</i> Chapter 2, Note 138</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 2:1:14" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.2.1.14"><i>Terumot</i> Chapter 2, Note 51</a>. Since he is forbidden only sanctified food, one may assume that he is pure for profane food. In rabbinical terms, the <i>ṭevul yom</i> after immersion but before sundown is treated as impure in the second degree.</i> or unclean hands<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Unwashed hands, of an otherwise pure person, are always rabbinically impure in the second degree until washed with at least a <i>revi‘it</i> (135 cl) of water; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bikkurim 2:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bikkurim.2.1.1"><i>Bikkurim</i> Chapter 2, Note 4</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 2:3:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.2.3.6"><i>Demay</i> Chapter 2, Note 160</a>.</i> touched it, one may take from it the heave of the tithe in purity because it is tertiary<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Tithe food in the hand of a Levite is totally profane. The Levite is required to give 10% of the tithe to the Cohen as heave of the tithe, which is sanctified food. While the tithe before the separation of the heave is forbidden as <i>ṭevel,</i> it is not sanctified and any impurity, even secondary, would eliminate the tithe as a source of heave (cf. <i>Ḥallah,</i> <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 3:1:5-2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.3.1.5-2.1">Chapter 3, Notes 43–44</a>). If the <i>ṭevul yom</i> may touch tithe from which no heave was given it proves that tertiary impurity does not apply to profane food.</i>, and the tertiary is pure for profane food.” He explains that it is a leniency about hands [whose impurity] is rabbinical. But did we not state: “A <i>ṭevul yom</i>”? Explain it by a <i>ṭevul yom</i> from [the impurity] of a broken field<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">A “broken field” is agricultural property of which it is known that it once contained a grave. The position of the grave is unknown; also it is unknown whether bones from the grave have been dispersed on the field by ploughing. Since there are doubts whether any impurity still exists or where it might be, and it is an open field (where a doubt of impurity is inactive, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 1:2:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.1.2.5">Chapter 1, Notes 87–96</a>), a person walking over the field is only rabbinically impure.</i>. Rebbi Zeïra said, you may even say a biblical <i>tevul yom.</i> There is a difference since it is written “pure, impure”<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:32" href="/Leviticus.11.32"><i>Lev.</i> 11:32</a> has expressions of purity and impurity: “It shall be immersed in water, be impure until evening, and be pure”. This implies that a <i>ṭevul yom</i> vessel is both impure and pure; it is according to tradition that one decides in which domain it is pure and in which impure. In 22:6–7, the <i>ṭevul yom</i> person is only forbidden sanctified food in his intermediary state.</i>. Pure for profane food during daytime and for heave when it gets dark. Rebbi Ḥaggai objected before Rebbi Yose: May we not say: Pure for touch and impure for eating<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Since the distinction of the influence of a <i>ṭevul yom</i> on profane or sanctified food is one of interpretation, could one not understand from the verses that the <i>ṭevul yom</i> contaminates any food but is free to touch?</i>? He answered him, vessels are mentioned in the paragraph! Can you say about vessels that they are pure for touch and impure for eating<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">The rules of purification in stages by immersion in water (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:32" href="/Leviticus.11.32"><i>Lev.</i>11:32</a>) deal with vessels, which are inedible.</i>?
A Mishnah<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ṭahorot</i> 2:3.</i> supports Rav: “Profane food which is impure primarily is impure and defiles, secondarily it invalidates<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Food whose impurity is contagious is called טמא “impure"; if it cannot be used for any purpose but is not contagious it is called פסול “invalid”. Secondarily impure profane food invalidates heave by its touch.</i> but does not defile, but the tertiary can be eaten in heave broth.<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">The Cohen who eats heave can cook his meal with profane spices that have touched secondarily impure food since secondarily impure profane food is inactive if it touches other profane food.</i>” This implies that it is forbidden to turn it into heave<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is only permitted to spice heave broth with spices that have touched secondarily impure food, it is implied that the potentially tertiary cannot itself be dedicated as heave.</i>. And it is said<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">In parallel with the later text, אסור is read as a misspelling for אמור, following all commentators.</i> after that<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ṭahorot</i> 2:4. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 30a" href="/Sotah.30a">Babli, 30a</a>, quotes <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Tahorot 2:2" href="/Mishnah_Tahorot.2.2">Mishnah <i>Tahorot</i> 2:2</a> in which R. Joshua disagrees.</i>: “Heave which is impure primarily or secondarily is impure and defiles, tertiarily impure it invalidates but does not defile, but the fourth degree can be eaten in sacrificial broth.” If you say that it is forbidden to turn it into sacrificial food, you would find that Rav’s statements are contradictory since Rebbi Abba said in the name of Rav: The tertiary comes because of the reptile but the fourth degree can be turned into sacrificial food, because they spoke about fourth degree [of impurity] for sacrifices only for dedicated sacrifices of the Temple<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 5:2:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.5.2.4">Note 38</a>. It is clear that <i>Lev.</i> knows of a status of invalid sacrificial food induced by touching impurity since v. 7:19 requires that sacrificial meat which touched impurity must be burned but is not labelled “impure”. In Chapter 11, vessels which touched a dead reptile are called “impure”. Therefore, it is established that for sacrifices, anything declared impure in v. 11:33 invalidates sacrifices by its touch. This means that for R. Aqiba in Rav’s interpretation, the fourth degree of impurity in the Temple is biblical, but for the rabbis (and for R. Aqiba in Samuel’s interpretation) only tertiary invalidity is biblical. (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 29b" href="/Sotah.29b">Babli 29b</a>).</i>! Rebbi Ḥuna said, are we not talking about heave? But it is forbidden to turn heave into sacrificial food in order to avoid accidents<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">It is a positive commandment for the Cohen to consume his heave (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 18:11" href="/Numbers.18.11"><i>Num.</i> 18:11</a>). Therefore, it is forbidden to expose heave to situations where it could no longer be consumed. But sacrificial food can become invalidated in many ways (such as leaving the Temple precinct, being left overnight, etc.) that would not invalidate heave. Therefore, mixing heave with sacrificial food exposes the Cohen to sinning.</i>! And it was said after that<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ṭahorot</i> 2:5.</i>: “Sacrifices which are impure primarily, secondarily, or tertiarily are impure and defile, impurity in the fourth degree invalidates but does not defile, but the fifth degree can be eaten in sacrificial broth.” If you say, it is forbidden to turn it into sacrificial food, is there any impurity of the fifth degree in sacrifices<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Since sacrificial food touching invalid food of the fourth degree remains pure by the definition of invalidity, there can be no reason why profane food prepared by the standards of sacrificial purity could not be dedicated as a valid sacrifice. Therefore, the argument that the Mishnah permits only the addition of profane food one step removed from invalidity to heave but not turning it into heave has no basis in the text and the Mishnah does not support Rav’s position.</i>? But you say there is not. So here also, there is not.
Rebbi Joḥanan said: The tertiary comes because of the reptile<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">See Note 34. An additional argument, mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 29b" href="/Sotah.29b">Babli 29b</a>, is needed to clarify the statement of R. Aqiba. An original source of impurity is either a “father of impurity” which in touching a pure vessel, person, or food induces primary impurity, or a “grandfather of impurity” creating a “father of impurity” by contact (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 2:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.2.3.1"><i>Demay,</i> Chapter 2, Note 137</a>). If a piece of “grandfather of impurity” falls into the clay vessel, the vessel becomes “father of impurity” and the loaf lying in it becomes impure in the first degree. In that case, one has ample biblical references that the loaf is still active in transmitting impurity, generating secondary impurity; the tertiary impurity mentioned by R. Aqiba is not addressed. Therefore, it is important to state that <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:33" href="/Leviticus.11.33"><i>Lev.</i> 11:33</a> is written in a paragraph dealing with the impurity of dead reptiles which are only “fathers of impurity”, inducing impurities that can be washed off by immersion in water. In the entire paragraph, no mention is made of sanctified food. Therefore, the impurity of dead reptiles implies the existence of tertiary impurity of profane food.</i> but it is (forbidden) [permitted]<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 29a" href="/Sotah.29a">Babli, 29a</a>, knows only that Rav holds that tertiary impurity does not exist for profane food. It does not mention any contrary opinion.</i> to turn it into heave. They asked before him: Even for Rebbi Aqiba? Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Hila: Even following Rebbi Aqiba. And why? The <i>ṭevul yom</i> makes invalid, secondary impurity makes invalid. Just as the <i>ṭevul yom</i> does not invalidate profane food that it could not be turned into heave<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">A person who was impure and cleansed himself by immersion in water during daytime. It is explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 22:6-7" href="/Leviticus.22.6-7"><i>Lev.</i>22:6–7</a> that impurity is removed by immersion in water during daytime “and when the sun disappears he will be pure; afterwards, he may eat sanctified food.” (The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 2" href="/Berakhot.2">Babli, <i>Berakhot</i>2a/b</a>, discusses whether וּבָא הַשֶּׁמֶשׁ means “the sun disappears” or “the sun rises”). The <i>ṭevul yom</i> therefore is no longer impure but before sundown he is not pure for sanctified food; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 2:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.2.3.1"><i>Demay</i> Chapter 2, Note 138</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 2:1:14" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.2.1.14"><i>Terumot</i> Chapter 2, Note 51</a>. Since he is forbidden only sanctified food, one may assume that he is pure for profane food. In rabbinical terms, the <i>ṭevul yom</i> after immersion but before sundown is treated as impure in the second degree.</i>, so secondary impurity does not invalidate profane food that it could not be turned into heave. Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: The tertiary comes because of the reptile<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">See Note 34. An additional argument, mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 29b" href="/Sotah.29b">Babli 29b</a>, is needed to clarify the statement of R. Aqiba. An original source of impurity is either a “father of impurity” which in touching a pure vessel, person, or food induces primary impurity, or a “grandfather of impurity” creating a “father of impurity” by contact (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 2:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.2.3.1"><i>Demay,</i> Chapter 2, Note 137</a>). If a piece of “grandfather of impurity” falls into the clay vessel, the vessel becomes “father of impurity” and the loaf lying in it becomes impure in the first degree. In that case, one has ample biblical references that the loaf is still active in transmitting impurity, generating secondary impurity; the tertiary impurity mentioned by R. Aqiba is not addressed. Therefore, it is important to state that <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:33" href="/Leviticus.11.33"><i>Lev.</i> 11:33</a> is written in a paragraph dealing with the impurity of dead reptiles which are only “fathers of impurity”, inducing impurities that can be washed off by immersion in water. In the entire paragraph, no mention is made of sanctified food. Therefore, the impurity of dead reptiles implies the existence of tertiary impurity of profane food.</i>, and it is (forbidden) [permitted]<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">While both mss. read אסור, the following argument shows that it must read מותר.</i> to turn it into heave but (permitted) [forbidden]<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">If the first term reads “permitted”, this one must read “prohibited”, against the testimony of both mss. While a point could be made here to read “permitted”, it would involve some twisting of the following argument.</i> to turn it into sacrificial food. Rebbi Ze‘ira asked before Rebbi Yasa: It defiles sacrifices and you say so<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Since tertiary impurity induces one of the fourth degree, what is the point in stating that one may not turn tertiary food into sacrifices? It is implied that vegetable sacrifices (flour, wine, oil, and incense) must be prepared by the standards of sacrificial food from the moment they become susceptible to impurity.</i>? He said to him, because of the distinction<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Except for R. Aqiba in the interpretation of Rav, invalidity of sacrifices of fourth degree impurity is purely rabbinic, to elevate sacrifices one step over heave which has no connection with the Temple; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 5:2:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.5.2.3">Note 55</a>.</i>. Rebbi Samuel ben Rebbi Isaac said, the source of its impurity is the distinction<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">This student of Rav and Rav Huna agrees with R. Yasa (Assi), student of Samuel and R. Joḥanan, that impurity of the fourth degree is purely rabbinical.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, why does it defile sacrifices? Because of the distinction. Rebbi Yudan said, even for the rabbis who say that there is no tertiary in profane food, why is it forbidden? It defiles sacrifices because of the distinction. Rebbi Yose said to him, do we not need to hear this from a great personality<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">That two fifth-generation Amoraïm have to confirm the consensus of the third generation shows that this purely theoretical statement, almost 300 years after the destruction of the Temple, failed to be accepted popularly. It is defended at length in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chagigah 22" href="/Chagigah.22">Babli, <i>Ḥagigah</i> 22a/b</a>.</i>?
There, they say, because Rebbi Joshua praised Rebbi Aqiba, it implies that practice follows his argument. The rabbis of Caesarea say, he praised him for his ingenuity, but in fact practice does not follow his argument, since Rebbi Aḥa, Rebbi Maisha, in the name of Rebbi Eleazar said, one whips for tertiary [impurity] for tithes<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the Second Tithe, food that the farmer retains for himself and his family, to be eaten in purity in Jerusalem. On that occasion, he has to declare in the Temple that he “did not separate it while impure” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 26:14" href="/Deuteronomy.26.14"><i>Deut.</i> 26:14</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bikkurim 2:2:2-11" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bikkurim.2.2.2-11"><i>Bikkurim</i> 2:2</a>). This implies that Second Tithe becomes desecrated by impurity and must be redeemed. The question is whether eating Second Tithe in any state of impurity is a criminal offense, for which the punishment is whipping. The thesis advanced here is that Second Tithe has a status equal to heave, so that tertiary impurity makes that tithe unusable.<br>The Babli certainly agrees that the statement of R. Joshua is not an endorsement of the practice since it quotes <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Tahorot 2:2" href="/Mishnah_Tahorot.2.2">Mishnah <i>Tahorot</i> 2:2</a> in which R. Joshua disagrees with R. Eliezer whom R. Aqiba follows.<br>It is always held that first tithe after separation of the heave of the tithe is totally profane in the hand of the Levite, even if consumption is restricted to Levites of good standing.</i>, even<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">he meaning of this word is unclear; possibly this is a reflection from “even” in the next sentence.</i> according to Rebbi Aqiba. Rebbi Yudan in the name of Rebbi Hilai: Even according to Rebbi Aqiba one does not whip. Why? The <i>ṭevul yom</i> makes invalid, secondary impurity makes invalid. Since the <i>ṭevul yom</i> has no touch for profane food<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">This means that as far as the status of purity is concerned, the touch of a <i>ṭevul yom</i> is totally irrelevant.</i>, also secondary impurity has no touch for profane food<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Since Second Tithe is eaten by the farmer’s family, without anything being given to Cohanim or Levites, it cannot have the status of sanctified food.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar said, as the count for profane food so is the count of tithes<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">Since for profane food one has only primary and secondary impurity, not tertiary, the same holds for tithes. This contradicts the statement quoted earlier in the name of R. Eleazar.</i>. This corresponds to what Rebbi Jonah, Rebbi Immi, said in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: We deal with somebody who ate<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">Reading בָּכַל as contraction of בְּאָכַל.</i> profane food, and that Chapter deals with tithe<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 2 of <i>Ṭahorot,</i> which was quoted in the previous paragraph and which details the stages of impurity, mentions only profane, heave, and sacrificial foods. Since the tithes are not mentioned and First Tithe is profane, it is clear that tithes are classified with profane food.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, even its primary [impurity] is not clear. Not “I will not eat” is written, but “I did not eat.”<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 26:14" href="/Deuteronomy.26.14"><i>Deut.</i> 26:14</a>. It is clear that if the farmer ate some Second Tithe in impurity, he cannot make the declaration in the Temple. But this does not imply that eating in impurity is forbidden as a criminal act.</i> Rebbi Abba Mari said, since it is written “following all Your commandments which You commanded me,” is it as if it were not clear<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Since the verse classifies all that is mentioned there as God’s commandments, necessarily the infractions which bar the farmer from making the declaration are criminal acts.</i>?
Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Mana<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">R. Mana I.</i>: Why did they say that secondarily impure profane food defiles profane fluids<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">It is a general principle that impure fluids are always primarily impure (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Parah 8:7" href="/Mishnah_Parah.8.7">Mishnah <i>Parah</i> 8:7</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 5:1:17" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.5.1.17"><i>Berakhot</i> Chapter 5, Note 46</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 2:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.2.3.1"><i>Demay</i> Chapter 2, Note 137</a>). This implies that fluids are never just invalid; if they were in contact with impurity, they are always active polluters. This is a rabbinic rule. The sources are discussed in detail in the author’s <i>The Scholar’s Haggadah</i> (Northvale 1995), pp. 230–231.</i>? Because of the hands. Since hands, [whose impurity] is rabbinical, defile profane fluids, secondarily impure food, [whose impurity] is biblical, not so much more<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">By rabbinic rule, unwashed hands are always secondarily impure. Washed hands remain pure as long as one does not forget about them. The argument here is that the rabbinic institution of impure hands would be incomplete and inconsistent if biblically impure foods did not have the same effect. There is no claim that secondarily impure food defiles fluids to be primarily impure by biblical standards.</i>? Also, since hands, in a case of doubt whether they defiled, impart impurity, so also secondarily impure fluids, in a case of doubt whether they defiled, impart impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">This statement parallels the formulation everywhere in the Babli that impure fluids of any degree act as if impure in the first degree.<br>The entire argument is not relevant for practice, as noted at the end of the paragraph, since <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Tahorot 4:2" href="/Mishnah_Tahorot.4.2">Mishnah <i>Tahorot</i> 4:2</a> declares the food that might have been touched by secondarily impure food as pure. This is a sure sign that the argument here follows R. Aqiba.</i>. Rebbi Ḥanina objected before Rebbi Mana: Look, if somebody eats secondarily impure food and drinks secondarily impure fluids, in a case of doubt whether they defiled, they impart impurity. But secondarily impure foods by biblical standards, in a case of doubt whether they defiled, leave it pure<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">Profane food cannot become impure by secondary impurity. Heave can become impure, but in a public place a doubtful impurity is inactive.</i>! Rebbi Ḥanina said before Rebbi Mana<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">He seems to be R. Ḥanania of Sepphoris arguing before R. Mana II.</i>: Do we not deal with heave? He said to him, even if you say that we deal with heave; is the one who argues “is impure”, “shall become impure”<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Probably he reads יְטַמֵּא “it will defile” in place of יִטְמָא “it shall become impure”. Revocalization of the consonantal text is a technique accepted by R. Aqiba, rejected by R. Ismael.</i> as word of the Torah not Rebbi Aqiba<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">If secondary impurity is active, it must follow R. Aqiba who equates profane and heave foods in their stages of impurity. For everybody else, the arguments are irrelevant.</i>?
There<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">In Babylonia. However, in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 17a" href="/Pesachim.17a">Babli, <i>Pesaḥim</i> 17a</a>, only one verse is discussed and Rav thinks that the question was about fourth degree impurity and the Cohen’s answer was wrong but Samuel thinks that the question was about fifth degree and the answer was correct. Modern commentaries on <a class="refLink" data-ref="Haggai 2:12-13" href="/Haggai.2.12-13"><i>Ḥaggai</i> 2:12–13</a> do not contribute to the understanding of the verses.</i>, they say: The prophet Haggai asked two questions; one they answered correctly and one incorrectly. <sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Haggai 2:12" href="/Haggai.2.12"><i>Ḥag.</i> 2:12</a>. In the verse, the question is הֲיִקְדָּשׁ “is it going to be forbidden as sanctified food?”; cf. Introduction to Tractate <i>Kilaim.</i> Sanctified food can be forbidden only if it is either impure or out of its proper place or time. Since the second type of prohibition is not applicable here, the question must be one of impurity.</i>“Assume a man carries holy meat in the corner of his garment.” The corner is of primary impurity, the holy meat of second degree, bread and soup of third degree, wine, oil, and food of the fourth. Does there exist a fourth degree of impurity for sanctified food? “The Cohanim answered, and said: No.” They did not answer correctly, since there is a fourth degree of impurity for sanctified food<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">This argument gives biblical status to impurity of the fourth degree but rabbinical to the primary impurity of all impure fluids. Therefore, it belongs to the tradition of R. Aqiba.</i>. “Ḥaggai said, if a person impure by the impurity of the dead would touch all of these, would they be impure?” If the corner would be impure by the impurity of the dead<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">In that case, the garment of the mourner would have original impurity and the sacrificial meat would be impure in the first degree.</i> and touched these. Would it be impure? “The Cohanim answered and said, it would be impure.” They answered correctly. But Rebbi Jeremiah, Rebbi Ḥiyya said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: He asked them before they decided about fourth degree [impurity] for sanctified food<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">In that case, both answers were correct.</i>. Then why did he curse them<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">He really did not curse them but needed the answer “impure” as a starting point of his sermon.</i>? As a person who looks for a pretext against his neighbor. What difference does it make for the House? Is that not what he said: “What they are sacrificing there is impure<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Haggai 2:14" href="/Haggai.2.14"><i>Ḥag.</i> 2:14</a>.</i>”? That is what Rebbi Simon bar Zavdi said, they found the skull of Oman the Jebusite<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">A person with gonorrhea imparts impurity to anything he moves, even if he never touched it and it was lying on a platform that could never become impure (such as a flat wooden plank.) Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 2:3:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.2.3.6"><i>Demay</i> Chapter 2, Note 163</a>.</i> under the altar.
Rebbi Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Abba bar Cahana: They were expert in pushings<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">A person with gonorrhea imparts impurity to anything he moves, even if he never touched it and it was lying on a platform that could never become impure (such as a flat wooden plank.) Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 2:3:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.2.3.6"><i>Demay</i> Chapter 2, Note 163</a>.</i> but not expert in slight impurities. <sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Haggai 2:12" href="/Haggai.2.12"><i>Ḥag.</i> 2:12</a>. In the verse, the question is הֲיִקְדָּשׁ “is it going to be forbidden as sanctified food?”; cf. Introduction to Tractate <i>Kilaim.</i> Sanctified food can be forbidden only if it is either impure or out of its proper place or time. Since the second type of prohibition is not applicable here, the question must be one of impurity.</i>“Assume a man carries holy meat,” he asked them whether someone impure by the impurity of the dead imparts impurity by pushing. “The Cohanim answered, and said: No;” they answered him correctly. He asked them whether someone impure by the impurity of the dead imparts slight impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">A person with gonorrhea imparts original impurity to anything he sits on (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 15:10" href="/Leviticus.15.10">Lev. 15:10</a>) directly or indirectly. Anything which is above the person becomes impure rabbinically in a “slight impurity”; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Zavim 4:6" href="/Mishnah_Zavim.4.6">Mishnah <i>Zavim</i> 4:6</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 2:3:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.2.3.6"><i>Demay</i> Chapter 2, Note 162</a> and J. Levy’s long explanation in his Dictionary, vol. 3, s. v. מדף, with H. L. Fleischer’s long etymological note, p. 305.</i>. “The Cohanim answered and said: It will be impure.” They did not answer correctly, for the impurity of the dead does not impart slight impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">Both kinds of impurity are restricted to persons whose own body is the source of the impurity.</i>.
Rebbi Tanḥuma, Rebbi Phineas, in the name of Rebbi Levi: He asked them about the fifth degree of sanctified food. <sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Haggai 2:12" href="/Haggai.2.12"><i>Ḥag.</i> 2:12</a>. In the verse, the question is הֲיִקְדָּשׁ “is it going to be forbidden as sanctified food?”; cf. Introduction to Tractate <i>Kilaim.</i> Sanctified food can be forbidden only if it is either impure or out of its proper place or time. Since the second type of prohibition is not applicable here, the question must be one of impurity.</i>“Assume a man carries holy meat in the corner of his garment.” The corner is of primary impurity, the holy meat of second degree, bread and soup third, wine and oil fourth, and the food of the fifth degree. Does there exist fifth degree impurity for sanctified food? “The Cohanim answered, and said: No.” They did answer correctly, since there is no fifth degree impurity for sanctified food<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">This argument gives biblical status to impurity of the fourth degree but rabbinical to the primary impurity of all impure fluids. Therefore, it belongs to the tradition of R. Aqiba.</i>. Then why did he curse them<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">He really did not curse them but needed the answer “impure” as a starting point of his sermon.</i>? As a person who looks for a pretext against his neighbor. What difference does it make for the House? Is that not what Rebbi Simon bar Zavdi said, they found the skull of Ornan the Jebusite<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">A person with gonorrhea imparts impurity to anything he moves, even if he never touched it and it was lying on a platform that could never become impure (such as a flat wooden plank.) Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 2:3:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.2.3.6"><i>Demay</i> Chapter 2, Note 163</a>.</i> under the altar.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Tzav, Section 9 2" href="/Sifra,_Tzav,_Section_9.2"><i>Sifra Ṣaw Parasha</i> 9:2</a>, quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Chagigah 3:2:2-10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Chagigah.3.2.2-10"><i>Ḥagigah</i> 3:2</a>, fol. 79b; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 29b" href="/Sotah.29b">Babli 29b</a>, <i>Pesaḥim</i> 18b/19a, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chagigah 24a" href="/Chagigah.24a"><i>Ḥagigah</i> 24a</a>.</i>: “Rebbi Yose said, from where that the fourth degree is disabled in sanctified food? It is an argument. Since one who lacks expiation<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">If a person whose body was an original source of impurity is healed, he needs immersion in water to be pure and also a ceremony of expiation to be admitted to the Sanctuary and sacrifices (for the person afflicted with skin disease, <i>Lev,</i> 14:32; for the persons healed from genital discharges 15:14–15, 29–30; for the woman after childbirth 12:6–8). After immersion in water, the person is totally pure at any place other than the Sanctuary.</i> is not disabled for heave<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Chagigah (Lieberman) 3:17" href="/Tosefta_Chagigah_(Lieberman).3.17">Tosephta <i>Ḥagigah</i> 3:17</a>.</i> but disabled for sanctified food, it is only logical that third degree [impurity] which is disabled for heave should disable for sanctified food. That means, we learned third degree from a verse<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">In the Mishnah, following R. Aqiba.</i> and fourth degree from an argument <i>de minore ad majus</i>.” Rebbi Joḥanan objected: Food that was touched by a <i>ṭevul yom</i> is a counterexample, because it is disabled in the case of heave but not disabled in the case of sanctified food<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Chagigah (Lieberman) 3:16" href="/Tosefta_Chagigah_(Lieberman).3.16">Tosephta <i>Ḥagigah</i> 3:16</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Emor, Chapter 4 8" href="/Sifra,_Emor,_Chapter_4.8"><i>Sifra Emor Pereq</i> 4(8</a>).<br>It is proved that sanctified food is not in all respects more restrictive than sacrificial meat. It is remarkable that the Babli does not argue against the thesis of R. Yose since it clearly violates the rule דַּיּוֹ לָבוֹא מִן הַדִּין לִהְיוֹת כְּנָדוּן “it is enough if inference drawn from an argument be equal to the premiss” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 25a" href="/Bava_Kamma.25a"><i>Baba Qama</i> 25a</a>). According to this rule, a passive impurity in the minor cannot become an active one in the major. The Yerushalmi knows no such rule; it needs the counter-example of R. Joḥanan. The difference between the Talmudim is that for the Yerushalmi, <i>de minore ad majus</i> is a rhetorical device but for the Babli it is part of a meta-logical system (cf. the author’s <i>Logical Problems in Jewish Tradition,</i> in: <i>Confrontations with Judaism,</i> Ph. Longworth, ed., London 1966; pp. 171–196.)</i>. Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Rebbi Yose argues the method of his teacher Rebbi Aqiba. Just as Rebbi Aqiba says “it will be impure”, it makes impure by the words of the Torah, so Rebbi Yose says “it will be impure”, it makes impure by the words of the Torah. Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Yose bar Ḥanina: Rebbi Yose does not need that argument <i>de minore ad majus.</i> Rebbi Yose explains the verse<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">A similar interpretation is given as an additional <i>baraita</i> in the Babli, cf. Note 94. The authorship of the <i>baraita</i> is not indicated in the Babli. The verse quoted is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:19" href="/Leviticus.7.19"><i>Lev.</i> 7:19</a>, the subject of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Tzav, Section 9" href="/Sifra,_Tzav,_Section_9"><i>Sifra Ṣaw Parasha</i> 9</a>.</i>: “Any meat which would touch”, that is second degree food because it touched primary impurity; “anything impure”, that is third degree food because it touched second degree impurity; “shall not be eaten”, the endstage of impurity cannot be eaten.
So far about solid food that became impure in the air space of a clay vessel<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:33" href="/Leviticus.11.33"><i>Lev.</i> 11:33</a>, speaking of dead reptiles (which are carriers of original impurity) falling into a clay vessel. In the biblical laws of impurity, no defilement is imparted to a clay vessel touched by impurities from the outside. But if the clay vessel encloses a space that can be covered and something of original impurity enters the space (even before it touches any wall), the entire vessel becomes impure in the first degree. {Degrees of impurity are explained in the commentary to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 2:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.2.3.1"><i>Demay</i>, Chapter 2, Note 137</a>.} Therefore, any food inside the vessel becomes impure in the second degree. If that food touches food susceptible to tertiary impurity, the latter becomes impure in the third degree. Which food can become impure in the third and fourth degrees is a matter of discussion in the Halakhah.<br>The same verse states that a clay vessel can be purified only by being broken. The shards become pure writing material.</i> which had become impure by a reptile<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">A dead reptile from the 8 kinds enumerated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:29-30" href="/Leviticus.11.29-30"><i>Lev.</i> 11:29–30</a>.</i>. What about solid food that became impure directly from a reptile? Is that not an argument? Since vessels, which cannot become impure in the air space of a clay vessel that became impure by a reptile, become impure by contact with a reptile<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">Vessels can become impure only from original impurity (a “father” or “grandfather” of impurity, never from derivative impurity.) There is no verse which would indicate otherwise (but in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 1:7:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.1.7.2-4"><i>Pesaḥim</i> 1:7</a>, fol. 27d, R. Ismael is quoted to the effect that <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:33" href="/Leviticus.11.33"><i>Lev.</i> 11:33</a> also applies to vessels. It may be a veiled reference to the argument presented here.)</i> to defile solid food, [is it not logical that solid food, which becomes impure in the air space of a clay vessel that became impure by a reptile, should become impure by contact with a reptile to defile solid food.] So far, following Rebbi Aqiba. Following Rebbi Ismael? Rebbi Ismael stated: <sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:19" href="/Leviticus.7.19"><i>Lev.</i> 7:19</a>.</i>“Any meat which would touch anything impure”, that is first degree food which touched any impurity, “shall not be eaten”, to add a second degree of impurity. The third degree from where? It is an argument. Since a <i>ṭevul yom</i> who is not disabled for profane food disables heave, it is only logical that a person secondarily impure, who disables profane food should disable heave. The fourth degree for sacrifices from where? It is an argument. Since one who lacks expiation<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">If a person whose body was an original source of impurity is healed, he needs immersion in water to be pure and also a ceremony of expiation to be admitted to the Sanctuary and sacrifices (for the person afflicted with skin disease, <i>Lev,</i> 14:32; for the persons healed from genital discharges 15:14–15, 29–30; for the woman after childbirth 12:6–8). After immersion in water, the person is totally pure at any place other than the Sanctuary.</i> who does not disable heave disables sanctified food<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Chagigah (Lieberman) 3:17" href="/Tosefta_Chagigah_(Lieberman).3.17">Tosephta <i>Ḥagigah</i> 3:17</a>.</i>, it is only logical that third degree [impurity] which disables<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">Everywhere here, פוסל “disables” should read פסול “is disabled”, except the second occurrence (which infringes on the rules of דַּיּוֹ, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 5:2:10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.5.2.10">Note 98</a>).</i> heave should disable sanctified food. That means, we learned first and second degrees from a verse, the third from an argument and the fourth from an argument <i>de minore ad majus.</i> Can one pile argument on argument<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">It is a principle accepted in both Talmudim that at least for any rules of sacrifices and connected matters, most hermeneutical rules cannot be used one after the other; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 8:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.8.1.4"><i>Yebamot</i> 8:1, Note 19</a>. A detailed table of legal and illegal combinations, derived from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 47a:12-57b:12" href="/Zevachim.47a.12-57b.12">Babli <i>Zebaḥim</i> Chapter 5</a>, appears in the author’s paper <i>Über ein bemerkenswertes logisches System aus der Antike,</i> Methodos 1951, pp. 150–164.</i>? Everything is subject to practice, i. e., that third degree disables heave and fourth degree disables sacrifices<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Chagigah (Lieberman) 3:8" href="/Tosefta_Chagigah_(Lieberman).3.8"><i>Tosephta Ḥagigah</i> 3:8</a>. “Practice” here corresponds to “Practice of Moses from Mount Sinai” in the Babli, generally accepted practice whose roots can no longer be ascertained. The status of such practice is more than rabbinic and less than biblical.</i>.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> On the same day<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">According to the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 28a" href="/Berakhot.28a">Babli (<i>Berakhot</i> 28a</a>), any Mishnah which starts “on the same day” was formulated on the day Rabban Gamliel was deposed. There is no proof that this ever was a Galilean tradition.</i>, Rebbi Aqiba explained: <sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 35:5" href="/Numbers.35.5"><i>Num.</i> 35:5</a>, speaking of the cities to be given to the Levites.</i>“You shall measure outside the city 2000 cubits to the East,” but in another verse it says <sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 35:4" href="/Numbers.35.4"><i>Num.</i> 35:4</a>.</i>“outside the city wall, one thousand cubits all around.” It is impossible to say 1000 cubits because already it was said 2000 cubits and it is impossible to say 2000 cubits because already it was said 1000 cubits. Why was 1000 said, and why 2000? But 1000 cubits open space<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">Not for living and not for agriculture.</i> and 2000 cubits Sabbath domain<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">To say that not only for levitic cities but for all cities a belt 2000 cubits wide belongs to the city. Therefore, when it says “Nobody shall leave his place of the Seventh Day” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 16:29" href="/Exodus.16.29"><i>Ex.</i> 16:29</a>), it is implied that on the Sabbath a city dweller may walk in a belt 2000 cubits wide around the city.</i>. Rebbi Eliezer, the son of Rebbi Yose the Galilean, said: 1000 cubits open space and two thousand fields and vineyards<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that the determination of the Sabbath domain as 2000 cubits outside the city wall is rabbinic, but that the belt 2000 cubits wide was given to the Levites and that the outer 1000 cubits were used for agriculture. {Sadducee practice limited walking on the Sabbath to 1000 cubits outside the city, agreeing with R. Eliezer; CD X:21.}</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “On the same day, Rebbi Aqiba explained: “You shall measure outside the city”, etc. Rebbi Eliezer, the son of Rebbi Yose the Galilean, said, 2000 cubits the domain of the levitic cities. Deduct from them 1000 cubits open space, there results one quarter open space and the remainder fields<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">This is difficult to understand. A little geometry is needed here (cf. H. Guggenheimer, <i>Applicable Geometry</i>, Huntington NY 1977.) Given an oval (a plane convex figure) of area <i>A</i> and perimeter <i>L</i>, the area of the <i>parallel</i> <i>figure</i> in distance <i>h,</i> obtained by adding to the figure every exterior point of distance ≤ <i>h</i> is<br><i>A</i>(<i>h</i>)<i>= A</i>+<i>hL</i>+ π<i>h</i><sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup>.<br>The excess area of the parallel figure over the original oval is<br><i>E</i>(<i>h</i>) = <i>hL</i> + π<i>h</i><sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup>.<br>However, if the city has rectangular shape, in tractate <i>Erubin</i> the Sabbath domain is determined as the rectangle whose sides are 2000 cubits from the (straight) walls of the city. In that case, the rectangle defined by lines in distance <i>h</i> has surface area<br><i>A</i>(<i>h</i>) = <i>A</i> + <i>hL</i> + <i>4h</i><sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><br>with excess <i>E</i>(<i>h</i>) = <i>hL</i> + <i>4h</i><sup>2</sup>. Let the unit of <i>h</i> be 1000 cubits. In both cases, the condition <i>E</i>(2) = 4<i>E</i>(1) yields 2<i>L</i> = 0. It follows that <i>L</i> = 0: the original oval was a point. The open space is one quarter of the area of the fields only if there is no town; it is a limit case. The parallel text of the entire Halakhah is in <i>Erubin</i> 5, fol. 22d; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Makkot 2:6:2-7:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Makkot.2.6.2-7.4"><i>Makkot</i> 2:7,</a> fol. 32a.<br>The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 56b" href="/Eruvin.56b">Babli, <i>Eruvin</i> 56b</a>, explains the problem away by replacing the condition <i>E</i>(2) = 4<i>E</i>(1) by <i>A</i>(2) = 4<i>E</i>(1), computing <i>A</i>(2) by the second formula and <i>E</i>(1) by the first, and assuming π = 3; clearly recognizing the impossibility of the original statement.</i>. Rebbi Jeremiah, Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac in the name of Rav: A quarter of 1000<sup>113</sup><i class="footnote">This is a radical re-interpretation of the verse: The open space is only 250 cubits wide on each side of the town. Then the problems disappear.</i>. Rebbi Isaac said, even if you say a quarter from every side, it is a quarter. He who wants to make a square from four amphoras needs sixteen<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">A row of length <i>n</i> defines a square of area <i>n</i><sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup>. For the problem at hand, the remark is irrelevant.</i>. Rebbi Mana estimated it from a brick, Rebbi Abin estimated it from a strip, Rebbi Oshaiah estimated it from a dish<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">Rebbis Mana and Abin derived the second formula of Note 112 by supposing the town walls to form a rectangle; Rebbi Oshaia supposed the town to be of circular shape and derived the first formula. Neither of them accepted the 1:4 ratio.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, 50 by 50 form a <i>bet se’ah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">An equivalent statement in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 23b" href="/Eruvin.23b">Babli <i>Eruvin</i> 23b</a>.</i>; 100 by 100 are four bet <i>se’ah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">Since 22 = 4.</i>. As it happened to the Resh Galuta when it was imposed on him<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">By the government, Parthian or Persan.</i> to fill a hall of 40 by 40 cubits with wheat. He came to Rav Huna who said to him, negotiate that they should take from you 20 by 20 now and 20 by 20 later; you will earn half<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">If the government officials are so ignorant of geometry that they do not realize that only half the surface area will be covered.</i>.
Rebbi Abba in the name of Rav Jehudah, Rebbi Ze‘ira in the name of Rav Uqba: One strip-measures<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">One determines the distance not on the ground but as if from a plane map of the domain. This means that on an incline one keeps the measuring rope horizontal.<br><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 5:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.5.4.1">Mishnah <i>Erubin</i> 5:4</a> states that “one measures only with a rope of 50 cubits, no less and no more”. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 58b" href="/Eruvin.58b">Babli, <i>Erubin</i> 58b</a>, disagrees with the Yerushalmi and notes that once one has to strip-measure, i. e. that the rope has to be held horizontally, away from the ground, a rope of 4 cubits is required. From a practical point of view, this seems to be the superior way of measuring. For מקדדין (cf. Arabic قدّ “to make strips”), the Babli has מקדרין.</i> only with a rope of 50 cubits. Rebbi Ze‘ira in the name of Rav Ḥisda: One strip-measures neither for the levitic cities nor from the distance for breaking the neck in the wadi<sup class="footnote-marker">121,</sup><i class="footnote">Breaking a calf's neck in a wild place to atone for an unsolved murder, Deut. 21:1:9.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli Erubin 58b this is a statement by Rav Nahman in the name of Rav Abba bar Abuh.</i>. This is acceptable for him who says, 1000 cubits of open space and 2000 cubits of Sabbath domain. But for him who says, 1000 cubits of open space and 2000 cubits of fields and vineyards, did they not learn the Sabbath domain from the levitic cities<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">Since the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 5:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.5.4.1">Mishnah, <i>Erubin</i> 5:4</a>, requires that Sabbath domains be measured as one would do on a map, it seems impossible that R. Eliezer ben R. Yose the Galilean accept R. Aqiba’s opinion that the extent of the Sabbath domain is fixed in the Torah by the extent of the levitic cities, since the methods of measurement are different in both cases. The Babli, Note 122, gives as reason that for levitic cities and the breaking of the calf’s neck one has to measure on the ground, that these are measurements prescribed by the Torah. The obvious inference is that the distance indicated for the Sabbath domain is rabbinic. (The opponents of R. Aqiba hold that the biblical Sabbath distance is the diameter of the encampment of the Israelites in the plains of Jericho, which in both Talmudim is described as 12 <i>mīl</i>, 24 000 cubits; cf. <i>Šebi‘it</i> 6:1, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 5:1:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.5.1.5">Note 28</a>). Therefore, it is permissible to extend the Sabbath domain even of a levitic city further than its political boundaries.</i>? For the main thing one does not strip-measure; does one strip-measure for the derivative?
From where that one does not bury in a levitic city? Rebbi Abbahu<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">Since the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 5:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.5.4.1">Mishnah, <i>Erubin</i> 5:4</a>, requires that Sabbath domains be measured as one would do on a map, it seems impossible that R. Eliezer ben R. Yose the Galilean accept R. Aqiba’s opinion that the extent of the Sabbath domain is fixed in the Torah by the extent of the levitic cities, since the methods of measurement are different in both cases. The Babli, Note 122, gives as reason that for levitic cities and the breaking of the calf’s neck one has to measure on the ground, that these are measurements prescribed by the Torah. The obvious inference is that the distance indicated for the Sabbath domain is rabbinic. (The opponents of R. Aqiba hold that the biblical Sabbath distance is the diameter of the encampment of the Israelites in the plains of Jericho, which in both Talmudim is described as 12 <i>mīl</i>, 24 000 cubits; cf. <i>Šebi‘it</i> 6:1, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 5:1:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.5.1.5">Note 28</a>). Therefore, it is permissible to extend the Sabbath domain even of a levitic city further than its political boundaries.</i> in the name of Rebbi Yose bar Ḥanina: “Their open spaces shall be for their animals, their property, and their lives.”<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 35:3" href="/Numbers.35.3"><i>Num</i>. 35:3</a>.</i> They were given for their animals and their lives; the were not given for burial<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">The cemetery of the Levites has to be on the territory of the surrounding tribe.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> On the same day<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">According to the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 28a" href="/Berakhot.28a">Babli (<i>Berakhot</i> 28a</a>), any Mishnah which starts “on the same day” was formulated on the day Rabban Gamliel was deposed. There is no proof that this ever was a Galilean tradition.</i>, Rebbi Aqiba explained: “<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 15:1" href="/Exodus.15.1"><i>Ex</i>. 15:1</a>.</i> Then would Moses and the Children of Israel sing the following song to the Eternal to tell.” The verse should not have said “to tell”; why does the verse say “to tell”? It was that Israel were repeating after Moses every word, as one reads the <i>Hallel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 113-118" href="/Psalms.113-118"><i>Ps</i>. 113–118</a>, the holiday songs. The way of recital is described in the Tosephta/Halakhah.</i>: “I shall sing to the Eternal for He is very highly high;” therefore “saying” was said. Rebbi Neḥemiah said, they were reciting it as one recites the <i>Šema</i>‘<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">The entire recital which starts with “Hear, o Israel,” cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.1.1.1"><i>Berakhot</i> Chapter 1, Note 1</a>.</i>, not as one reads the <i>Hallel</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “On the same day, Rebbi Aqiba explained: “Then would Moses sing,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sotah (Lieberman) 6:2-4" href="/Tosefta_Sotah_(Lieberman).6.2-4">Tosephta <i>Soṭah</i> 6:2–4</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 30b-31a" href="/Sotah.30b-31a">Babli 30b/31a</a>. A small part of the text is in <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael, Shirah, Parashah</i>1 (<i>Mekhilta dR. Simeon bar Ioḥai,</i> p. 72, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 5:4:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.5.4.3">Note 137</a>).</i> Like a minor who recites the <i>Hallel</i> in school and all repeat every word after him<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">The entire class, until they know <i>Hallel</i> by heart.</i>. Moses said, “I shall sing<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 15:1" href="/Exodus.15.1"><i>Ex</i>. 15:1</a>.</i>”, and they repeat after him: “I shall sing”. Moses said: “Strength”, and they say: “Strength<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 15:2" href="/Exodus.15.2"><i>Ex</i>. 15:2</a>.</i>”. Rebbi Eliezer the son of Rebbi Yose the Galilean said, like an adult who recites the <i>Hallel</i> in the synagogue, and they repeat the first word<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">This is prescribed by the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 38b" href="/Sukkah.38b">Babli, <i>Sukkah</i> 38b</a>, and retained in the Yemenite <i>balādī</i> rite, where the congregation answer <i>hallelujah</i> (the first word of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 113" href="/Psalms.113"><i>Ps</i>. 113</a>) at each caesura and end of sentence. The Galilean Amoraic way was for several people to recite the <i>Hallel</i>, with each of them saying half a verse (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 8:8:2-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.8.8.2-7"><i>Berakhot</i> 8:9</a> fol. 12c, <i>Megillah</i> 1:11 fol. 72a).</i>. Moses said, “I shall sing”, and they repeat: “I shall sing”. Moses said: “My strength”, and they repeat after him: “I shall sing”. Rebbi Yose the Galilean says, when our forefathers were in the sea, the toddler was resting on his mother’s knees and the baby was suckling from his mother’s breast; but when they saw the Divine Glory, the toddler lifted his head from his mother’s knees and the baby took away his mouth from his mother’s breast; they opened their mouths in song and praise and said, “that is my God and I shall declare Him Beautiful<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 15:2" href="/Exodus.15.2"><i>Ex</i>. 15:2</a>.</i>”. Rebbi Meïr said, even the fetuses were saying a song from their mothers’ wombs, as it is said: “<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 68:27" href="/Psalms.68.27"><i>Ps</i>. 68:27</a>. In Mishnaic Hebrew, מָקוֹר means the female womb; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 20:18" href="/Leviticus.20.18"><i>Lev</i>. 20:18</a>.</i> In choirs praise God, the Eternal from the source of Israel.” Rebbi Nehemiah said, when our forefathers rose from the sea they saw the corpses of the sinners who had subjected them to hard, forced labor, all of them dead corpses thrown on the sea shore. They wanted to sing and the holy spirit rested upon them. Even the most insignificant in Israel sang the song just as Moses did. That is what is written: “<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 63:11" href="/Isaiah.63.11"><i>Is</i>. 63:11</a>. The verse ends: “Where is He Who put His Holy Spirit in their midst.”</i> He remembered the days of old, Moses, his people; where is He Who raised them from the sea?” It is not written “the sheeps’ shepherd” but “His sheeps’ shepherd”; this teaches that He turned all of them into shepherds<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">Since the first half of the verse equates Moses and His people, the singular of the second clause is taken as collective.</i>.
Why does the verse say, “to tell”? To tell to [future] generations. Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Ḥanina, the entire verse. Moses said, “I shall sing”, and they recite after his lead: “I shall sing to the Eternal for He is very highly high; horse and rider He cast into the sea<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 15:1" href="/Exodus.15.1"><i>Ex</i>. 15:1</a>.</i>.” Moses said: “strength and growth”, and they recite after his lead: “strength and growth is Yah<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 15:2" href="/Exodus.15.2"><i>Ex</i>. 15:2</a>.</i>”.<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">In the <i>Mekhiltot</i>, this is attributed to R. Eleazar ben Thaddai, a Tanna whose time is difficult to determine and who is never quoted in Yerushalmi sources. Cf. B.-Z. Wacholder, <i>The Date of Mekilta deRabbi Ishmael</i>, HUCA 39 (1968), 117–144.</i> It is written: <sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Judges 5:2" href="/Judges.5.2"><i>Jud</i>. 5:2</a>. In rabbinic Hebrew, פרע means “to pay one’s debt”.</i>“When retribution is retributed for Israel, when the people volunteers;” the heads of the people volunteered. When the Holy One, praise to Him, does wonders for Israel, they should sing a song. They objected: There is the redemption from Egypt! That is something else since it was the beginning of their redemption<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">Meaning that the Song of the Sea also covers the Exodus, since the latter was confirmed only at the Sea. In the Babli and midrashic literature, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 113" href="/Psalms.113"><i>Ps</i>. 113</a> is the song of the Israelites during Passover night [<a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 117a" href="/Pesachim.117a"><i>Pesaḥim</i> 117a</a>; cf. <i>Cant. rabba</i> 1(37), <i>Mekhilta deR. Ismael, Shirah</i> 1; <i>deR. Simeon bar Ioḥai</i> p. 71]. Cf. the author’s <i>The Scholar’s Haggadah</i>, pp. 314–319.</i>. They objected. There is redemption of Mardocai and Esther<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">Who instituted a “day of feasting and joy” but not the recitation of <i>Hallel, Esth</i>. 9:22.</i>! That is something else since they were outside the Land<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 14a" href="/Megillah.14a">Babli, <i>Megillah</i> 14a</a>.</i>; some want to say that Mardocai and Esther were freed of their enemies, they were not freed from [Gentile] government<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 14a" href="/Megillah.14a">Babli, <i>Megillah</i> 14a</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> On the same day, Joshua ben Hyrkanos explained that Job served the Holy One, praise to Him, only out of love<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">His acts represent <i>amor dei</i>, not fear of punishment in the World to Come; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 5:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.5.6.1">Mishnah 7</a>.</i>, as it is said<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Job 13:15" href="/Job.13.15"><i>Job</i> 13:15</a>. The <i>Ketib</i> is לא איחל, the <i>Qere</i> לוֹ איחל. The <i>Qere</i> represents the majority of mss. before the Masoretes. The Mishnah shows that the difference of readings existed many Centuries before the Masoretes.</i>: “Behold, even if He will kill me, to Him I pray.” But the matter is still in suspense<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">Between authenticity of <i>Ketib</i> or <i>Qere</i>.</i>, “to Him I look forward” or “I do not look forward to Him”. The verse says, “until I die I shall not remove my innocence from me<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Job 27:5" href="/Job.27.5"><i>Job</i> 27:5</a>.</i>,” this teaches that he did it out of love.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “On the same day, Joshua ben Hyrcanos explained,” etc. It was stated in the name of Rebbi Jehudah: “God lives<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Job 27:2" href="/Job.27.2"><i>Job</i> 27:2</a>. This is an oath formula. Since nobody swears by the life of the king unless he loves the king, the verse supports R. Joshua ben Hyrkanos.</i> Who removed my lawsuit, and the Ruler made me bitter;” for nobody swears by the King’s life unless he loves Him.
