They said in the name of Rebbi Nathan: “Also He is my help, for insincerity will not come before Him.<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Job 13:16" href="/Job.13.16"><i>Job</i> 13:16</a>; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 5:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.5.5.1">Note 143</a>. The verse following the one in dispute shows that לו is the only consistent reading.</i>”
One verse says, “you shall love the Eternal, your God,<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 6:5" href="/Deuteronomy.6.5"><i>Deut</i>. 6:5</a>.</i>” and another verse says, “fear the Eternal, your God, and Him you should serve.<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 6:13" href="/Deuteronomy.6.13"><i>Deut</i>. 6:13</a>.</i>” <sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">From here to the end of the Halakhah the text, with minor variations, is also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 9:5:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.9.5.6"><i>Berakhot</i> 9:6, Notes 241–255</a>.</i> Act from love and act from fear. Act from love, because if you are tempted to hate, know that you must love and a lover is no hater. Act from fear, because if you are tempted to rebel, know that you must fear and one who fears does not rebel.
There are seven kinds of religious people: Religious on the shoulder, religious on credit, religious balancing, religious “what is the deduction,” religious “I shall do it when I realize my guilt,” religious from fear, religious from love. Religious on the shoulder, he carries his deeds on his shoulder. Religious on credit, “give me credit that I can perform commandments.” Religious balancing, he commits one sin and observes one commandment and balances one against the other. Religious “what is the deduction,” what I have that is what I am using to deduct for doing a commandment. Religious “I shall do it when I realize my guilt,” I committed that sin and therefore I shall do this good deed to counteract it<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">A slightly different list with completely different interpretations in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 22b" href="/Sotah.22b">Babli <i>Soṭa</i> 22b</a> declares explicitly that the first five are “destroyers of the world.” In the Yerushalmi, it is clear that the first five are considered in a negative light but they are still considered better than one who performs no commandments at all.</i>. Religious from fear, like Job. Religious from love, like Abraham. No one is beloved as much as the religious from love, like Abraham.
Our forefather Abraham turned the evil instincts into good ones. What is the reason? (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Nehemiah 9:8" href="/Nehemiah.9.8"><i>Neh.</i> 9:8</a>): “You found his heart trustworthy before You.” Rebbi Aḥa said, he compromised, from “concluding a covenant with him.” But David could not stand it and killed it in his heart. What is the reason? (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 109:22" href="/Psalms.109.22"><i>Ps.</i> 109:22</a>) “But my heart is slain in me.”
Rebbi Aqiba was tortured before the evil Tineius Rufus. There came the time for reciting the <i>Shema‘.</i> He started to read and laughed. He said to him: Old man, you are either a sorcerer or one contemptuous of suffering. He said to him: The spirit of this man should be blown away; I am neither a sorcerer nor contemptuous of sufferings. But all my life I read this verse (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 6:5" href="/Deuteronomy.6.5"><i>Deut.</i> 6:5</a>): “You must love the Eternal, your God, with all your heart, all your soul, and all your force.” I loved Him with all my heart. I loved Him with all my money. But whether with all my soul I could not test. But now, when “with all your soul” came, the time of reciting the <i>Shema‘</i> has arrived and my mind has not wavered, therefore I am reciting and laughing.
Neḥemiah from Emmaus served Rebbi Aqiba for 22 years and he taught him “את and גם mean additions, אך and רק mean exclusions.” He asked him: What means that which is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 6:13" href="/Deuteronomy.6.13"><i>Deut.</i> 6:13</a>) “את the Eternal, your God, you must fear.” He said to him, Him and His Torah.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Rebbi Joshua said, who would remove the dust from your eyes, Rabban Joḥanan ben Zakkai, since all your days you explained that Job served the Omnipresent only out of fear, as it was said: “A man, artless and straight, fearing God and fleeing from evil<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Job 1:1" href="/Job.1.1"><i>Job</i> 1:1</a>.</i>”; but behold, Joshua, your student’s student<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">It is not clear whose student Joshua ben Hyrkanos was. He probably is not mentioned anywhere else in tannaïtic literature.</i> deduced that he did it out of love!
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Rebbi Joshua said, who would remove the dust from your eyes, Rabban Joḥanan ben Zakkai,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote">The parallel is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 15" href="/Bava_Batra.15">Babli, <i>Baba Batra</i> 15a/b</a>.</i> When was Job? Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish in the name of Bar Qappara: He was in the days of our father Abraham; that is what is written: “<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Job 1:1" href="/Job.1.1"><i>Job</i> 1:1</a>.</i> A man was in the land of Oz, his name was Job.” And it is written, “<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Gen.</i> 23:21, in the list of Aramean Naḥor tribes.</i> Oz his firstborn.” Rebbi Abba said<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, this is the opinion of R. Nathan in an alternate version.</i>, in the days of our father Jacob and Dinah was his wife; that is what is written: “<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Job 2:10" href="/Job.2.10"><i>Job</i> 2:10</a>.</i> You speak like one of the impious ones”, and it is written: “<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 34:7" href="/Genesis.34.7"><i>Gen.</i> 34:7</a>.</i> For an impiety he did in Israel.” Rebbi Levi said, he was in the days of the tribes; that is what is written: “<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Job.</i> 15:18. The previous verse ends: This I have seen, I shall tell it; the verses are interpreted as referring to Judah and Reuben who confessed their sins; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 1:4:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.1.4.4">Chapter 1, Note 195</a>.</i> What Sages would tell, they did not conceal from their fathers.” Rebbi Yose ben Ḥalaphta said, he was born when they descended into Egypt and he died when they left<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote">This is an anonymous tannaïtic opinion in the Babli. The traditional duration of the tribes’ stay in Egypt is 210 years; cf. the author’s <i>The Scholar’s Haggadah,</i> pp. 283–284. Job lived another 140 years after his tribulations (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Job 42:16" href="/Job.42.16"><i>Job</i> 42:16</a>). Since God gave double restitution for everything he had lost, it is concluded that he suffered in his 70th year and lived for 210 years.</i>. A parable of a shepherd when a wolf came and attacked his flock. What did he do? He put up a ram against him<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 14b" href="/Sotah.14b">Babli, 14b</a>, this is hinted at by the statement that Job’s goats were able to attack wolves, being supernaturally safe from predators.</i>. That is what is written: “<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Job 16:11" href="/Job.16.11"><i>Job</i> 16:11</a>.</i> He delivered me to the evil one, he threw me amongst evildoers.” Rebbi Ismael stated: Job was one of Pharao’s servants, a great one in his government<sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote">In the later Midrash, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shemot Rabbah 1:12" href="/Shemot_Rabbah.1.12"><i>Ex. rabba</i>1(12</a>), this is extended to include in Pharao’s council Bileam, who voted for killing the Jewish children and was killed, Job who abstained from voting and suffered, and Jethro who voted against Pharao’s decree and was rewarded in that his descendants sat in the Synhedrion.</i>’<sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Familia</i> as a term for government is from the time of the principate when the emperor ran the state by his freedmen and slaves, his <i>familia.</i></i>. That is what is written: “<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 9:20" href="/Exodus.9.20"><i>Ex.</i> 9:20</a>.</i> One who feared the word of the Eternal etc.”, and it is written about him, “<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Job 1:1" href="/Job.1.1"><i>Job</i> 1:1</a>.</i> a man, artless and straight, fearing God and fleeing from evil<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Job 1:1" href="/Job.1.1"><i>Job</i> 1:1</a>.</i>”. Rebbi Yose bar Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, R. Eleazar.</i> says, he was in the days when the Judges judged; that is what is written: “Behold, you all did see, why do you turn all into vapor<sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Job 27:12" href="/Job.27.12"><i>Job</i> 27:12</a>.</i>.” You saw what my generation did, that they collect tithes on the threshing floors; “you loved whore’s wages on all grain threshing floors<sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Hosea 9:1" href="/Hosea.9.1"><i>Hos.</i> 9:1</a>. Since it is in the hand of the farmer to whom to give his tithes; the Levite or Cohen who comes to the threshing floor to collect his tithes is an extortionist (Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 6:2:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.6.2.7"><i>Demay</i> 6:3, Note 69</a>).</i>.” Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman in the name of Rebbi Jonathan<sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, R. Nathan.</i>: He was in the days of the queen of Seba, as it is said: “Seba attacked and took them<sup class="footnote-marker">171</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Job 1:15" href="/Job.1.15"><i>Job</i> 1:15</a>.</i>.” Rebbi Nathan<sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, the anonymous Sages.</i> said, he was in the days of the Chaldeans, as it is said: “The Chaldeans attacked from three sides.<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Job 1:17" href="/Job.1.17"><i>Job</i> 1:17</a>.</i>” Rebbi Joshua ben Qorḥa<sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote">Same argument in the Babli.</i> said, he was in the days of Asuerus, as it is said: “One shall look for beautiful virgins for the king<sup class="footnote-marker">175</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Esther 2:2" href="/Esther.2.2"><i>Esth.</i> 2:2</a>.</i>.” And it is written, “no women were found like Job’s daughters<sup class="footnote-marker">176</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Job 42:15" href="/Job.42.15"><i>Job</i> 42:15</a>.</i>.” Rebbi Joshua ben Levi said, he was of the returnees of the diaspora. Rebbi Joḥanan said, He was of the returnees from the diaspora and was a Jew<sup class="footnote-marker">177</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, R. Joḥanan and R. Eleazar.</i>. Therefore Rebbi Joḥanan learned from him the rules of mourning. “Job got up and tore his coat<sup class="footnote-marker">178</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Job 1:20" href="/Job.1.20"><i>Job</i> 1:20</a>.</i>”; Rebbi Jehudah ben Pazi in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: From here [one learns] that a mourner has to tear [his garment] while standing<sup class="footnote-marker">179</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Moed Katan 3:7:2-19" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Moed_Katan.3.7.2-19"><i>Mo‘ed Qaṭan</i> 3:7</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 20b" href="/Sotah.20b">Babli 20b</a>, the verse is quoted without asking whether Job was Jewish or not.</i>. Rebbi Ḥiyya<sup class="footnote-marker">180</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli this is an anonymous tannaïtic statement.</i> stated: In My world I had one just Gentile, I gave him his reward and removed him from My world<sup class="footnote-marker">181</sup><i class="footnote">Since Job, in contrast to Jewish saintly persons, received his reward in this world, he seems to be excluded from the World to Come.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish<sup class="footnote-marker">182</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 14a" href="/Sotah.14a">Babli, 14a</a>, this is the opinion of an anonymous author who proves that Scripture in effect contains tales that do not correspond to reality, such as Nathan’s tale of the poor man and his sheep.</i> said, Job did not exist and will never live. The opinion of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish is inconsistent: There, Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said in the name of Bar Qappara: He was in the days of our father Abraham, but here he says so? But he did exist but his suffering did not. Then why is it written about him? To tell you that if it had come upon him, he would have withstood it.
Rebbi Aqiba explained: “Elihu Ben Barakhel the Buzite from the family Ram got angry.<sup class="footnote-marker">183</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Job 32:2" href="/Job.32.2"><i>Job</i>32:2</a>.</i>” Elihu that is Bileam. Ben Barakhel, who came to curse Israel but blessed them. The Buzite<sup class="footnote-marker">184</sup><i class="footnote">From the root בוז “to insult, to degrade”.</i>, whose prophecy was degraded, “who falls down open-eyed.<sup class="footnote-marker">185</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 24:4" href="/Numbers.24.4"><i>Num.</i> 24:4</a>.</i>” From the family Ram, “from Aram did Balaq lead me<sup class="footnote-marker">186</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 23:7" href="/Numbers.23.7"><i>Num.</i> 23:7</a>.</i>.” Rebbi Eleazar ben Azariah said to him, if he is he, then the Omnipresent already covered it up. If he is not he, in the future [world] he will argue with you. But Elihu that is Isaac. Ben Barakhel, the son whom God blessed, as it was said: “The Eternal had blessed him<sup class="footnote-marker">187</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 26:12" href="/Genesis.26.12"><i>Gen.</i> 26:12</a>. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 15b" href="/Bava_Batra.15b">Babli, <i>Baba Batra</i>15b</a>, the consensus it that Elihu was Jewish; cf. Tosaphot <i>s. v.</i> אליהוא. Some commentators note that <a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 25:11" href="/Genesis.25.11"><i>Gen.</i>25:11</a> would have been a better quote since it mentions Isaac’s status as son and uses the term Elohim, contained in the name, and not YHWH, as in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 26:12" href="/Genesis.26.12"><i>Gen.</i>26:12</a>.</i>”. The Buzite<sup class="footnote-marker">184</sup><i class="footnote">From the root בוז “to insult, to degrade”.</i>, who degraded all temples of foreign worship at the moment he was bound on the altar. From the family Ram, the son of Abram.
Moses wrote the five books of the Torah and added the chapters about Balaq and Bileam. He also wrote the book of Job<sup class="footnote-marker">188</sup><i class="footnote">The same statement in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 13b" href="/Bava_Batra.13b">Babli, <i>Baba Batra</i> 13b</a>; only that the last eight verses of the Torah are attributed to Joshua. The chapter about Balaq and Bileam is a recitation of facts, ostensibly not needed as part of the Law.</i>.
“ “A man, artless and straight, fearing God and fleeing from evil<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Job 1:1" href="/Job.1.1"><i>Job</i> 1:1</a>.</i>”. Rebbi Taḥlifa from Caesarea said, he was conciliatory. Rebbi Ze‘ira said to him, is a person who is not conciliatory not acceptable? But he was conciliatory even if cursed<sup class="footnote-marker">189</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is forbidden to hate one’s neighbor in one’s heart (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:17" href="/Leviticus.19.17"><i>Lev.</i>19:17</a>), if the verse attests that he was “fleeing from evil”, this proves that even that prohibition he did not fail to honor.<br>In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 15b" href="/Bava_Batra.15b">Babli, <i>Baba Batra</i> 15b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 28a" href="/Megillah.28a"><i>Megillah</i> 28a</a>, he is declared to have been conciliatory in money matters.</i>.

Chapter 6



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody declared his jealousy to [his wife] and she went to a secluded place<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">He declared his jealousy to his wife and he has no witnesses that she went to a rendez-vous with the presumed paramour.</i>, even if he heard about it from a bird flying by<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">A rumor whose source he cannot trace.</i> he has to divorce her<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">R. Eliezer stated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.1.1.1">Mishnah 1:1</a> that a husband needs no witnesses to bring his wife to the Temple after he declared his jealousy before two witnesses. If he heard a rumor that would give him a reason to act, his wife becomes forbidden to him until after she drank the water and was found innocent. If he is unwilling or unable to bring her to the Temple, he must divorce his wife and pay the <i>ketubah</i> since the divorce is a result of his own action.</i> and pay the <i>ketubah</i>, the words of Rebbi Eliezer. Rebbi Joshua says, from the moment she is the subject of talk of women spinning by moonlight<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">That means, if her infidelity is the talk of the town. R. Joshua holds in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.1.1.1">Mishnah 1:1</a> that the wife can be brought to the Temple only on the testimony of two reliable witnesses. But if her conduct is the talk of the town, the husband would have to assume that the rumor is true and that any sexual relations with her would be potentially sinful.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody declared his jealousy and she went to a secluded place,” etc. Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Yannai: This entire chapter [deals with the case that] he warned her and said to her, do not be at a secluded place with man X, after he declared his jealousy and she went to a secluded place<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">That the husband had some information that his wife met the man forbidden to her.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, even if she did not go to a secluded place<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">If there is not even a single witness against her.</i>. Rebbi Ze‘ira said before Rebbi Yasa: Not that Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish disagrees, only he is lenient about witnesses to the hiding<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">There is no difference of interpretation between R. Joḥanan and R. Simeon ben Laqish; the latter only follows the opinion attributed to R. Eliezer in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.1.1.1">Mishnah 1:1</a>.</i>. We have stated a disagreement. Some state it anonymously<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The opinion attributed to R. Eliezer in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 6:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.6.1.1">Mishnah 6:1</a>.</i>. Rebbi Ze‘ira said before Rebbi Mana<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">R. Mana I.</i>: Rebbi Joshua does not disagree with what Rebbi Eliezer said, only that we have stated: “Rebbi Joshua says, only if<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">This is the reading of the Mishnah in the Babli, which can be read as meaning that if the wife is the talk of the town, even a rumor of unknown origin forces the husband to divorce his wife.</i> she is the subject of talk of women carding by moonlight.” Rebbi Abba Mari asked: There<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">‘<i>Orlah</i>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 2:1:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.2.1.6">Chapter 2, Note 30</a>.</i>, Rebbi Ḥizqiah, Rebbi Abbahu said in the name of Rebbi Eleazar, everywhere where Rebbi taught a disagreement and returned to the problem later and taught it anonymously, practice follows the anonymous opinion. And here he says so<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">If <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 6:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.6.1.1">Mishnah 6:1</a> is stated anonymously, it would imply that in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.1.1.1">Mishnah 1:1</a> practice follows R. Eliezer. However, it is evident not only that general practice follows R. Joshua against R. Eliezer but also that in the case of the suspected wife, two witnesses of her misbehavior are needed to prohibit her to her husband. Therefore, the argument that R. Joshua only makes an anonymous statement precise is invalid.</i>?
We have stated: carders. Some Tannaїm state: spinners. He who said carders, they card wool. He who said spinners, they spin flax.
Where do we hold<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">The information discussed by the women working by moonlight.</i>? If about him who heard and did not know from whom he heard, it is like a bird flying by. If about him who heard and knew from whom he heard, it is a witness reporting the words of another witness<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">This is hearsay evidence about which in the case of a suspected wife Rabbis Eliezer and Joshua disagree in Chapter 1 (Notes 27, 28).</i>. But here we deal with the case that X [heard] from Y and Y from Z<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Hearsay of hearsay.</i>; a word without foundation<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of a Geniza fragment: תמילווסיס, which identifies the final letter as <i>s</i>. <i>Arukh</i> explains as Greek θεμέλιος “of the foundation”; Krauss suggests θεμελίωσις, ἡ, -εως “foundation”.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If a single witness said “I saw her when she became impure”, she could not drink<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Even though by biblical standards a single witness usually is not considered, here he is accepted by biblical standards as explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 6:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.6.3.1">Mishnah 4</a> to bar the husband from bringing his wife to the Temple. He is not a witness in a criminal case (against the wife and her paramour) but his testimony indirectly forces the husband to divorce his wife and also frees him from payment of the <i>ketubah</i> since, as stated at the end of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 6:4:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.6.4.2-4">Halakhah 4</a>, his testimony “has feet”, i. e., after two witnesses for the declaration of jealousy and two witnesses that she went to a secluded place, the testimony that he saw them having sex is credible (if his testimony holds up under cross examination).</i>. Not only that, but even a slave or a slave-girl are believed to cause her to lose her <i>ketubah</i>. Her mother-in-law, the mother-in-law’s daughter, her co-wife, her sister-in-law, and her husband’s daughter are trustworthy<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">These women are barred from testifying to her husband’s death (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Yevamot 15:4" href="/Mishnah_Yevamot.15.4">Mishnah <i>Yebamot</i> 15:4, Notes 81</a>,82) because they are presumed to hate her.</i>. They said this not to make her lose her <i>ketubah</i> but that she could not drink<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Then the husband has to divorce her <i>and</i> pay the <i>ketubah</i>, which is not in the personal interest of these women.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If a single witness said ‘I saw her when she became impure’,” etc. “And there is no witness against her<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:13" href="/Numbers.5.13"><i>Num.</i> 5:13</a>.</i>”; not only a witness [of good standing], from where even a male or female slave? The verse says, “<i>and</i> there is no witness against her”, any one<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">This follows R. Aqiba, who interprets every “and” as an addition [more explicitly <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bamidbar Rabbah 9:6" href="/Bamidbar_Rabbah.9.6"><i>Num. rabba</i> 9(6</a>)].</i>. And following Rebbi Ismael? As Rebbi Ismael said<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 31b" href="/Sotah.31b">Babli, 31b</a> and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 30a" href="/Sanhedrin.30a"><i>Sanhedrin</i>30a</a>, and <i>Sifry Num.</i> 7, this is quoted as everybody’s opinion.</i>, at any place where the Torah mentions “a witness”, in principle that means two witnesses unless the verse makes it clear that he is a single witness. It was found stated: Rebbi Ismael says two witnesses<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">This seems to be the opinion of the Tanna of <i>Sifry Num</i>. 7. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 31b" href="/Sotah.31b">Babli, 31b</a>, explains the problem away by reading the verse “and <i>two</i> witnesses are not against her,” meaning that one is. If one does not need two witnesses, one does not need quality witnesses.</i>.
Was our Mishnah [formulated] after Rebbi Aqiba agreed with Rebbi Ṭarphon<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Mishnah states that the single witness can also deprive the wife of her <i>ketubah,</i> the Mishnah follows R. Aqiba before he changed his mind.</i>? As it was stated: Rebbi Ṭarphon says, a single witness is believed to declare her impure<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">This follows a general principle formulated in the Babli as: “A single witness is believable in matters of prohibitions.” For example, if a single witness shows that some food a person ate was of a forbidden kind, if the eater was unaware of the fact and therefore the sin was unintentional, he is obligated for a purification sacrifice on the testimony of a single witness.</i> but not to let her lose her <i>ketubah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Since this needs two witnesses.</i>. Rebbi Aqiba says, just as a single witness is believed to declare her impure, so he is believed to let her lose her <i>ketubah</i>. Rebbi Ṭarphon said to him: Where do we find that a single witness proves anything in money matters<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">While a single witness cannot prove anything in court (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 19:15" href="/Deuteronomy.19.15"><i>Deut</i>. 19:15</a>), the same verse is interpreted to mean that the testimony of a single witness in support of a claim of one of the parties can obligate a defendant to swear that he owes nothing (<i>Šebuot</i> 6:1 fol. 36d; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 87b" href="/Ketubot.87b">Babli <i>Ketubot</i> 87b</a>). In the case here, there is no claim from any of the concerned parties.</i>? Rebbi Aqiba answered him, but where do we find that a single witness proves anything in matrimonial matters<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Since they have implications in criminal law, marriage or divorce are invalid unless executed in front of two witnesses. It is true that a single witness can inform about the dissolution of a marriage by the husband’s death, but this does not have the status of testimony (<i>Yebamot</i> Chap. 15).</i>? But just as a single witness is believed to declare her impure, so he is believed to let her lose her <i>ketubah</i>. Rebbi Aqiba changed his mind and taught following Rebbi Ṭarphon<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">The rules of testimony for prohibitions (in cases which cannot lead to criminal prosecution) and money matters are distinct and testimony acceptable for one facet of a case may not be acceptable for the rest.</i>.
Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya asked: If her mother-in-law said “I saw her when she became impure”, [you say that she did not drink. If then somebody else came and said, “I saw her when she became impure”], about what does he testify? If to make her drink, it was clear beforehand that she does not drink. He would come only to deny her <i>ketubah</i> to her, but one cannot make a person lose money on the testimony of one witness<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Even if we agree with the Mishnah that a single witness is empowered to forbid the woman to her husband and as a consequence to make her forfait her <i>ketubah</i> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 6:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.6.2.1">Note 18</a>), this is because the court is biblically empowered to forbid her to her husband on the testimony (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 6:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.6.2.2">Note 24</a>). But if the acceptable testimony is not needed, it is questionable whether its consequence should be enforced.</i>! Rebbi Yose asked: If he heard from a bird flying by: “I saw her when she became impure”, you say that she did not drink. If then somebody came and said, “I saw her when she became impure”, about what does he testify? If to make her drink, it was clear beforehand that she does not drink. He would come only to deny her <i>ketubah</i> to her, but one cannot make a person lose money on the testimony of one witness<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Even if we agree with the Mishnah that a single witness is empowered to forbid the woman to her husband and as a consequence to make her forfait her <i>ketubah</i> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 6:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.6.2.1">Note 18</a>), this is because the court is biblically empowered to forbid her to her husband on the testimony (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 6:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.6.2.2">Note 24</a>). But if the acceptable testimony is not needed, it is questionable whether its consequence should be enforced.</i>!
From what Rebbi Yose said, “X ate suet, and they warned him,” he is not whipped<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Eating suet from cattle, sheep, or goats, the part of the fat of sacrificial animals which is burned on the altar, is a crime (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:23" href="/Leviticus.7.23"><i>Lev</i>. 7:23</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:25" href="/Leviticus.7.25">25</a>). If a single witness comes and accuses somebody of having eaten suet criminally, i. e., after having been duly informed by two adults that eating that suet would be a criminal offense, his testimony is nothing since he is a single witness.<br>The entire paragraph is also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 8:1:2-10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.8.1.2-10"><i>Nazir</i> 8:1,</a> fol. 57a</i>. If one person said to him, that is suet, and two warned him, he is whipped<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">If a single witness (after due examination by the court) testifies that a certain piece of meat is suet, that piece legally has the status of suet (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 6:2:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.6.2.3">Note 26</a>). If then two people warn a third person that eating the meat would be criminal (thereby establishing intent; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 3:4:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.3.4.7">Chapter 3, Note 121</a>) and two witnesses testify to the act, this is testimony by two witnesses, valid in court, even though the status of the piece of meat as suet was based on the testimony of only one witness.</i>. Is not the main testimony given by a single witness<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">By analogy, on the questions raised in the preceding paragraph, one might argue that the preceding information given either from a person precluded from being a witness in court or from an unsubstantiated rumor does not impair the validity of the later testimony.</i>? From what Rebbi Jehudah is saying: “X is a <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6" href="/Numbers.6"><i>Num</i>. 6</a>. The witness testified that he heard X declaring a vow of <i>nazir</i> (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 7:2:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.7.2.7"><i>Berakhot</i> Chapter 7, Note 79</a>) but that he did not keep it. Again he is a single witness whose word carries no weight in court.</i>, he became defiled, and I had warned him,” he is not whipped. If one person said to him, you are a <i>nazir</i>, and based on this testimony he behaved like a <i>nazir</i>, when then he drank wine or became impure in the impurity of the dead, if two warned him, he is whipped. Is not the main testimony given by a single witness<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">The argument is totally parallel to the first one.</i>? From what Rebbi Mana said, “X is the wife of a Cohen and whored; her husband, a Cohen, then had relations with her<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">After she was desecrated, a felony (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 1:2:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.1.2.8">Chapter 1, Note 123</a>).</i> but I had warned him;” he is [not] whipped. If she went to a secluded place in the presence of two, and one said, I saw her that she became impure; when her husband, the Cohen, had relations with her but two had warned him, he is whipped. Is not the main testimony given by a single witness<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">The argument is totally parallel to the first one.</i>?

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> It would have been logical: Since the first testimony,<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">As the Halakhah explains, the first testimony is the testimony about the wife’s going to a secluded place, where R. Joshua requires two witnesses of good standing. The last testimony is that of adultery, where the preceding Mishnaiot accept even the proverbial “bird flying by”. The confirmation is the acceptance of the testimony by a competent court.</i> which does not forbid her permanently, is not confirmed if not from two [witnesses], the last testimony,<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">As the Halakhah explains, the first testimony is the testimony about the wife’s going to a secluded place, where R. Joshua requires two witnesses of good standing. The last testimony is that of adultery, where the preceding Mishnaiot accept even the proverbial “bird flying by”. The confirmation is the acceptance of the testimony by a competent court.</i> which forbids her permanently, should reasonably only be confirmed by [witnesses]. The verse says: “There is <i>no</i> witness about her<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:13" href="/Numbers.5.13"><i>Num</i>. 5:13</a>.</i>”, any kind of testimony about her.<br><sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">This is the second half of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 6:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.6.3.1">Mishnah 3</a>.</i> There would now be an argument <i>de minore ad majus</i> for the first testimony! Since the last testimony, which forbids her permanently, is confirmed from a single witness, the first testimony, which does not forbid her permanently, should logically be confirmed from a single witness. The verse says, “for he found out about her a matter of sexual misbehavior,<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:1" href="/Deuteronomy.24.1"><i>Deut</i>. 24:1</a>. For the House of Shammai, this is the only reason admitted for a divorce (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 9:8:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.9.8.4">Mishnah <i>Giṭṭin</i> 9:10</a>); for the House of Hillel, this is a reason why there must be a divorce.</i>” and further, it says, “by the mouth of two witnesses a matter should be confirmed.<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 19:15" href="/Deuteronomy.19.15"><i>Deut</i>. 19:15</a>. The verse proves that in any judicial proceedings, דבר means a proof by two witnesses. Since the husband cannot make his wife drink unless he presents his case to the Temple court, all judicial rules apply here. But a divorce because of the wife’s adultery can be given by the husband on his own (even though to deprive her of her <i>ketubah</i> after the divorce he needs the judgment of a court.)</i>”
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “It would have been logical: Since the first testimony,” etc. The first testimony is about being in a secluded place; the last testimony is about impurity. The Mishnah follows Rebbi Joshua, since Rebbi Joshua says<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 1:1, Note 4.</i>, “he declares his jealousy and warns her by the testimony of two [witnesses], and makes her drink by the testimony of two [witnesses].” Rebbi Mana said, it even follows the Tanna, about whom was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 1:1:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.1.1.7">Chapter 1, Note 16</a>.</i>: “Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah says in the name of Rebbi Eliezer: He declares his jealousy before one witness or by himself, but makes her drink by the testimony of two [witnesses].”

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If one witness says, she became impure, but another witness says, she did not become impure, or one woman<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">This case has to be treated separately since a woman, while she can point out facts, cannot be a formal witness.</i> says, she became impure, but another woman says, she did not become impure, she would drink<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 6:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.6.1.1">Mishnah 1</a> stated that in case of certainty, the woman may not be brought to the Temple. In the case here, two contradicting statements cancel one another; there is uncertainty. In all cases, it is supposed that there are two witnesses who attest that the wife met another man in a secluded place.</i>. If one [witness] says, she became impure, but two [witnesses] say, she did not become impure, she did drink<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">One witness against two witnesses accounts for nothing.</i>. If two [witnesses] say, she became impure, but one [witness] says, she did not become impure, she would not drink.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “There would now be an argument <i>de minore ad majus</i> for the first testimony,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Essentially the same text, only referring to testimony of the husband’s death instead of the wife’s infidelity, in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 15" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.15"><i>Yebamot</i> Chapter 15, Notes 135–138</a> and here, Chapter 9, Halakhah 7.</i> Gidul bar Miniamin in the name of Rav: Anywhere they accepted the testimony of a woman parallel to that of a man, a man can contradict a woman and a woman can contradict a man. Then one should state: “A witness says that she became impure, and a woman says that she did not become impure; a woman said that she became impure and a witness said that she did not become impure.” In the House of Rebbi they stated it this way. It was stated in the name of Rebbi Neḥemiah: One follows the majority of the testimonies. How is that? Two women against one woman they considered as if there were two witnesses against one witness. What you say refers to a woman and women. But if there were a hundred women against one [male] witness, they are like one witness.
Rav Ada bar Aḥawah said, a single witness is believed to declare her impure, but a single witness is not believed to make her lose her <i>ketubah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">He strictly follows the rule that a single witness is acceptable about prohibitions but not in money matters.</i> Rav Ḥisda said, why did they say that a single witness is believed to declare her impure? Because the thing has feet to stand on<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">The single witness is believed to prohibit her to her husband because there are two other witnesses who accuse her of going to a secluded place with another man. Then he can also be believed, by rabbinic rule, that the husband does not have to pay (certainly for those who hold that the <i>ketubah</i> is a rabbinic institution.)</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">In the Yerushalmi text quoted by Tosaphot, 32b, <i>s. v.</i> הא, the discussion starts: עֵד אֶחָד אוֹמֵר נִיטְמֵאת וְעֵד אֶחָד אוֹמֵר לֹא נִיטְמֵאת. “A single witness says, she became impure, but another single witness says, she did not become impure, …”</i> Simeon bar Abba in the name of Rabbi Joḥanan: Here<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">In the first case treated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 6:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.6.4.1">Mishnah 5</a>, that there is the word of one witness against the word of another single witness, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 6:4:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.6.4.4">Note 59</a>. He amends the Mishnah to read: “She does not drink.” The reason seems to be the order in which the testimony is described in the Mishnah: The first witness says that she became impure. By rabbinic rules, he is believed. This means that his testimony counts as much as the testimony of two witnesses. In the language of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 32b" href="/Sotah.32b">Babli (<i>Soṭah</i> 32b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 47b" href="/Sotah.47b">47b</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 88b" href="/Yevamot.88b"><i>Yebamot</i> 88b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 117b" href="/Yevamot.117b">117b</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 22b" href="/Ketubot.22b"><i>Ketubot</i> 22b</a>): “In any case where the Torah declared that a single witness can be believed, he has the status of two.” If the Yerushalmi would accept such a statement, it would have to accept R. Joḥanan’s statement here. One has to assume that the second witness states that he observed the woman and the other man during the entire time they were together in a secluded place and that nothing untoward happened.</i>, she did not drink but in the case of breaking the calf’s neck they did break it<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 21:1-9" href="/Deuteronomy.21.1-9"><i>Deut</i>. 21:1–9</a>. If two witnesses came and one said that he saw the murderer but the other one said that he saw the murder but not the murderer, there is no testimony which has any standing in court. Therefore, the murderer is unknown.</i>. Rav said, she did drink. The Mishnah disagrees with Rav<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Since Rav accepts the text of the Mishnah as is, one attempts to show that that text is self-contradictory.</i>: “If one [witness] says, she became impure, but two [witnesses] say, she did not become impure, she would drink.” That<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Since the sentence states that in the presence of a witness who states that she became impure one needs at least two witnesses who state that she did not become impure in order to declare the matter one of doubt, it would follow that there is no scenario in which a single witness can invalidate the testimony of another single witness. This contradicts the first sentence of the Mishnah and Rav’s statement.</i> implies that if one witness was against another single witness, she would not drink. He explains it for those whose testimony is invalid<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">A witness who cannot testify in money matters because his integrity is in question (Mishnah <i>Sanhedrin</i> 3:3–4), or a woman or a slave. These have no standing against a single believable witness; one needs at least two, following R. Neḥemiah in the first paragraph.<br>In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 32b" href="/Sotah.32b">Babli, 32b</a>, the problem is solved in a first explanation that in the case of the first sentence, the two contradicting witnesses came to court together. Then there never was testimony and the woman is not held to be an adulteress. For the second case, the single witness came first and his testimony was accepted as fact. Then one needs two witnesses to annul the first testimony. The second explanation follows the Yerushalmi.</i>. But if for those whose testimony is invalid, does not the end say: “If two [witnesses] say, she became impure, but one witness says, she did not become impure, she would not drink.” That implies that if one witness was against another single witness, she would drink<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">If the Mishnah would state only the second and third cases, it would be a case of the undistributed middle and could be solved by postulating different scenarios for the different cases. But since the first case is stated apodictically, that way (the first explanation of the Babli) seems to be excluded and neither Rav nor R. Joḥanan can have a totally coherent position.</i>. How could Rav say, she would drink? <sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">In the Yerushalmi text quoted by Tosaphot, 32b, <i>s. v.</i> הא, the discussion starts: עֵד אֶחָד אוֹמֵר נִיטְמֵאת וְעֵד אֶחָד אוֹמֵר לֹא נִיטְמֵאת. “A single witness says, she became impure, but another single witness says, she did not become impure, …”</i> Rebbi Joḥanan said, she would not drink. A Mishnah disagrees with Rebbi Joḥanan. “If two [witnesses] say, she became impure, but one witness says, she did not become impure, she would not drink.” That implies that if one witness was against another single witness, she would drink. He explains it for those whose testimony is invalid. But if for those whose testimony is invalid, does not the first statement say: “If one [witness] says, she became impure, but two [witnesses] say, she did not become impure, she would drink.” That implies that if one witness was against another single witness, she would drink. How could Rebbi Joḥanan say, she would not drink<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">If the Mishnah would state only the second and third cases, it would be a case of the undistributed middle and could be solved by postulating different scenarios for the different cases. But since the first case is stated apodictically, that way (the first explanation of the Babli) seems to be excluded and neither Rav nor R. Joḥanan can have a totally coherent position.</i>? They said in the name of Rebbi Samuel, the Mishnah talks about those whose testimony is invalid<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, an anonymous statement in support of R. Ḥiyya (bar Abba, student of R. Joḥanan).</i>. They said in the name of Rebbi Samuel: The Mishnah speaks about another rendez-vous. Rebbi Ze‘ira asked before Rebbi Mana: What does it mean, “the Mishnah speaks about another rendez-vous”? He said to him, so says Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Yannai<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 6:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.6.1.2">Note 5</a>.</i>: This entire chapter [deals with the case that] he had declared his jealousy and she went to a secluded place. She went to a secluded place by the testimony of two [witnesses]. If one of them said, I saw her that she became impure while in the secluded place, there [is testimony] about the seclusion and about impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Since the testimony of this witness about the wife going to a secluded place must be accepted, since it is supported by a second witness, his testimony about her infidelity also must be accepted.</i>. After the time of seclusion, there [is testimony] about the seclusion but not about impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">There is no difference whether one of two witnesses first testifies together with another that the wife went with another man to a secluded place and then he alone testifies that at the occasion of another rendez-vous she committed adultery, or if the second occasion is mentioned by a third witness. In any case, since it is not claimed that the second witness was present at the second occasion, there are distinct testimonies and the statement about adultery does not have the status of validity conferred by two independent testimonies.</i>. If there were three [witnesses]. One said, I saw her that she became impure while in the secluded place, there [is testimony] about the seclusion but not about impurity. If there were three [witnesses]. One said, I saw that she did not become impure while in the secluded place, how do you treat this? As a testimony which, if part of the testimony is thrown out, the entire testimony is thrown out, or does one accept the remainder of the testimony<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">This is a separate case. The two witnesses agree about time and place when the wife went to a secluded place with another man. The first witness claims to have witnessed the sexual act, the second witness claims to have observed the couple all the time but that nothing untoward happened. Their testimonies about what happened in seclusion must be thrown out. Does this invalidate the testimony that a secluded rendez-vous took place? In criminal proceedings, the entire testimony would have to be thrown out.</i>? They said in the name of Rebbi Abba Mari: Even he who says, if part of it is thrown out, all is thrown out, here one accepts the remainder of the testimony, since the matter has feet to stand on<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">That there are two witnesses who testify that the husband formally declared his jealousy before them gives all following testimonies a basis which allows for the relaxation of the rules of criminal proceedings.</i>.

Chapter 7



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The following are recited in the vernacular: The verses of the suspect wife<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The verses the Cohen has to read to the wife before she drinks. While the scroll has to be written in Hebrew, the wife has to understand them and, therefore, they have to be translated into her vernacular.</i>, and the declaration of tithes<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 26:12-16" href="/Deuteronomy.26.12-16"><i>Deut.</i> 26:12–16</a>.</i>, and the recitation of the <i>Šema</i>‘<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <i>Berakhot</i>, Chapters 1–2.</i>, and prayer<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The eighteen (respectively 7 or 9) benedictions of the ‘<i>Amidah</i>; cf. <i>Berakhot</i>, Chapters 1–2.</i>, and grace<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">After a meal.</i>, and the oath of a witness<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">If somebody puts an oath on a possible witness that he should come and testify for him; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:1" href="/Leviticus.5.1"><i>Lev</i>. 5:1</a>. Testimony itself is given without an oath.</i>, and the oath about a deposit<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:6-12" href="/Exodus.22.6-12"><i>Ex</i>. 22:6–12</a>.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “The following are recited,” etc. It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:21" href="/Numbers.5.21"><i>Num.</i> 5:21</a>. The argument being that this clause is redundant. The full verse reads: “The Cohen shall make the woman swear by a curse-oath, the Cohen shall tell the woman: …”.</i>: “The Cohen shall tell the woman,” in any language she understands, the words of Rebbi Joshia. Rebbi Jonathan said to him, if she does not understand, how could she answer “Amen, Amen”<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Sifry</i> <i>Num</i>. 12, the objection is attributed to R. Ismael, R. Joshia’s teacher, not to R. Jonathan, R. Joshia’s student. R. Ismael’s interpretation is that the Cohen has to go into all details of the proceedings. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 32b" href="/Sotah.32b">Babli, 32b</a>, is the only source which accepts R. Joshia’s derivation. Then the Babli, without mentioning names, follows R. Ismael’s interpretation but treats it as purely rabbinic.</i>? But that he should not talk to her through an interpreter.
<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bereishit Rabbah 20:12" href="/Bereishit_Rabbah.20.12"><i>Gen. rabba</i> 20(12</a>), 45(14), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bereishit Rabbah 63:8" href="/Bereishit_Rabbah.63.8">63(8</a>).</i> Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Simeon: We do not find that the Omnipresent talked to a woman, except only to Sarah. But is there not written<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 3:16" href="/Genesis.3.16"><i>Gen</i>. 3:16</a>.</i>: “To the woman He said: I shall increase,” etc.? Rebbi Jacob of Kefar Ḥanin<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Elsewhere he is called “from Kefar Ḥanan”.</i> said, through an interpreter<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Adam is addressed by the Eternal in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 3:8:2-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.3.8.2-7">3:9</a>, the woman in 3:13, the snake in 3:14.</i>. But is there not written<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 25:23" href="/Genesis.25.23"><i>Gen</i>. 25:23</a>.</i>: “The Eternal said to her, two peoples are in your womb”? Rebbi Abba bar Cahana said, the Word fell to her<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">A word about her fell to a prophet (in the opinion of <i>Gen</i>. <i>rabba</i>, Sem ben Noah.)</i>. Rebbi Biri said, how many weaver’s shuttles! The Holy One, praise to Him, desires to hear the talk of the just women: “He said no, but you laughed.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">The only time we find a mention of God addressing a woman directly it is about an unimportant matter.</i>”
“The declaration of tithes, as it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">There is no such verse. It really should read (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 26:13" href="/Deuteronomy.26.13"><i>Deut</i>. 26:13</a>): “You should say before the Eternal, your God,” Who understands all languages, in contrast to the declaration of First Fruits (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 26:3" href="/Deuteronomy.26.3">v. 3</a>), where it says “you should say to him (the priest)”, who is supposed to speak only Hebrew. The quote contradicts <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 2:1:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.2.1.9">Halakhah 2, Notes 43ff</a>; it is correct in <i>Sifry Deut</i>. 303 and in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 32b" href="/Sotah.32b">Babli, 32b</a>, where it is pointed out that “declare and say” always means Hebrew only (as in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 27:14" href="/Deuteronomy.27.14"><i>Deut</i>. 27:14</a>).</i>: “You should declare and say.”
“And the recitation of the <i>Šema‘</i>.” For it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 6:7" href="/Deuteronomy.6.7"><i>Deut</i>. 6:7</a>. Nobody can discuss something he does not understand.</i>: “You shall talk about them.” Rebbi says, I say that the <i>Šema‘</i> is recited only in the holy language. What is the reason? “The words shall be<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 6:7" href="/Deuteronomy.6.7"><i>Deut</i>. 6:7</a>. It is understood that the verb היה “to be” in general describes a permanent state. In our case, this means that the words, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 6:1-9" href="/Deuteronomy.6.1-9"><i>Deut</i>. 6:1–9</a>, should not be changed. Then they cannot be recited in translation. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 32b" href="/Sotah.32b">Babli, 32b</a>, goes into an extended discussion on the relative merits of the two positions.</i>”. Rebbi Levi bar Ḥaita<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">In the <i>Arukh</i>, <i>s. v</i>. אלנסתין, reads בר הזותא but the reading here seems to be original. A Galilean preacher of the fourth/fifth generation.</i> went<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">In the <i>Arukh</i>, על “he ascended”, to Caesarea Philippi.</i> to Caesarea. He heard their voices when they were reading the <i>Šema‘</i> in Hellenistic<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ἑλληνιστί, adv., “in the Greek language”, here probably Judeo-Greek. [The final ן might indicate a nasal pronuciation]. In <i>Tanḥuma</i> texts, the word is always written אלנסטי. {While in the commentary to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 35:8" href="/Genesis.35.8"><i>Gen</i>. 35:8</a> [<i>Tanḥuma Buber Wayyišlaḥ</i> 26 (Note 100)], the word ἄλλον is Greek and is characterized as such in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bereishit Rabbah 81:5" href="/Bereishit_Rabbah.81.5"><i>Gen. rabba</i> 81(5</a>), the identification of the root in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Hosea 13:14" href="/Hosea.13.14">קָטָבְךָ (<i>Hos</i>. 13:14</a>) as κατάβα קאטאבא (“go down’, imperative, short for κατάβηθι) with a Semitic suffix [<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:1-8:36" href="/Leviticus.6.1-8.36"><i>Tanḥuma Buber Ṣaw</i> 4 (Note 30</a>), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:1-8:36" href="/Leviticus.6.1-8.36"><i>Tanḥuma Ṣaw</i> 2</a>] can only be described as Judeo-Greek.}</i> manner. He wanted to hinder them, but Rebbi Yose heard of it and was offended. He said, do I say that someone who does not know to read Assyrian letters<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Hebrew Square letters, which replaced the paleo-Hebrew script. Since the latter was retained by the Samaritans, the rabbinic authorities insisted on the exclusive use of the square alphabet. One might wonder whether the text expressly authorizes, e. g., Judeo-Arabic or Judeo-German translations written in “Assyrian” characters.</i> should not recite it at all? But he fulfills his duty in any language he knows. Rebbi Berekhiah objected: But there is the Esther scroll, where somebody who knows to read both Asssyrian script and Greek<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">לעז “barbaric”, is used in talmudic literature exclusively for Greek, for which approved translations existed.</i> can fulfill his obligation only from Assyrian script<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 18a" href="/Megillah.18a">Babli, <i>Megillah</i> 18a</a>, permits the bilingual person to hear the Esther story in Greek but rules that reading from a Hebrew scroll written in square letters is acceptable for everybody, even for those who do not know Hebrew. (In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 7:2:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.7.2.2-4">Halakhah 2</a>, there is a discussion whether square letters are “Assyrian” or “rich” script.)</i>. Rebbi Mana said: About the Esther scroll, somebody who knows to read both Asssyrian script and Greek can fulfill his obligation only from Assyrian script; Greek only, he fulfills his obligation in Greek; and so he can fulfill his obligation in any language he understands.
“And prayer,” that one should know how to ask according to his needs. “And grace,” that one should know to Whom he gives praise.
“And the oath of a witness and the oath about a deposit.” One makes him swear in his own language. If one made them swear not in their language and they said “Amen”, they are not prosecutable<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Nobody is prosecutable for perjury if he was not admonished in his own language by the court before answering the oath read to him. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 33a" href="/Sotah.33a">Babli, 33a</a>, the rule is derived from verses. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sotah (Lieberman) 7:1" href="/Tosefta_Sotah_(Lieberman).7.1">Tosephta, 7:1</a>, has a different version: If the court explained to him five times in any language he understands and he confirms his oath, he is guilty (if he swears falsely).</i>. As we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">The text is also in <i>Nedarim</i> 3:1; a parallel text in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 29a" href="/Shevuot.29a">Babli <i>Šebuot</i> 29a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 25a" href="/Nedarim.25a"><i>Nedarim</i> 25a</a>.</i>: The oath before the judges is according to our understanding, not according to your understanding. Rebbi Yudan said, it is to be assumed that he makes them swear according to his understanding. Why does he have to spell out this condition to him? Because of the uneducated, lest they say that there may be mental reservations for oaths. Rebbi Ḥanania stated before Rebbi Mana: Is there not written<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 29:14" href="/Deuteronomy.29.14"><i>Deut</i>. 29:14</a>. The argument is about what is written before and after the fragment quoted: “Not with you alone am I concluding this covenant and this oath. But with everybody who stands here with us today, and with those who are not with us today.” How can you have an oath with anybody not involved?</i>: “But with everybody who stands here with us today”? He said to him, the future generations do not have mental reservations<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Since the unborn cannot have mental reservations, so the mental reservations of the living are invalid.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> But the following must be said in the holy language: The recitation for the first fruits<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">The declaration in the Temple, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bikkurim 2:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bikkurim.2.3.1">Mishnah <i>Bikkurim</i> 2:5</a>.</i>, <i>ḥalîṣah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">The required declarations by the childless widow and brother-in-law in the ceremony which frees her to marry outside the family; cf. <i>Yebamot</i>12:3–4.</i>, blessings and curses<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">This is of purely antiquarian character, asserting that the curses detailed in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 27:11-26" href="/Deuteronomy.27.11-26"><i>Deut.</i> 27:11–26</a> (each curse prefaced by a corresponding blessing: “Blessed be the man who will not …”) were pronounced in Hebrew, based on <a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 8:34" href="/Joshua.8.34"><i>Jos</i>. 8:34</a>; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 7:4:2-9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.7.4.2-9">Halakhah 4</a>.</i>, the priestly blessing<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:22-26" href="/Numbers.6.22-26"><i>Num</i>. 6:22–26</a>.</i>, the benedictions of the High Priest<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">In the service of the Day of Atonement, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yoma 7:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yoma.7.1.1">Mishnah <i>Yoma</i> 7:1</a>; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 7:6:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.7.6.2-4">Halakhah 6</a>.</i>, the portion about the king<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">The public reading from the Torah at the end of a Sabbatical year, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 31:10-13" href="/Deuteronomy.31.10-13"><i>Deut</i>. 31:10–13</a>; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 7:7:2-8:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.7.7.2-8.2">Halakhot 7–8</a>.</i>, the portion about the calf whose neck was broken<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">The declarations <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 21:7" href="/Deuteronomy.21.7"><i>Deut</i>. 21:7</a> (the Elders), 8 (the priests); cf. Chapter 9.</i>, the [priest] anointed for war at the time he speaks to the people<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 20:1-9" href="/Deuteronomy.20.1-9"><i>Deut</i>. 20:1–9</a>; cf. Chapter 8.</i>. The recitation for the first fruits, how? “You shall begin and say before the Eternal, your God<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 26:5" href="/Deuteronomy.26.5"><i>Deut</i>. 26:5</a>.</i>” but further on it says, “the Levites shall begin and say to all the men of Israel in an elevated voice<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 27:14" href="/Deuteronomy.27.14"><i>Deut</i>. 27:14</a>. The implication of this verse is explained in the Halakhah.</i>”. Since “beginning” further on means in the holy language, so also here in the holy language<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">This is an application of the third hermeneutical rule of R. Ismael: The meaning of a word is defined by one paradigm. It does not fit the mold of the second rule, “equal cut”, preferred by the commentators.</i>.<br><i>Ḥalîṣah</i> how? “She shall begin and say: So shall be done to the man<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 25:9" href="/Deuteronomy.25.9"><i>Deut</i>. 25:9</a>.</i>” but further on it says, “the Levites shall begin and say to all the men of Israel in an elevated voice”. Since “beginning” further on means in the holy language, so also here in the holy language. Rebbi Jehudah says, “She shall begin and say so,” [it is invalid] unless she says exactly that text<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">He disregards the dividing accent in the sentence and reads: “She shall begin and say so: it will be done to the man who will not build his brother’s house” instead of “She shall begin and say: thus will be done …” It gives a different twist to a derivation originating with R. Jehudah’s father’s teacher R. Eliezer in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 12:3:1-4:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.12.3.1-4.5">Mishnah <i>Yebamot</i> 12:4</a>; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 33" href="/Sotah.33">Babli 33a/b</a>.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It was stated in the name of Rebbi Jehudah: Any place where it was said in any of these expressions: you begin and say, thus, and thus<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">The first and last expressions were mentioned in the Mishnah. כה “thus” is the introduction to the priests’ blessing, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:23" href="/Numbers.6.23"><i>Num</i>. 6:23</a>.</i>, it means in the holy language. Rebbi Eleazar said, the defining paradigm for all is: “Moses would speak and God would answer him by voice<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 19:19" href="/Exodus.19.19"><i>Ex</i>. 19:19</a>. This is not a reference to the statement of R. Jehudah, but to the argument of the Mishnah. While in that verse, the root ענה does not have the meaning of “to begin to speak” but “to answer”, the mention of קול “voice”, which was in Hebrew, transfers to the recitation of the Levites, who have to speak בְּקוֹל רָם “in the voice of the High”, where High is a Title of God, <i>Is</i>. 56:15; cf. below, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 7:2:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.7.2.4">Note 61</a>.</i>.” Rebbi Ḥaggai objected, is there not written, “Laban and Bethuel began<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 24:50" href="/Genesis.24.50"><i>Gen</i>. 24:50</a>. As noted later, they spoke Aramaic.</i>,” if you say because of “beginning” only, is there not written “and said<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">That verse contains both roots, ענה and אמר, even if they are separated by the names of the speakers. Therefore, the argument of R. Jehudah is invalid.</i>”? If you want to say, by “saying” only, is there not written “from the Eternal came the word”<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">This is a counter-argument. Since Laban and Bethuel admitted that the betrothal of Rebecca was a word of the Eternal, did they not speak Hebrew at that moment?</i>? If you want to say, in the holy language, is there not written “the stone heap of testimony<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 31:47" href="/Genesis.31.47"><i>Gen</i>. 31:47</a>. Since Laban had to translate Jacob’s גַלְעֵד into Aramaic, it follows that he did not speak Hebrew.</i>”? If you want to say, before the Torah was given, is there not the text of the declaration of tithes<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 26:16" href="/Deuteronomy.26.16"><i>Deut</i>. 26:16</a>. However, there only the root אמר is used.</i> which may be recited in any language<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 7:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.7.1.1">Mishnah 1</a>.</i>?
Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: The Syriac language should not be unimportant in your eyes, for it is mentioned in the Torah, the Prophets, and the Hagiographs. In the Torah, it is written: “Laban called it <i>the heap of testimony</i><sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 31:47" href="/Genesis.31.47"><i>Gen</i>. 31:47</a>. Since Laban had to translate Jacob’s גַלְעֵד into Aramaic, it follows that he did not speak Hebrew.</i>”. In Prophets, it is written: “<i>So you shall say to them</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jeremiah 10:11" href="/Jeremiah.10.11"><i>Jer.</i> 10:11</a>.</i>” In Hagiographs, it is written: “The Chaldeans spoke to the king in Aramaic.<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Daniel 2:4" href="/Daniel.2.4"><i>Dan.</i> 2:4</a>.</i>” Rebbi Jonathan from Bet Gubrin said, four languages are good for use: The foreign language<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Greek.</i> for song, Latin for war, Syriac for elegies, Hebrew for speech. Some people say, also Assyrian<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">“Hebrew” square script, which originally is an Aramaic development of paleo-Hebrew.</i> for writing. Assyrian has a script but no language<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is used for Hebrew, Aramaic, Mandaic etc.</i>, Hebrew has a language but no script<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Once paleo-Hebrew script was abandoned.</i>. They chose for them Assyrian script and Hebrew language<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">This is a shortened version of a Babylonian tradition (by the “Heads of the Diaspora”) that Moses wrote the Torah in Hebrew script and language, Ezra wrote the Torah in Aramaic translation and Assyrian script, but the people chose Hebrew text and Assyrian (Aramaic) letters: <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 21b" href="/Sanhedrin.21b"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 21b</a>; also cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 1:9:2-20" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.1.9.2-20"><i>Megillah</i> 1:11</a> (fol. 71b, line 67).</i>. Why is its name called אֲשׁוּרִי? Because one is made happy in writing it<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">The meaning of the sentence is not clear. One could translate: Because its writing is plain.</i>. Rebbi Levi said, because they brought it with them from Assyria<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Square script is not found before the return from Mesopotamia, where the exiles lived mostly in the Aramaic-speaking Northern parts.</i>.
It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 27:14" href="/Deuteronomy.27.14"><i>Deut</i>. 27:14</a>.</i>: “The Levites shall begin and say to all the men of Israel in an elevated voice<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Or, “the voice of the Most High,” cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 7:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.7.2.2">Note 44</a>.</i>”, this teaches that the Holy One, praise to Him, associated His voice to theirs. Another explanation, “the best of voices”. Rebbi Isaac said, not low, not high, but moderate.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Blessings and curses<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">This is of purely antiquarian character, asserting that the curses detailed in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 27:11-26" href="/Deuteronomy.27.11-26"><i>Deut.</i> 27:11–26</a> (each curse prefaced by a corresponding blessing: “Blessed be the man who will not …”) were pronounced in Hebrew, based on <a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 8:34" href="/Joshua.8.34"><i>Jos</i>. 8:34</a>; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 7:4:2-9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.7.4.2-9">Halakhah 4</a>.</i>, how? When Israel had crossed the Jordan they came to Mount Gerizim and Mount Ebal in Samaria, next to Sichem which is close to the terebinths of guidance, as it is said<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 11:30" href="/Deuteronomy.11.30"><i>Deut</i>. 11:30</a>: “They are on the other side of the Jordan, westward, on the road to sunset, in the Land of the Canaanite who dwells in the prairie, opposite Gilgal, near the terebinths of guidance.”</i>: “They are on the other side of the Jordan, etc.”, and at another place it says<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 12:6" href="/Genesis.12.6"><i>Gen</i>. 12:6</a>. The terebinth may have been a holy tree (or in the language of <i>Deut</i>., a holy grove) at the crossing on the North-South route (Damascus) - Ir Gannim - Beër Šeba - (Egypt) and the “road towards sundown” from Adam-the-City to the Mediterranean.</i>: “Abram traveled through the Land up to the place of Sichem, up to the terebinth of guidance.” Since the terebinth of guidance mentioned there is at Sichem so the the terebinths of guidance mentioned here are at Sichem<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">The lengthy discussion in the Mishnah is a polemic against R. Eleazar in the Halakhah. For all of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 7:3:2-5:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.7.3.2-5.6">Halakhot 3–5</a>, cf. <i>Seder ‘Olam</i>, Chapter 11, in the author’s edition pp. 109–119; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sotah (Lieberman) 8" href="/Tosefta_Sotah_(Lieberman).8">Tosephta Chapter 8</a>.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Parallel texts in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 33b" href="/Sotah.33b">Babli 33b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sotah (Lieberman) 8" href="/Tosefta_Sotah_(Lieberman).8">Tosephta Chapter 8</a>, <i>Sifry</i> <i>Deut</i>. 56.</i>“<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 11:30" href="/Deuteronomy.11.30"><i>Deut</i>. 11:30</a>: “They are on the other side of the Jordan, westward, on the road to sunset, in the Land of the Canaanite who dwells in the prairie, opposite Gilgal, near the terebinths of guidance.”</i> They are on the other side of the Jordan,” away from the Jordan. “Far away<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Rashi on Sotah 33b" href="/Rashi_on_Sotah.33b">Rashi, Commentary to <i>Soṭa</i> 33b</a>, attributes this meaning of אחרי to <i>Gen. rabba</i>. If one accepts the usual meaning “westward” (adopted by Rashi in his Commentary to <i>Deut.</i>), one would have to read “sunset” in the opinion of R. Jehudah and “sunrise” in that of R. Eleazar, as suggested by L. Finkelstein, <i>Sifry</i> <i>Deut</i>., pp. 123–124.</i> from the road towards the sun’s coming”, the place from where the sun shines. “In the Land of the Canaanite who dwells in the prairie, opposite Gilgal, near the terebinths of guidance.” That refers to Mount Gerizim and Mount Ebal among the Samaritans, the words of Rebbi Jehudah. Rebbi Eleazar<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Only in <i>Sifry</i> <i>Deut</i>. the reading is: R. Eliezer. That reading is very unlikely since R. Ilaï, R. Jehudah’s father, was R. Eliezer’s student. (The one reading “R.
