Eleazar” quoted by Finkelstein in his apparatus comes from a secondary source not necessarily dependent upon <i>Sifry</i>.)</i> said, this does not refer to Mount Gerizim and Mount Ebal of the Samaritans. “They are on the other side of the Jordan,” by the Jordan. “To the West, on the road to sunset,” a place where the sun goes down. “In the Land of the Canaanite,” but there is the Ḥiwwite<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 34:2" href="/Genesis.34.2">Sichem, <i>Gen</i>. 34:2</a>.</i>. “Who dwells in the prairie,” but there it is in the mountains. “Opposite Gilgal,” Gilgal is nowhere there<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">East of Jericho, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 4:19" href="/Joshua.4.19"><i>Jos</i>. 4:19</a>.</i>. “Near the terebinths of guidance.” The terebinths of guidance are not there<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">Here one speaks of a grove, in the verse referring to Abraham of a single tree.</i>. How does Rebbi Eleazar uphold “Mount Gerizim and Mount Ebal”? They made two elevations and called them Mount Gerizim and Mount Ebal. In the opinion of Rebbi Jehudah, they walked 120 <i>mil</i> on that day<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">The distance from Jericho to Sichem is estimated at 60 <i>mil</i> (in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 36a" href="/Sotah.36a">Babli, 36a</a>, and one Tosephta source, “more than 60 <i>mil</i>”). In R. Jehudah’s opinion they crossed the Jordan, put up the stones, walked to Sichem, completed the ceremony, and walked back, all in one day. This contradicts <a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 8:30-35" href="/Joshua.8.30-35"><i>Jos</i>. 8:30–35</a>.</i>. In the opinion of Rebbi Eleazar, they did not move at all.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 33b" href="/Sotah.33b">Babli 33b</a>, <i>Sifry</i> <i>Deut</i>. 56.</i>: “Rebbi Eliezer ben Jacob says, the verse comes only to prepare the road for them and say that they should walk on the road, not in fields, in inhabited regions, not in the desert, in the valley<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sifry</i> <i>Deut</i>. explains ערבה as מישור “plain”.</i>, not in the mountains.
Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Simeon<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">In the other sources (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 33b" href="/Sotah.33b">Babli 33b</a>, <i>Sifry Deut.</i> 56): R. Eleazar ben R. Yose. This is the Babylonian tradition which attributes the material of <i>Seder Olam</i> to the school of R. Yose.</i> said: I told the Samaritan scribes: You falsified your Torah and did not gain anything, because you cause to be written in your Torah “near the terebinths of guidance, Sichem<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">An additional word which is not original.</i>”. Is it not known that this is Sichem? It is only because you do not explain by “equal cut<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">The second hermeneutical rule which stipulates that a word in the Torah can have only one meaning; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 1:1:20" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.1.1.20"><i>Berakhot</i> 1:1, Note 70</a>.</i>”, but we explain by “equal cut”. <sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">See the Mishnah.</i> It is written here, “the terebinths of guidance”, and it is said there, “the terebinth<i>s</i> of guidance.” Since “the terebinth<i>s</i> of guidance” there are at Sichem, also “the terebinths of guidance” here are at Sichem; and following Rebbi Ismael. For Rebbi Ismael said, any “comings” which were said in the Torah refer to after fourteen years, seven during which they conquered and seven during which they distributed<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">Any commandment in the Torah which is prefaced by “when you come into the Land” is activated only after each tribe has received their portion of land (<i>Seder Olam</i> Chapter 11, pp. 108–109; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 118b" href="/Zevachim.118b">Babli <i>Zebaḥim</i> 118b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Arakhin 13a" href="/Arakhin.13a"><i>Arakhin</i> 13a</a>).</i>. Therefore, blessings and curses were recited only after fourteen years. Rebbi Ḥanania objected before Rebbi Mana: Is it not written<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 27:4" href="/Deuteronomy.27.4"><i>Deut.</i> 27:4</a>. These stones had to be erected on Mount Ebal.</i>, “It shall be when you cross the Jordan you shall erect these stones”? He said to him, they erected the stones immediately<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">In that case, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 4:8" href="/Joshua.4.8"><i>Jos.</i> 4:8</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 8:30-35" href="/Joshua.8.30-35">8:30–35</a>, support the interpretation of R. Eleazar.</i> but blessings and curses were said only after fourteen years.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Six tribes climbed to the top of Mount Gerizim and six tribes climbed to the top of Mount Ebal<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 27:12-13" href="/Deuteronomy.27.12-13"><i>Deut.</i> 27:12–13</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 8:33" href="/Joshua.8.33"><i>Jos.</i> 8:33</a>.</i> while the Cohanim, the Levites, and the ark were below in the middle. The Cohanim were surrounding the ark, the Levites the Cohanim, and all of Israel [were] on both sides, as it was said<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 8:33" href="/Joshua.8.33"><i>Jos.</i> 8:33</a>.</i>: “And all of Israel, its Elders, its policemen, its judges were standing on both sides of the ark …” They turned their faces towards Mount Gerizim and opened with the blessing: “Blessed be the man who will not make a hewn or molten [image], the Eternal’s abomination, made by an artisan, and will not put it up in secret.” These and those did answer and say: Amen. They turned their faces towards Mount Ebal and opened with the curse<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 27:15" href="/Deuteronomy.27.15"><i>Deut.</i> 27:15</a>.</i>: “Cursed be the man who would make a hewn or molten [image], the Eternal’s abomination, made by an artisan, and would put it up in secret.” These and those did answer and say: Amen, until they finished blessings and curses.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It was stated: Rebbi says, it is impossible to say that Levi is up<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 27:11-14" href="/Deuteronomy.27.11-14"><i>Deut</i> 27:11–14</a> seems to be self-contradictory. In v. 12, it is stated that the tribes of Simeon, Levi, Jehudah, etc. have to stand on Mount Gerizim but in v. 14 the Levites are in the middle to recite the curses.</i> because it already was said that Levi is down. It is impossible to say that Levi is down because it already was said that Levi is up. From this you say, the Elders of the Levites and the priests are down and the rest of the tribe are up<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sotah (Lieberman) 8:8" href="/Tosefta_Sotah_(Lieberman).8.8">Tosephta, 8:8</a>, and the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 37a" href="/Sotah.37a">Babli, 37a</a>, Rebbi is reported to say that all tribes were standing at the foot of the respective mountains (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 8:33" href="/Joshua.8.33"><i>Jos.</i> 8:33</a>) The opinion given here is there ascribed to R. Eliezer ben Jacob.</i>. Rebbi Simeon says, it is impossible to say that Levi is up because it already was said that Levi is down and it is impossible to say that Levi is down because it already was said that Levi is up. From this you say, anyone able to serve<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">In the Tabernacle, between the ages of 25 and 50 (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 8:24-25" href="/Numbers.8.24-25"><i>Num.</i> 8:24–25</a>). In Tosephta and Babli, this opinion is ascribed to R. Joshia.</i> is down and the rest of the tribe is up. Rebbi Simeon says, “Simeon and Levi”, since all of Simeon are up, also all of Levi are up. How do I confirm “in face of the Cohanim, the Levites”? <sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ma‘aśer Šeni</i> 5:5, Note 90; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 86b" href="/Yevamot.86b">Babli <i>Yebamot</i> 86b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 24b" href="/Chullin.24b"><i>Ḥulin</i> 24b</a>.</i> As Rebbi Joshua ben Levi said, in 24 places are Cohanim called Levites and this is one of them<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Ezekiel 44:15" href="/Ezekiel.44.15"><i>Ez.</i> 44:15</a>.</i>: “The Cohanim Levites, the descendants of Ṣadoq.”
One could think that those on Mount Gerizim did recite the blessings and those on Mount Ebal did recite the curses, the verse<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">There is no such verse; both in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 30:1" href="/Deuteronomy.30.1"><i>Deut.</i> 30:1</a> and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 8:34" href="/Joshua.8.34"><i>Jos.</i> 8:34</a> the singular is used. The plural is used for “blessings” in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 28:2" href="/Deuteronomy.28.2"><i>Deut.</i> 28:2</a>, for “curses” in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 28:15" href="/Deuteronomy.28.15"><i>Deut.</i> 28:15</a>.</i> says “the blessings and the curses”; both were saying blessings and curses. One could think that after they had recited the blessings they were reciting the curses, the verse<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 11:26" href="/Deuteronomy.11.26"><i>Deut.</i> 11:26</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 8:34" href="/Joshua.8.34"><i>Jos.</i> 8:34</a>. From here on, a similar argument is in <i>Sifry Deut.</i> 55; that text is reproduced in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 37b" href="/Sotah.37b">Babli, 37b</a>.</i> says “the blessing and the curse”, one blessing and one curse. One could think that those on Mount Gerizim did answer “Amen” after the blessings and those on Mount Ebal did answer “Amen” after the curses, the verse<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 27:15" href="/Deuteronomy.27.15"><i>Deut</i>. 27:15</a>.</i> says “the entire people will answer and say “Amen”; these and those did answer “Amen” after the blessings and after the curses. How is that? When they were reciting the blessings they were turning their faces towards Mount Gerizim; when they were reciting the curses they were turning their faces towards Mount Ebal.
<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 37" href="/Sotah.37">Babli 37 a/b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sotah (Lieberman) 8:10-11" href="/Tosefta_Sotah_(Lieberman).8.10-11">Tosephta 8:10–11</a>. The explanation follows Rashi in the Babli.</i> Blessed in general and blessed in particular<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 27:26" href="/Deuteronomy.27.26"><i>Deut.</i> 27:26</a> is general, referring to “the words of this Torah”, but <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 27:15-25" href="/Deuteronomy.27.15-25">vv. 15–25</a> refer to particular sins.</i>; cursed in general and cursed in particular. Sixteen covenants on every detail, to study, to teach, to keep, and to do<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">For each commandment one is obligated to study it (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 6:6" href="/Deuteronomy.6.6"><i>Deut.</i> 6:6</a>), to teach it (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 6:7" href="/Deuteronomy.6.7"><i>Deut.</i> 6:7</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 11:19" href="/Deuteronomy.11.19">11:19</a>), to keep and execute it (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 4:6" href="/Deuteronomy.4.6"><i>Deut.</i> 4:6</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 5:1" href="/Deuteronomy.5.1">5:1</a>). There are four obligations that bring blessings and four omissions that bring curses, in all 8. Since every commandment is also part of “the words of this Torah”, that equals 16 for each single commandment.</i>; and the same on Sinai<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 26:46" href="/Leviticus.26.46"><i>Lev.</i> 26:46</a>.</i> and the same in the prairie of Moab<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 28:69" href="/Deuteronomy.28.69"><i>Deut.</i> 28:69</a>.</i>. Rebbi Simeon does exclude that of Mount Gerizim and Mount Ebal<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">Since Joshua executed only a commandment of Moses; he did not conclude a new covenant at that moment, but did so later, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 24:25" href="/Joshua.24.25"><i>Jos.</i> 24:25</a>.</i> and instead includes that of the Tent of Meeting<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 27b" href="/Sotah.27b">Babli, 27b</a>, quotes here a disagreement between R. Aqiba, who holds that the entire Torah was given at Sinai and repeated in the Tent of Meeting, and R. Ismael who holds that the general outline was given at Sinai but the details only in the Tent of Meeting (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 1:1" href="/Leviticus.1.1"><i>Lev.</i> 1:1</a>).</i>. Also Rebbi Simeon<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">In the Tosephta, R. Simeon is credited with the statement that a total of 3 times 16 covenants were established for every commandment.</i> did say, there is no single detail in the Torah about which not 576 covenants were concluded. Twelve<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">One for each verse of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 27:15-26" href="/Deuteronomy.27.15-26"><i>Deut.</i> 27:15–26</a>.</i> in blessing, twelve in cursing. Twelve in general, twelve in detail. That makes 48 covenants. To study, to teach, to keep, and to do, that makes 192 covenants. The same on Sinai and the same in the prairie of Moab, that makes 576 covenants. Rebbi Simeon ben Jehudah from the village of Emmaus<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babylonian sources, the name of the village is עכוֹ or עיכוס, עיבום but in the Tosephta also once עמוס, cf. S. Lieberman, <i>Tosefta kiFshutah Našim</i> p. 711; A. Liss, ed., <i>The Babylonian Talmud with variant readings, Sotah,</i> vol. 2, p. 148, Notes 33,39*.</i> says in the name of Rebbi Simeon: 603’550<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">This is the census of the tribes in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 1:46" href="/Numbers.1.46"><i>Num.</i> 1:46</a>, excluding the Levites. This means that he counts one individual covenant for each Israelite. This is the explicit statement in the Tosephta. In the Babli: 48 covenants for 603’550, attributed to Rebbi in our text. The statement of Rebbi in the Tosephta is: 13 covenants for 603’550.</i>. Rebbi says, if one follows the words of Rebbi Simeon ben Jehudah from the village of Emmaus who said in the name of Rebbi Simeon, then there is no single detail in the Torah about which not 48 times 603’550 covenants were concluded.
So far subjects which were stated in general and in detail<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">Of the twelve blessings and curses (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 7:4:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.7.4.4">Note 102</a>), one is general and 11 are in detail. The ones in detail are also contained in the last, general, blessing and curse.</i>. Subjects which were stated in general but not in detail<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">Any other commandment in the Torah. Are there any covenants that force one to obey those?</i>? He said to him: So says Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Simeon<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">In all Babli-inspired sources, this is the 9th hermeneutic rule of R. Ismael; <i><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Braita d'Rabbi Yishmael" href="/Sifra,_Braita_d'Rabbi_Yishmael">Sifra, Introduction</a>.</i> In Chapter 9, the authorship is attributed to (tannaitic) rabbis who oppose R. Ismael; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 9:6:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.9.6.2">Chapter 9, Notes 87–89</a>.</i>. Anything which was in a set and and left the set to teach, not to teach only about itself did it leave but to teach about the entire set<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">The example given in <i>Sifra</i> is: It is written in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:20" href="/Leviticus.7.20"><i>Lev.</i> 7:20</a> that eating from a well-being sacrifice that had become impure is a deadly sin. But well-being sacrifices are only one element of the set of all sacrifices. In the absence of a biblical statement to the contrary, it can be asserted that eating from <i>any</i> sacrifice that had become impure is a deadly sin. The set is that of all sacrifices, from which the well-being sacrifice was singled out for a special statement.</i>. He said to him: Is that not about a single subject? Perhaps for two subjects? And here<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">And in this case there are 11 particular statements and one defining the set as all commandments of the Torah.</i>, we deal with two different subjects. Rebbi Tanḥuma said, since at the end is written “Amen”, it is as if everything were one subject.
It is written: “Cursed be he who would not raise all the words of this Torah.” Can the Torah fall down? Simeon ben Yaqim<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 27:26" href="/Deuteronomy.27.26">Nachmanides (Commentary to <i>Deut.</i> 27:26</a>) reads Rebbi Simeon ben Yaqim.</i> says, that is the <i>ḥazzan</i> who stands<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">Nachmanides does not read the last two words. It would seem that his reading is correct. In Europe, the word חזן always denoted the reader who <i>stood</i> before the congregation and led it in prayer. However, in the East, the חזן was always the employee of the congregation who functioned as rabbi, reader, ritual slaughterer, and general organizer of the community (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 3:1:14" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.3.1.14">Berakhot 3:1, Note 60</a>). At Torah readings, the <i>ḥazzan</i> had to see to it that the Torah scroll was lifted and shown to all present.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Ḥalaphta says, that is the earthly house<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">Nachmanides reads זֶה בֵּית דִּין שֶׁלְּמַטָּה “that is the earthly court” (which has to supervise the moral state of the community.)</i>, as Rav Ḥuna, Rav Jehudah said in the name of Samuel: Josia tore [his garments] about this verse<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Kings 22:11" href="/II_Kings.22.11"><i>2K.</i> 22:11</a>. He asserts that upon hearing the Torah [which by tradition had been forbidden by his father Amon (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 27:26" href="/Deuteronomy.27.26"><i>Midrash Haggadol Deut.</i> on <i>Deut.</i>27:26</a>)], Josia tore his garments on hearing <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 27:26" href="/Deuteronomy.27.26"><i>Deut.</i> 27:26</a>.</i> and said, I have to raise.
<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">A fundraiser’s sermon</i> Rebbi Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Tanḥum ben Rebbi Ḥiyya. If somebody studied and taught, kept and did, and it was in his power to strengthen<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">To give financial support to institutions of Torah learning.</i>, but he did not strengthen, that one is in the set of the cursed. Rebbi Jeremiah in the name of Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba: If somebody did not study, did not teach, did not keep and did not do, and it was not in his hand to strengthen but he did strengthen<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">He gave financial support to institutions of Torah learning even though he was not rich.</i>, that one is in the set of the blessed. And Rebbi Ḥana, Rebbi Jeremiah, said in the name of Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba: In the future [world], the Holy One, praise to Him, will make shadow of those who perform commandments<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">They give money to charity. A similar statement in the name of R. Joḥanan is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 53b" href="/Pesachim.53b">Babli, <i>Pesaḥim</i> 53b</a>.</i> in the shadow of those who study Torah. What is the reason? “For in the shadow of Torah, in the shadow of money<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Ecclesiastes 7:12" href="/Ecclesiastes.7.12"><i>Eccl.</i> 7:12</a></i>”. And it is said, “it<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">The Torah (taken here as symbol for the institutions of Torah study).</i> is a tree of life for those who support it.<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 3:18" href="/Proverbs.3.18"><i>Prov.</i> 3:18</a>. <i>Midrash rabba Lev.</i>25(1): “It does not say ‘it is a tree of life for those who study it’, but ‘who support it’.</i>”
Why does the verse<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 8:33" href="/Joshua.8.33"><i>Jos.</i> 8:33</a>, those who went to Mount Gerizim are חֶצְיוֹ “half”, but those on Mount Ebal are וְהַחֶצְיוֹ “and <i>the</i> half”. The appearance of the definite article has to be explained.</i> say: “and the half of it”? This teaches that a minority was on Mount Gerizim and a majority on Mount Ebal<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">Based on the figures of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 26" href="/Numbers.26"><i>Num.</i> 26</a>, there would be about 20’000 fewer persons on Mount Gerizim than on Mount Ebal, the Levites not counted. Since the Levites were counted separately, as unfit for war, and they were counted from one month of age, the numbers cannot be compared. Including children from the age of one month, there were only 23’000 Levites.</i>. Why? Because the tribe of Levi was not completely there. For if the tribe of Levi had been completely there, they would have been equal. Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman in the name of Rebbi Jonathan: If the tribe of Levi had been completely there, they would not have been equal. Why? Since already 24’000 had fallen at Shiṭṭim from the tribe of Simeon<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">In the count of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 1" href="/Numbers.1"><i>Num.</i> 1</a>, the tribe of Simeon had 59’300 warriors, but in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 26" href="/Numbers.26"><i>Num.</i> 26</a>, that number had shrunk to 22’200. The people who had died at Shiṭṭim were 24’000 (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 25:9" href="/Numbers.25.9"><i>Num.</i> 25:9</a>). This is less than the total diminution of the tribe of Simeon.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, if the tribe of Levi had been completely there, they would have been equal<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">This is difficult to understand. Following <a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 4:13" href="/Joshua.4.13"><i>Jos.</i> 4:13</a>, the Transjordanian tribes together delivered “about 40’000 frontline soldiers”. There is no indication that the Transjordanien tribe of Manasse was counted separately. In any case, the data in Scripture are insufficient to express any opinion in the matter.</i>. Why? Because from the tribes of Reuben and Gad only 40’000 front line troops came there.
Cahana said, just as they divide here, so they divide at the beginning of the second book of the Pentateuch<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 1:2-3" href="/Exodus.1.2-3"><i>Ex</i>. 1:2–3</a>. There, the order is quite different: First the sons of the wives, then the sons of the handmaidens. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 36" href="/Sotah.36">Babli, 36a/b</a>, the opinion is attributed to R. Ḥanina ben Gamliel.</i>. Some Tannaïm state: Just as they divide here, so they divide in the standards<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">This opinion is not found in the Babli. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 2" href="/Numbers.2"><i>Num</i>. 2</a>. There the order is: Standard of Judah: Judah, Issachar, Zebulun. Standard of Reuben: Reuben, Simeon, Gad. Standard of Ephraim: Ephraim, Manasse, Benjamin. Standard of Dan: Dan, Asher, Naftali. This also is incompatible with the order given in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 27:13-14" href="/Deuteronomy.27.13-14"><i>Deut</i>. 27:13–14</a>: Simeon, Levi, Jehudah, Issachar, Joseph, Benjamin - Reuben, Gad, Asher, Zebulun, Dan, Naftali.</i>. The sons of Leah on one side, the sons of Rachel one on one side, the other one on the other side, and the sons of the handmaidens in the middle<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">The verse indicates which tribes were standing on which mountain but does not indicate how they were standing. The opinion here is that on Mount Gerizim the two Rachel tribes, Joseph and Benjamin, were standing at the right and left extremes and the Leah tribes in the middle; on Mount Ebal the two Leah tribes, Reuben and Zebulun were standing at the right and left extremes and the handmaidens’ tribes in the middle. (Explanation of <i>Pene Moshe</i>). In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 36b" href="/Sotah.36b">Babli, 36b</a>.</i>. Rebbi Mattania said, the reason of this Tanna is: “Hear me, Jacob my servant, Israel my roofed in<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Jes</i>. 48:12. The verse is misquoted; “my servant” is not written. The interpretation of this conceit is based on rabbinic Hebrew קוֹרָא “roof girder”; modern Hebrew קוֹרָה.</i>.” Just as on the roof the thick part of one [beam] is next to the slim part of the other [beam]<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that their roofs were made from wooden beams of a slightly conical shape so that they could be pressed together to protect against the rains. A similar picture <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shemot Rabbah 1:6" href="/Shemot_Rabbah.1.6"><i>Ex. rabba</i> 1(6</a>).</i>. Some Tannaïm state: Just as they are split here, so they are split on the stones of the ephod<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 28:9-10" href="/Exodus.28.9-10"><i>Ex</i>. 28:9–10</a>. The order in which the names of the tribes were to be engraved on the Shoham stones is indicated only obliquely as כְּתוֹלְדֹתָם “in the order of their birth”; that would be Reuben, Simeon, Levi, Jehudah, Dan, Naftali; Gad, Asher, Issachar, Zebulun, Joseph, Benjamin. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 36a" href="/Sotah.36a">Babli, 36a</a>, this is the opinion of Rav Cahana.</i>. “In their fulnesses”, that there should be 25 on one side and 25 on the other side<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">The word is written not for the Shoham stones of the <i>ephod</i> but for the stones representing the tribes on the <i>ḥoshen</i>(<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 28:20" href="/Exodus.28.20"><i>Ex</i>. 28:20</a>), on which the names of the tribes were engraved (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 28:21" href="/Exodus.28.21">v. 21</a>) and which were set <i>to fill</i> the settings prepared for them.</i>. But they are only 49<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">As written, the names of the Gerizim tribes add up to 27 letters, those of the Ebal tribes to 22. The same objection in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 36b" href="/Sotah.36b">Babli, 36b</a>.</i>! Rebbi Joḥanan said, “Benjamin” of “and their births” is <i>plene</i><sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">On the stone, the name of Benjamin was written בנימין for a total of 28 letters. This opinion is not quoted in the Babli; it is dismissed in the Yerushalmi.</i>. Rebbi Judah bar Zabida said, Jehoseph is <i>plene</i>, “He put it up as a testimony in Jehoseph.<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 81:6" href="/Psalms.81.6"><i>Ps</i>. 81:6</a>. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 36b" href="/Sotah.36b">Babli, 36b</a>, this is the opinion of R. Isaac.</i>” But they are only 23 on one side and 27 on the other<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">This questioner reads זבולון <i>plene</i> but בנימן defective and יוסף in its usual form.</i>! Rebbi Joḥanan said, Benjamin was split, Ben on one side, Jamin on the other side<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">R. Joḥanan cannot follow his own opinion that Benjamin was spelled <i>plene</i> but must follow R. Judah bar Zabida. Then on the right hand side there were 27 + 1 - 3 = 25 letters and on the left hand side 22 + 3 = 25 letters.</i>. Rebbi Zabida said, this is fine. Does it say “their six names”? No, but, “of their names”, not their entire names<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 28:10" href="/Exodus.28.10"><i>Ex</i>. 28:10</a>, the group of the first six names is charaterized as מִשְּׁמֹתָם, “of their names”, taking the מ, as usual in rabbinic texts, as partitive. The other six names are “the six other names”, to be written in full (and, according to the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 36a" href="/Sotah.36a">Babli, 36a</a>, in the order of their births.)</i>! The first were written on the right hand side of the High Priest, to the left of the viewer. The last were written on the left hand side of the High Priest, to the right of the viewer. The first ones were not written in order, for Jehudah is king. The last ones were written in order<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">That means, not in the order in which they are enumerated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 27:14" href="/Deuteronomy.27.14"><i>Deut</i>. 27:14</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> After that they brought the stones<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">Which stones they were is a matter of discussion.</i>, built the altar<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">On Mount Ebal, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 27:4" href="/Deuteronomy.27.4"><i>Deut</i>. 27:4</a>.</i>, whitewashed it with lime<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">On Mount Ebal, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 27:4" href="/Deuteronomy.27.4"><i>Deut</i>. 27:4</a>.</i>, and wrote on it all the words of this Torah in seventy languages<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">The standard number of peoples on earth as enumerated in <i>Ber</i>. 10.</i>, as it is written: “well explained<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 27:8" href="/Deuteronomy.27.8"><i>Deut</i>. 27:8</a>. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 8:35" href="/Joshua.8.35"><i>Jos</i>. 8:35</a> this is taken to mean that Joshua had to explain the Torah to men, women, and children.</i>”. They took the stones<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">Which stones they were is a matter of discussion.</i>, went and stayed in their place overnight<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">At Gilgal, near Jericho.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It was stated: It was written on the stones of the dwelling place, the words of Rebbi Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">In the interpretation of Rashi, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 35b" href="/Sotah.35b">Babli 35b</a>, they took the stones, built with them the altar on Mount Ebal, disassembled the altar, took the stones to Gilgal, inscribed the Torah with 70 translations on the stones and covered everything with whitewash. The text of the Babli is paralleled in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sotah (Lieberman) 8:6" href="/Tosefta_Sotah_(Lieberman).8.6">Tosephta, 8:6</a>.<br>Both in the Babli and in the Tosephta this opinion is that of the anonymous majority.</i>; Rebbi Yose says, it was written on the stones of the altar<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">In Babli and Tosephta, this is ascribed to R. Jehudah..</i>. He who says, it was written on the stones of the dwelling place, every day the peoples of the world were sending their stenographers<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>notarius</i>, “stenographer”.</i> and they carried away the Torah which had been written in seventy languages<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">They lifted the whitewash from the stone and had the letters stand out, and transported everything back to its place. In the opinion of R. Simeon, the translations were written on the whitewash where they could be copied.<br>In talmudic theory, Adam was created literate since he had a book (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 5:1" href="/Genesis.5.1"><i>Gen</i>. 5:1</a>). If at any time in human history, peoples were found who were illiterate, they must have been degenerate [<a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 38b" href="/Sanhedrin.38b"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 38b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 5a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.5a"><i>Avodah zarah</i> 5a</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bereishit Rabbah 24:7" href="/Bereishit_Rabbah.24.7"><i>Gen. rabba</i> 24(7</a>)]. Adam was also created an inventor and engineer (cf. the author’s <i>The Scholar’s Haggadah</i>, pp. 219–221; <i>A Jewish Fundamentalist Philosophy of Science and Development</i>, Association F. Gonseth, Bulletin 63, 1992, pp. 9–20.)</i>. He who says, it was written on the stones of the altar, were they not only for a short time and then were hidden? That was another wonder, that the Holy One, praise to Him, gave insight to every people and they carried away the Torah which had been written in seventy languages. For him who says, it was written on the stones of the dwelling place, it is understandable that “you shall whitewash them with lime.” But he who says, it was written on the stones of the altar, how does he uphold “ “you shall whitewash them with lime”? Between the stones<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">This is not mentioned in Babli-Tosephta.</i>. Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥmani in the name of Rebbi Jonathan: “The peoples will be lime incinerators<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 33:12" href="/Isaiah.33.12"><i>Is</i>. 33:12</a>. In Babli and Tosephta, this is attributed to R. Simeon.</i>”, from the lime they took their death sentence<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ἀπόφασις “judgment, sentence”.</i>. Rebbi Abba bar Cahana in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: “But the Gentiles will be totally destroyed<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 60:12" href="/Isaiah.60.12"><i>Is</i>. 60:12</a>. This sermon is not mentioned in the parallel sources. It is based on the identification of חרב I, Akkadic <i>ẖarābu</i> “to dry up” and חרב II, Arabic خرب “to destroy”. The basis of this sermon and entire paragraph is the theory, based on <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 33:2" href="/Deuteronomy.33.2"><i>Deut</i>. 33:2</a>, that before the epiphany on Horeb/Mount Sinai, God had offered the Torah to all the other peoples of the earth, who refused, and only Israel accepted. Therefore, only Israel reaps the rewards in the Future World (cf. <i>Sifry Deut</i>. 343, ed. L. Finkelstein; all the other sources are given there in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 7:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.7.1.3">Note 14</a>.)</i>,” from Ḥoreb they took their death sentence.
You may say that there were three kinds of stones<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sotah (Lieberman) 8:6" href="/Tosefta_Sotah_(Lieberman).8.6">Tosephta 8:6</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 35b" href="/Sotah.35b">Babli 35b</a>.</i>: The stones of the resting place<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 4:3" href="/Joshua.4.3"><i>Jos</i>. 4:3</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 4:8" href="/Joshua.4.8">8</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 4:20" href="/Joshua.4.20">20</a>.</i>, and the stones which Joshua put under the soles of the feet of the Cohanim<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 4:9" href="/Joshua.4.9"><i>Jos</i>. 4:9</a>.</i>, and the steles<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">Greek στήλη. For the use of steles in ancient synagogues, cf. י. בראבד, ״איצטלין״, לשוננו 32 תשכח-1968 ע׳ 276.</i> that Moses had given them<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">This is explained in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 35b" href="/Sotah.35b">Babli, 35b</a>. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 1:5" href="/Deuteronomy.1.5"><i>Deut</i>. 1:5</a> it is stated that Moses <i>explained</i> the Torah. In 27:8 it is commanded to copy the Torah on the stones, <i>well explained</i>. Since in the latter cases, “explained” means “engraved in stone”, it follows that in the first case “explained” also must mean engraved in stone.</i>. Rebbi Ḥaninan said, it is obvious to us that there were four kinds of stones<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">That the stones on which the Torah was written translated into 70 languages were none of the above three kinds. This opinion is not mentioned in Babli/Tosephta.</i>. Rebbi Simon ben Zebid said, that is correct. If you would say, they were the stones of the resting place; these were temporary and were hidden<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">This is difficult to understand since the stones should be there as a permanent memorial, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 4:21-24" href="/Joshua.4.21-24"><i>Jos</i>. 4:21–24</a>. In any case, the verses make it clear that these stones were not to be used to be written on.</i>. If you would say, the stones which Joshua put under the soles of the feet of the Cohanim; these were submerged in the water. If you would say, the steles that Moses had given them; they already had moved with them into the Land<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote">Even if they had taken the steles with them, the Torah already was written on them and they could not be those on which the Torah was written anew.</i>. But we must deal with the stones that Joshua put up for them on the banks of the Jordan.
It was stated: Rebbi Jehudah bar Ilai says: Abba Ḥalaphta, Rebbi Eleazar ben Mattia, and Ḥanina bar Ḥakhinai stood on those stones and estimated that each one on them was a load of 40 <i>seah</i>. From here you can estimate how big was the bunch of grapes<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 34a" href="/Sotah.34a">Babli 34a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sotah (Lieberman) 8:6" href="/Tosefta_Sotah_(Lieberman).8.6">Tosephta 8:6</a>.</i>. It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 4:5" href="/Joshua.4.5"><i>Jos</i>. 4:5</a>.</i>: “Each one should lift a stone on his shoulder.” One cannot compare one who lifts from the ground to his shoulder to one who lifts first from the ground to his knee and then from his knee to his shoulder. One cannot compare one who lifts first from the ground to his knee and then from his knee to his shoulder to one onto whom others hang [the load]. One cannot compare one onto whom others hang [the load] to [a load] carried by two. It is written: “One man per tribe.<sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 1:23" href="/Deuteronomy.1.23"><i>Deut</i>. 1:23</a>. This is the wrong quote; intended is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 13:2" href="/Numbers.13.2"><i>Num</i>. 13:2</a>: “One man, one man per ancestral tribe you shall send.” The problem is the interpretation of the duplication.</i>” In the opinion of Rebbi Aqiba who says these are expressions of additions, there were 24, sixteen for the grape bunch and eight for figs, pomegranates, and their belongings<sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 34a" href="/Sotah.34a">Babli, 34a</a>, the verse <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 13:23" href="/Numbers.13.23"><i>Num</i>. 13:23</a>: “they carried [the grape bunch] on a pole by two” is interpreted that it was carried by eight people with the grape bunch hanging between two poles. There, one is said to have carried a fig, another a pomegranate; Joshua and Caleb did not carry anything.</i>. In the opinion of Rebbi Simeon<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote">This attribution seems to be wrong. R. Simeon is not known to reject R. Aqiba’s interpretations. Probably it should read: R. Ismael, who insists that the Torah is written in the vernacular and who is quoted in the next sentence.</i>, who says there are double expressions, there were twelve, eight for the grape bunch and four for figs, pomegranates, and their belongings. In the opinion of Rebbi Ismael a beam<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote">This is the traditional interpretation; in the absence of a convincing etymology the meaning is not guaranteed.<br>In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 34b" href="/Sotah.34b">Babli, 34b</a>, the interpretation given here following R. Aqiba is attributed to R. Isaac; the wording is טורטני וטרטני דטורטני. The word טורטני appears in quite a number of other sources (collected in Krauss, <i>Lehnwörter</i>) where it is perfectly adequate to see in it Greek τρυτάνη “pair of scales”. By the concurrent testimony of Rashi and R. Ḥananel, certainly based on Gaonic material, the interpretation of R. Isaac’s statement is that the grape bunch was pictured carried between two poles which at each end were fixed to a cross-beam. Each cross-beam at both extremities had smaller cross beams, giving space for 8 bearers, all marching in the same direction. There is no reason not to accept this interpretation for the Yerushalmi; but for the position of the Yerushalmi following R. Ismael, it seems rather that the image is that of a single pole, with four men preceding the grape bunch and four men following behind it.<br>It is possible that “scale” here stands for “scale bar”. But it is possible that טורטני may be a Babylonian corruption of a Semitic word טוּרְטוּר of <i>pilpel</i> type.</i>; in the opinion of Rebbi Aqiba a beam and beams of beams.
<sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 34a" href="/Sotah.34a">Babli 34a</a> and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sotah (Lieberman) 8:2-3" href="/Tosefta_Sotah_(Lieberman).8.2-3">Tosephta 8:2–3</a>.</i> It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 4:16" href="/Joshua.4.16"><i>Jos</i>. 4:16</a>.</i>: “The waters descending from higher up stood like a dam, very far, from the town of Adam which is besides Ṣāretān<sup class="footnote-marker">169a</sup><i class="footnote">Nelson Glueck (AASOR xxv-xxviii, 1945-1948) identifies Ṣāretān as Ṣā'idlyāh. [added from Addenda and Corrigenda by Guggeneheimer].</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, Adam is a town and Ṣāretān is a town; there are twelve <i>mil</i> from one to the other. That teaches that the water was stacked up and rose like the citadel of Ṣāretān. <sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to a tannaïtic discussion quoted in the Babli but not here.</i> Which one is lighter, water or man? Waters are lighter than man; you know that this is so. This teaches that the waters were stacked and increased, wave upon wave. Rebbi Levi said, up to the sky’s heart. There<sup class="footnote-marker">171</sup><i class="footnote">In Babylonia. This argument is not in the Babli.</i>, they said, as far as Babylon. It says here<sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 4:16" href="/Joshua.4.16"><i>Jos</i>. 4:16</a>.</i> “far”, and it says there<sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Kings 20:14" href="/II_Kings.20.14"><i>2K</i>. 20:14</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 39:3" href="/Isaiah.39.3"><i>Is</i>. 39:3</a>.</i> “they came from a very far land, from Babylon.” It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, and the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sotah (Lieberman) 8:3" href="/Tosefta_Sotah_(Lieberman).8.3">Tosephta 8:3</a>, this is attributed to R. Eleazar bar R. Simeon.</i>: Rebbi Eliezer ben Jacob said, the waters were stacked and rose, waves on waves, for more than 300 <i>mil</i> until all the peoples of the world saw them. That is what is written<sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 5:1" href="/Joshua.5.1"><i>Jos</i>. 5:1</a>.</i>: “It happened when all the kings of the Emorite on the other side of the Jordan, and all the Canaanite dwelling on the Sea, heard that the Eternal had dried up the waters of the river Jordan for the children of Israel until they had passed across,” etc.
Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, in the Jordan they accepted the secret matters<sup class="footnote-marker">175</sup><i class="footnote">That the people as a whole will be punished for secret sins of an individual. Moses had explicitly ruled out responsibility of the public for hidden deeds of the individual, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 29:28" href="/Deuteronomy.29.28"><i>Deut</i>. 29:28</a>.<br>In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 43b" href="/Sanhedrin.43b">Babli, <i>Sanhedrin</i> 43b</a>, the position of R. Simon ben Laqish is similar to that of the Tanna R. Jehudah. The responsibility of the public for hidden deeds of the individual is denied by R. Nehemiah.</i>. Joshua told them, if you do not accept the secret matters, the waters will come and sweep you away. Rebbi Simon bar Zabida said, that is correct. You should know that this is so, for Akhan sinned and the majority of the Synhedrion fell at Ai<sup class="footnote-marker">176</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 7:5" href="/Joshua.7.5"><i>Jos</i>. 7:5</a>; Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 44a" href="/Sanhedrin.44a">Babli <i>Sanhedrin</i> 44a</a>. In the opinion of the Babli, Akhan’s sin was not private since his family knew about it.</i>. Rebbi Levi said, at Jabneh the rope was untied; there came a disembodied voice and said: You have nothing to do with secret matters<sup class="footnote-marker">177</sup><i class="footnote">In later rabbinic language, this phrase was used in the sense that mystical sayings should have no standing in matters of religious law or practice.</i>.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> How is the priestly blessing? In the countryside they recited it as three blessings<sup class="footnote-marker">178</sup><i class="footnote">Each one of the verses <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:24-26" href="/Numbers.6.24-26"><i>Num</i>. 6:24–26</a> to be answered by “Amen”.</i>, but in the Temple as one blessing<sup class="footnote-marker">179</sup><i class="footnote">To be answered by the people at the end by “praised be the Name of the glory of His kingdom forever and ever”.</i>. In the Temple one says the Name as it is written, but in the countryside by its circumlocution<sup class="footnote-marker">180</sup><i class="footnote">“The Lord” אֲדוֹנָי or κύριος.</i>. In the countryside the Cohanim lift their hands to the height of their shoulders but in the Temple over their heads except for the High Priest who does not lift his hands over the diadem. Rebbi Jehudah says, the High Priest also lifts his hands over his head, as it is said<sup class="footnote-marker">181</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 9:22" href="/Leviticus.9.22"><i>Lev</i>. 9:22</a>; since the ritual of blessing with raised hands is derived from this verse, it would be unreasonable to have the Cohanim not conform to Aaron’s, the High Priest’s, example.</i>: “Aaron lifted his hands towards the people and blessed them.” How are the blessings of the High Priest<sup class="footnote-marker">182</sup><i class="footnote">On the Day of Atonement; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yoma 7:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yoma.7.1.1">Mishnah <i>Yoma</i> 7:1</a>.</i>? The organizer of the synagogue<sup class="footnote-marker">183</sup><i class="footnote">On the Temple Mount. This is a non-scriptural ceremony, purely Pharisaic, but followed, at least since Hasmonean times, even by Sadducee High Priests.</i> takes a Torah scroll and gives it to the president of the synagogue; the president of the synagogue gives it to the Second<sup class="footnote-marker">184</sup><i class="footnote">The second in command in the Temple after the High Priest; in effect his executive officer.</i>; the Second gives it to the High Priest. The High priest receives it standing, he stands and reads “after the death<sup class="footnote-marker">185</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 16:1-34" href="/Leviticus.16.1-34"><i>Lev</i>. 16:1–34</a>, the description of the Atonement service.</i>” and “but on the tenth<sup class="footnote-marker">186</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:26-32" href="/Leviticus.23.26-32"><i>Lev</i>. 23:26–32</a>. Winding from Chap. 17 to 23 does not take much time.</i>”; he rolls the Torah tight, puts it in his bosom and says: More than what I read before you is written here. “And on the tenth” in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 29" href="/Numbers.29">Numbers<sup class="footnote-marker">187</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Num</i>. 29:7–11</a>.</i> he recites by heart, and recites eight benedictions<sup class="footnote-marker">188</sup><i class="footnote">These are detailed in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 7:7:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.7.7.2-3">Halakhah 7</a>.</i>: For the Torah, for the Temple service, for thanksgiving, for forgiveness of sins, for the Temple, for Israel, for the Cohanim, and the remainder of the prayer.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rav Ḥisda said, they<sup class="footnote-marker">189</sup><i class="footnote">The scholars in his academy.</i> had great difficulty; <sup class="footnote-marker">190</sup><i class="footnote">From here to the end of the Halakhah, the text is also in <i>Yoma</i> 7:1.</i> elsewhere you say that one goes to the Torah, and here you say, one brings the Torah to him? But because they are important personalities, the Torah is honored by them<sup class="footnote-marker">191</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 69a" href="/Yoma.69a">Babli, <i>Yoma</i> 69a</a>, the ceremony is for the glory of the High Priest.</i>. Does one not bring the Torah to the Head of the Diaspora over there<sup class="footnote-marker">192</sup><i class="footnote">At solemn occasions in Babylonia.</i>? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, since the seed of David is immersed there<sup class="footnote-marker">193</sup><i class="footnote">The family of the Heads of the Diaspora claimed descent from king Yekhoniah (Yoyakhin); the Head of the Diaspora is treated as King (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 7:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.7.7.1">Mishnah 7</a>).</i> one treats him in the ancestral way.
There<sup class="footnote-marker">195</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 4:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.4.5.1">Mishnah <i>Megillah</i> 4:5</a>.</i>, we have stated: “One winds in Prophets<sup class="footnote-marker">196</sup><i class="footnote">In the public reading from Prophets after the Sabbath reading from the Torah, it is permissible to piece the reading together from different chapters of one prophet.</i> but one does not wind in the Pentateuch.” One winds in one prophet, but not from one prophet to another, but among prophets of the Twelve it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">197</sup><i class="footnote">Since all 12 minor prophets are written together in one scroll, moving from one to the next is like winding in one of the major prophets, each of which is written in his own scroll.</i>. But one does not wind in the Pentateuch; Rebbi Jeremiah in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: Because one does not wind the Torah in public. Rebbi Yose asked, think of it, if it was a small portion<sup class="footnote-marker">198</sup><i class="footnote">In the Palestinian 3½ year cycle, it might be that the portion was too small to be read by seven different persons; may some readers read from another place after the allotted portion was already recited?</i>? But that Israel should hear it in its order<sup class="footnote-marker">199</sup><i class="footnote">Even if one reads more than the allotted portion on one Sabbath, it must be a continuous text.</i>. But did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">200</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah here.</i>: “He reads ‘after the death<sup class="footnote-marker">185</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 16:1-34" href="/Leviticus.16.1-34"><i>Lev</i>. 16:1–34</a>, the description of the Atonement service.</i>’ and ‘but on the tenth<sup class="footnote-marker">186</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:26-32" href="/Leviticus.23.26-32"><i>Lev</i>. 23:26–32</a>. Winding from Chap. 17 to 23 does not take much time.</i>’ ”? It is different here, because that is the order of the day<sup class="footnote-marker">201</sup><i class="footnote">This is a holiday reading, not a Sabbath reading; it has to concentrate on the texts dealing exclusively with that holiday.</i>. Know that it is so since Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, one does not recite by heart, and here he recites by heart<sup class="footnote-marker">202</sup><i class="footnote">From the text too far removed to be wound without inconveniencing the public.</i>. Rebbi Yose commanded Bar Ulla, the organizer of the congregation of Babylonians: When the Torah has been brought back, wind it behind the curtain<sup class="footnote-marker">203</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Yoma</i>, the text reads: כַּד דְּהִיא חָדָא אוֹרִיָּא תְּהֵא גַייֵל לָהּ לְהָדֵי פָרוֹכְתָא. “When you have only one Torah, wind it by the curtain.” The text here is preferable since a good organizer will wind the Torah well before it is used the next time. The editors who mishandled the text, replacing the text here by that from Yoma in modern editions, probably never were Torah readers themselves.</i>. If there are two, return one and bring the other<sup class="footnote-marker">204</sup><i class="footnote">This is not the common usage where one takes out both scrolls and reads from them one after the other. This shows that in Babylonia already in Talmudic times one read from two different scrolls at special occasions (in particular, holidays). One has to correct the statement by Elbogen (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 7:6:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.7.6.4">Note 205, p. 127</a>) that this practice is not recorded before Rav Yehudai Gaon.</i>.
He recites eight benedictions: For the Torah, “He who chooses the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">205</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 41a" href="/Sotah.41a">Babli, 41a</a>: “The benediction for the Torah in the formulation one recites in the Synagogue.” In the opinion of Ismar Elbogen (התפילה בישׂראל בהתפתחוּתה ההיסטוֹרית, תרגם יהוֹשע עמיר, תל־אביב 1972 p. 129), the text given here is the original synagogal text; cf. also <i>Soferim</i>13:8; י. היינמן, התפילה בתקופת התנאים והאמוראים - טיבה ודפוסיה; ירושלים ת̇ש̇כ̇ו̇, ע׳ 105).</i>.” For the Temple service, “For You alone in fear we worship.<sup class="footnote-marker">206</sup><i class="footnote">The Palestinian version of the 16th benediction of the <i>Amidah</i>. It is to be assumed that in this and the following benedictions the entire text was recited, not only the final doxology.</i>” For thanksgiving, “the Good One, You one must thank.<sup class="footnote-marker">207</sup><i class="footnote">The 17th benediction of the <i>Amidah</i>.</i>” For forgiveness of sins, “He Who forgives the sins of His people Israel in mercy.<sup class="footnote-marker">208</sup><i class="footnote">The final paragraph of the middle benediction of the <i>Amidah</i> of the Day of Atonement.</i>” For the Temple, “He Who chooses the Temple”; and Rebbi Idi said, “He Who dwells in Zion.”<sup class="footnote-marker">209</sup><i class="footnote">The full text of this benediction is unknown. Since the statement of R. Idi is quoted as a commentary, not a disagreement, one has to assume that it refers to a prayer text of which R. Idi quotes the beginning.</i> For Israel, “He Who chooses Israel.”<sup class="footnote-marker">210</sup><i class="footnote">In the opinion of Elbogen (<i>loc. cit</i>. p.16), this is the benediction preceding the recitation of the <i>Šema‘</i>.</i> For the Cohanim, “He Who chooses the Cohanim.” And the remainder of the prayer, “entreaty and begging, for Your people Israel need to be helped before You; Praise to You, Eternal, Who hears prayer”<sup class="footnote-marker">211</sup><i class="footnote">  It seems that this is the only prayer text of the eight benedictions which applies only to the reading of the High Priest.</i>.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> How does the reading of the king proceed? At the end of the last<sup class="footnote-marker">212</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, Mishnah and Talmud text, “the first day”.</i> day of Tabernacles, on the eighth<sup class="footnote-marker">213</sup><i class="footnote">In Yerushalmi texts, the eighth day; in Babli texts, the eighth year. Cf. י. נ. אפשטיין, מבוא לנוסח המשנה,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup> ירושלים-תל 537 אביב ת̇ש̇כ̇ד̇, ע׳; A. Liss, ed., <i>The Babylonian Talmud with Variant Readings, Sotah</i> vol. 2, Jerusalem 1979, p. 196.</i>, at the end of the Sabbatical year, one makes a wooden platform<sup class="footnote-marker">214</sup><i class="footnote">Greek βῆμα.</i> for him and he sits on it, as it is said<sup class="footnote-marker">215</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 31:10-11" href="/Deuteronomy.31.10-11"><i>Deut</i>. 31:10–11</a>.</i>: “At the end of seven years, at the assembly of the Sabbatical years at the festival of Tabernacles, when all of Israel appears.” The organizer of the synagogue<sup class="footnote-marker">183</sup><i class="footnote">On the Temple Mount. This is a non-scriptural ceremony, purely Pharisaic, but followed, at least since Hasmonean times, even by Sadducee High Priests.</i> takes a Torah scroll and gives it to the president of the synagogue; the president of the synagogue gives it to the Second<sup class="footnote-marker">184</sup><i class="footnote">The second in command in the Temple after the High Priest; in effect his executive officer.</i>; the Second gives it to the High Priest, the High Priest gives it to the king. The king stands to receive it, he reads sitting down. King Agrippas<sup class="footnote-marker">216</sup><i class="footnote">Agrippas I, grandson of the Idumean Herod from his Jewish wife Mariamne, so that he was eligible for every office even according to the most restrictive standards (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 4:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.4.7.1">Mishnah <i>Qiddušin</i> 4:7</a>). It seems that the party who objected to an Idumean king (when he was inoffensive as Agrippas was) did base their opposition on <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 3:10:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.3.10.1">Mishnah <i>Qiddušin</i> 3:12</a> which states that in any lawful marriage that is not sinful, the child follows his father’s status. Therefore, the child of a convert would be a convert, even if his mother is from Jewish stock. This is the opinion opposed by the authoritative R. Yose in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 4:7:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.4.7.2-3"><i>Qiddušin</i> 4:7</a>.</i> stood to receive, he read while standing, and the Sages praised him. When he reached “you cannot put over yourselves a strange man who is not your brother<sup class="footnote-marker">217</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 17:15" href="/Deuteronomy.17.15"><i>Deut</i>. 17:15</a>.</i>”, tears flowed from his eyes. They said to him, do not fear Agrippas, you are our brother, you are our brother<sup class="footnote-marker">218</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <i>Sifry Deut</i>. 157.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Did not Rebbi Ḥiyya state<sup class="footnote-marker">219</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 40b" href="/Sotah.40b">Babli 40b</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 42b" href="/Sotah.42b">42b</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 101b" href="/Sanhedrin.101b"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 101b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tamid 27a" href="/Tamid.27a"><i>Tamid</i> 27a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 23b" href="/Kiddushin.23b"><i>Qiddušin</i> 23b</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 78b" href="/Kiddushin.78b">78b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 25a" href="/Yoma.25a"><i>Yoma</i>25a</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 69b" href="/Yoma.69b">69b</a>.</i>: Nobody could sit in the Temple courtyard except for kings of the Davidic dynasty, and Rebbi Immi said in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish, even the kings of the Davidic dynasty could not sit in the Temple courtyard!<sup class="footnote-marker">220</sup><i class="footnote">How could a Maccabean or Herodian king sit in the Temple courtyard?</i> Explain it, that he was leaning on the wall in a kind of sitting. But is it not written<sup class="footnote-marker">221</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Samuel 7:18" href="/II_Samuel.7.18"><i>2S</i>. 7:18</a>.</i>: “King David came and sat before the Eternal”? Rebbi Ayvo bar Naggari said, he concentrated on his prayer<sup class="footnote-marker">222</sup><i class="footnote">In rabbinic Hebrew, “concentration” is יִשׁוּב הַדַּעַת “sitting of the mind”.</i>. There<sup class="footnote-marker">223</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 5:10:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.5.10.1">Mishnah <i>Pesaḥim</i> 5:10</a>.</i>, we have stated: The first group left and sat on the Temple Mount, the second in the <i>Ḥel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">224</sup><i class="footnote">Inside the fortification of the Temple Mount but outside the Temple precinct.</i>, and the third at its place<sup class="footnote-marker">225</sup><i class="footnote">From the formulation of the Mishnah, it seems that the third group of people sacrificing the Passover lamb was sitting down in the Temple court while waiting for sundown.</i>! Rebbi Naḥman in the name of Rebbi Mana: Did we state that the third <i>sat</i> at its place? It stood at its place!
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">226</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 42b" href="/Sotah.42b">Babli, 42b</a>, a similar disapproving statement in the name of R. Nathan.</i>: Rebbi Ḥanina ben Gamliel says, many slain fell on the day they were flattering him.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> And he reads from the beginning of “These are the words<sup class="footnote-marker">228</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 1" href="/Deuteronomy.1"><i>Deut</i>. 1</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 1" href="/Deuteronomy.1">1</a>.</i>” to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 6:4-9" href="/Deuteronomy.6.4-9"><i>Šema‘</i>,<i>Šema‘</i><sup class="footnote-marker">229</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Deut</i>. 6:4–9</i></a><i class="footnote">.</i>, “And it shall be if you listen,<sup class="footnote-marker">230</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 11:13-21" href="/Deuteronomy.11.13-21"><i>Deut</i>. 11:13–21</a>.</i>” “tithing you shall tithe,<sup class="footnote-marker">231</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:22-27" href="/Deuteronomy.14.22-27"><i>Deut</i>. 14:22–27</a>.</i>” “when you will have finished tithing,<sup class="footnote-marker">232</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 26:12-15" href="/Deuteronomy.26.12-15"><i>Deut</i>. 26:12–15</a>.</i>” blessings and curses<sup class="footnote-marker">233</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 28" href="/Deuteronomy.28"><i>Deut</i>. 28</a>.</i> [and the paragraph of the king]<sup class="footnote-marker">234</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 17:8-20" href="/Deuteronomy.17.8-20"><i>Deut</i>. 17:8–20</a>.</i>, until he finishes the entire paragraph. The benedictions which the High Priest recites, the king recites, except that he substitutes “for the holidays of pilgrimage<sup class="footnote-marker">235</sup><i class="footnote">The last paragraph of the middle benediction of the <i>Amidah</i> for holidays.</i>” instead of “forgiving of sins”.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Abbahu said, why does one read “ “tithing you shall tithe,<sup class="footnote-marker">231</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:22-27" href="/Deuteronomy.14.22-27"><i>Deut</i>. 14:22–27</a>.</i>”, “when you will have finished tithing,<sup class="footnote-marker">232</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 26:12-15" href="/Deuteronomy.26.12-15"><i>Deut</i>. 26:12–15</a>.</i>”? Because Israel went from the Sabbatical year to the eighth, not to have them forget tithes.<sup class="footnote-marker">236</sup><i class="footnote">Since no tithes may be given from Sabbatical produce.</i> Rebbi Ḥaggai asked before Rebbi Yose, does he not to make <i>havdalah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">237</sup><i class="footnote">The required benediction when leaving a sanctified state (such as Sabbath or holidays) to enter into profane status. If such a benediction is required after the Sabbath (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 5:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.5.2.1"><i>Berakhot</i> 5:2, Note 62</a>), it should also be required after the Sabbatical year in order to permit agricultural work.</i>? He said to him, one already said <i>havdalah</i> on New Year’s Day.

Chapter 8



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The priest anointed for war<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Mishnah 7:2, Note 37.</i> was speaking holy language when he was addressing the people, as it is said<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 20:2-3" href="/Deuteronomy.20.2-3"><i>Deut</i>. 20:2–3</a>.</i>: “It shall be when you approach war, the Cohen shall come near”, that is the priest anointed for war, “shall speak to the people”, in the holy language, “and say to them: Hear, o Israel, you are approching today war against your enemies,” against your enemies but not against your brothers, not Jehudah against Simeon and not Simeon against Benjamin, for if you would fall in their hands they would have mercy on you<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">It is probably unintended that only tribes of the kingdom of Judea are mentioned since <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 8:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.8.2.1">Mishnah 2</a> speaks of Judahites and Ephraimites.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “The priest anointed for war,” etc. Why<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Why must he speak in Hebrew only?</i> the priest anointed for war? Because it is written “he shall speak<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 20:2" href="/Deuteronomy.20.2"><i>Deut</i>. 20:2</a>.</i>”? But about the reading of the <i>Šema‘</i> it is written: “You shall speak about them,” and it may be recited in any language. But because it is written there “saying<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 20:3" href="/Deuteronomy.20.3"><i>Deut</i>. 20:3</a>.</i>”. But for the declaration of tithes it is written “saying<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 26:13" href="/Deuteronomy.26.13"><i>Deut</i>. 26:13</a>.</i>” and it may be recited in any language! Rebbi Ḥaggai said, it says here “to come close<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 20:2" href="/Deuteronomy.20.2"><i>Deut</i>. 20:2</a>.</i>” and it says there, “the Levitic Cohanim shall come close<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 21:5" href="/Deuteronomy.21.5"><i>Deut</i>. 21:5</a>.</i>;” since “coming close” there implies [recitation in] the holy language<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 7:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.7.2.1">Mishnah 7:2</a>.</i>, so also “coming close” here implies [recitation in] the holy language<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">This is an example of an argument outside the hermeneutic rules, used quite frequently in the Babli but very sparingly in the Yerushalmi. It is <i>not</i> an “equal cut” since there is no transfer of meaning involved; it is what is known as הֶקֵּשׁ “tying together”. Both in the case of the priest chosen for war and the calf selected to atone for an unsolved murder case, it should be quite clear that the people involved cannot be heard unless they are reasonably close. In both cases, the note that they have to come near is somewhat redundant. Therefore, one may conclude that the additional expression was used to indicate similar circumstances, which by rabbinic authority is declared to concern the language to be used.</i>. That follows Rebbi Aqiba who says, these are expressions of additions<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the ceremony for the unsolved murder case, where R. Jehudah in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 7:2:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.7.2.2-4">7:2</a> states that common use of the roots אמר, ענה implies use of the holy language. That argument is acceptable only following R. Aqiba, not following R. Ismael. For the latter, the argument of R. Ḥaggai does not prove anything.</i>. Following Rebbi Ismael who says, these are double expressions? Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba said, it says here “to come close” and it says there, “Moses shall come close to the mist<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 20:21" href="/Exodus.20.21"><i>Ex</i>. 20:21</a>; the word order is incorrect in the quote.</i>”, since “coming close” there implies [recitation in] the holy language<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Since Moses was deputized by the people to speak to God Who obviously spoke to them and him in Hebrew. This reference to the holy language is acceptable to R. Ismael.</i>, so also “coming close” here implies [recitation in] the holy language.
Would it not be reasonable that “the commanders should take charge at the head of the people<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 20:9" href="/Deuteronomy.20.9"><i>Deut</i>. 20:9</a>.</i>” and then “the Cohen shall come close<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 20:2" href="/Deuteronomy.20.2"><i>Deut</i>. 20:2</a>.</i>”? He who directs them should inform them! The text is not in the correct order<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">As the text stands, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 20:2-4" href="/Deuteronomy.20.2-4">vv. 2–4</a> are said by the Cohen when the people are formed in battle order, but <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 20:5-9" href="/Deuteronomy.20.5-9">vv. 5–9</a> are said before the army units are formed, before the border is crossed.</i>. At the border, you say that the policeman hears from the Cohen the text and communicates it to the people in any language. But at the battle order (they provide water and food and repair the roads)<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">End of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 8:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.8.5.1">Mishnah 5</a>, speaking of the duties of the men freed from combat duty; a larger text must be missing here in both mss. to the effect that only warriors stood in battle order.</i>. Rebbi Ḥaggai asked, since at the border the policeman hears from the Cohen the text and communicates it to the people in any language, should it not be the same with the battle order? Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Ada objected, is it reasonable that “when the policemen finished talking to the people,” after that “the Cohen shall come close and speak to the people”? He said to him, since you say, the text is not in the correct order, you cannot infer anything.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Continuation of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 8:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.8.1.1">Mishnah 1</a>. The separate count of this Mishnah is characteristic of Palestinian texts.</i> As it was said further<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Chronicles 28:15" href="/II_Chronicles.28.15"><i>2Chr</i>. 28:15</a>.</i>: “The men who were mentioned by name got up, they took charge of the prisoners and all their naked ones they clothed from the booty.” You are going against your enemies and if you should fall into their hands they would not have mercy on you.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “As it was said further,” etc. Rebbi Joḥanan said, anybody who is not in the position of those [men]<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">The “heads of Ephraim”, “princes and community” who forbade to the Ephraimite army to treat the Judahite prisoners as prisoners of war, <a class="refLink" data-ref="II Chronicles 28:12" href="/II_Chronicles.28.12"><i>2Chr</i>. 28:12</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="II Chronicles 28:14" href="/II_Chronicles.28.14">14</a>.</i> who had the power to object, should not object. <sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">The same text <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 5:3:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.5.3.5"><i>Terumot</i> 5:9, Notes 113–115</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Chagigah 1:8:2-14" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Chagigah.1.8.2-14"><i>Ḥagigah</i> 1:8</a> (76d, line 50).</i> Rebbi Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Jonathan: Just as it is a commandment to speak on things that will be done, so it is a commandment not to talk about things that would not be done<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, this is formulated as: “Let them be in error, do not turn them into intentional sinners” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 30a" href="/Beitzah.30a"><i>Beṣah</i> 30a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 60b" href="/Bava_Batra.60b"><i>Baba batra</i> 60b</a>). A formulation similar to the one given here in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 65b" href="/Yevamot.65b"><i>Yebamot</i> 65b</a> in the name of R. Eleazar.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar said, just as it is forbidden to declare the impure pure, so it is forbidden to declare the pure impure. Rebbi Abba bar Jacob in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If a practical case comes before you and you do not know whether to burn or to suspend<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Heave that is impure by biblical standards has to be burned, if impure by rabbinical standards it only has to be kept until it rots and is no longer food and therefore pure by all standards. As a practical matter, it would be preferable to burn heave rabbinically impure in the second degree so that it should not cause third degree disability by inadvertent touching.</i>, always prefer burning to suspension since nothing is more beloved in the Torah than burned oxen and burned rams<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Animals whose blood was used for a purification rite inside the Temple building (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:12" href="/Leviticus.4.12"><i>Lev</i>. 4:12</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:21" href="/Leviticus.4.21">21</a>; 8:17; 16:27).</i>, and they are burned. Rebbi Yose asked: Does one infer something which is not commanded from something which is commanded<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">R. Joḥanan’s argument does not prove anything for rabbinic decisions.</i>?

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> “Your heart should not soften, do not fear, nor become impulsive, do not become impressed before them.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 20:3" href="/Deuteronomy.20.3"><i>Deut</i>. 20:3</a>. This is the speech of the priest appointed for the war.</i>” Your heart should not soften because of the neighing of horses or the gleam of swords. Do not fear, because of the shaking of shields<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Greek θυρεός, ὁ; “oblong shield”; shutter, stone put against a door”, from where modern Hebrew “shutter”.</i> and the stomping of military boots<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>caliga, -ae</i>, f., “military boot; military service.”</i>. Do not become impulsive because of the sound of horns, nor be impressed because of the sound of cries. “For the Eternal, your God, is He Who goes with you to make war on your enemies to save you.” They come with victories of flesh and blood, but you come with the victory of the Omnipresent. The Philistines came with the victory of Goliath; how did he end? In the end, he fell by the sword and they fell with him. But you, you are not so but “for the Eternal, your God, is He Who goes with you to make war on your enemies to save you,” that is the camp of the Ark.
