<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Your heart should not soften,” etc. It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 18:13-15" href="/Psalms.18.13-15"><i>Ps</i>. 18:13–15</a>.</i>: “From the radiation that surrounds Him, His clouds,” against their cavalry squads<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>turma</i> “squadron”. In similar stories in <i>Yalqut Šim‘oni</i> 230, 232: טורמיות.</i>, “passed,” against their cohorts. “Hailstorm,” against their missile throwers<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>ballista, ballistra</i> “military machine, stone-throwing machine”. In a Genizah fragment, the word is spelled בליצרה.</i>. “And embers,” against their throwing machines<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>tormentum</i>, “missile throwing machine”.</i>. “Of fire,” against their naphta. “He thundered from Heaven<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">In the verse, “He thundered <i>in</i> Heaven”.</i>,” against their torches<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">In a Genizah fragment, כנגד סלפידס שלהן. One might read סַלְפִּידְס for Greek σάλπιγξ “heavenly trumpet, thunder”. This fits much better to “thunder” than the “torches” of both mss.</i>. “The Most High made His voice heard,” against the sound of their horns. “He sent His arrows,” against their arrows. “And dispersed them,”, this teaches that they were dispersing them. Rebbi Abba bar Cahana said, this teaches that they were in rectangular formations, that the arrows dispersed them but the lightnings were bringing them together. “Lightnings,” against their swords. “And confused them,” He confused them, made them disoriented, and felled their standard<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>signum</i>, “standard”. In a similar version, <i>Tanḥuma Bešallaḥ</i> 23, “He felled their standard and so made them disoriented.” In a Genizah fragment, the word is vocalized הֲסִיגְנַס.</i>. Rebbi said,<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">In a Genizah fragment: דָּבָר אַחֵר “another explanation”.</i> the expression “and confounded them” means only pestilence, as it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 7:23" href="/Deuteronomy.7.23"><i>Deut</i>. 7:23</a>.</i>: “He will confuse them in a great confusion until He destroyed them.”
“ ‘To save you’, that is the camp of the Ark.” But some say, that is the Name which was given in the Ark<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Similarly, the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 42b-43a" href="/Sotah.42b-43a">Babli (<i>Soṭah</i>42b/43a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 14b" href="/Bava_Batra.14b"><i>Baba batra</i> 14b</a>) notes that “The Name and all its replacements are deposited in the Ark” (in one of Rashi’s versions, “standing in the Ark”). In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sotah (Lieberman) 7:17" href="/Tosefta_Sotah_(Lieberman).7.17">Tosephta (7:17</a>): “For the Eternal, your God, is He Who goes with you, “that is the Name deposited in the Ark”. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bamidbar Rabbah 4:20" href="/Bamidbar_Rabbah.4.20">Midrash <i>Num. rabba</i> 4(20</a>) refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="I Chronicles 13:6" href="/I_Chronicles.13.6"><i>1Chr</i>. 13:6</a> which calls the ark Name: “God’s ark, the Eternal, Who resides above the Cherubim, which is called Name.”</i>, as it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">From here to the end of the Halakhah the text is in <i>Šeqalim</i> 7:1, (49c, line 26 ff.)</i>’<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sotah (Lieberman) 7:18" href="/Tosefta_Sotah_(Lieberman).7.18"><i>Tosephta</i> 7:18</a>; <i>Baraita Melekhet Hamishkan</i> 6.</i>: “Rebbi Jehudah ben Laqish says, two arks were travelling with Israel in the desert; one in which the Torah was deposited and one in which the broken pieces of the tablets were deposited<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">The ark mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 10:1" href="/Deuteronomy.10.1"><i>Deut</i>. 10:1</a>.</i>. The one in which the Torah was deposited was put into the Tent of Meeting; that is what is written<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 14:44" href="/Numbers.14.44"><i>Num</i>. 14:44</a>; the exact description of the ark implies that another ark went out with the people.</i>: “Moses and the Ark of the Eternal’s covenant did not move from the camp.” The one in which the broken pieces of the tablets were deposited was going out and coming in with them. But the Rabbis say, it was only one<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 8b" href="/Berakhot.8b">Babli <i>Berakhot</i> 8b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 14b" href="/Bava_Batra.14b"><i>Baba batra</i>14b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 99a" href="/Menachot.99a"><i>Menaḥot</i> 99a</a>.</i>, and once it went out in the days of Eli and was taken prisoner. A verse supports the Rabbis: <sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 4:8" href="/I_Samuel.4.8"><i>1Sam</i>. 4:8</a>.</i>“Woe to us, who will save us from this mighty god?” A word [which shows that] they never had seen it before. A verse supports Rebbi Jehudah ben Laqish. “Saul said to Aḥiya: present God’s Ark<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 14:18" href="/I_Samuel.14.18"><i>1Sam</i>. 14:18</a>.</i>”. But was the Ark not at Qiryat Ye‘arim<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 7:1-2" href="/I_Samuel.7.1-2"><i>1Sam</i>. 7:1–2</a>.</i>? What do the rabbis with it? ‘Present to me the High Priest’s diadem.’ Another verse supports Rebbi Jehudah ben Laqish: “The Ark, Israel, and Jehudah, dwell in huts.<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Samuel 11:11" href="/II_Samuel.11.11"><i>2Sam</i>. 11:11</a>.</i>”. Was the Ark not in Zion? What do the rabbis with it? The straw roof cover<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">The legal definition of a “hut” to be used on Tabernacles is any dwelling place whose roof is made from vegetable matter immune to impurity, called סְכָךְ. The construction of the walls is immaterial.</i> that was in the walls<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">In the Genizah fragment: כַקִּירוּי “acted as roof”. This is the better reading.</i>, since the Temple was not yet built.
<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 52b" href="/Yoma.52b">Babli <i>Yoma</i> 52b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Horayot 12a" href="/Horayot.12a"><i>Horaiot</i> 12a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Keritot 5b" href="/Keritot.5b"><i>Keritut</i> 5b</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Yoma (Lieberman) 2:15" href="/Tosefta_Yoma_(Lieberman).2.15">Tosephta <i>Kippurim</i> 2:15</a>, <i>Soṭah</i> 13:1; <i>Seder ‘Olam</i> 24; <i>Abot dR. Nathan A</i> 31. In the Babli and the sources depending on it, some Tannaïm hold that the Ark was brought to Babylon.</i> When the ark was hidden, there were hidden with it the flask of Manna<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 16:33-34" href="/Exodus.16.33-34"><i>Ex</i>. 16:33–34</a>.</i>, the bottle of anointing oil<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 30:22-33" href="/Exodus.30.22-33"><i>Ex</i>. 30:22–33</a>.</i>, Aaron’s staff with its flowers and almonds<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 17:25" href="/Numbers.17.25"><i>Num</i>. 17:25</a>.</i>, and the chest which the Philistines returned as a reparation sacrifice for Israel’s God<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 6" href="/I_Samuel.6"><i>1Sam</i>. 6</a>.</i>. Who hid it? Josiah! When he saw that it was written<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 28:36" href="/Deuteronomy.28.36"><i>Deut</i>. 28:36</a>.</i>: “The Eternal will lead you and your king whom you will have put above you, to a people whom neither you nor your fathers had known,” he started and hid it. That is what is written<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Chronicles 35:3" href="/II_Chronicles.35.3"><i>2Chr</i>. 35:3</a>. The Levites had not carried the Ark since the time of Samuel.</i>: “He said to the Levites, who instruct all of Israel, the ones holy to the Eternal, put the Ark into the House that Salomon, son of David, king of Israel, built; you do not have to carry it on your shoulder.” He said, if it is exiled with you to Babylonia, you will never return it to its place. But “<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Chronicles 35:3" href="/II_Chronicles.35.3"><i>2Chr</i>. 35:3</a>. The Levites had not carried the Ark since the time of Samuel.</i> now, serve the Eternal, your God, and his people Israel.”
<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Keritot 5a" href="/Keritot.5a">Babli <i>Keritut</i> 5a</a>.</i> The preparation of the anointing oil. “Take for yourself select spices: Flowing myrrh 500, etc., and casia 500 in Temple sheqel, altogether 1500 parts<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 30:23-24" href="/Exodus.30.23-24"><i>Ex</i>. 30:23–24</a>. The amounts given there are myrrh 500, cinnamon half the weight, 250, spice sticks 250, casia 500 in the holy šeqel weight, together 1500 (šeqel). The Babli notes that the verse could be translated: “myrrh 500, the half of the cinnamon weight 250, spice sticks 250; casia 500 in the holy šeqel weight,” for a total of either 2000 or 1750. The <i>baraita</i> shows that the traditional interpretation is as given first. A similar argument must be understood in the Yerushalmi.<br>It is impossible to translate מנה as “mina” since the holy šeqel by archeological evidence was between 13.77 and 14.28 g (Y. Meshorer, <i>Ancient Means of Exchange, Weights and Coins</i>, Haifa 1998), practically equal to the official weight of a tetradrachma. In the tradition of the Babli, the holy šeqel was twice the weight of the common šeqel; in Babylonian weight this would make it about 18 g. (In any case, spices in the weight of at least 21 kg are a large quantity for only 6.4 liters of oil.) But a mina is defined in the Talmudim as 100 tetradrachmas. This is out of line with the small amount of oil used. It may be that the word “mina”, which is missing in the parallel Babli text, is an intrusion from the <i>baraita</i> on the preparation of the holy incense of which 365 portions were prepared for an entire year at once and where all weights, missing in the biblical text, are indicated in minas [<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yoma 4:5:2-8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yoma.4.5.2-8"><i>Yoma</i> 4:5</a> (41d line 27), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Keritot 6a" href="/Keritot.6a">Babli <i>Keritut</i>6a</a>].</i>. “And olive oil one <i>hin</i>,” that is twelve <i>log</i><sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>log</i> is the Roman <i>sextarius, cf. Berakhot</i> 3:4, Note 164. 12 <i>log</i> are three quarters of a <i>modius</i> or about 6.4 liter.</i>, in which the roots were cooked, the words of Rebbi Meïr. Rebbi Jehudah says, they cooked in water, then poured the oil on them, and when it had absorbed the fragrance one removed it, just as the perfumers do. That is what is written: “Make it into holy anointing oil.<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 30:25" href="/Exodus.30.25"><i>Ex</i>. 30:25</a>.</i>” “That will be for Me for your generations.<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 30:31" href="/Exodus.30.31"><i>Ex</i>. 30:31</a>.</i>” It was stated, <sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Keritot 5" href="/Keritot.5">Babli <i>Keritut</i> 5a/b</a>.</i> Rebbi Jehudah bar Ilaï says: The anointing oil made by Moses in the desert was from the start to the end a work of wonders, since there were only twelve <i>log</i> to start with, as it was said, “and olive oil one <i>hin</i><sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 30:24" href="/Exodus.30.24"><i>Ex</i>. 30:24</a>.</i>”. It would not have been enough to rub the wooden planks with it; so much more since the fire swallows, wood absorbs, and the kettle absorbs! From it the Tabernacles and all its vessels were anointed, the table and its vessels, the candelabra and all its vessels. From it Aaron and his sons were anointed all of the seven days of induction, from it all high priests and kings were anointed. A king who is first needs anointing, a king who is a king’s son does not need anointing, for it is said<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 16:12" href="/I_Samuel.16.12"><i>1Sam</i>. 16:12</a>.</i>: “Do anoint him, for this one is it,” this one needs anointing but his son does not need anointing. But a High Priest who is the son of a High Priest needs anointing, even for ten generations<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:13" href="/Leviticus.6.13"><i>Lev</i>. 6:13</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:10" href="/Leviticus.21.10">21:10</a>. The same statement in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Keritot 5a" href="/Keritot.5a">Babli <i>Keritut</i> 5a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Horayot 11b" href="/Horayot.11b"><i>Horaiot</i> 11b</a>.</i>. Nevertheless, it is all there for the future, as it was said, “a holy anointing oil will this<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, an amoraic gloss in this <i>baraita</i> of R. Jehudah notes that the numerical value of ז̇ה̇ “this” is 12, implying that all 12 <i>log</i> remain for the Lord.</i> be for Me, for all your generations.<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 30:31" href="/Exodus.30.31"><i>Ex</i>. 30:31</a>.</i>”
<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">A parallel to the entire paragraph is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Keritot 5b" href="/Keritot.5b">Babli, <i>Keritut</i> 5b</a>.</i> One anoints kings only at a spring, as it was said<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Kings 1:33-34" href="/I_Kings.1.33-34"><i>1K</i>. 1:33–34</a>.</i>: “Let Solomon, my son, ride on my mule and take him down to the Giḥon; there Ṣadoq the priest and Nathan the prophet shall anoint him, etc.” One anoints kings only because of disputes. Why was Solomon anointed? Because of the dispute of Adoniahu; Joash because of Athaliah, Jehu because of Joram. Is it not written<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 16:12" href="/I_Samuel.16.12"><i>1Sam</i>. 16:12</a>.</i>: “Do anoint him, for this one is it,” this one needs anointing, but the kings of Israel do not need anointing<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">There is no indication that Yehu was anointed with the oil deposited in Jerusalem.</i>! But Joaḥaz because of his brother Joakim who was two years his elder<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="II Kings 23:31" href="/II_Kings.23.31"><i>2K</i>. 23:31</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="II Kings 23:36" href="/II_Kings.23.36">36</a> and <a class="refLink" data-ref="II Chronicles 36:2" href="/II_Chronicles.36.2"><i>2Chr</i>. 36:2</a>,5 it is noted that Joaḥaz was 23 years old in the same year in which Joakim was 25. Cf. <i>Seder ‘Olam</i>, Chapter 24 (in the author’s edition, pp. 213–215); <a class="refLink" data-ref="Arakhin 12a" href="/Arakhin.12a">Babli <i>Arakhin</i> 12a</a>.</i>. But did not Josiahu hide it<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">As indicated in the previous paragraph. Then Josiah’s son could not have been anointed with the oil made by Moses. The explanation given here is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Horayot 11b" href="/Horayot.11b">Babli, <i>Horaiot</i> 11b</a>, in the name of the later Amora Rav Papa.</i>? That means, they anointed with balsamum. One anoints kings only from a horn. Saul and Jehu were anointed from a can because their kingdom was temporary; David and Solomon were anointed from a horn because their kingdom was permanent<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">Quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 14a" href="/Megillah.14a">Babli, <i>Megillah</i> 14a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Keritot 6a" href="/Keritot.6a"><i>Keritut</i> 6a</a>.</i>. One does not anoint priests as kings<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">The Maccabean kings were anointed as High Priests, not as kings.</i>. Rebbi Yudan Antordiyya said, because of “the scepter shall not be removed from Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 49:10" href="/Genesis.49.10"><i>Gen</i>. 49:10</a>.</i>”. Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Ada said, because “he shall have many days of his kingdom, he and his sons in the midst of Israel<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 7:20" href="/Deuteronomy.7.20"><i>Deut</i>. 7:20</a>, last verse in the chapter.</i>.” What is written after that? “The levitic Cohanim should not.<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 8:1" href="/Deuteronomy.8.1"><i>Deut</i>. 8:1</a>.</i>”
<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">The entire paragraph is paralleled in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 3:2:2-5:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.3.2.2-5.7"><i>Horaiot</i> (3:3</a>; 47c, line 35 ff.), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Horayot 11b" href="/Horayot.11b">Babli <i>Horaiot</i> 11b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Keritot 5b" href="/Keritot.5b"><i>Keritut</i> 5b</a>.</i> Rebbi Joḥanan said, Joḥanan is Joaḥaz. But is it not written<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Chronicles 3:15" href="/I_Chronicles.3.15"><i>1Chr</i>. 3:15</a>. Joaḥaz does not appear in the list.</i>: “The first born Joḥanan”? The first in kingdom. Rebbi Joḥanan said, Shallum is Sedekia. But is not written<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Chronicles 3:15" href="/I_Chronicles.3.15"><i>1Chr</i>. 3:15</a>. Joaḥaz does not appear in the list.</i>: “The third Sedekiah, the fourth Shallum”? The third to be born, the fourth in kingdom<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">Since Sedekiah was 21 when he became king, 11 or 12 years after his brother Joakim, he was 15 years younger than Joakim.</i>. “Sedekiah” because he accepted the judgment on himself<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">From the root צדק “equity, justice”.</i>. “Shallum” because in his days the dynasty of David was completed<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">From the root שלם “complete; perfect; paid-up”.</i>. His name was neither Shallum nor Sedekiah but Mattaniah. That is what is written<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote"><i>1K</i> 24:17.</i>: “The king of Babylon made his uncle Mattaniah king in his stead and changed the latter’s name to Sedekiah.”
Rebbi Joḥanan said, the Ark was made with a cubit of six handbreadths. Who stated “a cubit of six handbreadths”? This is Rebbi Meïr. As we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Kelim 17:10" href="/Mishnah_Kelim.17.10">Mishnah <i>Kelim</i> 17:10</a>.</i>: “Rebbi Meïr says, all cubits were average<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">There was also a small cubit of 5 handbreadths and a large one of 7.</i>.” According to Rebbi Meïr who says that the Ark was made with a a cubit of six handbreadths, the length of the Ark was fifteen cubits, as it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 25:10" href="/Exodus.25.10"><i>Ex</i>. 25:10</a>.</i>: “Its length two cubits and a half.” Each cubit was six and half a cubit three<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">This Aramaic insert of elementary counting seems to be a gloss.</i>. Four tablets were in it, two broken ones and two whole ones<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 14b" href="/Bava_Batra.14b">Babli (<i>Baba batra</i> 14b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 99a" href="/Menachot.99a"><i>Menaḥot</i> 99a</a>) attributed to the fourth generation Amora Rav Joseph.</i>, as it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 10:2" href="/Deuteronomy.10.2"><i>Deut</i>. 10:2</a>. This explanation of the verse requires the removal of the masoretic dividing accent. and a revocalization וַשַּׂמְתָּם with <i>waw conversive</i>.</i>: “Which you broke and put into the Ark.” Each of the tablets was six handbreadths in length and three in width<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 14a" href="/Bava_Batra.14a">Babli (<i>Baba batra</i> 14a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 38a" href="/Nedarim.38a"><i>Nedarim</i> 38a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 99a" href="/Menachot.99a"><i>Menaḥot</i> 99a</a>), the tablets are said to have been six by six handbreadths with a height of three, lying one after the other in the Ark for a total length of 12 and width 6.</i>. If the widths of the tablets were in the length of the Ark, there were three handbreadths left<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">Four tablets lying side by side with their long sides touching one another fill an area of 12 by 6 handbreadths.</i>. Apply them to the cylinder<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">Probably the Torah scroll, cf. Note 95. The Torah scroll should have been “next to the Ark”, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 31:26" href="/Deuteronomy.31.26"><i>Deut</i>. 31:26</a>.</i>. The width of the Ark was nine handbreadths, as it is written: “A cubit and half a cubit.” A cubit six and half a cubit three. Four tablets were in it, two broken ones and two whole ones. Each of the tablets was six handbreadths in length and three in width. If the lengths of the tablets were in the length of the Ark<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Šeqalim</i>: תֵּן אָרְכָּן שֶׁלַּלּוּחוֹת לְאָרְכּוֹ שֶׁלָּאָרוֹן “If the lengths of the tablets were in the length of the Ark”. This text is the correct one since in the text here the second case is identical with the first.</i>, there were three handbreadths left<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">If the tablets were put into the Ark lengthwise in pairs, they fill a rectangle of length 12 and width 6.</i>. On each side half a handbreadth to have a handle, and two handbreadths as place to put there the Torah scroll<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">The first hand in <i>Šeqalim</i> here also has “for the cylinder” (اٌسطونة), proving the equivalence of “cylinder” and “Torah scroll”; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 8:3:11" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.8.3.11">Note 121</a>.</i>.
Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, the Ark was made using a cubit of five handbreadths. Who stated “a cubit of five handbreadths”? This is Rebbi Jehudah. As we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Kelim 17:10" href="/Mishnah_Kelim.17.10">Mishnah <i>Kelim</i> 17:10</a>.</i>: “Rebbi Jehudah says, the builders’ cubit was of six handbreadths, but that of vessels five handbeadths.” And the Ark is a vessel. According to Rebbi Jehudah who says that the Ark was made with a cubit of five handbreadths, the length of the Ark was twelve and one half cubits, as it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 25:10" href="/Exodus.25.10"><i>Ex</i>. 25:10</a>.</i>: “Its length two cubits and a half.” Each cubit was five and half a cubit two and one half<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">This Aramaic insert of elementary counting seems to be a gloss.</i>. Four tablets were in it, two whole ones and two broken ones<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 14b" href="/Bava_Batra.14b">Babli (<i>Baba batra</i> 14b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 99a" href="/Menachot.99a"><i>Menaḥot</i> 99a</a>) attributed to the fourth generation Amora Rav Joseph.</i>, as it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 10:2" href="/Deuteronomy.10.2"><i>Deut</i>. 10:2</a>. This explanation of the verse requires the removal of the masoretic dividing accent. and a revocalization וַשַּׂמְתָּם with <i>waw conversive</i>.</i>: “Which you broke and put into the Ark.” Each of the tablets was six handbreadths in length and three in width<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 14a" href="/Bava_Batra.14a">Babli (<i>Baba batra</i> 14a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 38a" href="/Nedarim.38a"><i>Nedarim</i> 38a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 99a" href="/Menachot.99a"><i>Menaḥot</i> 99a</a>), the tablets are said to have been six by six handbreadths with a height of three, lying one after the other in the Ark for a total length of 12 and width 6.</i>. If the widths of the tablets were in the length of the Ark, there was half a handbreadth left<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">12.5 - 4·3 = 0.5.</i>. One finger’s thickness for the wall on either side. The width of the Ark was seven and one half handbreadths, as it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 25:10" href="/Exodus.25.10"><i>Ex</i>. 25:10</a>.</i>: “A cubit and half a cubit.” A cubit five and half a cubit two and one half. Four tablets were in it, two whole ones and two broken ones, as it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 10:2" href="/Deuteronomy.10.2"><i>Deut</i>. 10:2</a>. This explanation of the verse requires the removal of the masoretic dividing accent. and a revocalization וַשַּׂמְתָּם with <i>waw conversive</i>.</i>: “Which you broke and put into the Ark.”. Each of the tablets was six handbreadths in length and three in width. If the lengths of the tablets were in the width of the Ark, there were one and a half handbreadths left<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">7.5 - 6 = <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 1:5:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.1.5.2-5">1.5</a>.</i>. One finger’s thickness for the wall on either side, and on each side half a handbreadth to have a handle<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">In this opinion, the Torah scroll by necessity was outside the Ark; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 8:3:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.8.3.8">Note 106</a>.</i>.
How did Beṣalel make the Ark? Rebbi Ḥanina said<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 72b" href="/Yoma.72b">Babli, <i>Yoma</i> 72b</a>, this is the opinion of Rav Jehudah, contemporary of R. Ḥanina. The other opinion is not mentioned there.</i>, he made three boxes, two of gold and one of wood. He put one of gold inside the one of wood, and the one of wood inside the other one of gold. That is what is written<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 25:11" href="/Exodus.25.11"><i>Ex</i>. 25:11</a>: “You shall cover it with pure gold, inside and out you shall cover it”.</i>: “You shall cover it inside and out with pure gold inside and out.” Why does the verse say, “you shall cover it”? To include the upper rim<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">Which was covered by the gold cover separate from the Ark (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 25:17-21" href="/Exodus.25.17-21"><i>Ex</i>. 25:17–21</a>.)</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, he made one box and gilded it. That is what is written: “You shall cover it inside and out with pure gold inside and out.” Why does the verse say, “you shall cover it”? Rebbi Phineas said, also between the planks.
How were the tablets written<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Ex. rabba</i> 47(10) (a somewhat suspect source), the opinions are attributed to R. Jehudah and R. Neḥemiah. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 18:1-20:23" href="/Exodus.18.1-20.23">Mekhilta dR. Ismael (<i>Yitro Masekhta dibeḥodeš</i> 8</a>), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shir HaShirim Rabbah 5:14" href="/Shir_HaShirim_Rabbah.5.14"><i>Cant. rabba</i> on 5:14</a>, <i>Tanḥuma Eqeb</i> 9, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 30:11-34:35" href="/Exodus.30.11-34.35"><i>Tanḥuma Buber Ki Tissa</i> 20</a>, <i>Pesiqta rabbati</i> 21(7), the first two opinions given here.</i>? Rebbi Ḥanania ben Gamliel says, five on one tablet each. But the rabbis say, ten on each tablet, as it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 4:13" href="/Deuteronomy.4.13"><i>Deut</i>. 4:13</a>. The verse ends: “He wrote them on two stone tablets.” The emphasis on <i>two</i> tablets is taken to mean that the Ten Commandments were written twice, once on each of the tablets.</i>: “He informed you of His covenant which He had commanded you to do, the ten words”, ten on each tablet. Rebbi Simeon ben Ioḥai said, twenty on each tablet, as it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">It is difficult to see what this quote means. A more appropriate quote would have been <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 32:15" href="/Exodus.32.15"><i>Ex</i>. 32:15</a>: “The tablets were written on both sides, on each side they were written.”</i>: “He informed you of His covenant which He had commanded you to do, the ten words”, twenty on each tablet. Rebbi Simai says, forty on each tablet, as it is written, “on each side they were written,” a square<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">Greek τετράγωνον “a square”. He seems to think that the tablets were cubes, an opinion not found elsewhere.</i> Ḥananiah, the son of Rebbi Joshua’s brother<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">His name was Ḥananiah ben Ḥananiah, which shows that he was a posthumous child. Names indicating this kind of bad luck are usually replaced by circumlocutions; cf. E. und H. Guggenheimer, <i>Etymologisches Lexikon der jüdischen Familiennamen</i>, München 1996, p. xviii; <i>Jewish Family Names and Their Origins</i>, Hoboken 1992, p. xviii.</i>, says: Between every two commandments, the details and the letters [of the Torah] were written. “Filled with <i>taršiš</i><sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Song of Songs 5:14" href="/Song_of_Songs.5.14"><i>Cant</i>. 5:14</a>. “His hands are golden cylinders, inlaid with <i>taršiš</i>,” the cylinders being Torah scrolls (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Shir HaShirim Rabbah 5:12" href="/Shir_HaShirim_Rabbah.5.12"><i>Cant. rabba</i> 5:12</a>), cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 8:3:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.8.3.8">Note 108</a>. The Palestinian Targum to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 28:20" href="/Exodus.28.20"><i>Ex</i>. 28:20</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 39:13" href="/Exodus.39.13">39:13</a> translates <i>taršiš</i> by כרוֹם יַמָּא רַבָּא “the color of the Great Sea.”. One may assume that the scribe of the Yerushalmi did not understand the Greek כרוֹם χρῶμα “color” and shortened it to כְּ. The Targum to <i>Cant</i>. translates תרשיש by the Syriac/Pahlevi word פֵּירוֹזַג (Farsi فيروزه) “turquoise”.</i>”, like the Great Sea. When Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish had occasion to discuss this verse, he said, Ḥananiah, the son of Rebbi Joshua’s brother, did teach us correctly. Just as in the sea there are small waves between a large wave and the next, so between any two commandments there are the details and the letters of the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shir HaShirim Rabbah 5:12" href="/Shir_HaShirim_Rabbah.5.12"><i>Cant. rabba</i> 5(12</a>), this is a commentary of R. Joḥanan.</i>.
Rebbi Tanḥuma said, I asked before Rebbi Phineas: It is acceptable following Rebbi Jehudah, it is not acceptable following Rebbi Meïr<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">The verses seem to imply that the Torah scroll was not in the ark.</i>. What is the reason of Rebbi Jehudah? “To take this Torah scroll<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 31:26" href="/Deuteronomy.31.26"><i>Deut</i>. 31:26</a>, “and put it <i>beside</i> the Ark”, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 8:3:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.8.3.8">Note 106</a>.</i>”. In the opinion of Rebbi Jehudah, who said, where the Torah scroll was put? They made for it a kind of case<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">Read גלוסקום for גלוסקוס; Greek γλωσσόκομον “case, covering, chest”.</i> outside and the Torah scroll was put into it. What is the reason of Rebbi Meïr? “You should put the cover on top of the Ark.<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 25:21" href="/Exodus.25.21"><i>Ex</i>. 25:21</a>.</i>” In the opinion of Rebbi Meïr who says that there is no earlier and later in the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">The order of verses in the Torah does not imply either logical or temporal dependency. Accepted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 6b" href="/Pesachim.6b">Babli, <i>Pesaḥim</i> 6b</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bamidbar Rabbah 9:44" href="/Bamidbar_Rabbah.9.44"><i>Num. rabba</i> 9(44</a>).</i>, “in the Ark you shall put the testimony that I shall give to you,<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 25:16" href="/Exodus.25.16"><i>Ex</i>. 25:16</a>, five verses earlier.</i>” and after that, “you should put the cover on top of the Ark.” Rebbi Phineas in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <i>Tanḥuma Berešit</i> 1, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shir HaShirim Rabbah 5:12" href="/Shir_HaShirim_Rabbah.5.12"><i>Cant. rabba</i> 5(12</a>).</i>: The Torah which the Holy One, Praise to Him, gave, its leather was white fire, its inscription was black fire, it was fire mixed with fire; hewn from fire, given from fire: “From His right hand, the fiery law to them.<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 33:2" href="/Deuteronomy.33.2"><i>Deut</i>. 33:2</a>.</i>”

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> “The policemen shall speak to the people, saying: Who is the man who built a new house and did not inaugurate it; he should go and return to his house,<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 20:5" href="/Deuteronomy.20.5"><i>Deut</i>. 20:5</a>. “Lest he should die in the war and another would inaugurate it.” He is recruited for auxiliary services (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 8:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.8.7.1">Mishnah 7</a>). The policemen speak after the Cohen whose words were treated in the preceding Mishnaiot. It is not necessary that the original builder inaugurate his house; anybody in legal possession of a not inaugurated house is included.</i>” etc. Not only he who builds a house, but also one who builds a barn, a cow-shed, a wood-shed, a storage facility. Not only he who builds, but also one who buys, or inherits, or who received as a gift.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">Partial parallels are in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 43a" href="/Sotah.43a">Babli, 43a</a>; <i>Sifry Deut</i>. 194; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sotah (Lieberman) 7:17" href="/Tosefta_Sotah_(Lieberman).7.17">Tosephta 7:17</a>.</i>“Who built a house”, not only who built; if he bought, inherited, or it was given to him as a gift, from where? The verse says, “the man,” “who is the man”<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">In the interpretation of Rashi, this means that the verse would have been intelligible if the word האיש were missing: מִ אֲשֶׁה בָּנָה “Who built …”. Therefore, the additional word must imply an extension of the circle of those who have to go and start using their new property.</i>. From where he who builds a barn, a cow-shed, a wood-shed, a storage facility, from where? The verse says, “who builds”. I could think that one who builds a portico<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">A formal structure at the entrance to a courtyard common to several houses. It has no walls and confers no privacy.</i>, a covered walkway<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ἐξέδρα, cf. <i>Ma‘serot</i> 3:6, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 8:3:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.8.3.9">Note 101</a>.</i>, and a verandah would return; the verse says “house”; the house is distinguished by the fact that it can be used as a dwelling<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">Even if it is used as agricultural facility, a barn etc. could be used as a dwelling, having walls on all sides.</i>. This excludes those items which cannot be dwellings. It also excludes a house which does not enclose four by four cubits. As it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ma‘serot</i> 3:7, Notes 114–122. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 3" href="/Sukkah.3">Babli <i>Sukkah</i> 3a/b</a>.</i>: A house which does not enclose four by four cubits is free from the obligation of a parapet<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:8" href="/Deuteronomy.22.8"><i>Deut</i>. 22:8</a>.</i>, or a <i>mezuzah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 6:9" href="/Deuteronomy.6.9"><i>Deut</i>. 6:9</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 11:20" href="/Deuteronomy.11.20">11:20</a>.</i>, or an <i>erub</i><sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">If a courtyard belongs to a single owner except that a hut enclosing an area less that four cubits square belongs to another person, that courtyard may be used on the Sabbath by the majority owner without an <i>eruv</i> (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 1:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.1.4.1"><i>Demay</i> 1, Notes 192–193</a>).</i>. It also does not induce <i>ṭevel</i> for tithes<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">Freshly harvested produce may be eaten untithed. Once the harvest has been removed to a storage area, it is forbidden as food until heave and tithes were given; cf. <i>Ma‘serot</i> 3:5–8. A small shed does not qualify as a storage area.</i> and one does not give it four cubits in front of its door<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">In a courtyard belonging to several owners, the four cubits in front of the entrance to each house are the private domain of this house, to be used to load and unload, and is out of bounds for the other owners. This does not apply to a small hut.</i>, and it is not counted as a connection to the town<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">On the Sabbath, one may not go outside one’s town more than 2000 cubits (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 8:4:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.8.4.4"><i>Peah</i> 8, Note 56</a>). Any house which is within 70 cubits of a house or the city wall of the town is also counted as part of the town; the count of 2000 cubits starts only at the outermost house. A small building does not count as a house.</i>, and one who forswears any use of a house is permitted to sit in it<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">In popular language, a small shelter is not called a house. Therefore, a person vowing not to sit in a house may sit in a small shelter.</i>, and it does not remain with the buyer in the Jubilee year<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">It is agricultural property, not a city dwelling; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:30" href="/Leviticus.25.30"><i>Lev</i>. 25:30</a>.</i>, and it cannot become impure by disease<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:34" href="/Leviticus.14.34"><i>Lev</i>. 14:34</a> ff. The rules of diseased structures are explicitly restricted to “a <i>house</i> of the Land of your inheritance”.</i>, and its owner does not become exempt from the war because of it.
Why does the verse say, “who built”<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 20:5" href="/Deuteronomy.20.5"><i>Deut</i>. 20:5</a>. The quote is only to indicate the verse, not the reason for that deduction. The emphasis is on “who built a <i>new</i> house”. Cf. also <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 8:7:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.8.7.2-3">Halakhah 7</a>.</i>? This excludes him whose house collapsed and he rebuilt it. (Rebbi Yose said, this implies that one who remarries his divorcee does not return.)<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">This sentence is paralleled by a tannaïtic statement in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.6">Halakhah 6, Note 186</a>. The person who had married a woman in preliminary marriage (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 4:1:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.4.1.6"><i>Demay</i> 4:2, Note 19</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 6:2:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.6.2.6"><i>Peah</i> 6:2, Note 46</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 1:1:15" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.1.1.15"><i>Yebamot</i> 1:1, Note 63</a>) but had not lived with her returns from the war if she is a <i>new</i> wife; i. e., not one previously married to him.</i> One could think that somebody who builds a house outside the Land would return; the verse said, “and he did not inaugurate it,” meaning where there is an obligation of inauguration; this excludes cases where there is no obligation of inauguration<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">It is inferred from here that a formal inauguration of a newly built house is a religious commandment in the Land.</i>. If he received prepaid rent, it is as if he inaugurated it<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">If the house was built not for the family of the builder but for rental purposes, receipt of the first rental payment is the inauguration, but only if the payment is made not later than the end of the usual rental period of 12 months.</i>; if [the rent is payable] only after twelve months, it is as if he had not inaugurated it. If he locked it, if it contains valuables for which one usually stops working<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">If the value of the things stored is so high that either the owner has to be present to guard against thieves or he has to pay a watchman; that is use equivalent to living in the building.</i>, it is as if he inaugurated it; otherwise, it is as if he had not inaugurated it.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> “And who is the man who planted a vineyard and did not redeem it,<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 20:6" href="/Deuteronomy.20.6"><i>Deut</i>. 20:6</a>. The fruit of a newly planted vineyard or orchard cannot be used unless it was redeemed in his fourth year, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:23-24" href="/Leviticus.19.23-24"><i>Lev</i>. 19:23–24</a>.</i>” etc. Not only one who planted a vineyard but also one who planted five fruit trees<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">An orchard, whose rules of redemption are identical to those of a vineyard; cf. <i>Ma‘aser Šeni</i> 5:4.</i>, even from five different kinds. Not only one who planted but also one who sank<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote">He produces a new tree by covering a branch of an existing tree with earth; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 7:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.7.1.1"><i>Kilaim</i> 7:1, Note 1</a>.</i> or grafted, also one who buys, or inherits, or who received as a gift.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Who planted”. Not only who planted; if he bought, inherited, or it was given to him as a gift, from where? The verse says, “the man,” “who is the man”<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">The same argument as in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 8:4:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.8.4.2">Note 133</a>, applied to v. 6, not v. 5. Parallels in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 43b" href="/Sotah.43b">Babli 43b</a>; <i>Sifry Deut</i>. 195.</i>. From where about him who planted a vineyard but also who planted five fruit trees<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">An orchard, whose rules of redemption are identical to those of a vineyard; cf. <i>Ma‘aser Šeni</i> 5:4.</i>, even from five different kinds? The verse says, “who planted”, in any circumstance. I could think that one who planted four fruit trees or five futile trees<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">The definition of a futile tree is in dispute (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 6:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.6.3.1">Mishnah <i>Kilaim</i> 6:6</a>); the majority opinion holds that a tree is futile if and only if its fruits are not edible.</i> may return? The verse says, “a vineyard.” Since a vineyard is special in that it is composed of five vines<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">The minimal size of a legal vineyard, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 4:1:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.4.1.7"><i>Kilaim</i> 4:3, Note 38</a>. The vineyard must be planted in orderly rows; in an orchard, the trees must grow at least four and at most √250 (= 15.8) cubits apart; cf. <i>Ševi‘it</i> 1:2, Notes 18,22.</i>, this excludes the case where there are not five vines<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">Or fruit trees.</i>.
If the wine<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote">In the Rome ms. שְׁתָיוֹ “if he drank it as …” Since this paragraph is not connected with the preceding or the following ones, it is difficult to decide between the readings.</i> is freshly pressed, if the wine<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote">In the Rome ms. שְׁתָיוֹ “if he drank it as …” Since this paragraph is not connected with the preceding or the following ones, it is difficult to decide between the readings.</i> is sour<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote">This is wine for which it is a matter of opinion whether it is sour wine or sweet vinegar. It still must contain some alcohol. The first hand of the Leiden ms. wrote יַיִן תּוֹסֵס “wine fermenting”. This may be an intrusion from a similar Babylonian <i>baraita, Sanhedrin</i> 70a.</i>? As it was stated: Sour wine may be used to make an <i>eruv</i><sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote">If several houses share a common courtyard, the latter can be turned into common property to be available to all dwellers for use on the Sabbath by everyone donating food for a common meal, This is called <i>eruv</i>, “mixing”. If the inhabitants of a dead-end stret decide to turn their street into common property for Sabbath purposes, they have to install a symbolic door at the entrance to the street and prepare common food by “participating”. Cf. <i>Demay</i> 1:4, Notes 192, 193.</i> and participating, one makes a benediction for it<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote">Before one drinks sour wine, one has to recite the benediction for wine, not vinegar (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 6:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.6.1.1">Mishnaiot <i>Berakhot</i> 6:1</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 6:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.6.3.1">3</a>).</i>, and one may use it for the preliminary marriage of a bride<sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 4:1:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.4.1.6"><i>Demay</i> 4:2, Note 19</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 6:2:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.6.2.6"><i>Peah</i> 6:2, Note 46</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 1:1:15" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.1.1.15"><i>Yebamot</i> 1:1, Note 63</a>.</i>, for consoling the mourner<sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote">The first meal of a mourner after the burial of a close relative must be given to him by others and it includes wine; cf. <i>Berakhot</i> 3:1, Notes 48,49,54; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 8b" href="/Ketubot.8b">Babli <i>Ketubot</i> 8b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 90a" href="/Sanhedrin.90a"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 90a</a>.</i>, and it may be sold in a store as wine. If somebody made an unqualified contract for wine, he did not sell sour wine<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote">On the wholesale level, sour wine has to be labelled as such. However, if an entire inventory was sold, the buyer must expect 10% of the barrels to contain sour wine (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 6:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.6.1.1">Mishnah <i>Baba batra</i> 6:1</a>).</i>. One is prohibited from delivering a decision, or to dissolve a vow, or to enter the Temple<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 10:8-11" href="/Leviticus.10.8-11"><i>Lev</i>. 10:8–11</a> forbids entering the Temple, officiating, and delivering any religious instruction to anybody who is still under the influence of any alcoholic beverage. Since sour wine contains alcohol, it disables as wine.</i>. Only it is forbidden on the altar<sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Malachi 1:8" href="/Malachi.1.8"><i>Mal</i>. 1:8</a> declares sinful any offering on the altar which would not be received by the Pasha as a bribe.</i>. And about the rebellious son it is problematic<sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote">The rebellious son (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 21:18-21" href="/Deuteronomy.21.18-21"><i>Deut</i>. 21:18–21</a>) is not guilty unless he eats and drinks immoderately, including half a <i>log</i> of wine (2.7 dl) in one sip. It is undecided whether drinking almost-vinegar is drinking wine in this case. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 96a" href="/Bava_Batra.96a">Babli (<i>Baba batra</i> 96a</a>) discusses whether smell or taste decide between wine and vinegar. It is obvious (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 70a" href="/Sanhedrin.70a"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 70a</a>) that grape juice does not qualify as wine in this respect.</i>.
I could think that somebody who redeems a vineyard outside the Land would return, the verse says<sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 20:6" href="/Deuteronomy.20.6"><i>Deut</i>. 20:6</a>.</i>: “And he did not redeem it”. In case one is commanded to redeem it<sup class="footnote-marker">171</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:23" href="/Leviticus.19.23"><i>Lev</i>. 19:23</a>, the duty of redemption is clearly restricted to the Promised Land.</i>; this excludes where there is no commandment to redeem. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote">Also quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 43b" href="/Sotah.43b">Babli, 43b</a>. R. Eliezer ben Jacob rejects the extension of the exemption from combat duty given in the Mishnah.</i>: Rebbi Eliezer ben Jacob says, one only understands “vineyard”. And similarly, one only understands “planted”. It was stated: “And he did not redeem it<sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 20:6" href="/Deuteronomy.20.6"><i>Deut</i>. 20:6</a>.</i>”, that excludes him who sinks or grafts. Rebbi Joḥanan said, this follows Rebbi Eliezer ben Jacob. Rav Ḥisda said, this is everybody’s opinion if he grafted fruits of sin<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote">Since reciting a benediction over any forbidden fruit is blasphemy (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Challah 1:5:5-8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Challah.1.5.5-8"><i>Ḥallah</i> 1:9</a>, 58a line 53; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 94a" href="/Bava_Kamma.94a">Babli <i>Baba qama</i> 94a</a>), it is obvious that since an orchard of <i>kilaim</i> cannot be redeemed, the person who planted it cannot be exempted.</i>. Where do we hold?<sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote">What kind of grafting entitles a man to leave the war zone following the Mishnah but not R. Eliezer ben Jacob?</i> If he grafted a fruit tree on another fruit tree of a different kind; these are fruits of sin<sup class="footnote-marker">175</sup><i class="footnote">These are unquestionably forbidden <i>kilaim</i>.</i>. But if he grafted a fruit tree on a futile tree, as if it were its own kind, he is planting anew<sup class="footnote-marker">176</sup><i class="footnote">The stump of the futile tree is simply considered as earth; according to everybody grafting on such a stem is the same as planting in the earth.</i>. But we hold if he grafted a black fig tree on a white one<sup class="footnote-marker">177</sup><i class="footnote">This is permitted; the owner returns following the Mishnah but not R. Eliezer ben Jacob. A “black” fig is a purple one; a “white” fig is green.</i>.
When does he redeem it<sup class="footnote-marker">178</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:24" href="/Leviticus.19.24"><i>Lev</i>. 19, v. 24</a> states that in the fourth year after planting, “all fruit shall be holy” as quoted in this paragraph. This is considered a burden rather than a joy. V. 25 permits the fruits of the fifth and later years without any restriction.</i>? In the fourth or the fifth [year]? It seems reasonable in the fifth, since in the fourth it costs him money. But the rabbis of Caesarea say, it is only reasonable in the fourth, since it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">179</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:24" href="/Leviticus.19.24"><i>Lev</i>. 19:24</a>. Because of the disappearence of gutturals, the word הילולים is interpreted as חילולים, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 7:5:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.7.5.4"><i>Peah</i> 7:6, Notes 112–113</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 35a" href="/Berakhot.35a">Babli <i>Berakhot</i> 35a</a>.</i>: “And in the fourth year, all its fruit shall be holy for praise to the Eternal.”

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> “<sup class="footnote-marker">180</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 20:7" href="/Deuteronomy.20.7"><i>Deut</i>. 20:7</a>.</i> Who is the man who became betrothed<sup class="footnote-marker">181</sup><i class="footnote">He married her in the preliminary ceremony (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 8:4:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.8.4.2">Note 146</a>) but did not take her into his house.</i> to a woman and did not take her,” etc. Not only one betrothed to a virgin, even one betrothed to a widow, even to one who waits for her brother-in-law<sup class="footnote-marker">182</sup><i class="footnote">The widow of his childless brother. The surviving brother can marry her based on the dead brother’s preliminary marriage; but rabbinically one requires a new preliminary “bespeaking”, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 2:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.2.1.1"><i>Yebamot</i> 2:1, Note 6</a>. The next sentence shows that the Mishnah does not refer to this rabbinic ceremony.</i>. Even if he heard that his brother had died in the war he then returns and leaves. All those who listen to the Cohen in the front lines return and provide food and water and improve the roads<sup class="footnote-marker">183</sup><i class="footnote">They serve the army in the rear.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Who became betrothed”. Not only a young man who married a young woman. From where even a young man who married a widow, a widower who married a young woman, a widower who married a widow? From where? The verse says, “a woman”, of any kind. Why does the verse say, “new”? That excludes him who remarries his divorcee<sup class="footnote-marker">186</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 44a" href="/Sotah.44a">Babli, 44a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, this implies that one who marries a sheram<sup class="footnote-marker">187</sup><i class="footnote">A woman without secondary female sex characteristics who is considered sterile; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 1:1:25" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.1.1.25"><i>Yebamot</i> 1:1, Note 65</a>. Her marriage has rabbinic status only.</i> does not return since he has no religious obligation to live with her.
Even one who waits for her brother-in-law with five brothers<sup class="footnote-marker">188</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the statement that a man who hears that his childless brother has died, returns from the army to marry his sister-in-law. This implies that <i>every</i> brother returns in this case. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 43b" href="/Sotah.43b">Babli agrees, 43b</a>.</i>. Similarly, even one house for five brothers<sup class="footnote-marker">189</sup><i class="footnote">Why does Mishnah 4 not make a proviso that every man who built a house together with his brothers does return?</i>? There not one of them would be able to live in it<sup class="footnote-marker">190</sup><i class="footnote">In the language of the Mishnah, בית always means a one-room house, in which only a single family can live.</i>. But here, each one of them is able to marry in levirate<sup class="footnote-marker">191</sup><i class="footnote">While only one man can marry the widow, each one could be the one taking her away from the others.</i>. What would be like it? Somebody who preliminarily married a woman from now on in twelve months<sup class="footnote-marker">192</sup><i class="footnote">The prospective groom delivers an object of value to his bride and stipulates that what she receives and acknowledges at that time shall be valid for preliminary marriage in 12 months’ time. This is a valid contract and after 12 months he is preliminarily married and obligated to marry in final form. (If she sleeps with another man during the 12 months it would be criminal if and only if the preliminary husband is alive at the end of the 12 months’ period and the contract is activated.)</i> and the time is up while he is in the war; he returns and goes.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The following do not return: If somebody builds a portico<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">A formal structure at the entrance to a courtyard common to several houses. It has no walls and confers no privacy.</i>, a covered walkway<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ἐξέδρα, cf. <i>Ma‘serot</i> 3:6, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 8:3:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.8.3.9">Note 101</a>.</i>, or a verandah, or planted four fruit trees or five futile<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">The definition of a futile tree is in dispute (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 6:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.6.3.1">Mishnah <i>Kilaim</i> 6:6</a>); the majority opinion holds that a tree is futile if and only if its fruits are not edible.</i> trees, or remarried his divorcee. [In the case of] a widow for the High Priest, a divorcee or one having received <i>ḥalîṣah</i> for an ordinary Cohen, a bastard or Gibeonite girl for an Israel, a Jewish girl for a bastard or a Gibeonite<sup class="footnote-marker">193</sup><i class="footnote">These unions are all forbidden, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Yevamot 2:4" href="/Mishnah_Yevamot.2.4"><i>Yebamot</i>, Mishnah 2:4, Notes 68–73</a>.</i>, he would not be returning. Rebbi Jehudah says, also he who rebuilt his house on its foundations<sup class="footnote-marker">194</sup><i class="footnote">Rebuilding a collapsed house on the remaining foundations is not the same as building a new house.</i> would not be returning. Rebbi Eleazar said, also one who built a brick house<sup class="footnote-marker">195</sup><i class="footnote">An adobe house, built from unfired clay bricks, which needs thorough repair before each rainy season.</i> in the Sharon would not return.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It was stated: Rebbi Jehudah said, if he changed anything, he would be returning. If he whitewashed it or added windows<sup class="footnote-marker">197</sup><i class="footnote">While the original room was not whitewashed or had fewer windows.</i>, he would not return, if it had been great and he made it small he returns, or one room and he made it two.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">198</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sotah (Lieberman) 7:19" href="/Tosefta_Sotah_(Lieberman).7.19">Tosephta 7:19</a>.</i>: Rebbi Eleazar<sup class="footnote-marker">199</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli in all cases: R. Eliezer. This reading also in the Tosephta, except the Erfurt ms. The reading of the Yerushalmi is superior since R. Eliezer would have to precede R. Jehudah, his student’s son.</i> said, also the people from the Sharon did not return since they have to rebuild two times in a Sabbatical period. Also the High Priest was praying for them<sup class="footnote-marker">200</sup><i class="footnote">In the Holiest of Holies. This prayer is mentioned in all liturgical compositions for the <i>musaf</i> prayer of the Day of Atonement.</i> on the Day of Atonement that their houses should not become their graves.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> But the following do not leave at all<sup class="footnote-marker">201</sup><i class="footnote">They do not join the army even to the border, even though the verse mentions only the newly-wed.</i>: If he built a house and inaugurated it, planted a vineyard and redeemed it, he who married his preliminarily married spouse, he who took in his sister-in-law, for it is said<sup class="footnote-marker">202</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:5" href="/Deuteronomy.24.5"><i>Deut</i>. 24:5</a>: If a man take a new wife, he shall not go out with the army, nor should anything be imposed on him; free for his house he shall be for one year and give pleasure to his wife whom he took.</i>: “Free he shall be for his house for one year”. “His house”, that is his house; “shall be”, that is his vineyard; “and give pleasure to his wife”, that is his wife; “whom he took”, to include his sister-in-law<sup class="footnote-marker">203</sup><i class="footnote">The same text in <i>Sifry Deut</i>. 271.</i>. These do not provide food and water and do not improve the roads.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If he built a house and inaugurated it,” but did not use it for twelve months. “If he planted a vineyard and redeemed it,” but did not use it for twelve months. And all are derived from the wife: Since the wife needs twelve months, also all of them need twelve months. It was stated: All those mentioned remain unoccupied in town, [do not pay city taxes], and do not provide food and water nor improve the roads, as it is said<sup class="footnote-marker">202</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:5" href="/Deuteronomy.24.5"><i>Deut</i>. 24:5</a>: If a man take a new wife, he shall not go out with the army, nor should anything be imposed on him; free for his house he shall be for one year and give pleasure to his wife whom he took.</i>: “nor should anything be imposed on him,” but it may be imposed on others<sup class="footnote-marker">205</sup><i class="footnote">In the opinon of the Babli, 44a, and Sifry Deut. 271, people preliminarily but not finally married, etc. Following the Genizah fragment, this would include people gainfully employed in the first year of their marriage, etc.</i>.

Halakhah 9

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> “The policemen shall add and say to the people: ‘Who is the man fearful and faint-hearted, let him go and return to his house. Rebbi Aqiba says, “fearful and faint-hearted” in its simple meaning, that he cannot stand in military engagements and see a drawn sword. Rebbi Yose the Galilean says, “fearful and faint-hearted” is one who is fearful because of his sins; therefore the Torah appended him to all of these<sup class="footnote-marker">207</sup><i class="footnote">The person betrothed or who had built, etc.</i> that he should return because of them<sup class="footnote-marker">208</sup><i class="footnote">That only his officer but not the public would know that he returns as a coward but not as one of the legitimate cases.<br>In <i>Sifry Deut</i>. 197, R. Yose is quoted to exclude anyone over 40 years old. But in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sotah (Lieberman) 7:22" href="/Tosefta_Sotah_(Lieberman).7.22">Tosephta 7:22</a>, and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 20:8" href="/Deuteronomy.20.8"><i>Midrash Tannaïm Deut</i>. 20:8</a>, the reading is similar to the Mishnah.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, a widow for the High Priest, a divorcee or one having received <i>ḥalîṣah</i> for an ordinary Cohen, a bastard or Gibeonite girl for an Israel, a Jewish girl for a bastard or a Gibeonite<sup class="footnote-marker">193</sup><i class="footnote">These unions are all forbidden, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Yevamot 2:4" href="/Mishnah_Yevamot.2.4"><i>Yebamot</i>, Mishnah 2:4, Notes 68–73</a>.</i>, that is the “fearful and faint-hearted”<sup class="footnote-marker">209</sup><i class="footnote">The Babli considers the cases quoted by R. Yose the Tanna as examples of biblical prohibitions and, therefore, holds that R. Yose the Galilean also recognizes the right of return for one who has overstepped rabbinic decrees. There is no hint in the Yerushalmi that R. Yose includes anything except forbidden but valid marriages.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It is written: “The policemen shall add.” This expression “shall add” only means an addition<sup class="footnote-marker">210</sup><i class="footnote">The last declarations are not said on the authority of the Cohen.</i>. Like a man who says, I am adding to the words of my teacher. It was stated: The latter [words] are additions. Rebbi Yose said, there are two Tannaïm<sup class="footnote-marker">211</sup><i class="footnote">The statement explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 8:9:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.8.9.2">Note 210</a> also a <i>baraita</i>, different from the one quoted last.</i>. He who said, “I am adding to the words of my teacher,” both the first<sup class="footnote-marker">212</sup><i class="footnote">The first declarations are those attributed to the Cohen, the later ones those put in the mouth of the policemen.</i> and the later declarations both he and his teacher said<sup class="footnote-marker">213</sup><i class="footnote">The addition is the policemen’s explanation in the vernacular.</i>. He who said, the later ones are additions, the first [declarations] both he and his teacher said, but the later ones he said but not his teacher. Rebbi Mana said, there is only one Tanna. The first [declarations] both he and his teacher said, but the later ones he said but not his teacher. A <i>baraita</i> disagrees with Rebbi Yose: He hears a sentence from the Cohen and tells it to the people in all languages<sup class="footnote-marker">214</sup><i class="footnote">This follows only the first Tanna recognized by R. Yose (the Amora, colleague of R. Mana II).</i>. But its end disagrees with Rebbi Mana: He adds a final word of his own<sup class="footnote-marker">215</sup><i class="footnote">But any text reported in the verses was first spoken by the Cohen.</i>. If you say so, it does not disagree at all with Rebbi Yose, as it was stated: The first [declarations] both he and his teacher said, but the later ones he said but not his teacher<sup class="footnote-marker">216</sup><i class="footnote">One is free to interpret “the later ones” either as the declaration of the policemen spelled out in the verse or as the additions after the official text.</i>.
Also, it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">218</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sifry Deut</i>. 192.</i>: They all have to bring proof except from this one whose witnesses are with him<sup class="footnote-marker">219</sup><i class="footnote">The military instructors realize that they are better off without the coward. But all others have to prove their case.</i>. This follows him who said that he cannot stand in military engagements and see a drawn sword. But following him who says that he is one who is fearful because of his sins, he has to bring proof. Therefore the Torah appended him to all of these<sup class="footnote-marker">207</sup><i class="footnote">The person betrothed or who had built, etc.</i> that he should return because of them<sup class="footnote-marker">208</sup><i class="footnote">That only his officer but not the public would know that he returns as a coward but not as one of the legitimate cases.<br>In <i>Sifry Deut</i>. 197, R. Yose is quoted to exclude anyone over 40 years old. But in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sotah (Lieberman) 7:22" href="/Tosefta_Sotah_(Lieberman).7.22">Tosephta 7:22</a>, and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 20:8" href="/Deuteronomy.20.8"><i>Midrash Tannaïm Deut</i>. 20:8</a>, the reading is similar to the Mishnah.</i>, not to publicize the sinners. This comes as Rebbi Levi said in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish<sup class="footnote-marker">220</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 32b" href="/Sotah.32b">Babli, 32b</a>, a similar statement by R. Joḥanan in the name of R. Simeon ben Ioḥai. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 8:3:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.8.3.9"><i>Yebamot</i> 8:3, Note 198</a>.</i>: <sup class="footnote-marker">221</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:18" href="/Leviticus.6.18"><i>Lev</i>. 6:18</a>.</i>“At the place where the elevation offering is being slaughtered, the purification offering is being slaughtered before the Eternal,” not to publicize the sinners<sup class="footnote-marker">222</sup><i class="footnote">Even though elevation offerings are male and purification offerings female, the difference can be seen only by close inspection, so also the sinner could be detected only by inquiry.</i>.

Halakhah 10

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> “When the policemen finish to speak to the people, the officers will have taken up position at the head of the people.<sup class="footnote-marker">223</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 20:9" href="/Deuteronomy.20.9"><i>Deut</i>. 20:9</a>.</i>” Behind the people one puts up people with backups armed with iron axes and they have permission to break the legs of anyone who desires to abandon his post since flight is the start of falling, as it is said<sup class="footnote-marker">224</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 4:17" href="/I_Samuel.4.17"><i>1S</i>. 4:17</a>.</i>: “Israel fled from before the Philistines and a great plague was among the people.” Further on it says<sup class="footnote-marker">225</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 31:1" href="/I_Samuel.31.1"><i>1S</i>. 31:1</a>.</i>, “the men of Israel fled from before the Philistines and slain fell on Mount Gilboa.” About what have these things be said<sup class="footnote-marker">226</sup><i class="footnote">The preceding Mishnaiot which gave permission to a number of people to be excused from military service.</i>? About a war of choice, but in a war of obligation<sup class="footnote-marker">227</sup><i class="footnote">The definitions are a matter of discussion in the Halakhah.</i> everybody goes out, even a groom from his room and a bride from her bridal chamber<sup class="footnote-marker">228</sup><i class="footnote">One might assume that the groom’s room is the bride’s bridal chamber. The expression is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Joel 2:16" href="/Joel.2.16"><i>Joel</i>2:16</a>.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah said, about what have these things been said? About a war of obligation, but in a war of duty everybody goes out, even a groom from his room and a bride from her bridal chamber.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It is written: “When the policemen finish to speak to the people, the officers will have taken up position at the head of the people.” Not only in front of the people, from where also behind the people? The verse says, “take up position”, “<i>and</i> take up position”<sup class="footnote-marker">229</sup><i class="footnote">R. Aqiba considers every <i>vaw</i> at the beginning of a word as indication of an addition, even in a case of a <i>vaw</i> <i>conversive</i> when an imperative would need the same number of letters.</i>. That follows Rebbi Aqiba. Following Rebbi Ismael? Rebbi Ismael follows Rebbi Meïr, since Rebbi Meïr used to call the end of a rope its head.
Rebbi Meïr said: It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">230</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Ecclesiastes 2:14" href="/Ecclesiastes.2.14"><i>Eccl</i>. 2:14</a>.</i>, “the wise man’s eyes are in his head.” The stupid one’s are where? In his legs<sup class="footnote-marker">231</sup><i class="footnote">This parallels the German saying that one must have in one’s legs what he does not have in his head.</i>. Rebbi Abba Mari said, the wise man knows what will be the final outcome when he starts something. “Gad, a troop will gang up on him<sup class="footnote-marker">232</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 49:19" href="/Genesis.49.19"><i>Gen</i>. 49:19</a>. The reference naturally is to the entire verse, “Gad, a troop will gang up on him but he will catch their heel.”</i>.” A troop comes to rob him, and he robs them.
So is the Mishnah: “Since flight is the start of falling<sup class="footnote-marker">233</sup><i class="footnote">This is the text of the Mishnah in the Leiden ms., but not in the Rome ms. nor in the Babli or the overwhelming majority of Mishnah mss. The original text reads שֶׁתְּחִילַּת נִיסָה נְפִילָה “since falling is the start of flight.” In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 44b" href="/Sotah.44b">Babli, 44b</a>, the text is corrected as it is here.</i>”.
Rebbi Joḥanan said, usage is between them; Rebbi Jehudah called a war of choice a war of obligation. “But in a war of duty everybody goes out, even a groom from his room and a bride from her bridal chamber.” Rav Ḥisda said<sup class="footnote-marker">235</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 44b" href="/Sotah.44b">Babli, 44b</a>, this is the opinion of Rav Ḥisda’s son-in-law Rava.</i>, they disagree. The rabbis say, a war of obligation was the war of David. A war of duty was the war of Joshua. Rebbi Jehudah called it a war of obligation if we went out against them<sup class="footnote-marker">236</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, this is restricted to a preventive attack.</i>; a war of duty if they attacked us. It is written: “King Asa proclaimed in all of Jehudah, nobody is free<sup class="footnote-marker">237</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Kings 15:22" href="/I_Kings.15.22"><i>1K</i>. 15:22</a>.</i>”. What means “nobody is free”? Rebbi Simon and the rabbis. Rebbi Simon said, nobody is free to return home even for one hour<sup class="footnote-marker">238</sup><i class="footnote">He abolished the exemption of the newlywed, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:5" href="/Deuteronomy.24.5"><i>Deut</i>. 24:5</a>.</i>. But the rabbis say, there is no exemption for a scholar, son of a scholar<sup class="footnote-marker">239</sup><i class="footnote">Who claim a general exemption from public works. In the opinion of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 10a" href="/Sotah.10a">Babli, 10a</a>, he abolished the exemptions both of the newlywed and of the scholar.</i>.

Chapter 9



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> [The portion of] the calf whose neck was broken<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">To atone for an unsolved murder; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 7:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.7.2.1">Chapter 7:2, Note 36</a>.</i>, in the holy language. For it is said<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 21:1" href="/Deuteronomy.21.1"><i>Deut</i>. 21:1</a>.</i>: “If a murder victim be found on the land, etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 21:2" href="/Deuteronomy.21.2"><i>Deut</i>. 21:2</a>.</i> Then your Elders and judges should go out.” Three from the High Court in Jerusalem had to go out<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 1:2:1-4:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.1.2.1-4.2">Mishnah <i>Sanhedrin</i> 1:3</a> and <i>Sifry Deut</i>, 205, this opinion is ascribed to R. Simeon.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah says five, since it is written “your Elders”, two, “and your judges”, two<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Since a plural means at least two, an unspecified plural always means 2. (Cf. H. Guggenheimer, <i>Logical Problems in Jewish Tradition</i>, in: Confrontations with Judaism, P. Longworth, ed., London 1966; pp. 174–175).</i>.
