Since no court may have an even number of members<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">So that an immediate verdict can be rendered in all cases that come before the court.</i>, one adds another one.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “The calf whose neck was broken,” etc. It is reasonable only from “they begin and say”<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the discussion which shows that the Levites and the Elders have to recite the verses as written, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 7:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.7.2.2">Chapter 7:2, Notes 43</a> ff.</i>. He starts from the beginning of the paragraph<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The Tanna of the Mishnah quotes from v. 1 when the recitation starts only at v. 9, to explain that the rules of the ceremony have to be deduced from the earlier verses.</i>. “If there be found,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 21:1" href="/Deuteronomy.21.1"><i>Deut</i>. 21:1</a>.</i>” not in a time when they are frequent<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 9:9:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.9.9.1">Mishnah 9</a>. The same statement in <i>Sifry Deut</i>. 205.</i>. “If there be found,” not that you have to search<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">If the body of a missing person is found after a search, this does not qualify for the ceremony.</i>. “If there be found,” finding anywhere is only by witnesses<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">The High Court in Jerusalem will be involved only if two witnesses of good character testify that the corpse was found.</i>. “It is not known who killed him”, therefore if it was known, even by a male or female slave, one did not break the neck<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">To avoid the ceremony, even a single witness of questionable character is sufficient whose testimony could not lead to a conviction.</i>. There, you say, if it was known, even by a male or female slave, one did not break the neck, and here, you say so? One for the killer, one for the victim<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">As explained before, to aid the hunt for the killer, one witness is enough. To involve the High Court for the victim, two witnesses are needed.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">This and the next paragraph are also in <i>Roš Haššanah</i> 3:1 (58c 1. 73), partially in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 1:2:2-35" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.1.2.2-35"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 1:2</a> (18c 1. 17).</i> And that which you say that if it was known, even by a male or female slave, one did not break the neck, [is it only] if they say that if we see him we will be able to identify him. Even those who say, if we see him we will not be able to identify him, one did not break the neck since it is written: “Our eyes did not see.” But somebody did see!
If the court saw the killer. Some Tannaïm state, two of them should stand up and testify before them. There are Tannaïm who state that they all should go and testify at another place<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Rosh Hashanah 25b" href="/Rosh_Hashanah.25b">Babli, <i>Roš Haššanah</i> 25b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 90b" href="/Bava_Kamma.90b"><i>Baba qama</i> 90b</a>, makes it clear that a judge who is an accidental witness is by that fact disqualified as judge in the case. There, the <i>baraitot</i> are interpreted to mean that R. Ṭarphon holds that a judge can appear as witness before his colleagues, but R. Aqiba states that a judge as witness has to appear before a different court.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah ben Pazi in the name of Rebbi Ze‘ira: As they differ here, so they differ about testimony for the New Moon<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">And in effect in all court proceedings based on biblical standards. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 21b" href="/Ketubot.21b">Babli, Ketubot 21b</a>, relaxes these standards in rabbinic cases involving money; accepting that a judge from his own knowledge may recognize a signature on a contract.</i>.
Rebbi Jehudah bar Pazi in the name of Rebbi Ze‘ira: “A slain one”, not one in convulsions; “a slain one”, not one in death-throes<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">These are tannaïtic statements in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 45b" href="/Sotah.45b">Babli, 45b</a>, and <i>Sifry Deut</i>. 205.</i>. If one saw him in convulsions and after some time he came back and did not find him, I say that a miracle occurred and he survived. If one saw him in death-throes here and then found him at another place, one measures from the place where he was found. “Which the Eternal, your God, is giving to you,” this excludes places outside the Land. “To inherit it,” this excludes Jerusalem<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">The same argument in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 45b" href="/Sotah.45b">Babli, 45b</a>, where however one accepts the possibility of an opposing opinion. For the details of the argument for the exclusion of Jerusalem from the rules of inheritance, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Orlah 1:2:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Orlah.1.2.4"><i>Orlah</i> 1:2, Note 64</a>.</i> which belongs to all tribes, and following Rebbi Ismael. For Rebbi Ismael said, any “comings” which were said in the Torah mean after fourteen years, seven during which they conquered and seven during which they distributed<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 7:3:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.7.3.4">Chapter 7, Note 80</a>; in detail <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Orlah 1:2:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Orlah.1.2.3"><i>Orlah</i> 1:2, Note 56</a> and the sources mentioned there; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 37a-38a" href="/Kiddushin.37a-38a">Babli <i>Qiddušin</i> 37a–38a</a>; <i>Seder ‘Olam</i> Chapter 11.</i>. Rebbi Phineas ben Rebbi Abun said, we may say that all these fourteen years they were not breaking the neck, for it is written “if there be found,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 21:1" href="/Deuteronomy.21.1"><i>Deut</i>. 21:1</a>.</i>” not in a time when they are frequent<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 9:9:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.9.9.1">Mishnah 9</a>. The same statement in <i>Sifry Deut</i>. 205.</i>. And all these fourteen years they were frequent<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">From the Genizah ms. In the Leiden ms. and <i>editio princeps</i>: נֹאמַר כָּל־אוֹתָן אַרְבַּע עֶשְׂרֶה שָׁנָה מְצוּייָנִים הָיוּ “we may say, all these fourteen years they were identified” (the case simply never happened). There are no conclusive arguments for or against either reading.</i>.
It was stated: How is it done? The emissaries of the court<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">The local court which has to conduct the burial of persons without local relatives.</i> come out, collect all his signs and bury him. They put up a marker on his grave so that the court who come from the stone hall<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">The place of residence of the High Court in Jerusalem.</i> may measure.
<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">This and the following two paragraphs are also in <i>Ma‘aser Šeni</i> 5:1, explained there in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 9:1:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.9.1.2-4">Notes 14–25</a>; <i>Šeqalim</i>1:1, fol. 46a; <i>Mo‘ed Qaṭan</i> 1:1, fol. 80b/c. Similar arguments, in the name of different authorities, in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Moed Katan 5a" href="/Moed_Katan.5a">Babli <i>Mo‘ed Qaṭan</i> 5a</a>.</i> From where about marks? Rebbi Berekhiah, Rebbi Jacob the son of the daughter of Jacob, in the name of Rebbi Onias from Hauran. Rebbi Yose said it in the name of Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Onias from Hauran. Rebbi Ḥizqiah, Rebbi Uziel the son of Rebbi Onias from Hauran in the name of Rebbi Onias from Hauran (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:45" href="/Leviticus.13.45"><i>Lev</i>. 13:45</a>): “impure, impure, he shall call out;” the impurity itself has to call out and say to you: go away! Rebbi Hila in the name of Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Ezekiel 39:15" href="/Ezekiel.39.15"><i>Ez</i>. 39:15</a>): “The emissaries shall crisscross the land; if one sees a bone”, from here that one makes markers for bones. <i>A human</i>, from here that one makes markers for spine and skull. <i>He builds</i>, from here that one makes markers on fixed stones. If you say on loose stones, it would move and make other places impure. <i>Near it</i>, on a place of purity. <i>A marker</i>, from here the marks.
<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Shekalim (Lieberman) 1:5" href="/Tosefta_Shekalim_(Lieberman).1.5">Tosephta <i>Šeqalim</i> 1:5</a>. The Tosephta follows the other Yerushalmi sources which read “pure” in the first sentence, against the mss. here and the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Moed Katan 6a" href="/Moed_Katan.6a">Babli, <i>Moëd Qaṭan</i> 6a</a>, which read “impure”.</i> If one found a single marked stone, even though one should not keep it so, if somebody forms a tent over it he is impure; I say a compressed corpse was under it. If there were two, he who forms a tent over any one of them is pure; between them he is impure. If between them was a ploughed strip they are single stones, between them the area is pure and around them impure.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Shekalim (Lieberman) 1:5" href="/Tosefta_Shekalim_(Lieberman).1.5">Tosephta <i>Šeqalim</i> 1:5</a>. The Tosephta follows the other Yerushalmi sources which read “pure” in the first sentence, against the mss. here and the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Moed Katan 6a" href="/Moed_Katan.6a">Babli, <i>Moëd Qaṭan</i> 6a</a>, which read “impure”.</i>: One does not mark flesh, for perhaps it will decompose. Rebbi Justus bar Shunem asked before Rebbi Mana: Will that not cause pure foods to be retroactively made impure? He said to him, it is better that these should become unusable for a limited time than that [the earth] become unusable forever.
<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The parallel text is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 1:2:36-45" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.1.2.36-45"><i>Sanhedrin</i>1:3</a>.</i> There<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 1:2:1-4:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.1.2.1-4.2">Mishnah <i>Sanhedrin</i> 1:3</a>.</i>, we have stated: The leaning of the hands of the Elders<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:15" href="/Leviticus.4.15"><i>Lev</i>. 4:15</a>. If the entire community (or their representative, the High Court) have erred, the purification offering requires that the Elders shall lean their hands on the head of the bull.</i> and the breaking of the neck of the calf is done by three [judges]; Rebbi Jehudah says, by five. What is the reason of Rebbi Simeon? “The Elders shall lean<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:15" href="/Leviticus.4.15"><i>Lev</i>. 4:15</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 44b" href="/Sotah.44b">Babli 44b</a>. The quote of the verb is unnecessary. The plural “Elders” means “at least two Elders,”, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 9:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.9.1.1">Note 5</a>.</i>,” two. No court has an even number of members; one adds another one, that makes three. What is the reason of Rebbi Jehudah? “They shall lean,” two; “the Elders,” two. No court has an even number of members; one adds another one, that makes five. And for the calf whose neck is broken, what is the reason of Rebbi Simeon? “Your Elders, your judges,<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 21:2" href="/Deuteronomy.21.2"><i>Deut</i>. 21:2</a>; <i>Sifry Deut</i>. 205.</i>” two. No court has an even number of members; one adds another one, that makes three. What is the reason of Rebbi Jehudah? “Your Elders shall go out,” two, “and your judges,” two. No court has an even number of members; one adds, that makes five. Rebbi said, the words of Rebbi Simeon are reasonable for the leaning of the hands and those of Rebbi Jehudah for the breaking of the neck. The words of Rebbi Simeon are reasonable for the leaning of the hands, for he does not refer to “they shall lean”<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Since the number of the verb is in general determined by the number of the subject, this number cannot be considered independent information.</i>. And those of Rebbi Jehudah for the breaking of the neck, for he does not refer to “they shall go out.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">This argument is less clear since R. Simeon likewise does not refer to the verb. The difference between R. Simeon and R. Jehudah is that for R. Jehudah “Elders, judges” represent independent plurals but for R. Simeon one is restricted to “judges who are Elders” (the Babli concurs in this, 34b).</i>” If you would say that the words of Rebbi Jehudah are reasonable for the calf whose neck is broken because he refers to both to “they shall lean” and “they shall go out,” it turns out that you have to say “there shall go out” two, “your Elders” two, “and your judges,” two. No court has an even number of members; one adds another one, that makes seven<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Since this number is never mentioned, no author considers the plural form of the verb as information.</i>. How do the rabbis<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, “the select of your judges”, i. e., one disqualifies any judges who are not members of the High Court.</i> explain “your Elders and your judges”? Your Elders who are your judges<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, “the select of your judges”, i. e., one disqualifies any judges who are not members of the High Court.</i>. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 45a" href="/Sotah.45a">Babli 45a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 14b" href="/Sanhedrin.14b"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 14b</a>.</i>: Rebbi Eliezer ben Jacob says “your Elders,” that is the High Court; “and your judges” these are king and High Priest<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">He requires the entire High Court to appear (Rashi in Babli 45a). The king (or, in a tribal society, the head of the tribe) is involved since he represents the police powers who alone can determine whether the murderer is unknown. The High Priest is involved since he has to send the priests to conduct the ceremony.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If [the corpse] was found hidden in a heap<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Of earth or debris.</i>, or hanging from a tree, or floating on the water, one did not break the neck as it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 21:1" href="/Deuteronomy.21.1"><i>Deut</i>. 21:1</a>. If there only had been written “if one finds a corpse and the murderer is unknown”, one would have had to bring a calf in any case. The added details should be interpreted as necessary conditions in whose absence the rules do not apply.</i> “on the earth” but not hidden in a heap, “fallen” but not hanging from a tree, “on the field” but not floating on the water. If he was found close to the border, a town in which Gentiles live, or a town without a court<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Without a permanent court with authority over the local police. In Mishnaic Hebrew, עיר denotes any cluster of human dwellings, from hamlet to village to town; occasionally it may refer even to a walled city.</i>, one did measure only from a town with a court<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">This is the reading in most mss., including independent Mishnah mss. and those of the Babli. A few mss. and <i>Sifry Deut</i>. 205 show that the clause “one did not measure” does double duty and the meaning of the sentence is: “If it was found close to the border, a town in which Gentiles live, or a town without a court, one did not measure; one did measure only from a town with a court.”</i>. If he was found exactly in the middle between two towns, both bring a total of two calves, the words of Rebbi Eliezer; <sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">This sentence is found only in the Leiden ms., a single Mishnah ms., and <i>Sifry Deut</i>. 206; not in the Rome ms. nor in the Babli. Since the Yerushalmi discusses the position of the Sages but the Babli does not, it is clear that the omission in the Babylonian Mishnah is original.</i> but the Sages say that one town brings one calf whose neck is broken but two towns do not bring two calves, and Jerusalem does not bring a calf whose neck is broken<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Since Jerusalem was not divided as tribal property, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 9:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.9.1.4">Note 24</a>.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It was stated: Rebbi Eliezer<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">In the Genizah ms., the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sotah (Lieberman) 9:1" href="/Tosefta_Sotah_(Lieberman).9.1">Tosephta (9:1</a>), <i>Sifry Deut</i>. 205, and the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 45b" href="/Sotah.45b">Babli (45b</a>) except for Ashkenazic mss.: R. Eleazar.</i> says, if he was found strangled and hanging from a tree they did not break its neck. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah said, one does not have to say strangled <i>and</i> hanging from a tree, from where even if he was found cast away? The verse<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 21:1" href="/Deuteronomy.21.1"><i>Deut</i>. 21:1</a>. If there only had been written “if one finds a corpse and the murderer is unknown”, one would have had to bring a calf in any case. The added details should be interpreted as necessary conditions in whose absence the rules do not apply.</i> says “fallen”, why does the verse say “slain”? But even if he was found strangled <i>or</i> hanging from a tree they did not break its neck.
Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun stated<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">The word is missing in the Genizah ms. It can be justified in that R. Yose b. Abun recited a <i>baraita</i> on the authority of R. Joḥanan. In the version of the Genizah, this is an amoraic statement intended to brand a contradicting tannaitic one as unreliable.</i> in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: “If he was found standing on his bed and the knife was sticking in his heart, one did not break its neck. Rebbi Simeon ben Ioḥai stated, one did break its neck.” But that of Rebbi Simeon ben Ioḥai is of those which are so-and-so<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">The statement is not from a recognized <i>baraita</i> collection, nor does it contain a name of a tradent which would vouchsafe the text for the 100 years between R. Simeon and R. Joḥanan.</i>. As Rebbi Joḥanan said, all those [statements] of Rebbi Simeon ben Ioḥai which are so-and-so are isolated opinions and one cannot rely on them.
If he was found at the entrance of the city one did break its neck<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 45a" href="/Sotah.45a">Babli sources (45a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sotah (Lieberman) 9:1" href="/Tosefta_Sotah_(Lieberman).9.1">Tosephta 9:1</a>): “one did measure”. In all cases one has to measure the distanec of the corpse from the nearest city even if it is obvious which city is the nearest one.</i> in order to perform the commanded measurement.
<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 6:3:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.6.3.5"><i>Peah</i> 6:3, Notes 78–80</a>.</i> When Rav descended there<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">“There” always means Babylonia; going to Babylonia is “descending,” going to the Land of Israel is “ascending.” The story appears in the same context in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 45a" href="/Sotah.45a">Babli 45a</a>, the actor being Abbaie, three generations after Rav.</i>, he declared: I am this place’s Ben Azai<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Ben Azai, one of the most outstanding students of Rebbi Aqiba, was a walking encyclopedia and used to stroll through the markets of Tiberias, ready to immediately answer any question of Jewish learning. He died during mystical studies before he could marry R. Aqiba’s daughter. In the Talmudim, several Sages are reported to have tried to imitate Ben Azai but all of them were quickly confronted with a question for which they gave the wrong answer or did not know any answer at all. The story is inserted in <i>Peah</i> because a few paragraphs down hidden sheaves will be discussed.</i>. There came an old man and asked him, two slain people, one on top of the other? Rav was of the opinion that one breaks its neck. He told him, one does not break its neck. He asked him, why? He said to him, not the lower one for he is hidden<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">The lower body is not “found”; he is only discovered when the body on top is removed. The upper body is not found <i>on the land</i>.</i>, not the upper one because he floats. When he ascended here, he came to Rebbi, who told him: He told you correctly, “if he is found,” and not “if they are found.” It is reasonable to say “on the land,” not floating on the water, “on a field”, not hidden in a heap. That is how it was stated in the House of Rebbi<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">However, our parallel sources follow the Mishnah.</i>.
The argument of the rabbis seems inverted; there they say to exclude the hidden things<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to Mishnah <i>Peah</i> 6:9, <i>Sifry Deut</i>. 283, interpreting <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:19" href="/Deuteronomy.24.19"><i>Deut</i>. 24:19</a>: If you cut <i>your harvest</i> on <i>your field</i> and you forget a sheaf on <i>the field</i>, you may not return to take it. R. Jehudah says, this excludes anything hidden, the Sages say this includes everything hidden. But in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 21:1" href="/Deuteronomy.21.1"><i>Deut</i>. 21:1</a>, on <i>your field</i> is interpreted as “not hidden in a heap,” excluding any hidden corpse.</i>, but here they say to include the hidden things. There, “your field,” in the open, to exclude that which is hidden; “your harvest,” in the open, to exclude that which is hidden. It is a restriction after a restriction<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">The principle that a restriction after a restriction is an extension is universally accepted in both Talmudim; in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 1:1:2-10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.1.1.2-10"><i>Horaiot</i> 1:1</a> it is generalized to the statement that any sequence of restrictions of an even number of elements is an extension; for an odd number of elements it is a restriction.</i> to add the hidden things. But here, “on the land,” not floating on the water.
“If he was found close to the border,” I say that Saracens killed him. “To a town in which Gentiles live,” I say that Gentiles killed him. “Or a town without a court, one did measure only from a town with a court,” as it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 21:7" href="/Deuteronomy.21.7"><i>Deut</i>. 21:7</a>. Since the members of the local court have to recite the declaration which acknowledges that the crime was perpetrated within the domain of their authority, it is clear that they must come from the seat of the court.</i>: “Our hands did not spill this blood.”
What is the reason of the rabbis? As it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 21:3" href="/Deuteronomy.21.3"><i>Deut</i>. 21:3</a>.</i>: “It shall be <i>the town</i> close to the slain.” What is the reason of Rebbi Eliezer? As it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 21:2" href="/Deuteronomy.21.2"><i>Deut</i>. 21:2</a>. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 16b" href="/Bekhorot.16b">Babli, <i>Bekhorot</i>16b</a>, which never quotes the Sages, disagrees, and holds that for R. Eliezer it is possible to determine lengths with the utmost precision because if it were possible to measure only with limited precision, the two towns together could bring one calf and stipulate that it should count for that town which really is closest if unlimited precision were possible.</i>: “They shall measure to <i>the towns</i> which surround the slain.”
What is the status of cities of refuge<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">The levitic cities which served as refuges for the homicide. These cities certainly have a court which has to supervise the trial of the homicide. The problem alluded to here is not mentioned in the Babli; the main reference in the Yerushalmi is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Makkot 2:6:2-7:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Makkot.2.6.2-7.4"><i>Makkot</i>2:7</a> (fol. 32a); the statement is alluded to in <i>Ma‘aser Šeni</i> 5:8 (fol. 56d, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 9:8:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.9.8.2">Note 165</a>). If the Levitical cities and the cities of refuge were parcelled out to their inhabitants, they would own land given by God and would be under the obligation of the calf, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 21:1" href="/Deuteronomy.21.1"><i>Deut</i>. 21:1</a>. But if the cities were tribal property and the houses given to the inhabitants as living quarters by the tribal council, not as property, then individual Levites and Cohanim never obtained land and their court cannot participate in the ceremony.</i>? If you say, they were given as distribution, they bring a calf whose neck is broken. If you say, they were given as living quarters, they do not bring a calf whose neck is broken.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> From where did they measure? Rebbi Eliezer says, from his navel. Rebbi Aqiba says, from his nose. Rebbi Eliezer ben Jacob said, from the place which makes him slain, from his neck.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “From where did they measure? Rebbi Eliezer says, from his navel”, from the place the child is formed. “Rebbi Aqiba says, from his nose,” from the place from which one is identified<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 45b" href="/Sotah.45b">Babli, 45b</a>, agrees for the reason of R. Eliezer and disagrees for R. Aqiba.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">The rest of the paragraph is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 16:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.16.3"><i>Yebamot</i> 16:3, Notes 39–44</a>.</i> This follows what Rav Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Yebamot</i>: R. Jeremiah.</i> said in the name of Rav: “The recognition of their faces testified about them<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 3:9" href="/Isaiah.3.9"><i>Is</i>. 3:9</a>.</i>,” that is the nose. Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba said, if somebody does not want to be recognized, he should put a patch on his nostrils, then he will not be recognized. As in the following: In the times of king Ursicinus, some people from Sepphoris were under arrest warrants. They put patches on their nostrils and were not recognized. Finally they were denunciated and all were caught because of lies.
“Rebbi Eliezer ben Jacob said, from the place which makes him slain, from his neck.” What is the reason? Rebbi Simon in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: “To put you<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">The sword, mentioned in the preceding verse; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ezekiel 21:34" href="/Ezekiel.21.34"><i>Ez</i>. 21:34</a>. The implication is that חלל means “slain by the sword” and that most victims of the sword have their throats cut. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 45b" href="/Sotah.45b">Babli agrees, 45b</a>.</i> on the throats of the corpses of sinners.”

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If his head is found at another place than his body, one brings<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">For burial since an unknown slain person must be buried at the place he was found. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 45b" href="/Sotah.45b">Babli, 45b</a>, and most Mishnah mss., the positions of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 9:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.9.3.1">Mishnaiot 3</a> and 4 are switched.</i> his head to his body, the words of Rebbi Eliezer; Rebbi Aqiba says, the body to his head.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Eleazar said, they disagree about burial<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 45b" href="/Sotah.45b">Babli, 45b</a>, R. Isaac based on tannaitic sources.</i>. Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman in the name of Rebbi Jonathan: When do they disagree? If the head was higher but the body lower. But if the head was lower and the body higher everybody agrees that one brings the head to the body<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">Because the body fell where he was killed but the head rolled down the incline.</i>. If there was an incline<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">The corresponding Babli word is מדרון.</i> on both sides, do I say that the head fell to one side and the body to the other<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">If head and body are found at two different sides of a mountain ridge.</i> or does this head have no body and that body no head? If it was level, any distance it is usual to chop it off<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">It is reasonable to assume that a chopped-off head would fall so far away from the body which fell at the place he was killed.</i> is one body; otherwise, the head has no body and the body no head<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">And they must be buried separately.</i>.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> When the Elders of Jerusalem had completed their work<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">To determine which town is responsible for bringing the calf.</i> and left, the Elders of that town bring “a cattle calf, one which never had been used for work, which never had drawn under a yoke.<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 21:3" href="/Deuteronomy.21.3"><i>Deut</i>. 21:3</a>.</i>” Bodily defects do not disqualify it. One brings it down to an <i>etan</i> valley; the meaning of <i>etan</i> is “hard”<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">Not ploughable.</i>. Even if it is not hard it is qualified. One breaks its neck with a stiletto from behind. Its place is forbidden to be used for sowing or agricultural works; it may be used to card flax or to hew stones.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One which never had been used for work,” intentionally, “which never had drawn under a yoke,” intentionally or unintentionally<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">The problem discussed in this paragraph is that if no work ever had been done with the calf, it should be obvious that it never was harnessed under a yoke to draw anything. Therefore the mention of the yoke must give harnessing the animal a status different from any other agricultural work. For the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 46a" href="/Sotah.46a">Babli, 46a</a>, imposing a yoke disables the calf whether it was used for work or not, while other agricultural work disables only if it was actually used. This avoids the problem discussed here.<br>The intention here is that of the farmer who has to decide to use his calf for work. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 26" href="/Pesachim.26">Babli, <i>Pesaḥim</i> 26a/b</a>, gives as example that if the farmer lets the calf accompany its mother while she is threshing and by this helps in threshing, it remains acceptable but if the farmer intends that the calf should suckle and thereby help in threshing, it becomes disqualified.</i>. Rebbi Jonah explains the Mishnah: “One which never had been used for work,” intentionally, “which never had drawn under a yoke,” intentionally or unintentionally, if it actually had drawn<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">Imposing the yoke does not disqualify the calf, only actually drawing (a cart or a plow) does.</i>. Rebbi Yose explained the Mishnah: Intentionally even if it had not drawn<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">In that case, imposing the yoke on the calf disqualifies it.</i>, unintentionally if it actually had drawn. It turns out that Rebbi Jonah follows Rebbi Ismael and Rebbi Yose the rabbis. Rebbi Jonah follows Rebbi Ismael, since Rebbi Ismael says, anything which was in a set and left the set to teach leaves its set and stands in its new meaning<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">This is a version of the hermeneutical rules not found in the Babli or the <i>Sifra</i>; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 7:4:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.7.4.5">Chapter 7, Note 108</a>. Drawing ploughs or carts was part of the set of works for which cattle are used. If drawing is singled out for separate attention, in this opinion the general rules valid for agricultural works, that the animal has to be made to work, indicated by the passive, are no longer valid. The active mode seems to imply that if the calf drew something on its own initiative, it still will be disabled.<br>This rule is not to be identified with the 12th rule (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Braita d'Rabbi Yishmael" href="/Sifra,_Braita_d'Rabbi_Yishmael"><i>Sifra</i> Introduction</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Temurah 13b" href="/Temurah.13b">Babli <i>Temurah</i> 13b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 25b" href="/Shevuot.25b"><i>Šebuot</i> 25b</a>): “Anything which was in a set and was taken out to be judged by different rules you cannot restore into the set unless the verse does this explicitly.” Once an animal was dedicated as sacrifice, it is forbidden to exchange it (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:10" href="/Leviticus.27.10"><i>Lev</i>. 27:10</a>). The same is true for animal tithes (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:33" href="/Leviticus.27.33"><i>Lev</i>. 27:33</a>), which are sacrifices. The fact that the rules are spelled out again for tithes (whose status of sanctity is automatic) shows that the differences in the rules are intended; one cannot infer from the rules of one for those of the other.</i>. Therefore, he has to spell out “one which never had been used for work,” intentionally, “which never had drawn under a yoke,” intentionally or unintentionally, if it actually had drawn<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">Since the verse puts the emphasis on drawing, not on carrying the yoke.</i>. Rebbi Yose follows the rabbis, since the rabbis say, anything which was in a set and left the set to teach remains in its set and in its new meaning<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">This is close to the formulation of rule 9 (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Braita d'Rabbi Yishmael" href="/Sifra,_Braita_d'Rabbi_Yishmael"><i>Sifra</i>, Introduction</a>): “Anything which was in a set and left the set to teach did not leave to teach about itself alone but to teach about the entire set.” The new teaching is that active drawing automatically disables even if not desired by the farmer. This implies that drawing is only an example; the same rule will be valid for any agricultural work.</i>. Therefore, he has to spell out: intentionally even if it had not drawn, unintentionally if it actually had drawn.
How far would it have to draw<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">To become disqualified.</i>? Rebbi said, its<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">The yoke’s width. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 46a" href="/Sotah.46a">Babli, 46a</a>, this is defined as one handbreadth (4 thumbbreadths). Rebbi’s opinion is quoted as R. Joḥanan’s in the Babli.</i> width. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah said, three finger breadths. Rebbi Simon in the name of Rebbi Yose ben Nehorai said, the entire length of the yoke in its width<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">The length of the yoke is measured by the breadth of the animal. This opinion is mentioned as a possibility in the Babli only to be immediately dismissed.</i>.
Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya asked before Rebbi Ze‘ira: “One which never had been used for work,” a general statement, “which never had drawn under a yoke,” a detailed statement. If a general statement is followed by a detail, the general does not contain more than the detail<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">The fifth hermeneutical rule. Since the detail is logically contained in the general, its mention serves as definition for the general statement. The same objection is noted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 46a" href="/Sotah.46a">Babli, 46a</a>; the short answer given in the Babli is intelligible only in the light of the Yerushalmi.</i>. He said to him, if it were written “which never had worked, which never had drawn,” you would be justified; but it is written “one which never had been used for work.” This does not describe general and detail, but the additional reference to “yoke” for an equal cut<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">The second hermeneutical rule used in an extended sense, not only that a word has identical meaning in all its occurrences in the Pentateuch but also that all rules connected with it are identical in the two cases being compared. This extended rule requires that the word be <i>free</i>, i. e., not needed to derive other rules. It is difficult to construct the word “yoke” as free in the case of the red cow.</i>. Since relative to “yoke” said for the calf He treated all work as a yoke<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>gezerah šawah</i> invoked by R. Ze‘ira strictly follows the interpretation of (the later) R. Yose. The interpretation of R. Jonah therefore is rejected implicitly.</i>, so for the “yoke” said for the cow<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">The red cow whose ashes are used for purification from the impurity of the dead, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19" href="/Numbers.19"><i>Num</i>. 19</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19:2" href="/Numbers.19.2">Verse 19:2</a> states that the cow (1) must be unblemished and (2) cannot have borne a yoke. Condition (1) is not mentioned for the calf and is explicitly excluded by the Mishnah. (2) implies that the cow is disabled even if no work was ever done or intended. In rabbinic interpretation, the cow had “borne a yoke” if she was mounted by a male.</i> we have to treat all work as a yoke<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">Work is not mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19:2" href="/Numbers.19.2"><i>Num</i>. 19:2</a>; its prohibition is inferred from the case of the calf.</i>. Since relative to “yoke” said for the calf work disables whether intentional or unintentional<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">Following R. Yose, the yoke disables irrespective of intention (with some qualifications). Since R. Yose extends the rules of the yoke to all work, he will do the same for the red cow.</i>, so for the “yoke” said for the cow work disables whether intentional or unintentional. Since relative to “yoke” said for the cow, the yoke disables<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">For the cow, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19:2" href="/Numbers.19.2"><i>Num</i>. 19:2</a> makes it clear that even if a yoke accidentally fell on the cow, it disables permanently.</i>, also for the “yoke” said for the calf the yoke disables. Then since for the “yoke” said for the cow blemishes disable, so also for the “yoke” said for the calf blemishes disable<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">This would contradict the Mishnah.</i>? The verse<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19:2" href="/Numbers.19.2"><i>Num</i>. 19:2</a>.</i> says, “where <i>this one</i> has no blemish.” Blemishes disable the cow, blemishes do not disable the calf<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">The same argument <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 46a" href="/Sotah.46a">Babli 46a</a>, in an expanded version <i>Sifry Num</i>. 123.</i>. Then it also says<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 21:3" href="/Deuteronomy.21.3"><i>Deut</i>. 21:3</a>.</i> “<i>one</i> which never had been used for work,” for this one work disables, does for the cow work not disable? Can you say there, “where <i>this one</i> has no blemish,” this one is disabled by blemishes, sacrifices are not disabled by blemishes?<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">This is blatantly false, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 22:17-25" href="/Leviticus.22.17-25"><i>Lev</i>. 22:17–25</a>. Therefore, the only case which the emphatic <i>this one</i> excludes is that of the calf. In <i>Sifry Num</i>. 123, it is concluded that the cow must be unblemished but not the Cohen who burns it in a ceremony outside the sanctuary.</i>
Would missing limbs disable it? That would not be even like sacrifices of the descendants of Noah<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">All of mankind, who have to follow the “natural law” given to Noah. It is accepted both in the Yerushalmi (also <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 9:4:2-10:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.9.4.2-10.2"><i>Pesaḥim</i> 9:4</a> 36d line 71, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 1:11:2-8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.1.11.2-8"><i>Megillah</i>1:13</a> 72b line 39) and in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 5b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.5b">Babli (<i>Avodah zarah</i> 5b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 51a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.51a">51a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 116a" href="/Zevachim.116a"><i>Zebaḥim</i> 116a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Temurah 7a" href="/Temurah.7a"><i>Temurah</i> 7a</a>) that a Gentile’s sacrifice on his own altar is accepted by Heaven if only the animal is of the pure kinds and not missing anything observable from the outside. Only in the Temple are Gentile sacrifices subject to the rules of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:21-25" href="/Leviticus.21.21-25"><i>Lev</i>. 21:21–25</a>. The same verse is quoted in the Babli as in the Yerushalmi.</i>! Did not Rebbi Yasa say that Rebbi Eleazar explained to the colleagues: “From all living, from all flesh<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 6:19" href="/Genesis.6.19"><i>Gen</i>. 6:19</a>. The interpretation of the verse is: From all living creatures, each one with his entire flesh, i. e., a whole body.</i>,” that they should be complete in their limbs? There, some were for the altar<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">Since Noaḥ used some of the animals for elevation sacrifices (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 8:20" href="/Genesis.8.20"><i>Gen</i>. 8:20</a>) it is inferred that all pure animals entering his ark were acceptable sacrifices.</i>, but here<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">The calf is buried at the place it is killed.</i>, the altar has no part. Rebbi Ḥuna in the name of Rebbi Jeremiah: Since you say, purgation is written there as for sacrifices<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 21:8" href="/Deuteronomy.21.8"><i>Deut</i>. 21:8</a>; the purpoise of the entire ceremony is to purge the Land from the guilt of innocent blood. The goal of purgation is spelled out for all purification and reparation offerings in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4" href="/Leviticus.4"><i>Lev</i>. 4</a> ff.</i>, it is as if some of it were for the altar. Does <i>ṭerefa</i><sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">Here, the word is used in its original meaning (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:30" href="/Exodus.22.30"><i>Ex</i>. 22:30</a>) “Flesh torn on the field you shall not eat,” meaning that a carcass torn by a predator is forbidden as food. From this root, the technical term <i>ṭerefa</i> means that any life-threatening injury forbids the afflicted animal as food. There is no difference whether the injury is caused by outside action or sickness. For example, tuberculous lesions of the lung are a main source of <i>ṭerefa</i>. Obviously there are two kinds of <i>ṭerefa</i>: those that can be observed by outward inspection and those which can only be noted when the animal is cut up after slaughter. Since all animals offered on the altar (except birds) must be offered in pieces, it is clear that all kinds of <i>ṭerefa</i> are forbidden for the altar. But in the case of the calf, only what is verifiable on the living animal is intended. The position of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 11a" href="/Chullin.11a">Babli, <i>Ḥulin</i> 11a</a>, is that most animals do not have life-threatening internal injuries and, therefore, one may assume that a healthy-looking calf is healthy. (The list of what is considered life-threatening is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Chullin 3:1" href="/Mishnah_Chullin.3.1">Mishnah <i>Ḥulin</i> 3:1</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Chullin 3:3" href="/Mishnah_Chullin.3.3">3</a>).</i> disable it? Since you say, it is as if some of it were for the altar, in that it should be clear to you that <i>ṭerefa</i> disables it. If its foot was undeveloped, as a donkey’s foot<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">The calf does not have split hooves.</i>, how do you treat that? If you treat it as if missing a limb, can it be enabled<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">It would seem natural to emend the text by switching the places of “enabled” and “disabled” and consider the text as declarative sentences. That is what all commentators and editors do but it is unacceptable since even the Genizah ms., which derives from an archetype different from the Leiden and Rome mss., does have “enabled” and “disabled” in the positions indicated. It seems necessary to treat the sentences as rhetorical questions.</i>? If you treat it as a bodily defect, can it be disabled<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">Since it was slated in the Mishnah that bodily defects do not disable.</i>? Does its blood prepare<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">If some of the killed calf’s blood comes in touch with fresh food, does it make that food susceptible to ritual impurity. For preparation for impurity, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 2:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.2.3.2"><i>Demay</i> 2, Note 141</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 1:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.1.1.3"><i>Terumot</i> 1, Note 9</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Makhshirin 6:4" href="/Mishnah_Makhshirin.6.4">Mishnah <i>Makhširin</i> 6:4</a>.</i>? Since Rebbi Ḥiyya stated that its blood is impure<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">Since any impure fluid is impure at least in the first degree, it transmits its impurity and, therefore, does prepare for impurity.</i>, its blood prepares.
An old man asked the rabbis of Caesarea: Does the breaking of its neck clear its <i>ṭerefa</i> from its impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">In all cases of ritual slaughter except for the calf, cutting the throat שחיטה is required. The same is prescribed for profane slaughter of animals intended as food (Deut. 12:21). Any animal which is killed in any other way becomes a carcass whose impurity is original and severe (Lev. 11:39-40). The question arises naturally whether the carcass of the calf is severely impure, not having been slaughtered in the required form, or maybe is pure since its neck was broken by divine decree.</i>? They said to him, is that not a Mishnah: “He who cuts the throat and it turns out to be <i>ṭerefa.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Chullin 6:2" href="/Mishnah_Chullin.6.2">Mishnah <i>Ḥulin</i> 6:2</a>. This refers to the obligation to cover a wild animal’s blood with earth if it was slaughtered for food (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 17:13" href="/Leviticus.17.13"><i>Lev</i>. 17:13</a>). R. Meïr holds that the intent is all that counts, even if it should turn out in the end that the animal could not be used as food. The argument here is that the animal was slaughtered in the way prescribed and the fact that it is forbidden for consumption (or for any use) should be irrelevant.</i>” He said to them, but I asked about breaking the neck and you answer me cutting the throat! They said to him, but did not Rebbi Yannai say<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">This is quoted in full in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 9:6:3-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.9.6.3-7">Halakhah 6</a>. The statement refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 22:28" href="/Leviticus.22.28"><i>Lev</i>. 22:28</a>: “Cattle or sheep, you should not cut their and their calves throats on the same day”. There is one opinion which holds that guilt is incurred only if both animals are validly slaughtered as food on the name day. It is generally acknowledged in both Talmudim that R. Meïr considers even ineffective cutting the throat as falling under all rules of “cutting the throat.” In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 9:6:3-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.9.6.3-7">Halakhah 6</a>, the reading of the mss. is אפילו עריפה חייב “even for breaking its neck he is guilty.” It seems that this should be the reading here also, but unfortunately the Genizah fragment is illegible at this spot. Since the reading as written is not impossible, it is better not to disturb the text.</i>, the words of Rebbi Meïr, even if it was <i>ṭerefa</i> he is guilty? He said to them, you answer me from that of Rebbi Yannai<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 9:6:3-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.9.6.3-7">Halakhah 6</a>, R. Simeon ben Laqish disagrees with R. Yannai. Therefore, there is no proof along these lines.<br>The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 70b" href="/Zevachim.70b">Babli, <i>Zebaḥim</i> 70b</a>, argues that the dead calf must be pure since “purification is written there as for sacrifices” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 9:5:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.9.5.5">Note 110</a>).</i>?
“The meaning of <i>etan</i> is “hard”, “Hard is your seat, make your nest in the rock<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 24:21" href="/Numbers.24.21"><i>Num</i>. 24:21</a>. The same quote in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 46" href="/Sotah.46">Babli. 46a/b</a>.</i>.” “Even if it is not hard it is qualified.” Similarly, if there is no brook? Rebbi Simeon ben Ioḥai stated, “they shall bring down, and they shall bring down,” even if there is no brook<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 21:4" href="/Deuteronomy.21.4"><i>Deut</i>. 21:4</a>. While the <i>vaw</i> starting the sentence is quite necessary as <i>vaw consecutive</i>, R. Simeon wants to construe it as unnecessary addition which enlarges the domain in which the calf could have its neck broken. There are no parallels to this opinion.</i>. But that of Rebbi Simeon ben Ioḥai is of those which are so-and-so<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">The statement is not from a recognized <i>baraita</i> collection, nor does it contain a name of a tradent which would vouchsafe the text for the 100 years between R. Simeon and R. Joḥanan.</i>. As Rebbi Joḥanan said, all those [statements] of Rebbi Simeon ben Ioḥai which are so-and-so are isolated opinions and one cannot rely on them.
Does he break its neck until he has cut two blood arteries<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">The carotid arteries leading to the brain and the windpipe which must be cut in ritual slaughter.</i> or most of them? Let us hear from the following, that Rebbi Ze‘ira said, Rav Jehudah in the name of Samuel: Any place the bottom of the neck is acceptable for slaughtering, the back of the neck is acceptable for breaking<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">This breaking, מְלִיקָה, is done by the finger nail on the neck of a pidgeon used as a purification offering. Since it says “he may not separate (the head from the body)” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:8" href="/Leviticus.5.8"><i>Lev</i>. 5:8</a>), it is clear that the Cohen has to cut the arteries and the windpipe. In the absence of an indication to the contrary, this requirement may be transferred to the breaking of the calf’s neck.</i>; here he would say the same.
There<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Makkot 3:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Makkot.3.7.1">Mishnah <i>Makkot</i> 3:9</a>. A person who is both a Cohen and a Nazir ploughing in a graveyard in which both vines and grain grow, on a holiday of a Sabbatical year, with an ox and a donkey together which both were dedicated to the Temple.</i>, we have stated: “One might plough one furrow and infringe thereby on eight prohibitions.” Rebbi Hoshaia asked: Could one not state “one who ploughs the place where the calf’s neck was broken,” for a total of nine<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">The same questions in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Makkot 22a" href="/Makkot.22a">Babli, <i>Makkot</i> 22a</a>. The obvious answer is that this could not be a graveyard in which both vines and grain grow.</i>? The colleagues said before Rebbi Yose: Explain it by the past<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">Since verse 21:4 says of the hard valley: “which would not be worked on and would not be sown”. The imperfect may be interpreted as a past.</i>. He said to them, since it was for the past, so certainly for the future<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">The primary meaning of the imperfect is as a future.</i>.
The place where it<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">The primary meaning of the imperfect is as a future.</i> falls down and its surroundings are forbidden. How much are its surroundings? Four cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">Where the calf falls down. The <i>hapax</i> גיסה in the opinion of S. Lieberman may be Syriac. In the two Tosephta mss. (9:1) the readings are גיתה, גיזתה.</i>. Rebbi says, I am saying that its surroundings are fifty cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">In the Tosephta: 40 cubits. Since no reason is given for that number and it does not appear in other agricultural contexts, the reading of the Yerushalmi is preferable. The Genizah ms. is not available at this point. The notion that a strip 4 cubits wide is attached to a given domain appears in several contexts, agricultural (as boundary strip betwen fields) and not agricultural (as private domain on the Sabbath, as reserved domain in front of a house in a multi-family courtyard).</i>. Rebbi Samuel the son of Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun: “which would not be worked on,” a general statement. “And would not be sown,” a detail. If a general statement is followed by a detail, the general does not contain more than the detail<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">The fifth hermeneutical rule. Since the detail is logically contained in the general, its mention serves as definition for the general statement. The same objection is noted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 46a" href="/Sotah.46a">Babli, 46a</a>; the short answer given in the Babli is intelligible only in the light of the Yerushalmi.</i>. He said to him, if it were written “which would not be worked on, which would not be sown,” you would be justified; but it is written “which would not be worked on.” This does not describe general statement and detail, but an additional reference<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">That “work” means agricultural use, but that industrial use of the place is permitted.</i>.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The Elders of that town wash their hands with water at the place where the calf's neck was broken and say<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">Deut. 21:7. The Yerushalmi version of this Mishnah is rather shorter than the Babli one. The Yerushalmi version is that of Sifry Deut., 210.</i>: "Our hands did not spill this blood," etc. Did we ever think that the court was spilling blood? But, that he did not come to our attention and we sent him away, or that we did not see him and left him<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">The court can perform the ceremony only if it did not tolerate a breakdown of social services.</i>. <br>The priests say<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 21:8" href="/Deuteronomy.21.8"><i>Deut</i>. 21:8</a>.</i>: “Purge for Your people Israel which You did redeem, o Eternal.” They did not have to say that<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 21:8" href="/Deuteronomy.21.8"><i>Deut</i>. 21:8</a>.</i> “the blood was purged for them,” but the Holy Spirit announces to them that when they follow this procedure the blood will be purged for them. If the killer was found before the calf should have its neck broken, it should leave<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">It becomes totally profane.</i> and graze in the flock. If after the calf’s neck was broken it should be buried at its place because if came from the start in a doubtful case; it purged its doubt and left. If the killer was found after the calf’s neck was broken, he should be executed<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">The calf atones for the community, not the murderer.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> The rabbis here explain the verse<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">In all occurrences, the Genizah ms. reads "the Mishnah", which is the more accurate text.</i> about the murderer, the rabbis there explain the verse about the murder victim. The rabbis here explain the verse about the murderer, "he did not come to our attention and we sent him away," and did not execute him; "or that we did not see him and left him," and muddled his trial. But the rabbis there<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">Babli 46b; an extended version by R. Joshua ben Levi, insisting that unsolved murder cases exist only if social services arc insufficient, Babli 38b.</i> explain the verse about the murder victim, "he did not come to our attention and we sent him away," without company; "or that we did not see him and left him," without provisions.
The Elders wash their hands with water at the place where the calf’s neck was broken and say<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">The court can perform the ceremony only if it did not tolerate a breakdown of social services.</i>: “Our hands did not spill this blood,” etc. The priests say<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 21:8" href="/Deuteronomy.21.8"><i>Deut</i>. 21:8</a>.</i>: “Purge for Your people Israel.” The Holy Spirit says, “the blood was purged for them.” Three verses were said on one topic, what this one said the other did not say, and vice-versa. Similarly, you say<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 38:25-26" href="/Genesis.38.25-26"><i>Gen</i>. 38:25–26</a>. A different version in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Makkot 23b" href="/Makkot.23b">Babli, <i>Makkot</i> 23b</a>.</i>: “She said, please recognize this seal, strings, and staff,” so far Tamar spoke. “Jehudah recognized and said, she is more just than I am because I did not give her to my son Shelah,” so far Jehudah spoke. The Holy Spirit said, “he never knew her again.<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">On could also translate: He never stopped knowing her.</i>” Three verses were said on one topic, what this one said the other did not say, and vice-versa. Similarly, you say<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 13:27-30" href="/Numbers.13.27-30"><i>Num</i>. 13:27–30</a>. The interpretation saves Joshua’s reputation, who is silent in the biblical narrative.</i>: “They told him and said, we came to the land you sent us to; it really is flowing of milk and honey and that is its fruit;” so far Joshua spoke. “But the people are strong who dwell in the land, the cities are very highly fortified, and also the young giants we saw there;” so far said the spies. “Caleb silenced the people to Moses and said, we certainly can mount and inherit it, for certainly we can do it;” so far Caleb spoke. Three verses were said on one topic, what this one said the other did not say, and vice-versa. Similarly, you say<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Judges 5:28-31" href="/Judges.5.28-31"><i>Jud</i>. 5:28–31</a>.</i>: “Sisera’s mother looked out and whimpered, etc.;” so far Sisera’s mother spoke. “Her wise princesses answered her, …, will they not find and distribute booty;” so far said her daughters-in-law. And the Holy Spirit says, “so all enemies of the Eternal will be lost.”
Rebbi Phineas said, it is worthy to provide purification all the time back to the Exodus<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Sifry Deut</i>. 210, this is an anonymous tannaitic statement. A comment which there entered the text from the margin explains: “This (the response of the Holy Spirit to the priest’s prayer) teaches that the homicide sins against the Jewish people in all generations going back to those who left Egypt.”</i>.
Rebbi Yannai said, the words of Rebbi Meïr: Even if one broke its neck he is guilty<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">If a calf has its neck broken the same day its mother was slaughtered for food, cf. Note 120.</i>. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa, Rebbi Immi, in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: The words of Rebbi Meïr: Even if one broke its neck he is guilty. The words of Rebbi Simeon: Even if he cut its throat, he is not punishable<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">R. Simeon holds that any use of the verb שחט in the Pentateuch refers to slaughter either for the altar or human consumption (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 82a" href="/Chullin.82a"><i>Ḥulin</i> 82a</a>). Since the verse which forbids slaughtering mother and young on the same day uses the word תשחטו, it follows that for him only the provision of food either for the altar or humans is forbidden; nothing done with an atoning calf falls under that law.</i>. Rebbi Yannai said, I heard about a boundary, from when it is forbidden: “They shall bring it down,” from the time of bringing down<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 82a" href="/Chullin.82a">Babli, <i>Ḥulin</i> 82a</a>, R. Yannai stated only that he heard that there is a well defined moment in which the calf becomes sanctified and loses its quality as potential food; the members of his Academy determined that moment to be when the calf enters the unworkable area of the valley.</i>. Rebbi Ila said, to free it from “it and its young” following Rebbi Simeon<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">This qualifies the statement of R. Simeon ben Laqish in the name of R. Simeon (ben Ioḥai). If the mother was slaughtered while her calf was still potential profane food, the calf cannot be slaughtered on the same day. If the mother was slaughtered after the calf had lost its status as potential food, even if the calf was wrongly slaughtered by cutting its throat instead of breaking its neck, the prohibition was not infringed upon. The Babli does not follow this argument.</i>.
Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac said, it is holy while still alive. The Mishnah said so: “it should leave and graze in the flock.” What shall it leave? It shall leave its holiness.
Rebbi Mattania said, this is correct<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the statement that if the calf was killed before the murderer was found, the murderer still can be executed.</i>. What is written? “The blood shall be purged <i>for them</i><sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">The community, not the murderer.</i>;” then it is silent. Nevertheless, “but you shall eliminate innocent blood from your midst”<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 21:9" href="/Deuteronomy.21.9"><i>Deut</i>. 21:9</a>. Babylonian sources (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 47b" href="/Sotah.47b"><i>Soṭah</i> 47b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 37b" href="/Ketubot.37b"><i>Ketubot</i> 37b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Keritot 4:3" href="/Tosefta_Keritot.4.3"><i>Tosephta</i> <i>Keritut</i> 4:3</a>) quote an unrelated verse, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 35:33" href="/Numbers.35.33"><i>Num</i>. 35:33</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If one witness says, I saw the murderer<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">He could identify the murderer if he was caught.</i>, and one witness says, I did not see him<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">He saw the murder being committed but did not see the murderer and could not identify him in court. Then the murderer cannot be prosecuted under biblical law. (This means that when caught he could not be executed. It does not exclude that under the king’s police powers he could be punished.)</i>; or a woman<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">Or any other witness who can testify to facts but not in law. (For example, the testimony of gamblers, criminals, women, or slaves cannot be used to impose the death penalty or to notarize documents.)</i> said, I saw, but another woman said, I did not see, they did break the neck. If one witness said I saw, but two said, we did not see, they did break the neck. I two said, we saw, but one said to them, you did not see<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote">He was with them and testifies that from their position it was impossible to see the murderer. Since he is one against two, his testimony is disregarded.</i>, they did not break the neck.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote">See the parallel texts <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 15" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.15"><i>Yebamot</i> 15, Notes 135–138</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 6:4:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.6.4.2-4"><i>Soṭah</i> 6:4</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 47b" href="/Sotah.47b">Babli 47b</a>.</i> Gidul bar Benjamin in the name of Rav: Anywhere they accepted the testimony of a woman parallel to that of a man, a man can contradict a woman and a woman can contradict a man. One should state: “A witness says that he saw the murderer, and a woman says that she did not see; a woman said that he saw and a witness said that he did not see,” as it was stated in the name of Rebbi Neḥemiah: One follows the majority of the testimonies. How is that? Two women against one woman they considered as if there were two witnesses against one witness. What you say refers to a woman and women. But if there were a hundred women against one [male] witness, they are like one witness.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> When professional murderers prevailed, breaking of the calf’s neck became obsolete, when Eleazar ben Dinai came<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote">Josephus, <i>Antiquities</i> XX, 6,1; <i>Jewish War</i> II, 12,4; 13,6; VII, 8,1.</i> who was called Teḥina ben Perisha. They turned to call him murderous son.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Murderous son,” son, professional murderer<sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote">This shows that professional names of the pattern <i>pĕ‘ôlā</i> were felt to be Aramaic, not Hebrew.</i>.

Halakhah 9

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> When the adulterers prevailed, the spell-inducing water stopped and Rabban Joḥanan ben Zakkai<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote">It is unclear when he abolished the ritual of the suspected wife and whether this was part of his program to divorce Judaism from Temple rituals.</i> abolished it, as it is said<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Hosea 4:14" href="/Hosea.4.14"><i>Hos</i>. 4:14</a>.</i>: “I shall not care if your daughters are whoring,” etc.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> For it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Hosea 4:14" href="/Hosea.4.14"><i>Hos</i>. 4:14</a>.</i>, “for they behave like donkeys with the prostitutes,” and it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:27" href="/Numbers.5.27"><i>Num.</i> 5:27</a>.</i>: “The woman will be a swearword among her people.” In times when her people are peaceful, not in times when her people are dissolute. “The man shall be free from sin.<sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:31" href="/Numbers.5.31"><i>Num.</i> 5:31</a>. In the Genizah ms., the entire verse is copied, as required by the following homily: “The man shall be free from sin, then this woman will bear her iniquity.”</i>” When does the woman carry her iniquity? If the man is free from sin<sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote">A more extended treatment in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 47b" href="/Sotah.47b">Babli, 47b</a>; <i>Num. rabba</i> 9(53); <i>Sifry</i> <i>Num</i>. 21. An opposing opinion <i>Sifry zuṭa Naśo</i>31.</i>.

Halakhah 10

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> When Yose ben Yo‘ezer from Ṣereda and Yose ben Joḥanan from Jerusalem died<sup class="footnote-marker">171</sup><i class="footnote">The first couple of leaders mentioned in <i>Abot</i> (1:4).</i>, the grape bunches<sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote">People who united in their personalities knowledge, exemplary behavior, and leadership.</i> disappeared, as it was said<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Micah 7:1" href="/Micah.7.1"><i>Micah</i> 7:1</a>. The reference is to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Micah 7:2" href="/Micah.7.2">v. 2</a>: “The devout man has perished from the earth, there is no upright person among humankind.”</i>: “There is no bunch to eat, no early ripe fig that my soul desires.”
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> There did not arise another grape bunch until Rebbi Aqiba rose. Were the other couples<sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote">Those mentioned in the first Chapter of Mishnah <i>Abot</i> after the two Yose.</i> not also grape bunches? But these served in a position of leadership<sup class="footnote-marker">175</sup><i class="footnote">They served just before the Maccabean revolt; Yose ben Yo‘ezer was killed as an opponent of the Seleucids. It seems that the two were the heads of the ruling council of the province of Judea. After the revolt, the political power was totally divorced from the spiritual leadership and the later couples could not attain the political role played by the first or by R. Aqiba in the Bar Kokhba years.</i>; the others did not serve in a position of leadership.
It was stated: It is possible to raise questions about all couples<sup class="footnote-marker">177</sup><i class="footnote">The president (נָשִׂיא) and executive officer (אַב בֵּית דִּין) of the high court of the nation at any given moment.<br>The text here is the opposite of the tradition of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Temurah 15b" href="/Temurah.15b">Babli (<i>Temurah</i> 15b</a>) which states that the couples who held office between Moses and the two Yoses either taught Torah as well as Moses did or that it was impossible to find anything questionable about them.</i> from the time that Moses died until Yose ben Yoezer from Ṣereda and Joseph ben Joḥanan from Jerusalem. It is possible to raise questions about all couples from the time that Yose ben Yoezer from Ṣereda and Joseph ben Joḥanan from Jerusalem died until Rebbi Jehudah ben Baba arose. They said about Rebbi Jehudah ben Baba that all he did was in the name of Heaven except that he raised an animal of the flock<sup class="footnote-marker">178</sup><i class="footnote">There exists a rabbinic rule (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Temurah 15b" href="/Temurah.15b">Babli <i>Temurah</i> 15b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 80a" href="/Bava_Kamma.80a"><i>Baba qama</i> 80a</a>) which forbids raising sheep and goats in agricultural lands because their destructive feeding habits.</i>. This happened the following way. Once he fell sick and the medical men went to visit him. They told him: there is no healing for you except freshly warm milk, because he had <i>angina pectoris</i>. What did he do? He bought a goat and bound it to the legs of his bed<sup class="footnote-marker">179</sup><i class="footnote">That it could not roam and damage plants.</i> and when he had an attack of angina he sucked from it freshly warm milk. But when the Sages wanted to visit him, they said how could we enter when the robber<sup class="footnote-marker">180</sup><i class="footnote">Greek λῃστής, “robber”.</i> dwells with him in the house? When he died, he said: I did never sin except that in this case I disregarded the words of my colleagues. After his death they checked all he had done and did not find any sin except that goat alone<sup class="footnote-marker">181</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Temurah 15b" href="/Temurah.15b">Babli, <i>Temurah</i> 15b</a>, tells this story about “a pious man” and doubts whether it is about R. Jehudah ben Baba, who was executed by the Roman government in the aftermath of the war of Bar Kokhba, or R. Jehudah bar Ilai, known simply as R. Jehudah.</i>.
Rebbi Simeon from Shezur said, my father’s family were landowners in Galilee. Why were they ruined? Because they judged civil matters alone<sup class="footnote-marker">182</sup><i class="footnote">While a competent judge may sit alone in judgment, it is bad behavior since only God judges alone (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 1:1:1-3:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.1.1.1-3.3">Mishnah <i>Sanhedrin</i> 1:1</a>).</i> and were raising animals of the flock. And this was so: We had a copse close to the village but a field was between it and us, and the flock entered it going and coming there<sup class="footnote-marker">183</sup><i class="footnote">The goats did damage to agriculture while being driven to the woods. He denies that R. Jehudah ben Baba did anything wrong since he was careful to keep his goat from doing damage and holds him a blameless pious man.</i>.

Halakhah 11

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">184</sup><i class="footnote">This Mishnah and its Halakhah are from <i>Ma‘aser Šeni</i> 5, Mishnah 13, explained there in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 9:10:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.9.10.1">Notes 171ff</a>. to the end of that Tractate.</i> The High Priest Joḥanan disestablished the declaration of tithes. He also eliminated the arousers and the hitters. Up to his days the hammer was hitting in Jerusalem and in his days nobody had to ask about <i>demay</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Jeremiah, Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: Our Mishnah after the people were suspected of giving tithe to Cohanim. There is support for Rebbi Joḥanan in one and disagreement with him in one. Disagreement with him as we have stated there: “Similarly, the daughter of a Cohen [married] to a Levite should eat neither heave nor tithe.” We understand that she should not eat heave. But tithe any way you take it, if she is a Cohen’s daughter she should eat, if she is a Levite’s wife she should eat. Rebbi Hila in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: [It follows] him who says one does not give tithe to Cohanim. That means, he himself says one gives heave to Cohanim.
It supports him because he says it is all praise, as Rebbi Joḥanan said, Joḥanan the High Priest sent and checked in all localities of Israel and found that they separated only Great Heave. But First and Second Tithes some were separating and some did not. He said, since [omitting] First Tithe is a deadly sin and [omitting] Second Tithe [implies] the sin of <i>ṭevel</i>, a person should give a name to heave and heave of the tithe and give them to the Cohen; Second Tithe he exchanges for coins. About the remaining tithe of the poor, he who has a claim on another person must bring proof.
Why should he not make the declaration? Rebbi Hila said, he causes anger before the Omnipresent by saying “I did” when he did not. If it is so, he who separated should make the declaration, he who did not separate should not make the declaration, as we have stated: “Up to ‘Look down’ they were saying it in an undertone, from ‘Look down’ onwards in a high voice.”
